<div class="tab-pane fade" id="tab-typescript" role="tabpanel">
  <div class="accordion" id="acc-typescript">

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q1">
          <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;Why use TypeScript with React?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q1" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          TypeScript adds static type checking to JavaScript, which catches entire categories of bugs at compile time rather than runtime. In a React application, this means mistyped prop names, incorrect data shapes passed to components, and missing required props are all surfaced immediately in your editor rather than discovered in production. TypeScript also dramatically improves developer experience through autocomplete, inline documentation, and safe refactoring — renaming a prop across an entire codebase becomes a single operation rather than a manual search-and-replace. The TypeScript compiler acts as a living contract between components: if a parent passes the wrong shape of data, the build fails, enforcing correctness across team boundaries. Large React codebases especially benefit because the type system makes it safe to modify shared utilities or component APIs with confidence that all call sites are updated. The ecosystem around React has strong TypeScript support — the <code>@types/react</code> package provides comprehensive typings for all React APIs, and most popular libraries ship their own types.
<pre class="code-block">// Without TypeScript — silent runtime bug
function Greeting({ name }) {
  return &lt;h1&gt;Hello, {name.toUpperCase()}&lt;/h1&gt;;
}
&lt;Greeting /&gt; // name is undefined — crashes at runtime

// With TypeScript — compile-time error
interface GreetingProps {
  name: string;
}
function Greeting({ name }: GreetingProps) {
  return &lt;h1&gt;Hello, {name.toUpperCase()}&lt;/h1&gt;;
}
&lt;Greeting /&gt;
// Error: Property 'name' is missing in type '{}'
// Required by type 'GreetingProps'</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q2">
          <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the difference between React.FC and a regular function for components?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q2" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <code>React.FC</code> (also written as <code>React.FunctionComponent</code>) is a generic type that was historically popular for typing React components, but the community has largely moved away from it in favour of plain typed functions. The main practical difference is that <code>React.FC</code> used to implicitly include <code>children</code> in the props type (this was removed in React 18's type definitions), and it always declares the return type as <code>React.ReactElement | null</code>. A plain function with an explicit props interface is now considered the idiomatic approach because it is more explicit, easier to read, and does not carry hidden behaviour. With a regular function you type the props parameter directly and can return <code>JSX.Element</code>, <code>React.ReactNode</code>, or simply let TypeScript infer the return type. The <code>React.FC</code> type also makes it slightly harder to use generics in components. The React team's own documentation now uses plain function syntax with explicit prop types.
<pre class="code-block">// Old style — React.FC (discouraged)
const Button: React.FC&lt;{ label: string }&gt; = ({ label }) =&gt; {
  return &lt;button&gt;{label}&lt;/button&gt;;
};

// Modern idiomatic style — plain function
interface ButtonProps {
  label: string;
  onClick?: () =&gt; void;
}

function Button({ label, onClick }: ButtonProps) {
  return &lt;button onClick={onClick}&gt;{label}&lt;/button&gt;;
}

// Arrow function variant — also fine
const Button = ({ label, onClick }: ButtonProps) =&gt; (
  &lt;button onClick={onClick}&gt;{label}&lt;/button&gt;
);</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q3">
          <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type component props with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q3" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Component props are typed by defining either an <code>interface</code> or a <code>type</code> alias that describes the shape of the props object, then annotating the function parameter. Both <code>interface</code> and <code>type</code> work for this purpose — interfaces are preferred when you might need to extend or merge later, while types work better for unions and computed types. Optional props are marked with <code>?</code>, and you can provide default values via destructuring defaults. It is good practice to name the interface after the component with a <code>Props</code> suffix (e.g., <code>CardProps</code>) so it is easy to find and export for consumers. For components that need to be highly reusable, exporting the props interface is important so downstream components can compose or extend those types. TypeScript will enforce all required props at every call site and provide autocomplete for optional ones.
<pre class="code-block">interface CardProps {
  title: string;            // required
  subtitle?: string;        // optional
  imageUrl?: string;        // optional
  onClick: () =&gt; void;      // required callback
  variant?: 'primary' | 'secondary' | 'ghost'; // literal union
}

function Card({
  title,
  subtitle,
  imageUrl,
  onClick,
  variant = 'primary',  // default value
}: CardProps) {
  return (
    &lt;div className={`card card--${variant}`} onClick={onClick}&gt;
      {imageUrl &amp;&amp; &lt;img src={imageUrl} alt={title} /&gt;}
      &lt;h2&gt;{title}&lt;/h2&gt;
      {subtitle &amp;&amp; &lt;p&gt;{subtitle}&lt;/p&gt;}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Call site — TypeScript enforces required props
&lt;Card title="Hello" onClick={() =&gt; console.log('clicked')} /&gt;
&lt;Card title="Hello" /&gt;
// Error: Property 'onClick' is missing</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q4">
          <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type useState with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q4" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <code>useState</code> is a generic function — you can pass a type argument to tell TypeScript what type the state variable holds. In many cases TypeScript can infer the type from the initial value, so <code>useState(0)</code> automatically infers <code>number</code> and <code>useState('')</code> infers <code>string</code>. Explicit type arguments are necessary when the initial value is <code>null</code> or <code>undefined</code> (because TypeScript would otherwise infer a too-narrow type), when state can hold multiple different types (a union), or when the initial value is an empty array or empty object where TypeScript cannot infer the element/property types. Typing state with a proper interface makes the entire component safer because every setter call, every read, and every comparison is checked. For complex state shapes, define an interface and pass it as the type argument.
<pre class="code-block">// Inferred — simple primitives
const [count, setCount] = useState(0);          // number
const [name, setName] = useState('');           // string
const [active, setActive] = useState(false);    // boolean

// Explicit type argument — needed for null/undefined initial value
const [user, setUser] = useState&lt;User | null&gt;(null);

// Explicit type argument — needed for empty array
interface Product {
  id: number;
  name: string;
  price: number;
}
const [products, setProducts] = useState&lt;Product[]&gt;([]);

// Explicit type argument — union state
type Status = 'idle' | 'loading' | 'success' | 'error';
const [status, setStatus] = useState&lt;Status&gt;('idle');

// Complex state object
interface FormState {
  email: string;
  password: string;
  rememberMe: boolean;
}
const [form, setForm] = useState&lt;FormState&gt;({
  email: '',
  password: '',
  rememberMe: false,
});</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q5">
          <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type event handlers in React with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q5" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          React provides a suite of generic event types in the <code>React</code> namespace that correspond to DOM events but are wrapped in React's synthetic event system. The most common pattern is to use <code>React.ChangeEvent&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;</code> for input change handlers, <code>React.MouseEvent&lt;HTMLButtonElement&gt;</code> for click handlers, and <code>React.FormEvent&lt;HTMLFormElement&gt;</code> for form submissions. These types provide full access to the underlying DOM element through <code>event.currentTarget</code> (typed to the element type you specify) and to event properties like <code>event.target.value</code>. When you define a handler inline, TypeScript can often infer the event type from the JSX attribute — so <code>onChange={(e) =&gt; ...}</code> on an <code>&lt;input&gt;</code> will automatically infer <code>e</code> as <code>React.ChangeEvent&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;</code>. When you define the handler separately as a named function, you must annotate the parameter type explicitly.
<pre class="code-block">// Inline — TypeScript infers the event type
function SearchBox() {
  const [query, setQuery] = useState('');
  return (
    &lt;input
      value={query}
      onChange={(e) =&gt; setQuery(e.target.value)} // e inferred correctly
    /&gt;
  );
}

// Named handler — must annotate explicitly
function Form() {
  const handleChange = (e: React.ChangeEvent&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;) =&gt; {
    console.log(e.target.value);
  };

  const handleClick = (e: React.MouseEvent&lt;HTMLButtonElement&gt;) =&gt; {
    e.preventDefault();
    console.log('clicked');
  };

  const handleSubmit = (e: React.FormEvent&lt;HTMLFormElement&gt;) =&gt; {
    e.preventDefault();
    // handle submission
  };

  return (
    &lt;form onSubmit={handleSubmit}&gt;
      &lt;input onChange={handleChange} /&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={handleClick}&gt;Submit&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q6">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What are React.MouseEvent, React.ChangeEvent, React.FormEvent?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q6" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          These are generic types in the <code>React</code> namespace that represent React's synthetic event objects, each corresponding to a category of DOM event. They are all generic over the element type that fired the event, which determines what <code>event.currentTarget</code> is typed as. <code>React.MouseEvent&lt;T&gt;</code> wraps native mouse events (click, mousedown, mouseenter, etc.) and gives access to mouse coordinates, modifier keys, and button info. <code>React.ChangeEvent&lt;T&gt;</code> is specifically for input/select/textarea change events and exposes <code>event.target.value</code> as a string, as well as <code>event.target.checked</code> for checkboxes. <code>React.FormEvent&lt;T&gt;</code> covers form-level events like submit and reset. Beyond these three, <code>React.KeyboardEvent&lt;T&gt;</code>, <code>React.FocusEvent&lt;T&gt;</code>, <code>React.DragEvent&lt;T&gt;</code>, and <code>React.WheelEvent&lt;T&gt;</code> cover other interaction categories. All extend from <code>React.SyntheticEvent&lt;T&gt;</code>, which provides common properties like <code>preventDefault()</code>, <code>stopPropagation()</code>, <code>target</code>, and <code>currentTarget</code>.
<pre class="code-block">// React.MouseEvent — click and pointer interactions
const handleMouseEnter = (e: React.MouseEvent&lt;HTMLDivElement&gt;) =&gt; {
  console.log(`x: ${e.clientX}, y: ${e.clientY}`);
  console.log(`shift held: ${e.shiftKey}`);
};

// React.ChangeEvent — input, select, textarea changes
const handleInputChange = (e: React.ChangeEvent&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;) =&gt; {
  console.log(e.target.value);    // string
  console.log(e.target.checked);  // boolean (for checkboxes)
  console.log(e.target.files);    // FileList | null (for file inputs)
};

const handleSelectChange = (e: React.ChangeEvent&lt;HTMLSelectElement&gt;) =&gt; {
  console.log(e.target.value);
};

// React.FormEvent — form submit / reset
const handleSubmit = (e: React.FormEvent&lt;HTMLFormElement&gt;) =&gt; {
  e.preventDefault();
  const formData = new FormData(e.currentTarget); // currentTarget is HTMLFormElement
};

// React.KeyboardEvent — keyboard interactions
const handleKeyDown = (e: React.KeyboardEvent&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;) =&gt; {
  if (e.key === 'Enter') {
    // handle enter key
  }
  if (e.ctrlKey &amp;&amp; e.key === 's') {
    e.preventDefault();
    // handle save
  }
};</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q7">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type the children prop?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q7" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          The <code>children</code> prop in React can hold many different kinds of values — JSX elements, strings, numbers, arrays, fragments, portals, or <code>null</code>. TypeScript provides <code>React.ReactNode</code> as the broadest type that covers all valid children values, and this is the correct type to use in most cases when a component simply passes children through or renders them. If you want to restrict <code>children</code> to only a single React element (not strings or arrays), use <code>React.ReactElement</code>. In React 18's type definitions, <code>React.FC</code> no longer includes <code>children</code> implicitly, so every component that accepts children must declare it explicitly in its props interface — this is actually a good thing because it makes the API contract explicit. The <code>React.PropsWithChildren&lt;T&gt;</code> utility type is a convenience that adds <code>children?: React.ReactNode</code> to an existing props type. For layout components like wrappers or providers that accept any children, <code>React.ReactNode</code> is almost always the right choice.
<pre class="code-block">import React from 'react';

// Explicit children in interface — most common approach
interface ContainerProps {
  children: React.ReactNode;  // broadest: JSX, strings, arrays, null
  className?: string;
}

function Container({ children, className }: ContainerProps) {
  return &lt;div className={className}&gt;{children}&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// Restrict to a single React element only
interface WrapperProps {
  children: React.ReactElement;  // only one JSX element, no strings
}

// PropsWithChildren utility — adds children?: ReactNode automatically
type ButtonProps = React.PropsWithChildren&lt;{
  onClick: () =&gt; void;
  disabled?: boolean;
}&gt;;

function Button({ children, onClick, disabled }: ButtonProps) {
  return &lt;button onClick={onClick} disabled={disabled}&gt;{children}&lt;/button&gt;;
}

// Typed children as a function (render prop)
interface ListProps&lt;T&gt; {
  items: T[];
  children: (item: T, index: number) =&gt; React.ReactNode;
}

function List&lt;T&gt;({ items, children }: ListProps&lt;T&gt;) {
  return &lt;ul&gt;{items.map((item, i) =&gt; &lt;li key={i}&gt;{children(item, i)}&lt;/li&gt;)}&lt;/ul&gt;;
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q8">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is React.ReactNode vs React.ReactElement?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q8" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <code>React.ReactElement</code> is the type of a single JSX element — it is the object that <code>React.createElement()</code> returns, with a <code>type</code>, <code>props</code>, and <code>key</code> property. It represents exactly one React component or DOM element in the virtual DOM. <code>React.ReactNode</code> is a much broader union type that includes <code>ReactElement</code>, <code>ReactPortal</code>, <code>string</code>, <code>number</code>, <code>boolean</code>, <code>null</code>, <code>undefined</code>, and arrays of any of these — essentially anything that React can render. For the <code>children</code> prop of most components, <code>React.ReactNode</code> is the correct type because it matches what JSX actually allows between tags. <code>React.ReactElement</code> is more appropriate when you need to guarantee a single element is passed and you may need to clone or inspect it (e.g., using <code>React.cloneElement</code>). The return type of a React component can be <code>JSX.Element</code> (an alias for <code>ReactElement</code>), <code>React.ReactNode</code>, or <code>React.ReactElement | null</code> — all of these are acceptable for component return types.
<pre class="code-block">// ReactElement — single JSX element object
const element: React.ReactElement = &lt;div&gt;Hello&lt;/div&gt;;

// ReactNode — anything React can render
const node1: React.ReactNode = &lt;div&gt;element&lt;/div&gt;;
const node2: React.ReactNode = 'plain string';
const node3: React.ReactNode = 42;
const node4: React.ReactNode = null;
const node5: React.ReactNode = [&lt;span key="a"&gt;a&lt;/span&gt;, &lt;span key="b"&gt;b&lt;/span&gt;];
const node6: React.ReactNode = false; // renders nothing but is valid

// When to use ReactElement — cloneElement requires it
interface IconButtonProps {
  icon: React.ReactElement;  // must be a single element to clone
  label: string;
}
function IconButton({ icon, label }: IconButtonProps) {
  const clonedIcon = React.cloneElement(icon, {
    className: 'icon-button__icon',
    'aria-hidden': true,
  });
  return (
    &lt;button aria-label={label}&gt;
      {clonedIcon}
      &lt;span&gt;{label}&lt;/span&gt;
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
}

// When to use ReactNode — render anything
interface SectionProps {
  header: React.ReactNode;   // could be string or JSX
  children: React.ReactNode;
}
function Section({ header, children }: SectionProps) {
  return (
    &lt;section&gt;
      &lt;h2&gt;{header}&lt;/h2&gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/section&gt;
  );
}
// Both usages valid:
&lt;Section header="Plain string" children={&lt;p&gt;content&lt;/p&gt;} /&gt;
&lt;Section header={&lt;span className="fancy"&gt;Fancy&lt;/span&gt;} children="text" /&gt;</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q9">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you create generic React components with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q9" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Generic components use TypeScript's generic syntax to let the component work with different data types while retaining full type safety — similar to how generic functions or containers work in TypeScript. You declare a type parameter on the function and use it in the props interface, allowing the component's internal types to flow through to the caller. A classic use case is a list or table component that works with any data shape: the component's <code>items</code> prop is typed as <code>T[]</code>, and a render function prop is typed as <code>(item: T) =&gt; React.ReactNode</code>, so the caller gets type-safe access to item properties in their render callback. TypeScript usually infers the type parameter from usage, so callers rarely need to provide it explicitly. One syntactic caveat in <code>.tsx</code> files: the JSX parser can mistake <code>&lt;T&gt;</code> for an opening tag, so you must write <code>&lt;T,&gt;</code> (trailing comma) or constrain the type with <code>&lt;T extends object&gt;</code> to disambiguate.
<pre class="code-block">// Generic list component
interface SelectListProps&lt;T&gt; {
  items: T[];
  getKey: (item: T) =&gt; string | number;
  renderItem: (item: T) =&gt; React.ReactNode;
  onSelect: (item: T) =&gt; void;
}

// Note the &lt;T,&gt; comma in .tsx files — avoids ambiguity with JSX
function SelectList&lt;T,&gt;({
  items,
  getKey,
  renderItem,
  onSelect,
}: SelectListProps&lt;T&gt;) {
  return (
    &lt;ul&gt;
      {items.map((item) =&gt; (
        &lt;li key={getKey(item)} onClick={() =&gt; onSelect(item)}&gt;
          {renderItem(item)}
        &lt;/li&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/ul&gt;
  );
}

// Usage — T is inferred as User
interface User {
  id: number;
  name: string;
  email: string;
}
const users: User[] = [...];

&lt;SelectList
  items={users}
  getKey={(user) =&gt; user.id}       // user is typed as User
  renderItem={(user) =&gt; (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;strong&gt;{user.name}&lt;/strong&gt;
      &lt;span&gt;{user.email}&lt;/span&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  )}
  onSelect={(user) =&gt; console.log(user.id)} // user is User, not any
/&gt;

// Generic with constraint — T must have an id field
interface TableProps&lt;T extends { id: string | number }&gt; {
  rows: T[];
  columns: { key: keyof T; label: string }[];
}

function Table&lt;T extends { id: string | number }&gt;({
  rows,
  columns,
}: TableProps&lt;T&gt;) {
  return (
    &lt;table&gt;
      &lt;tbody&gt;
        {rows.map((row) =&gt; (
          &lt;tr key={row.id}&gt;
            {columns.map((col) =&gt; (
              &lt;td key={String(col.key)}&gt;{String(row[col.key])}&lt;/td&gt;
            ))}
          &lt;/tr&gt;
        ))}
      &lt;/tbody&gt;
    &lt;/table&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q10">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type useRef with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q10" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <code>useRef</code> has two distinct use cases in React — holding a mutable value that persists across renders without triggering re-renders, and holding a reference to a DOM element. TypeScript's overloads for <code>useRef</code> reflect this distinction through how you initialize it. For a DOM ref, you pass <code>null</code> as the initial value and supply the element type as the type argument: <code>useRef&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;(null)</code>. This returns a <code>React.RefObject&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;</code> whose <code>.current</code> is readonly and typed as <code>HTMLInputElement | null</code>. For a mutable ref (storing arbitrary values), you provide an initial value and the type argument: <code>useRef&lt;number&gt;(0)</code> — this returns a <code>React.MutableRefObject&lt;number&gt;</code> whose <code>.current</code> is writable. The key practical difference is that DOM refs should be attached to JSX elements via the <code>ref</code> prop, and TypeScript will catch mismatches (attaching a <code>HTMLInputElement</code> ref to a <code>&lt;div&gt;</code> is an error).
<pre class="code-block">import { useRef, useEffect } from 'react';

// DOM ref — attach to a JSX element
function AutoFocusInput() {
  const inputRef = useRef&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;(null);

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    // inputRef.current is HTMLInputElement | null
    inputRef.current?.focus();
  }, []);

  return &lt;input ref={inputRef} type="text" /&gt;;
}

// Mutable ref — store a value without triggering re-renders
function Timer() {
  const intervalRef = useRef&lt;ReturnType&lt;typeof setInterval&gt; | null&gt;(null);

  const start = () =&gt; {
    intervalRef.current = setInterval(() =&gt; {
      console.log('tick');
    }, 1000);
  };

  const stop = () =&gt; {
    if (intervalRef.current !== null) {
      clearInterval(intervalRef.current);
      intervalRef.current = null;
    }
  };

  return &lt;&gt;&lt;button onClick={start}&gt;Start&lt;/button&gt;&lt;button onClick={stop}&gt;Stop&lt;/button&gt;&lt;/&gt;;
}

// Mutable ref — track previous value
function usePrevious&lt;T&gt;(value: T): T | undefined {
  const prevRef = useRef&lt;T | undefined&gt;(undefined);
  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    prevRef.current = value;
  });
  return prevRef.current;
}

// Using a ref callback for complex DOM interactions
function MeasuredDiv() {
  const divRef = useRef&lt;HTMLDivElement&gt;(null);
  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (divRef.current) {
      const { width, height } = divRef.current.getBoundingClientRect();
      console.log(width, height);
    }
  }, []);
  return &lt;div ref={divRef}&gt;Measured content&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q11">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type useContext with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q11" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <code>useContext</code> automatically infers its return type from the type argument of the context created by <code>React.createContext</code>, so the key is typing <code>createContext</code> correctly. The most important decision is what to use as the default value: passing a real default value infers the type from it, but many contexts don't have a meaningful default and <code>null</code> is used instead — this requires the consumer to handle the <code>null</code> case. A robust pattern is to create a typed context with <code>createContext&lt;MyContextType | null&gt;(null)</code> and then write a custom hook that asserts non-null and throws a descriptive error if the context is used outside its provider. This gives you both type safety and helpful runtime errors. The context's provider's <code>value</code> prop is also type-checked — if your context type is <code>ThemeContext</code>, the provider's <code>value</code> must match that shape.
<pre class="code-block">// 1. Define the context shape
interface AuthContextType {
  user: User | null;
  login: (email: string, password: string) =&gt; Promise&lt;void&gt;;
  logout: () =&gt; void;
  isAuthenticated: boolean;
}

// 2. Create context — null default requires null-check in consumers
const AuthContext = React.createContext&lt;AuthContextType | null&gt;(null);

// 3. Custom hook with null guard — throws if used outside provider
function useAuth(): AuthContextType {
  const context = useContext(AuthContext);
  if (context === null) {
    throw new Error('useAuth must be used within an AuthProvider');
  }
  return context; // TypeScript knows this is AuthContextType now
}

// 4. Provider component
function AuthProvider({ children }: { children: React.ReactNode }) {
  const [user, setUser] = useState&lt;User | null&gt;(null);

  const login = async (email: string, password: string) =&gt; {
    const user = await authService.login(email, password);
    setUser(user);
  };

  const logout = () =&gt; setUser(null);

  const value: AuthContextType = {
    user,
    login,
    logout,
    isAuthenticated: user !== null,
  };

  return &lt;AuthContext.Provider value={value}&gt;{children}&lt;/AuthContext.Provider&gt;;
}

// 5. Consumer — fully type-safe
function ProfileButton() {
  const { user, logout, isAuthenticated } = useAuth();
  if (!isAuthenticated) return &lt;LoginButton /&gt;;
  return &lt;button onClick={logout}&gt;Logout {user?.name}&lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q12">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type useReducer with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q12" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <code>useReducer</code> is TypeScript-friendly by design because the action type system maps naturally onto discriminated unions. You define a state interface, define an action type as a discriminated union of all possible action shapes, and then type the reducer function as <code>(state: State, action: Action) =&gt; State</code>. TypeScript infers the state and dispatch types from the reducer and initial state — <code>dispatch</code> will be typed as <code>React.Dispatch&lt;Action&gt;</code>, which means passing an invalid action object is a compile-time error. The discriminated union for actions is the key — each action variant has a <code>type</code> literal string and optional <code>payload</code>, and the reducer's <code>switch</code> statement narrows the action type in each case branch so you get full autocomplete and type safety for the payload. This approach scales well for complex state logic and is essentially a typed Redux-lite pattern within a component.
<pre class="code-block">// State type
interface CartState {
  items: CartItem[];
  total: number;
  isLoading: boolean;
}

// Action union — discriminated by 'type'
type CartAction =
  | { type: 'ADD_ITEM'; payload: CartItem }
  | { type: 'REMOVE_ITEM'; payload: { id: string } }
  | { type: 'UPDATE_QUANTITY'; payload: { id: string; quantity: number } }
  | { type: 'CLEAR_CART' }
  | { type: 'SET_LOADING'; payload: boolean };

// Initial state
const initialState: CartState = {
  items: [],
  total: 0,
  isLoading: false,
};

// Reducer — TypeScript narrows action in each case
function cartReducer(state: CartState, action: CartAction): CartState {
  switch (action.type) {
    case 'ADD_ITEM':
      // action.payload is CartItem here
      return {
        ...state,
        items: [...state.items, action.payload],
        total: state.total + action.payload.price,
      };
    case 'REMOVE_ITEM':
      // action.payload is { id: string } here
      return {
        ...state,
        items: state.items.filter((item) =&gt; item.id !== action.payload.id),
      };
    case 'CLEAR_CART':
      // action has no payload here
      return initialState;
    case 'SET_LOADING':
      return { ...state, isLoading: action.payload };
    default:
      return state;
  }
}

// Usage — dispatch is React.Dispatch&lt;CartAction&gt;
function ShoppingCart() {
  const [state, dispatch] = useReducer(cartReducer, initialState);

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'CLEAR_CART' })}&gt;
        Clear
      &lt;/button&gt;
      {/* Error: type 'INVALID_ACTION' is not assignable */}
      {/* dispatch({ type: 'INVALID_ACTION' }) */}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q13">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is a discriminated union and how is it useful in React props?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q13" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          A discriminated union (also called a tagged union) is a TypeScript union type where each member has a common literal property — the discriminant — that TypeScript uses to narrow the type inside conditional blocks. In the context of React props, discriminated unions are extremely powerful for modeling components that have mutually exclusive configurations — for example, a button that is either a link or a button, an alert that has different props depending on its variant, or a form field that works differently for text versus file inputs. When TypeScript sees an <code>if</code> or <code>switch</code> check on the discriminant property, it narrows the entire props type, giving you full type safety for the variant-specific properties. This prevents impossible combinations (like providing both a link <code>href</code> and a form <code>type</code> at the same time) and makes the component's API self-documenting.
<pre class="code-block">// Without discriminated union — props allow impossible combinations
interface BadButtonProps {
  href?: string;      // link mode
  type?: 'button' | 'submit' | 'reset'; // button mode
  onClick?: () =&gt; void;
  children: React.ReactNode;
}

// With discriminated union — mutually exclusive modes are enforced
type ButtonProps =
  | {
      as: 'button';
      type?: 'button' | 'submit' | 'reset';
      onClick?: () =&gt; void;
      href?: never;  // explicitly forbidden
      children: React.ReactNode;
    }
  | {
      as: 'link';
      href: string;  // required in link mode
      type?: never;  // explicitly forbidden
      onClick?: () =&gt; void;
      children: React.ReactNode;
    };

function Button(props: ButtonProps) {
  if (props.as === 'button') {
    // TypeScript knows: props.type is available, props.href is never
    return (
      &lt;button type={props.type} onClick={props.onClick}&gt;
        {props.children}
      &lt;/button&gt;
    );
  }
  // TypeScript knows: props.href is string (required), props.type is never
  return &lt;a href={props.href} onClick={props.onClick}&gt;{props.children}&lt;/a&gt;;
}

// Valid usage
&lt;Button as="button" type="submit" onClick={handleSubmit}&gt;Submit&lt;/Button&gt;
&lt;Button as="link" href="/home"&gt;Go Home&lt;/Button&gt;

// Compile errors:
// &lt;Button as="button" href="/home"&gt;Bad&lt;/Button&gt; — href not allowed in button mode
// &lt;Button as="link"&gt;Bad&lt;/Button&gt; — href is required in link mode</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q14">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type a component that accepts either controlled or uncontrolled mode?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q14" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Controlled mode means the parent owns the state (provides <code>value</code> and <code>onChange</code>), while uncontrolled mode means the component manages its own state internally (accepts <code>defaultValue</code> but no <code>value</code>). Typing this correctly requires a discriminated union or mutually exclusive prop constraints so that providing <code>value</code> without <code>onChange</code> (or vice versa) is a compile error. The pattern typically uses two separate type branches — one for controlled (both <code>value</code> and <code>onChange</code> required) and one for uncontrolled (<code>defaultValue</code> optional, both <code>value</code> and <code>onChange</code> set to <code>never</code>). This mirrors how the native HTML input element itself works and prevents the common runtime warning "A component is changing an uncontrolled input to be controlled." Using <code>never</code> for the properties from the other mode means TypeScript will error if you accidentally mix the modes.
<pre class="code-block">// Controlled branch — value and onChange both required
type ControlledProps = {
  value: string;
  onChange: (value: string) =&gt; void;
  defaultValue?: never;
};

// Uncontrolled branch — defaultValue optional, value/onChange forbidden
type UncontrolledProps = {
  defaultValue?: string;
  value?: never;
  onChange?: never;
};

// Shared props
type SharedProps = {
  label: string;
  placeholder?: string;
  disabled?: boolean;
};

type TextInputProps = SharedProps &amp; (ControlledProps | UncontrolledProps);

function TextInput({ label, placeholder, disabled, ...modeProps }: TextInputProps) {
  const isControlled = 'value' in modeProps &amp;&amp; modeProps.value !== undefined;

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;label&gt;{label}&lt;/label&gt;
      {isControlled ? (
        &lt;input
          value={(modeProps as ControlledProps).value}
          onChange={(e) =&gt; (modeProps as ControlledProps).onChange(e.target.value)}
          placeholder={placeholder}
          disabled={disabled}
        /&gt;
      ) : (
        &lt;input
          defaultValue={(modeProps as UncontrolledProps).defaultValue}
          placeholder={placeholder}
          disabled={disabled}
        /&gt;
      )}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Valid:
&lt;TextInput label="Name" value={name} onChange={setName} /&gt;  // controlled
&lt;TextInput label="Name" defaultValue="John" /&gt;               // uncontrolled

// Errors:
// &lt;TextInput label="Name" value={name} /&gt;  // controlled needs onChange
// &lt;TextInput label="Name" value={name} defaultValue="x" /&gt;  // can't mix</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q15">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is ComponentProps and how do you extend native HTML element props?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q15" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <code>React.ComponentProps&lt;T&gt;</code> is a utility type that extracts the props type from any React component or HTML element string. For HTML elements you use it as <code>React.ComponentProps&lt;'button'&gt;</code> or <code>React.ComponentPropsWithRef&lt;'input'&gt;</code>, which gives you the full union of all valid attributes for that element — <code>onClick</code>, <code>disabled</code>, <code>className</code>, <code>aria-*</code> attributes, and everything else the browser supports. This is the idiomatic way to build wrapper components that pass through all native HTML attributes to the underlying element without manually listing every possible prop. You combine it with your own custom props using the intersection operator <code>&amp;</code>, and typically you omit any props you're overriding to avoid type conflicts. <code>React.ComponentPropsWithoutRef&lt;T&gt;</code> and <code>React.ComponentPropsWithRef&lt;T&gt;</code> are the variants for when you need explicit control over whether the <code>ref</code> prop is included. This pattern is sometimes called "prop spreading" and is essential for building accessible design system components.
<pre class="code-block">// Extend native button — inherits ALL button HTML attributes
interface CustomButtonProps extends React.ComponentPropsWithoutRef&lt;'button'&gt; {
  variant?: 'primary' | 'secondary' | 'danger';
  isLoading?: boolean;
}

function CustomButton({
  variant = 'primary',
  isLoading = false,
  children,
  disabled,
  className,
  ...rest  // all remaining native button props (onClick, type, form, etc.)
}: CustomButtonProps) {
  return (
    &lt;button
      {...rest}
      className={`btn btn--${variant} ${className ?? ''}`}
      disabled={disabled || isLoading}
    &gt;
      {isLoading ? &lt;Spinner /&gt; : children}
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
}

// Call site — all native button props work
&lt;CustomButton
  variant="primary"
  type="submit"
  form="my-form"
  aria-label="Save changes"
  data-testid="save-btn"
  onClick={handleSave}
&gt;
  Save
&lt;/CustomButton&gt;

// Extract props from an existing component
type MotionDivProps = React.ComponentProps&lt;typeof MotionDiv&gt;;

// Omit to override a prop type
interface TextareaProps extends Omit&lt;React.ComponentPropsWithoutRef&lt;'textarea'&gt;, 'onChange'&gt; {
  onChange?: (value: string) =&gt; void;  // simplified onChange
}

// ComponentProps on a custom component
type MyButtonProps = React.ComponentProps&lt;typeof CustomButton&gt;;</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q16">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a polymorphic component with TypeScript (as prop)?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q16" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          A polymorphic component accepts an <code>as</code> prop that determines which HTML element or component it renders as, while carrying all the correct props for that element. For example, a <code>Text</code> component could render as a <code>&lt;p&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;h1&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;span&gt;</code>, or even a third-party component, and the call site gets full TypeScript autocomplete for the props appropriate to that element. The implementation requires a generic type parameter constrained to <code>React.ElementType</code>, a utility type that combines the component's own props with the props of the element type (minus conflicts), and careful use of <code>React.ComponentPropsWithoutRef</code>. The type machinery is non-trivial — you need to <code>Omit</code> the component's own props from the element props to avoid conflicts, then intersect them. Wrapping this in reusable utility types (<code>AsProp</code>, <code>PolymorphicProps</code>) makes it composable across multiple components. When combined with <code>forwardRef</code>, it becomes even more complex, often requiring type assertion.
<pre class="code-block">// Utility types for polymorphic components
type AsProp&lt;C extends React.ElementType&gt; = {
  as?: C;
};

type PropsToOmit&lt;C extends React.ElementType, P&gt; = keyof (AsProp&lt;C&gt; &amp; P);

type PolymorphicComponentProp&lt;
  C extends React.ElementType,
  Props = {}
&gt; = React.PropsWithChildren&lt;Props &amp; AsProp&lt;C&gt;&gt; &amp;
  Omit&lt;React.ComponentPropsWithoutRef&lt;C&gt;, PropsToOmit&lt;C, Props&gt;&gt;;

// The component's own props (minus the as prop)
type TextOwnProps = {
  size?: 'sm' | 'md' | 'lg' | 'xl';
  color?: 'primary' | 'muted' | 'danger';
};

// Full polymorphic props type
type TextProps&lt;C extends React.ElementType&gt; = PolymorphicComponentProp&lt;C, TextOwnProps&gt;;

// The component itself
function Text&lt;C extends React.ElementType = 'span'&gt;({
  as,
  size = 'md',
  color = 'primary',
  children,
  ...rest
}: TextProps&lt;C&gt;) {
  const Component = as ?? 'span';
  return (
    &lt;Component
      {...rest}
      className={`text text--${size} text--${color}`}
    &gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/Component&gt;
  );
}

// Usage — TypeScript checks element-specific props
&lt;Text&gt;Default span&lt;/Text&gt;
&lt;Text as="h1" size="xl"&gt;Page Title&lt;/Text&gt;
&lt;Text as="a" href="/home" color="primary"&gt;A link&lt;/Text&gt;
&lt;Text as="button" onClick={handleClick} type="submit"&gt;Button&lt;/Text&gt;

// Error — href is not valid on a button element:
// &lt;Text as="button" href="/home"&gt;Bad&lt;/Text&gt;

// Error — type="submit" is not valid on an anchor:
// &lt;Text as="a" type="submit"&gt;Bad&lt;/Text&gt;</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q17">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type forwardRef with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q17" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <code>React.forwardRef</code> is a generic function that takes two type arguments: the type of the DOM element (or component instance) the ref will point to, and the props type of the component. The syntax is <code>React.forwardRef&lt;RefType, PropsType&gt;</code>. The function receives <code>(props, ref)</code> where <code>ref</code> is typed as <code>React.ForwardedRef&lt;RefType&gt;</code> — this is a union of <code>React.RefObject&lt;RefType&gt;</code>, a ref callback, and <code>null</code>. When attaching the forwarded ref to the underlying element, TypeScript validates that the element type matches the declared ref type. The component returned by <code>forwardRef</code> can accept a <code>ref</code> prop from the call site, typed as <code>React.Ref&lt;RefType&gt;</code>. For polymorphic components with <code>forwardRef</code>, the types become complex enough that a type assertion (<code>as</code> cast) after the component definition is the pragmatic solution. Custom display names should be set via <code>.displayName</code> for DevTools readability.
<pre class="code-block">interface InputProps extends React.ComponentPropsWithoutRef&lt;'input'&gt; {
  label: string;
  error?: string;
}

// forwardRef&lt;RefType, PropsType&gt;
const Input = React.forwardRef&lt;HTMLInputElement, InputProps&gt;(
  ({ label, error, className, ...rest }, ref) =&gt; {
    // ref is React.ForwardedRef&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;
    return (
      &lt;div className="input-wrapper"&gt;
        &lt;label&gt;{label}&lt;/label&gt;
        &lt;input
          {...rest}
          ref={ref}           // TypeScript validates this matches HTMLInputElement
          className={`input ${error ? 'input--error' : ''} ${className ?? ''}`}
          aria-invalid={!!error}
        /&gt;
        {error &amp;&amp; &lt;span className="input__error"&gt;{error}&lt;/span&gt;}
      &lt;/div&gt;
    );
  }
);
Input.displayName = 'Input';

// Usage — ref is React.Ref&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt; at call site
function LoginForm() {
  const emailRef = useRef&lt;HTMLInputElement&gt;(null);

  const focusEmail = () =&gt; emailRef.current?.focus();

  return (
    &lt;form&gt;
      &lt;Input
        ref={emailRef}
        label="Email"
        type="email"
        error="Email is required"
      /&gt;
      &lt;button type="button" onClick={focusEmail}&gt;Focus Email&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}

// Exposing an imperative handle instead of the raw element
interface DialogHandle {
  open: () =&gt; void;
  close: () =&gt; void;
}

const Dialog = React.forwardRef&lt;DialogHandle, { children: React.ReactNode }&gt;(
  ({ children }, ref) =&gt; {
    const [isOpen, setIsOpen] = useState(false);

    React.useImperativeHandle(ref, () =&gt; ({
      open: () =&gt; setIsOpen(true),
      close: () =&gt; setIsOpen(false),
    }));

    return isOpen ? &lt;div className="dialog"&gt;{children}&lt;/div&gt; : null;
  }
);

// Call site
const dialogRef = useRef&lt;DialogHandle&gt;(null);
dialogRef.current?.open();</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q18">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type custom hooks with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q18" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Custom hooks are just TypeScript functions, so they follow the same typing rules: annotate parameters, let the return type be inferred or annotate it explicitly. The main subtlety is the return type when returning a tuple (array with fixed positions) — TypeScript infers an array type by default, which loses the distinct types of each element. To return a proper tuple, either annotate the return type explicitly as a tuple, or use <code>as const</code> on the return value. When a hook returns an object, TypeScript infers the object type correctly, so no explicit annotation is usually needed. Hooks that accept callbacks in dependencies should type those carefully to avoid overly broad <code>Function</code> types. Generic custom hooks follow the same generics pattern as generic functions. For hooks that return async state (loading/data/error), defining an explicit return type interface makes the API clear and stable.
<pre class="code-block">// Hook returning a tuple — must assert as tuple or annotate
function useToggle(initialValue = false): [boolean, () =&gt; void, (val: boolean) =&gt; void] {
  const [value, setValue] = useState(initialValue);
  const toggle = useCallback(() =&gt; setValue((v) =&gt; !v), []);
  const set = useCallback((val: boolean) =&gt; setValue(val), []);
  return [value, toggle, set];
}

// Alternative: const assertion trick
function useToggle(initialValue = false) {
  const [value, setValue] = useState(initialValue);
  const toggle = () =&gt; setValue((v) =&gt; !v);
  return [value, toggle] as const;
  // return type: readonly [boolean, () =&gt; void]
}

// Generic custom hook — typed through to the consumer
interface AsyncState&lt;T&gt; {
  data: T | null;
  isLoading: boolean;
  error: Error | null;
}

function useFetch&lt;T&gt;(url: string): AsyncState&lt;T&gt; {
  const [state, setState] = useState&lt;AsyncState&lt;T&gt;&gt;({
    data: null,
    isLoading: true,
    error: null,
  });

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    let cancelled = false;
    fetch(url)
      .then((res) =&gt; res.json() as Promise&lt;T&gt;)
      .then((data) =&gt; {
        if (!cancelled) setState({ data, isLoading: false, error: null });
      })
      .catch((error: unknown) =&gt; {
        if (!cancelled)
          setState({ data: null, isLoading: false, error: error as Error });
      });
    return () =&gt; { cancelled = true; };
  }, [url]);

  return state;
}

// Usage — T is inferred from how you use the data
interface User { id: number; name: string; }

function UserCard({ userId }: { userId: number }) {
  const { data, isLoading, error } = useFetch&lt;User&gt;(`/api/users/${userId}`);
  // data is User | null, fully typed
  if (isLoading) return &lt;Spinner /&gt;;
  if (error) return &lt;div&gt;Error: {error.message}&lt;/div&gt;;
  return &lt;div&gt;{data?.name}&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q19">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is type narrowing and how do you use it in React components?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q19" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Type narrowing is TypeScript's ability to refine a broader union type down to a more specific type within a conditional block, based on runtime checks. TypeScript recognizes several narrowing patterns: <code>typeof</code> checks, <code>instanceof</code> checks, truthiness checks, equality checks, the <code>in</code> operator, and user-defined type guard functions. In React, narrowing is used constantly — checking whether a prop is a string or a React element before rendering it differently, distinguishing between union variants before accessing variant-specific properties, and validating that a nullable value is non-null before using it. User-defined type guards are functions that return <code>value is Type</code> and encode custom narrowing logic — they are essential when TypeScript's built-in narrowing is not sufficient. Narrowing also interacts with discriminated unions in props: after checking the discriminant field in an <code>if</code> or <code>switch</code>, TypeScript narrows the entire props type for that branch.
<pre class="code-block">// typeof narrowing
interface LabelProps {
  value: string | number | React.ReactNode;
}

function Label({ value }: LabelProps) {
  if (typeof value === 'string') {
    // TypeScript knows: value is string
    return &lt;span&gt;{value.toUpperCase()}&lt;/span&gt;;
  }
  if (typeof value === 'number') {
    // TypeScript knows: value is number
    return &lt;span&gt;{value.toFixed(2)}&lt;/span&gt;;
  }
  // TypeScript knows: value is React.ReactNode
  return &lt;span&gt;{value}&lt;/span&gt;;
}

// User-defined type guard
interface ApiError {
  message: string;
  code: number;
}

function isApiError(error: unknown): error is ApiError {
  return (
    typeof error === 'object' &amp;&amp;
    error !== null &amp;&amp;
    'message' in error &amp;&amp;
    'code' in error
  );
}

function ErrorDisplay({ error }: { error: unknown }) {
  if (isApiError(error)) {
    // TypeScript knows: error is ApiError
    return &lt;div&gt;API Error {error.code}: {error.message}&lt;/div&gt;;
  }
  return &lt;div&gt;Unknown error occurred&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// 'in' operator narrowing on discriminated union props
type AlertProps =
  | { kind: 'success'; message: string }
  | { kind: 'error'; message: string; retryFn: () =&gt; void }
  | { kind: 'info'; title: string; body: string };

function Alert(props: AlertProps) {
  switch (props.kind) {
    case 'success':
      return &lt;div className="alert-success"&gt;{props.message}&lt;/div&gt;;
    case 'error':
      // props.retryFn is available here
      return (
        &lt;div className="alert-error"&gt;
          {props.message}
          &lt;button onClick={props.retryFn}&gt;Retry&lt;/button&gt;
        &lt;/div&gt;
      );
    case 'info':
      return (
        &lt;div className="alert-info"&gt;
          &lt;h4&gt;{props.title}&lt;/h4&gt;
          &lt;p&gt;{props.body}&lt;/p&gt;
        &lt;/div&gt;
      );
  }
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q20">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type dynamic object keys in component props?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q20" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Dynamic object keys arise when a component accepts a map or dictionary where the keys are not known at type-authoring time. TypeScript provides several mechanisms: index signatures (<code>[key: string]: ValueType</code>), the <code>Record&lt;KeyType, ValueType&gt;</code> utility type, and mapped types derived from a generic type parameter. Index signatures are the simplest but broadest — they allow any string key. <code>Record&lt;K, V&gt;</code> is cleaner syntax for the same concept but also allows constraining keys to a specific union. The most powerful and precise approach is using a generic type parameter constrained to <code>string</code> or a known union, then using it as a key type — this preserves the exact key names through the type system. For React component props, a common scenario is a component that accepts style overrides or class name maps keyed by slot names, or a data table where column keys are dynamic. Mapped types with <code>keyof</code> let you build props that correspond exactly to the fields of a data model.
<pre class="code-block">// Simple index signature — any string key allowed
interface StyleMapProps {
  styles: { [key: string]: React.CSSProperties };
}

// Record utility — same as index signature but cleaner
interface ClassMapProps {
  classNames: Record&lt;string, string&gt;;
}

// Constrained keys — only specific string literals
type SlotName = 'root' | 'label' | 'icon' | 'helperText';
interface SlottedProps {
  classNames?: Partial&lt;Record&lt;SlotName, string&gt;&gt;;
}

// Generic — caller determines the key constraints
interface DataGridProps&lt;T extends object&gt; {
  data: T[];
  // columns keys must be actual keys of T
  columns: {
    key: keyof T;
    header: string;
    render?: (value: T[keyof T]) =&gt; React.ReactNode;
  }[];
}

function DataGrid&lt;T extends object&gt;({ data, columns }: DataGridProps&lt;T&gt;) {
  return (
    &lt;table&gt;
      &lt;thead&gt;
        &lt;tr&gt;{columns.map((c) =&gt; &lt;th key={String(c.key)}&gt;{c.header}&lt;/th&gt;)}&lt;/tr&gt;
      &lt;/thead&gt;
      &lt;tbody&gt;
        {data.map((row, i) =&gt; (
          &lt;tr key={i}&gt;
            {columns.map((c) =&gt; (
              &lt;td key={String(c.key)}&gt;
                {c.render ? c.render(row[c.key]) : String(row[c.key])}
              &lt;/td&gt;
            ))}
          &lt;/tr&gt;
        ))}
      &lt;/tbody&gt;
    &lt;/table&gt;
  );
}

interface Product { id: number; name: string; price: number; stock: number; }

// TypeScript validates column keys must be keyof Product
&lt;DataGrid
  data={products}
  columns={[
    { key: 'name', header: 'Product Name' },
    { key: 'price', header: 'Price', render: (v) =&gt; `$${v}` },
    // { key: 'invalid', header: 'X' }  // Error: not a key of Product
  ]}
/&gt;</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q21">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the difference between interface and type for React props?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q21" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Both <code>interface</code> and <code>type</code> can define the shape of React props and in most cases are interchangeable. The key practical differences are: interfaces support declaration merging (multiple declarations with the same name are merged), while type aliases do not — this makes interfaces useful when you want to allow augmentation, but it can also cause hard-to-debug issues if accidental merging occurs. Type aliases are more powerful for advanced type operations — only types can express unions (<code>A | B</code>), intersections used as a primary definition, conditional types, mapped types, and tuple types. For simple object shapes (most React props), either works fine. The community convention is to use <code>interface</code> for object shapes that represent public API contracts and <code>type</code> for derived types, unions, and utility types. Within a React codebase, consistency matters more than which one you choose — pick one and stick to it for your props definitions.
<pre class="code-block">// interface — can be extended and merged
interface ButtonProps {
  label: string;
  onClick: () =&gt; void;
}

// Extend with interface
interface IconButtonProps extends ButtonProps {
  icon: React.ReactElement;
}

// Declaration merging — only possible with interface
// (useful for augmenting third-party types)
interface ButtonProps {
  testId?: string; // merged into original ButtonProps
}

// type — required for unions and advanced patterns
type ButtonVariant = 'primary' | 'secondary' | 'ghost';

type AlertProps =
  | { type: 'success'; message: string }
  | { type: 'error'; message: string; onRetry: () =&gt; void };

// type intersection — same visual result as interface extends
type InputProps = React.ComponentPropsWithoutRef&lt;'input'&gt; &amp; {
  label: string;
  error?: string;
};

// Both are equivalent for simple object shapes:
interface CardPropsI {
  title: string;
  subtitle?: string;
}

type CardPropsT = {
  title: string;
  subtitle?: string;
};

// Practical rule of thumb:
// - Use interface for component prop objects
// - Use type for unions, tuples, and utility-derived types
// - Be consistent within a project</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q22">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type an array of components passed as props?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q22" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Passing an array of rendered elements (JSX) uses <code>React.ReactElement[]</code> or <code>React.ReactNode[]</code>. Passing component constructors/functions (not yet rendered) uses <code>React.ComponentType&lt;P&gt;[]</code> where <code>P</code> is the props type those components accept. The distinction is important: <code>React.ReactElement</code> is an already-created virtual DOM node, while <code>React.ComponentType&lt;P&gt;</code> is a class or function that can create one when called. For a tab panel that receives tab components to render, you might pass rendered elements. For a component registry or a plugin system, you would pass the component constructors. <code>React.ComponentType&lt;P&gt;</code> is a union of <code>React.ComponentClass&lt;P&gt;</code> and <code>React.FunctionComponent&lt;P&gt;</code>, so it works for both class and function components. When the component array is generic, you can use a generic type parameter to constrain what kinds of components can be passed.
<pre class="code-block">// Array of rendered elements
interface TabsProps {
  tabs: React.ReactElement[];  // already-rendered JSX
}

function Tabs({ tabs }: TabsProps) {
  return &lt;div className="tabs"&gt;{tabs}&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// Usage
&lt;Tabs tabs={[
  &lt;Tab key="a" label="Tab A" /&gt;,
  &lt;Tab key="b" label="Tab B" /&gt;,
]} /&gt;

// Array of component types (constructors/functions)
interface StepWizardProps {
  steps: React.ComponentType&lt;{ onNext: () =&gt; void; onBack: () =&gt; void }&gt;[];
}

function StepWizard({ steps }: StepWizardProps) {
  const [current, setCurrent] = useState(0);
  const StepComponent = steps[current];
  return (
    &lt;StepComponent
      onNext={() =&gt; setCurrent((i) =&gt; i + 1)}
      onBack={() =&gt; setCurrent((i) =&gt; i - 1)}
    /&gt;
  );
}

// Generic — accept components with any props superset
type AnyComponent = React.ComponentType&lt;any&gt;;

interface PluginSystemProps {
  plugins: AnyComponent[];
  sharedProps: Record&lt;string, unknown&gt;;
}

// Array of typed component definitions (more structured)
interface Column&lt;T&gt; {
  key: keyof T;
  header: string;
  Cell: React.ComponentType&lt;{ value: T[keyof T]; row: T }&gt;;
}

interface TableProps&lt;T extends object&gt; {
  data: T[];
  columns: Column&lt;T&gt;[];
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q23">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type render prop patterns?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q23" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          A render prop is a prop whose value is a function that returns JSX — the component calls it to produce its output, sharing state or logic with the caller. The type of a render prop is simply a function type that specifies the arguments the component will pass and the return type (<code>React.ReactNode</code>). When the arguments to the render function are typed precisely, the caller gets full type inference and autocomplete on the injected values. Render props are often compared to custom hooks (which largely replaced them), but they still appear in libraries and when you need to scope rendering within a JSX tree. The function prop can be named <code>children</code> (function-as-children pattern) or any other name like <code>render</code>. Generic render props let the component work with any data type while preserving type flow to the caller's render function.
<pre class="code-block">// Basic render prop
interface MousePositionProps {
  render: (position: { x: number; y: number }) =&gt; React.ReactNode;
}

function MouseTracker({ render }: MousePositionProps) {
  const [pos, setPos] = useState({ x: 0, y: 0 });
  return (
    &lt;div onMouseMove={(e) =&gt; setPos({ x: e.clientX, y: e.clientY })}&gt;
      {render(pos)}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Usage — pos is { x: number; y: number } — fully typed
&lt;MouseTracker render={(pos) =&gt; &lt;span&gt;{pos.x}, {pos.y}&lt;/span&gt;} /&gt;

// Function-as-children pattern (render prop via children)
interface ToggleProps {
  children: (state: {
    isOn: boolean;
    toggle: () =&gt; void;
    setOn: () =&gt; void;
    setOff: () =&gt; void;
  }) =&gt; React.ReactNode;
}

function Toggle({ children }: ToggleProps) {
  const [isOn, setIsOn] = useState(false);
  return (
    &lt;&gt;
      {children({
        isOn,
        toggle: () =&gt; setIsOn((v) =&gt; !v),
        setOn: () =&gt; setIsOn(true),
        setOff: () =&gt; setIsOn(false),
      })}
    &lt;/&gt;
  );
}

&lt;Toggle&gt;
  {({ isOn, toggle }) =&gt; (
    &lt;button onClick={toggle}&gt;{isOn ? 'ON' : 'OFF'}&lt;/button&gt;
  )}
&lt;/Toggle&gt;

// Generic render prop — type flows from data to render callback
interface DataProviderProps&lt;T&gt; {
  data: T;
  render: (data: T, helpers: { refresh: () =&gt; void }) =&gt; React.ReactNode;
}

function DataProvider&lt;T,&gt;({ data, render }: DataProviderProps&lt;T&gt;) {
  const refresh = () =&gt; { /* refetch logic */ };
  return &lt;&gt;{render(data, { refresh })}&lt;/&gt;;
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q24">
          <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is Readonly&lt;T&gt; and when should you use it for props?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q24" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <code>Readonly&lt;T&gt;</code> is a TypeScript utility type that makes all properties of <code>T</code> read-only — they cannot be reassigned after creation. React props are conceptually immutable (a component should never mutate its own props), and wrapping your props type in <code>Readonly&lt;T&gt;</code> enforces this at the TypeScript level. If any code inside the component tries to assign to a prop (e.g., <code>props.value = 'new'</code>), TypeScript will error. Note that <code>Readonly&lt;T&gt;</code> is shallow — nested objects within the props are not made readonly unless you use <code>DeepReadonly</code> (which is not built-in and must be custom-authored or imported from a library). In practice, direct prop mutation is rare and React's linting rules catch it, so <code>Readonly</code> is more about documentation and team discipline than catching real bugs. Array props are often paired with <code>ReadonlyArray&lt;T&gt;</code> or <code>readonly T[]</code> to prevent mutation of the array itself inside the component.
<pre class="code-block">// Readonly wrapping entire props — prevents any prop mutation
interface UserCardProps {
  name: string;
  age: number;
  tags: string[];
}

function UserCard(props: Readonly&lt;UserCardProps&gt;) {
  // props.name = 'new name'; // Error: Cannot assign to 'name' (read-only)
  return &lt;div&gt;{props.name}&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// Readonly array — prevents array mutation
interface ListProps {
  items: readonly string[];  // same as ReadonlyArray&lt;string&gt;
}

function List({ items }: ListProps) {
  // items.push('new');   // Error: Property 'push' does not exist on readonly
  // items[0] = 'edit';  // Error: Index signature is read-only
  return &lt;ul&gt;{items.map((item, i) =&gt; &lt;li key={i}&gt;{item}&lt;/li&gt;)}&lt;/ul&gt;;
}

// Custom DeepReadonly for deeply nested props
type DeepReadonly&lt;T&gt; = {
  readonly [K in keyof T]: T[K] extends object ? DeepReadonly&lt;T[K]&gt; : T[K];
};

interface Config {
  theme: {
    colors: {
      primary: string;
      secondary: string;
    };
  };
}

function ThemeDisplay({ config }: { config: DeepReadonly&lt;Config&gt; }) {
  // config.theme.colors.primary = 'red'; // Error — deeply readonly
  return &lt;div style={{ color: config.theme.colors.primary }}&gt;Themed&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// Practical convention — inline readonly modifier
interface TableProps {
  rows: ReadonlyArray&lt;{ id: string; label: string }&gt;;
  selectedIds: ReadonlySet&lt;string&gt;;
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q25">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type a higher-order component (HOC) with TypeScript?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q25" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          A higher-order component is a function that takes a component and returns a new component with enhanced behaviour. Typing HOCs correctly with TypeScript is one of the more challenging tasks because the types must correctly express: the props the HOC injects, the props the wrapped component needs, and the resulting props the final component exposes. The key pattern uses generic type parameters to capture the wrapped component's props type, then uses <code>Omit</code> to remove the injected props from the outer component's props (since the HOC fills those in). <code>React.ComponentType&lt;P&gt;</code> is used for the wrapped component type. The HOC should also forward the display name for DevTools. Custom hooks have largely replaced HOCs in modern React, but HOCs still appear in library code, patterns like <code>connect()</code> from Redux, and when wrapping class components. The types below demonstrate the canonical pattern.
<pre class="code-block">// HOC that injects a 'theme' prop
interface WithThemeProps {
  theme: Theme;
}

interface Theme {
  primary: string;
  secondary: string;
  background: string;
}

function withTheme&lt;P extends WithThemeProps&gt;(
  WrappedComponent: React.ComponentType&lt;P&gt;
) {
  // The returned component's props are P minus the injected theme prop
  type OuterProps = Omit&lt;P, keyof WithThemeProps&gt;;

  function WithTheme(outerProps: OuterProps) {
    const theme = useThemeContext(); // assume this provides a Theme

    // Cast needed because TypeScript can't verify Omit + WithThemeProps = P
    const allProps = { ...outerProps, theme } as P;

    return &lt;WrappedComponent {...allProps} /&gt;;
  }

  WithTheme.displayName = `withTheme(${
    WrappedComponent.displayName ?? WrappedComponent.name ?? 'Component'
  })`;

  return WithTheme;
}

// Component that needs a theme
interface ButtonProps extends WithThemeProps {
  label: string;
  onClick: () =&gt; void;
}

function ThemedButton({ label, onClick, theme }: ButtonProps) {
  return (
    &lt;button style={{ backgroundColor: theme.primary }} onClick={onClick}&gt;
      {label}
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
}

// HOC application — theme is no longer required at call site
const Button = withTheme(ThemedButton);

&lt;Button label="Click me" onClick={handleClick} /&gt;
// theme is injected by the HOC — no need to pass it

// HOC with additional generic constraints
function withAuth&lt;P extends { isAuthenticated?: never }&gt;(
  WrappedComponent: React.ComponentType&lt;P &amp; { isAuthenticated: boolean }&gt;
) {
  return function WithAuth(props: P) {
    const { isAuthenticated } = useAuth();
    return &lt;WrappedComponent {...props} isAuthenticated={isAuthenticated} /&gt;;
  };
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q26">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What are template literal types and how can they improve component APIs?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q26" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Template literal types (introduced in TypeScript 4.1) allow you to construct string literal union types by combining other string literal types using template literal syntax, exactly mirroring JavaScript's template literals. In React component APIs, this is powerful for creating strongly typed prop systems where valid values are combinations of known parts — for example, spacing scales, color variants with shade levels, CSS utility class names, or compound identifiers. Instead of manually listing every combination (<code>'sm-primary' | 'sm-secondary' | 'md-primary' | 'md-secondary' | ...</code>), you define the parts as unions and combine them. TypeScript generates the full cross-product of all combinations. This is especially valuable for design systems where sizes, colors, and states combine to form class names or style tokens. Template literal types also work with inference through infer and with string manipulation utility types like <code>Uppercase</code>, <code>Lowercase</code>, <code>Capitalize</code>, and <code>Uncapitalize</code>.
<pre class="code-block">// Building compound size + color tokens
type Size = 'xs' | 'sm' | 'md' | 'lg' | 'xl';
type Color = 'primary' | 'secondary' | 'danger' | 'success' | 'warning';
type Shade = '100' | '200' | '300' | '400' | '500' | '600' | '700';

// Compound size-color token
type SizeColorToken = `${Size}-${Color}`;
// "xs-primary" | "xs-secondary" | "xs-danger" | ... (25 combinations)

// Color with shade
type ColorWithShade = `${Color}-${Shade}`;
// "primary-100" | "primary-200" | ... "warning-700" (35 combinations)

// Event handler naming pattern
type EventName = 'click' | 'change' | 'focus' | 'blur';
type HandlerName = `on${Capitalize&lt;EventName&gt;}`;
// "onClick" | "onChange" | "onFocus" | "onBlur"

// Component with template literal typed props
interface SpacingProps {
  margin?: `${number}px` | `${number}rem` | `${number}%`;
  padding?: `${number}px` | `${number}rem`;
}

// Design system class name builder
type Direction = 'top' | 'right' | 'bottom' | 'left' | 'x' | 'y';
type SpacingSize = '0' | '1' | '2' | '3' | '4' | '5' | '6' | '8' | '10' | '12';
type SpacingClass = `p${Direction extends string ? Direction[0] : never}-${SpacingSize}` | `m${string}-${SpacingSize}`;

// Practical: type-safe test IDs
type DataTestId = `${string}-${'button' | 'input' | 'label' | 'container' | 'list'}`;

interface ComponentProps {
  'data-testid'?: DataTestId;
}

// Infer from template literal — extract sub-parts
type ExtractColor&lt;T extends string&gt; =
  T extends `${Size}-${infer C extends Color}` ? C : never;

type ColorOf = ExtractColor&lt;'md-primary'&gt;; // 'primary'
type ColorOf2 = ExtractColor&lt;'lg-danger'&gt;;  // 'danger'</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q27">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type exhaustive switch statements for action types?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q27" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          An exhaustive switch statement is one that TypeScript can verify handles every case in a union — if you add a new action type but forget to add a case, you get a compile-time error rather than a silent runtime bug. The standard technique is the "never check" pattern: in the <code>default</code> branch of the switch, assign the action to a variable of type <code>never</code>. Since <code>never</code> is the bottom type (nothing can be assigned to it), if all cases are handled TypeScript narrows the action to <code>never</code> in the default branch — and the assignment succeeds. But if you add a new action type without handling it, TypeScript narrows it to that new type in the default branch, which is not assignable to <code>never</code>, producing a compile error. This pattern is essential for large Redux-style reducers with many action types — it turns action management from a "remember to update" problem into a "the compiler tells you" problem. A helper function like <code>assertNever</code> makes the intent explicit and also throws at runtime if the code path is somehow reached.
<pre class="code-block">// The assertNever helper
function assertNever(value: never, message?: string): never {
  throw new Error(message ?? `Unhandled value: ${JSON.stringify(value)}`);
}

// Discriminated union of all actions
type AppAction =
  | { type: 'INCREMENT'; payload: number }
  | { type: 'DECREMENT'; payload: number }
  | { type: 'RESET' }
  | { type: 'SET_LOADING'; payload: boolean };

function reducer(state: AppState, action: AppAction): AppState {
  switch (action.type) {
    case 'INCREMENT':
      return { ...state, count: state.count + action.payload };
    case 'DECREMENT':
      return { ...state, count: state.count - action.payload };
    case 'RESET':
      return initialState;
    case 'SET_LOADING':
      return { ...state, isLoading: action.payload };
    default:
      // action is 'never' here if all cases handled
      // If you add a new action type without a case, this line errors:
      // "Type 'NewAction' is not assignable to type 'never'"
      return assertNever(action);
  }
}

// What happens when a new action is added without a case:
