type AppAction =
  | { type: 'INCREMENT'; payload: number }
  | { type: 'DECREMENT'; payload: number }
  | { type: 'RESET' }
  | { type: 'SET_LOADING'; payload: boolean }
  | { type: 'UNDO' };  // NEW — no case added

// default branch now errors:
// Argument of type '{ type: "UNDO" }' is not assignable to
// parameter of type 'never'

// Exhaustive type helper (alternative to function)
type Exhaustive&lt;T extends never&gt; = T;

// In reducer default:
// const _exhaustive: Exhaustive&lt;typeof action&gt; = action;
// Errors if action is not narrowed to never</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q28">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you avoid the any type when typing third-party library integrations?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q28" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          The <code>any</code> type disables TypeScript's type checking entirely for that value, spreading unsafety to everything that touches it. When integrating third-party libraries, there are several strategies to avoid it. First, check if the library ships its own types or has a <code>@types/</code> package — most popular libraries do, and this is always the first step. When a library has incomplete or incorrect types, you can use declaration merging to augment them. When a library returns unknown data (e.g., an API response or parsed JSON), type-guard functions or validation libraries (Zod, io-ts, Yup) let you safely narrow <code>unknown</code> to your typed interface without resorting to <code>any</code>. <code>unknown</code> is the type-safe alternative to <code>any</code> — values of type <code>unknown</code> cannot be used without a type narrowing check. For event system callbacks that pass untyped data, use type assertions (<code>as</code>) only after validating the shape, and prefer narrow assertions over <code>as any</code>. If <code>any</code> is truly unavoidable, isolate it behind a typed wrapper function so the unsafe boundary is small and contained.
<pre class="code-block">// Strategy 1: Use @types/ package
// npm install --save-dev @types/lodash
import { debounce } from 'lodash'; // now fully typed

// Strategy 2: Cast unknown API response safely
async function fetchUser(id: string): Promise&lt;User&gt; {
  const response = await fetch(`/api/users/${id}`);
  const data: unknown = await response.json(); // unknown, not any

  // Type guard before using
  if (!isUser(data)) {
    throw new Error('Invalid user data from API');
  }
  return data; // typed as User
}

function isUser(value: unknown): value is User {
  return (
    typeof value === 'object' &&
    value !== null &&
    'id' in value &&
    'name' in value &&
    typeof (value as Record&lt;string, unknown&gt;).id === 'string'
  );
}

// Strategy 3: Zod schema validation — runtime + type safety
import { z } from 'zod';

const UserSchema = z.object({
  id: z.string(),
  name: z.string(),
  email: z.string().email(),
  age: z.number().min(0),
});

type User = z.infer&lt;typeof UserSchema&gt;; // derived from schema

async function fetchUser(id: string): Promise&lt;User&gt; {
  const data = await fetch(`/api/users/${id}`).then((r) =&gt; r.json());
  return UserSchema.parse(data); // throws if invalid, returns typed User
}

// Strategy 4: Declare module augmentation for missing types
// In a .d.ts file:
declare module 'some-untyped-library' {
  export function doSomething(input: string): Promise&lt;{ result: string }&gt;;
  export interface LibConfig {
    timeout: number;
    retries: number;
  }
}

// Strategy 5: Isolate any behind a typed wrapper
function parseEventPayload(raw: unknown): EventPayload {
  // eslint-disable-next-line @typescript-eslint/no-explicit-any
  const unsafe = raw as any; // contained unsafe zone
  return {
    type: String(unsafe.type ?? ''),
    timestamp: Number(unsafe.timestamp ?? Date.now()),
    data: unsafe.data ?? null,
  };
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q29">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is declaration merging and when is it useful in React TypeScript projects?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q29" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Declaration merging is TypeScript's behaviour of combining multiple declarations with the same name in the same scope into a single, merged definition. It applies to interfaces (multiple <code>interface Foo</code> declarations merge their members), namespaces, and module declarations. In React TypeScript projects, declaration merging has several important practical uses: augmenting third-party library types without modifying <code>node_modules</code>, extending the global JSX namespace to add custom attributes or element types, extending module augmentation for libraries like <code>react-router</code> to add typed route params, and adding properties to the <code>Window</code> or <code>HTMLElement</code> interfaces. A common real-world example is adding custom attributes to all HTML elements (for analytics or testing) by merging into <code>React.HTMLAttributes&lt;HTMLElement&gt;</code> or adding environment-specific globals to the TypeScript type system. Declaration merging only works with <code>interface</code> and <code>namespace</code>, not with <code>type</code> aliases — this is one concrete reason to prefer <code>interface</code> for extensible contracts.
<pre class="code-block">// Augmenting HTML attributes to add data-testid to all elements
// In global.d.ts or a .d.ts file included in tsconfig:
declare namespace React {
  interface HTMLAttributes&lt;T&gt; {
    'data-testid'?: string;
    'data-cy'?: string;  // Cypress attribute
  }
}
// Now all JSX elements accept data-testid:
&lt;div data-testid="container"&gt;Content&lt;/div&gt;

// Augmenting Window global with app-specific properties
interface Window {
  analytics: {
    track: (event: string, properties?: Record&lt;string, unknown&gt;) =&gt; void;
  };
  __APP_VERSION__: string;
}
// Safe access without casting:
window.analytics.track('page_view');

// Augmenting a third-party library's types
// react-router-dom doesn't know your route param types:
declare module 'react-router-dom' {
  interface PathParams {
    '/users/:userId': { userId: string };
    '/products/:id/:slug': { id: string; slug: string };
  }
}

// Module augmentation for styled-components theme
declare module 'styled-components' {
  export interface DefaultTheme {
    colors: {
      primary: string;
      secondary: string;
      background: string;
    };
    spacing: (factor: number) =&gt; string;
  }
}
// Now props.theme is fully typed in styled components

// Extending an interface from a library
// Original library defines:
// interface AxiosRequestConfig { headers?: Record&lt;string, string&gt; }

// You can add custom properties:
declare module 'axios' {
  interface AxiosRequestConfig {
    _retry?: boolean;
    _requestId?: string;
  }
}
// config._retry = true; // now valid without casting</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#typescript-q30">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you type context with a default value of undefined safely?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="typescript-q30" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-typescript">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          When a context has no meaningful default value, the common pattern is to pass <code>undefined</code> (or <code>null</code>) to <code>createContext</code>. This forces TypeScript to include <code>undefined</code> in the context type, meaning every consumer must null-check before using the value. The cleanest approach is to encapsulate the null check inside a custom hook that throws a descriptive error if the context is consumed outside its provider — this converts a potential runtime <code>TypeError: Cannot read property of undefined</code> into an early, explicit error with a useful message. A more advanced pattern uses a factory function to create typed context+hook pairs, eliminating boilerplate. The <code>undefined</code> approach is safer than using a fake default value (which would silently let components render incorrectly outside a provider), and it forces proper provider placement during development. TypeScript's control flow analysis means once you've done the null check in the custom hook, the return type is the non-nullable context type — no type assertions required.
<pre class="code-block">// Pattern 1: null default + custom hook with guard
interface ThemeContextType {
  theme: 'light' | 'dark';
  toggleTheme: () =&gt; void;
  setTheme: (t: 'light' | 'dark') =&gt; void;
}

// null initial value — safe because we check in the hook
const ThemeContext = React.createContext&lt;ThemeContextType | null&gt;(null);

function useTheme(): ThemeContextType {
  const ctx = useContext(ThemeContext);
  if (ctx === null) {
    throw new Error(
      'useTheme must be used within a ThemeProvider. ' +
      'Make sure your component is wrapped in &lt;ThemeProvider&gt;.'
    );
  }
  return ctx; // TypeScript narrows to ThemeContextType (not null)
}

// Provider
function ThemeProvider({ children }: { children: React.ReactNode }) {
  const [theme, setTheme] = useState&lt;'light' | 'dark'&gt;('light');
  const value: ThemeContextType = {
    theme,
    toggleTheme: () =&gt; setTheme((t) =&gt; (t === 'light' ? 'dark' : 'light')),
    setTheme,
  };
  return &lt;ThemeContext.Provider value={value}&gt;{children}&lt;/ThemeContext.Provider&gt;;
}

// Pattern 2: Factory function — creates context + hook pairs without boilerplate
function createContext&lt;T&gt;(displayName: string) {
  const Context = React.createContext&lt;T | undefined&gt;(undefined);
  Context.displayName = displayName;

  function useCtx(): T {
    const value = useContext(Context);
    if (value === undefined) {
      throw new Error(
        `use${displayName} must be used within a ${displayName}Provider`
      );
    }
    return value;
  }

  return [Context, useCtx] as const;
}

// Usage of factory
const [CartContext, useCart] = createContext&lt;CartContextType&gt;('Cart');

function CartProvider({ children }: { children: React.ReactNode }) {
  const value = useCartLogic();
  return &lt;CartContext.Provider value={value}&gt;{children}&lt;/CartContext.Provider&gt;;
}

// Consumer — fully type-safe, no null checks needed
function CartIcon() {
  const { itemCount } = useCart(); // throws if outside CartProvider
  return &lt;span&gt;Cart ({itemCount})&lt;/span&gt;;
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

  </div>
</div>

  <div class="accordion" id="acc-architecture">
<!-- ============================================================
     Tab 10 · Architecture & Patterns · Questions 1-25
     Pattern: accordion-item, id="architecture-qN"
     data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture"
     ============================================================ -->

<!-- Q1 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q1">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q1"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q1">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;
      1. What is the single responsibility principle applied to React components?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q1" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q1"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>Single Responsibility Principle (SRP)</strong> states that every module, class, or function
        should have only one reason to change — one job it does well. Applied to React components, this means
        each component should do <em>one thing</em>: either fetch data, or render UI, or handle a specific piece
        of business logic — never all three at once.
      </p>
      <p>
        A component that violates SRP is easy to spot: it makes API calls, manages multiple unrelated state
        slices, formats data, and renders JSX all in the same file. Such components become impossible to test
        in isolation, hard to reuse, and brittle when requirements change. Breaking them apart into focused
        units reduces the blast radius of each change.
      </p>
      <p>
        In practice, SRP guides you to extract custom hooks for data-fetching logic, separate utility functions
        for data transformation, and keep JSX components purely concerned with presentation. A
        <code>UserProfileCard</code> component should only know how to <em>display</em> a user profile — a
        <code>useUserProfile</code> hook handles the fetch, and a <code>formatUserDate</code> utility handles
        date formatting.
      </p>
      <p>
        The rule of thumb: if you struggle to name a component with a single noun or short phrase, it probably
        does too much. Components named <code>UserDashboardWithDataAndExport</code> are a red flag.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// VIOLATES SRP — one component doing too many things
function UserCard({ userId }) {
  const [user, setUser] = useState(null);
  const [posts, setPosts] = useState([]);

  useEffect(() => {
    fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`).then(r => r.json()).then(setUser);
    fetch(`/api/users/${userId}/posts`).then(r => r.json()).then(setPosts);
  }, [userId]);

  const formattedJoinDate = user
    ? new Date(user.joinedAt).toLocaleDateString('en-US', { year: 'numeric', month: 'long' })
    : '';

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;h2&gt;{user?.name}&lt;/h2&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;Joined: {formattedJoinDate}&lt;/p&gt;
      &lt;ul&gt;{posts.map(p =&gt; &lt;li key={p.id}&gt;{p.title}&lt;/li&gt;)}&lt;/ul&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// FOLLOWS SRP — each piece has one job
// 1. Custom hook: data concern
function useUserProfile(userId) {
  const [user, setUser] = useState(null);
  const [posts, setPosts] = useState([]);
  useEffect(() => {
    fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`).then(r => r.json()).then(setUser);
    fetch(`/api/users/${userId}/posts`).then(r => r.json()).then(setPosts);
  }, [userId]);
  return { user, posts };
}

// 2. Utility: formatting concern
function formatJoinDate(isoString) {
  return new Date(isoString).toLocaleDateString('en-US', {
    year: 'numeric', month: 'long'
  });
}

// 3. Component: display concern only
function UserCard({ userId }) {
  const { user, posts } = useUserProfile(userId);
  if (!user) return &lt;p&gt;Loading...&lt;/p&gt;;
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;h2&gt;{user.name}&lt;/h2&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;Joined: {formatJoinDate(user.joinedAt)}&lt;/p&gt;
      &lt;ul&gt;{posts.map(p =&gt; &lt;li key={p.id}&gt;{p.title}&lt;/li&gt;)}&lt;/ul&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q2 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q2">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q2"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q2">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;
      2. What is a presentational vs container component pattern?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q2" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q2"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>Presentational / Container</strong> split, popularised by Dan Abramov, divides components
        into two camps. <em>Presentational</em> (a.k.a. "dumb") components care only about how things look —
        they receive data through props, emit events through callback props, and contain almost no state or
        side effects. <em>Container</em> (a.k.a. "smart") components care about how things work — they
        connect to Redux/Context, make API calls, and pass data down to presentational components.
      </p>
      <p>
        The benefit is a clean seam between UI and logic. Presentational components are trivially testable
        (just pass props), trivially storybook-able (no mocking needed), and easily reusable across different
        data sources. Container components centralise all complexity in one place so it is easy to audit.
      </p>
      <p>
        It is worth noting that Dan Abramov himself later said he no longer recommends this split as a hard
        rule now that hooks exist — a custom hook achieves the same separation without the extra wrapper
        component. The pattern remains valid conceptually and is still common in codebases using Redux
        <code>connect()</code>.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// --- Presentational component ---
// Knows nothing about Redux or APIs. Pure UI.
function UserList({ users, onSelectUser, isLoading }) {
  if (isLoading) return &lt;p&gt;Loading users...&lt;/p&gt;;
  return (
    &lt;ul className="user-list"&gt;
      {users.map(user =&gt; (
        &lt;li key={user.id} onClick={() =&gt; onSelectUser(user.id)}&gt;
          &lt;img src={user.avatar} alt={user.name} /&gt;
          &lt;span&gt;{user.name}&lt;/span&gt;
        &lt;/li&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/ul&gt;
  );
}

// --- Container component ---
// Knows about Redux store; passes data and callbacks down.
import { useSelector, useDispatch } from 'react-redux';
import { fetchUsers, selectUser } from './usersSlice';

function UserListContainer() {
  const dispatch = useDispatch();
  const users = useSelector(state =&gt; state.users.list);
  const isLoading = useSelector(state =&gt; state.users.loading);

  useEffect(() =&gt; { dispatch(fetchUsers()); }, [dispatch]);

  return (
    &lt;UserList
      users={users}
      isLoading={isLoading}
      onSelectUser={id =&gt; dispatch(selectUser(id))}
    /&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q3 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q3">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q3"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q3">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;
      3. What are smart and dumb components?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q3" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q3"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        "Smart" and "dumb" are informal names for the same container/presentational split described above,
        but the terminology emphasises <em>awareness</em>. A <strong>dumb component</strong> is blissfully
        unaware of the application around it — it receives props, renders UI, fires callbacks. It could
        live in a completely different app and work identically. A <strong>smart component</strong> is
        "aware" of application infrastructure: the store, the router, the auth context, API endpoints.
      </p>
      <p>
        The key measure is: does the component import anything application-specific (Redux, React Query,
        React Router)? If yes, it is smart. If it only imports React and maybe some UI library types,
        it is dumb. Dumb components should dominate by count — the pyramid should be wide at the bottom
        (dumb) and narrow at the top (smart).
      </p>
      <p>
        Modern hooks blur the line: a component can use a custom hook that internally calls
        <code>useSelector</code>, making the component technically "aware" without any direct Redux
        import. The spirit of the pattern still applies — keep the JSX layer thin and push complexity
        into hooks.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// DUMB — zero application knowledge
function Badge({ label, color = 'blue' }) {
  return (
    &lt;span style={{ background: color, borderRadius: 4, padding: '2px 8px' }}&gt;
      {label}
    &lt;/span&gt;
  );
}

// ALSO DUMB — receives everything via props, fires callbacks
function ProductCard({ name, price, stock, onAddToCart }) {
  return (
    &lt;div className="card"&gt;
      &lt;h3&gt;{name}&lt;/h3&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;${price.toFixed(2)}&lt;/p&gt;
      &lt;Badge label={stock &gt; 0 ? 'In Stock' : 'Out of Stock'}
             color={stock &gt; 0 ? 'green' : 'red'} /&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={onAddToCart} disabled={stock === 0}&gt;
        Add to Cart
      &lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// SMART — knows about the store and dispatches actions
function ProductCardContainer({ productId }) {
  const product = useSelector(state =&gt;
    state.products.items.find(p =&gt; p.id === productId)
  );
  const dispatch = useDispatch();

  return (
    &lt;ProductCard
      {...product}
      onAddToCart={() =&gt; dispatch(addToCart(productId))}
    /&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q4 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q4">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q4"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q4">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;
      4. What is a higher-order component (HOC)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q4" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q4"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        A <strong>Higher-Order Component</strong> is a function that takes a component as input and returns
        a new, enhanced component. It is the React application of the higher-order function concept from
        functional programming. HOCs add cross-cutting concerns — authentication guards, logging, data
        fetching, theming — without modifying the original component.
      </p>
      <p>
        The canonical naming convention is to prefix the HOC with <code>with</code>:
        <code>withAuth</code>, <code>withTheme</code>, <code>withRouter</code>. The returned component
        should forward all props it doesn't consume to the wrapped component, and it should copy over
        <code>displayName</code> for better DevTools readability.
      </p>
      <p>
        HOCs are less common in modern React since custom hooks cover most of the same use cases with less
        indirection and no wrapper component nesting. They remain relevant when you need to intercept the
        component itself (e.g., wrap it in an error boundary or inject a ref) or when working with
        class-based codebases where hooks aren't available.
      </p>
      <p>
        Pitfalls: HOCs can cause "wrapper hell" (deeply nested components in DevTools), they must not be
        called inside render (they should be applied at module scope), and they can accidentally swallow
        static methods unless you use <code>hoist-non-react-statics</code>.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Generic HOC that adds authentication guard
function withAuth(WrappedComponent) {
  function AuthGuard(props) {
    const { isAuthenticated, isLoading } = useAuth();

    if (isLoading) return &lt;LoadingSpinner /&gt;;
    if (!isAuthenticated) return &lt;Navigate to="/login" replace /&gt;;

    return &lt;WrappedComponent {...props} /&gt;;
  }

  // Preserve display name for React DevTools
  AuthGuard.displayName = `withAuth(${
    WrappedComponent.displayName || WrappedComponent.name || 'Component'
  })`;

  return AuthGuard;
}

// HOC that injects analytics tracking
function withTracking(WrappedComponent, eventName) {
  return function TrackedComponent(props) {
    const { track } = useAnalytics();

    useEffect(() =&gt; {
      track(`${eventName}_viewed`);
    }, [track]);

    const handleClick = useCallback((e) =&gt; {
      track(`${eventName}_clicked`);
      props.onClick?.(e);
    }, [track, props.onClick]);

    return &lt;WrappedComponent {...props} onClick={handleClick} /&gt;;
  };
}

// Usage
const ProtectedDashboard = withAuth(Dashboard);
const TrackedButton = withTracking(Button, 'cta_button');

// Composing HOCs
const ProtectedTrackedDashboard = withAuth(withTracking(Dashboard, 'dashboard'));
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q5 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q5">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q5"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q5">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;
      5. What is the render props pattern?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q5" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q5"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>render props pattern</strong> is a technique for sharing stateful logic between
        components by passing a function as a prop. The component with the shared logic calls that
        function to produce its output, handing back control over what gets rendered. This inverts the
        normal relationship: instead of the parent dictating what renders, the parent supplies the
        rendering instructions to the logic-holding child.
      </p>
      <p>
        The function prop is often literally named <code>render</code>, but it doesn't have to be —
        using <code>children</code> as a function ("function-as-children") achieves the same pattern with
        cleaner JSX. React Router's early versions used render props extensively;
        <code>&lt;Route render={({ match }) =&gt; ...} /&gt;</code> is the canonical example.
      </p>
      <p>
        Render props avoid the <code>displayName</code> and static-method forwarding problems of HOCs,
        and they make the data flow explicit — you can see exactly what values are being injected.
        The downside is potential "callback hell" when multiple render-prop components are nested, which
        custom hooks have largely solved.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Logic-holding component exposes its state via render prop
function MouseTracker({ render }) {
  const [position, setPosition] = useState({ x: 0, y: 0 });

  const handleMouseMove = useCallback((e) =&gt; {
    setPosition({ x: e.clientX, y: e.clientY });
  }, []);

  return (
    &lt;div style={{ height: '100vh' }} onMouseMove={handleMouseMove}&gt;
      {render(position)}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Consumer decides what to render with the position data
function App() {
  return (
    &lt;MouseTracker
      render={({ x, y }) =&gt; (
        &lt;h1&gt;Mouse is at ({x}, {y})&lt;/h1&gt;
      )}
    /&gt;
  );
}

// --- children-as-function variant (cleaner JSX) ---
function DataFetcher({ url, children }) {
  const [data, setData] = useState(null);
  const [error, setError] = useState(null);
  const [loading, setLoading] = useState(true);

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    fetch(url)
      .then(r =&gt; r.json())
      .then(setData)
      .catch(setError)
      .finally(() =&gt; setLoading(false));
  }, [url]);

  return children({ data, error, loading });
}

// Usage
&lt;DataFetcher url="/api/products"&gt;
  {({ data, loading, error }) =&gt; {
    if (loading) return &lt;Spinner /&gt;;
    if (error)   return &lt;ErrorBanner error={error} /&gt;;
    return &lt;ProductGrid products={data} /&gt;;
  }}
&lt;/DataFetcher&gt;
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q6 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q6">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q6"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q6">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      6. What is the compound components pattern?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q6" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q6"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong>Compound components</strong> are a set of components that work together to form a single
        cohesive UI unit, sharing implicit state through React Context rather than through explicit prop
        drilling. The parent component owns state and distributes it to child sub-components that are
        namespaced under it. The consumer assembles the final UI by composing those sub-components in any
        order — achieving high flexibility without exposing complex configuration props.
      </p>
      <p>
        HTML's <code>&lt;select&gt;</code> + <code>&lt;option&gt;</code> is the original compound
        component pair — they communicate implicitly, the browser handles their shared state, and you
        can compose them however you like. React's <code>&lt;Select&gt;</code> +
        <code>&lt;Select.Option&gt;</code> pattern mimics this.
      </p>
      <p>
        The pattern avoids "prop explosion" — instead of a single <code>&lt;Tabs tabs={[...]} /&gt;</code>
        with a complex config object, you get composable
        <code>&lt;Tabs&gt;&lt;Tabs.List&gt;...&lt;/Tabs.List&gt;&lt;Tabs.Panels&gt;...&lt;/Tabs.Panels&gt;&lt;/Tabs&gt;</code>.
        Consumers can insert extra DOM elements, reorder sub-components, or omit optional parts entirely.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Internal context
const TabsContext = React.createContext(null);

// Root compound component — owns the shared state
function Tabs({ children, defaultIndex = 0 }) {
  const [activeIndex, setActiveIndex] = useState(defaultIndex);
  return (
    &lt;TabsContext.Provider value={{ activeIndex, setActiveIndex }}&gt;
      &lt;div className="tabs"&gt;{children}&lt;/div&gt;
    &lt;/TabsContext.Provider&gt;
  );
}

// Sub-component: tab list
Tabs.List = function TabList({ children }) {
  return &lt;div role="tablist" className="tabs__list"&gt;{children}&lt;/div&gt;;
};

// Sub-component: individual tab trigger
Tabs.Tab = function Tab({ children, index }) {
  const { activeIndex, setActiveIndex } = useContext(TabsContext);
  return (
    &lt;button
      role="tab"
      aria-selected={activeIndex === index}
      className={`tabs__tab ${activeIndex === index ? 'active' : ''}`}
      onClick={() =&gt; setActiveIndex(index)}
    &gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
};

// Sub-component: panels container
Tabs.Panels = function TabPanels({ children }) {
  return &lt;div className="tabs__panels"&gt;{children}&lt;/div&gt;;
};

// Sub-component: individual panel
Tabs.Panel = function TabPanel({ children, index }) {
  const { activeIndex } = useContext(TabsContext);
  if (activeIndex !== index) return null;
  return (
    &lt;div role="tabpanel" className="tabs__panel"&gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
};

// Consumer — total control over composition
function App() {
  return (
    &lt;Tabs defaultIndex={0}&gt;
      &lt;Tabs.List&gt;
        &lt;Tabs.Tab index={0}&gt;Overview&lt;/Tabs.Tab&gt;
        &lt;Tabs.Tab index={1}&gt;Details&lt;/Tabs.Tab&gt;
        &lt;Tabs.Tab index={2}&gt;Reviews&lt;/Tabs.Tab&gt;
      &lt;/Tabs.List&gt;
      &lt;Tabs.Panels&gt;
        &lt;Tabs.Panel index={0}&gt;&lt;OverviewContent /&gt;&lt;/Tabs.Panel&gt;
        &lt;Tabs.Panel index={1}&gt;&lt;DetailsContent /&gt;&lt;/Tabs.Panel&gt;
        &lt;Tabs.Panel index={2}&gt;&lt;ReviewsContent /&gt;&lt;/Tabs.Panel&gt;
      &lt;/Tabs.Panels&gt;
    &lt;/Tabs&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q7 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q7">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q7"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q7">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      7. What is the controlled components pattern applied to custom components?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q7" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q7"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        React's built-in controlled component concept (where <code>value</code> + <code>onChange</code>
        give the parent full control of form input state) extends naturally to custom components. A
        <strong>controlled custom component</strong> accepts its current value via a prop and notifies
        the parent of changes via a callback — it holds <em>no</em> internal state for that value.
        An <strong>uncontrolled custom component</strong> manages its own internal state and only
        surfaces it on demand (via a ref or a final submit callback).
      </p>
      <p>
        Many component libraries (Headless UI, Radix UI, React Aria) support both modes using a pattern
        called <strong>uncontrolled + controlled duality</strong>: if a value prop is passed, the
        component is controlled; if only a defaultValue prop is passed, it manages its own state.
        Implementing this yourself requires careful handling to avoid the "controlled → uncontrolled"
        React warning.
      </p>
      <p>
        The practical decision: use controlled mode when the parent needs to read or manipulate the value
        at any time (form validation, dependent field logic, testing); use uncontrolled when the component
        is self-contained and the parent only needs the final value.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// A controlled/uncontrolled dual-mode custom Rating component
function Rating({ value, defaultValue = 0, onChange }) {
  // If `value` is provided externally, component is controlled
  const isControlled = value !== undefined;

  const [internalValue, setInternalValue] = useState(defaultValue);
  const displayValue = isControlled ? value : internalValue;

  const handleSelect = (star) =&gt; {
    if (!isControlled) {
      setInternalValue(star);
    }
    onChange?.(star);
  };

  return (
    &lt;div className="rating" aria-label={`Rating: ${displayValue} of 5`}&gt;
      {[1, 2, 3, 4, 5].map(star =&gt; (
        &lt;button
          key={star}
          type="button"
          className={star &lt;= displayValue ? 'star filled' : 'star'}
          onClick={() =&gt; handleSelect(star)}
          aria-label={`${star} star`}
        /&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// --- Controlled usage (parent drives state) ---
function ReviewForm() {
  const [rating, setRating] = useState(0);
  return (
    &lt;form&gt;
      &lt;Rating value={rating} onChange={setRating} /&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;You selected: {rating} stars&lt;/p&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}

// --- Uncontrolled usage (component drives its own state) ---
function QuickRating() {
  return &lt;Rating defaultValue={3} onChange={val =&gt; console.log('Rated:', val)} /&gt;;
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q8 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q8">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q8"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q8">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      8. What is the provider pattern?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q8" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q8"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>provider pattern</strong> uses React Context to make data or services available to
        any descendant component in the tree without explicit prop drilling. A Provider component sits
        high in the component tree and a corresponding <code>useXxx</code> hook (or direct
        <code>useContext</code> call) lets any descendant subscribe to that data.
      </p>
      <p>
        The pattern is used for genuinely global or subtree-wide concerns: authentication state, theme,
        locale/i18n, feature flags, toast notifications. It should not be used as a shortcut to avoid
        passing props down two levels — that is an anti-pattern that makes data flow hard to trace.
      </p>
      <p>
        A well-designed provider module exports three things: the Provider component, a custom hook
        (<code>useAuth</code>, <code>useTheme</code>), and optionally the context object itself for
        advanced cases. The custom hook should throw a descriptive error if called outside the provider,
        making misconfiguration obvious immediately.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// auth-context.tsx
const AuthContext = React.createContext(null);

export function AuthProvider({ children }) {
  const [user, setUser] = useState(null);
  const [loading, setLoading] = useState(true);

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    // Rehydrate from session/token on mount
    getCurrentUser()
      .then(setUser)
      .catch(() =&gt; setUser(null))
      .finally(() =&gt; setLoading(false));
  }, []);

  const login = useCallback(async (credentials) =&gt; {
    const loggedInUser = await authService.login(credentials);
    setUser(loggedInUser);
  }, []);

  const logout = useCallback(async () =&gt; {
    await authService.logout();
    setUser(null);
  }, []);

  const value = useMemo(
    () =&gt; ({ user, loading, login, logout, isAuthenticated: !!user }),
    [user, loading, login, logout]
  );

  return &lt;AuthContext.Provider value={value}&gt;{children}&lt;/AuthContext.Provider&gt;;
}

// Custom hook with guard
export function useAuth() {
  const ctx = useContext(AuthContext);
  if (ctx === null) {
    throw new Error('useAuth must be used inside &lt;AuthProvider&gt;');
  }
  return ctx;
}

// Any descendant can consume without prop drilling
function NavBar() {
  const { user, logout, isAuthenticated } = useAuth();
  return (
    &lt;nav&gt;
      {isAuthenticated ? (
        &lt;&gt;
          &lt;span&gt;Hello, {user.name}&lt;/span&gt;
          &lt;button onClick={logout}&gt;Log out&lt;/button&gt;
        &lt;/&gt;
      ) : (
        &lt;a href="/login"&gt;Log in&lt;/a&gt;
      )}
    &lt;/nav&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q9 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q9">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q9"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q9">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      9. What is the observer pattern in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q9" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q9"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>observer pattern</strong> defines a one-to-many dependency so that when one object
        (the subject/observable) changes state, all its dependents (observers) are notified automatically.
        In React, this manifests in several ways: components <em>observe</em> Redux state slices via
        <code>useSelector</code>, components observe Context values via <code>useContext</code>, and
        components observe external stores via <code>useSyncExternalStore</code>.
      </p>
      <p>
        A custom event bus or pub/sub system is the most explicit implementation of the observer pattern
        outside of React's built-in primitives. It is useful for communication between completely
        independent parts of a large app — for example, a file-upload widget deep in the tree emitting
        an event that a top-level notification banner subscribes to.
      </p>
      <p>
        React Query and SWR implement the observer pattern internally — multiple components subscribed
        to the same query key all re-render when fresh data arrives, without any shared state boilerplate
        in the application code.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Simple event emitter (observer pattern) for cross-subtree communication
class EventEmitter {
  constructor() { this.listeners = new Map(); }

  on(event, listener) {
    if (!this.listeners.has(event)) this.listeners.set(event, new Set());
    this.listeners.get(event).add(listener);
    // Return cleanup function
    return () =&gt; this.listeners.get(event)?.delete(listener);
  }

  emit(event, payload) {
    this.listeners.get(event)?.forEach(fn =&gt; fn(payload));
  }
}

export const eventBus = new EventEmitter();

// Hook to subscribe to an event (observer)
function useEventBus(event, handler) {
  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const unsubscribe = eventBus.on(event, handler);
    return unsubscribe; // cleanup on unmount
  }, [event, handler]);
}

// Publisher component — deep inside the tree
function FileUploader() {
  const handleUpload = async (file) =&gt; {
    await uploadFile(file);
    eventBus.emit('file:uploaded', { fileName: file.name });
  };
  return &lt;input type="file" onChange={e =&gt; handleUpload(e.target.files[0])} /&gt;;
}

// Observer component — completely separate part of the tree
function NotificationBanner() {
  const [message, setMessage] = useState('');

  useEventBus('file:uploaded', useCallback(({ fileName }) =&gt; {
    setMessage(`${fileName} uploaded successfully!`);
    setTimeout(() =&gt; setMessage(''), 3000);
  }, []));

  if (!message) return null;
  return &lt;div className="banner banner--success"&gt;{message}&lt;/div&gt;;
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q10 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q10">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q10"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q10">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      10. What is feature-based folder structure?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q10" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q10"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong>Feature-based (domain-based) folder structure</strong> organises code by business
        domain rather than by technical type. Instead of top-level <code>components/</code>,
        <code>hooks/</code>, and <code>utils/</code> folders that mix all domains together, you have
        top-level <code>features/</code> folders where each feature folder contains its own components,
        hooks, state, API calls, types, and tests. Everything related to "authentication" lives in
        <code>features/auth/</code>, everything related to "checkout" in <code>features/checkout/</code>.
      </p>
      <p>
        This structure scales far better than type-based organisation. In a type-based layout, adding a
        single feature touches 6-8 different top-level directories. In a feature-based layout you work
        entirely within one folder. Features can be added, removed, or moved with minimal cross-feature
        changes.
      </p>
      <p>
        Shared utilities and truly cross-cutting UI primitives live in a <code>shared/</code> or
        <code>common/</code> folder at the top level. The discipline is to keep shared things genuinely
        shared and resist the temptation to put everything shared into feature folders.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Type-based (poor at scale)
src/
  components/
    UserCard.tsx
    ProductCard.tsx
    CheckoutForm.tsx
  hooks/
    useUser.ts
    useProducts.ts
    useCart.ts
  api/
    userApi.ts
    productApi.ts
    cartApi.ts
  store/
    userSlice.ts
    productSlice.ts
    cartSlice.ts

// Feature-based (scales well)
src/
  features/
    auth/
      components/
        LoginForm.tsx
        SignupForm.tsx
      hooks/
        useAuth.ts
        useSession.ts
      api/
        authApi.ts
      store/
        authSlice.ts
      types/
        auth.types.ts
      index.ts          // public API — barrel export
    products/
      components/
        ProductCard.tsx
        ProductGrid.tsx
        ProductFilter.tsx
      hooks/
        useProducts.ts
        useProductSearch.ts
      api/
        productApi.ts
      store/
        productSlice.ts
      index.ts
    cart/
      components/ ...
      hooks/ ...
      index.ts
  shared/               // truly cross-cutting
    components/
      Button.tsx
      Modal.tsx
      Spinner.tsx
    hooks/
      useDebounce.ts
      useLocalStorage.ts
    utils/
      formatDate.ts
      currency.ts
  app/
    App.tsx
    router.tsx
    store.ts
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q11 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q11">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q11"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q11">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      11. What is the barrel export pattern?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q11" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q11"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        A <strong>barrel</strong> is an <code>index.ts</code> (or <code>index.js</code>) file that
        re-exports everything from a module folder, acting as a single public entry point. Consumers
        import from the folder path rather than from specific internal file paths, decoupling them from
        internal file organisation.
      </p>
      <p>
        Benefits: import paths are shorter and more stable; internal refactors (renaming or splitting
        files) don't break consumers as long as the public API stays the same; the barrel acts as an
        explicit contract for what a feature or module exposes.
      </p>
      <p>
        The primary risk is <strong>tree-shaking degradation</strong>. Bundlers that cannot statically
        analyse re-exports may import entire barrel files when only one export is needed. Modern bundlers
        (Vite, webpack 5 with ESM) handle this well, but circular dependency issues can still arise.
        As a rule, avoid importing from a feature's barrel file <em>inside</em> that same feature — only
        cross-feature imports should go through the barrel.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// features/auth/components/LoginForm.tsx
export function LoginForm() { ... }

// features/auth/components/SignupForm.tsx
export function SignupForm() { ... }

// features/auth/hooks/useAuth.ts
export function useAuth() { ... }

// features/auth/index.ts  ← the barrel
export { LoginForm }  from './components/LoginForm';
export { SignupForm } from './components/SignupForm';
export { useAuth }    from './hooks/useAuth';
// Note: do NOT export internal implementation details

// --- Consumer imports (clean) ---
import { LoginForm, useAuth } from '@/features/auth';
// vs. without barrel (fragile internal path):
import { LoginForm } from '@/features/auth/components/LoginForm';
import { useAuth }   from '@/features/auth/hooks/useAuth';

// --- Typed barrel with explicit public API ---
// features/products/index.ts
export type { Product, ProductFilter } from './types/product.types';
export { ProductCard }   from './components/ProductCard';
export { ProductGrid }   from './components/ProductGrid';
export { useProducts }   from './hooks/useProducts';
export { productApi }    from './api/productApi';
// productSlice is intentionally NOT exported — internal to the feature
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q12 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q12">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q12"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q12">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      12. What is colocation in React architecture?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q12" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q12"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong>Colocation</strong> is the principle of placing code as close as possible to where it
        is used. Kent C. Dodds summarises it as: "Things that change together should be located together."
        In React this means: tests next to the components they test, styles next to the components they
        style, GraphQL queries in the same file as the component that uses them, and state managed at
        the lowest component level that needs it.
      </p>
      <p>
        The opposite of colocation is centralisation — putting all tests in a single <code>__tests__/</code>
        top-level folder, all styles in a top-level <code>styles/</code> folder. While centralisation
        feels tidy, it creates a split-brain problem: to understand a component you must navigate between
        multiple distant folders. Colocation keeps all relevant context in one place.
      </p>
      <p>
        State colocation is especially impactful: state that only one component uses should live in that
        component, not in a global store. Lifting state only as high as it needs to go — no higher —
        reduces unnecessary re-renders and makes each component easier to reason about.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Non-colocated (scattered across the project)
src/
  components/UserCard.tsx
  tests/components/UserCard.test.tsx
  styles/UserCard.module.css
  queries/userQueries.ts          // GraphQL queries
  stories/UserCard.stories.tsx

// Colocated (everything lives with the component)
src/
  features/users/
    components/
      UserCard/
        UserCard.tsx
        UserCard.test.tsx         // test next to component
        UserCard.module.css       // styles next to component
        UserCard.stories.tsx      // storybook story next to component
        UserCard.graphql          // query next to component (or inside .tsx)
        index.ts                  // barrel

// State colocation example
// WRONG: global store holds UI state only one component uses
const globalStore = {
  isDropdownOpen: false,        // only Dropdown cares
  dropdownSelectedItem: null,   // only Dropdown cares
  products: [...],              // truly global — fine
};

// RIGHT: local state stays local
function Dropdown({ options, onSelect }) {
  const [isOpen, setIsOpen] = useState(false);         // colocated
  const [selected, setSelected] = useState(null);      // colocated
  // ...
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q13 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q13">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q13"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q13">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      13. What are feature flags and how do you implement them in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q13" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q13"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong>Feature flags</strong> (also called feature toggles) are configuration values that
        enable or disable features at runtime without deploying new code. They allow teams to merge
        code continuously without releasing it (<em>trunk-based development</em>), run A/B tests,
        do percentage rollouts, grant early access to specific users, and kill a misbehaving feature
        instantly.
      </p>
      <p>
        Implementation ranges from a simple JSON config file (as used in this YMS project) to a
        full-featured remote service like LaunchDarkly or Statsig. The React integration follows the
        provider pattern: a <code>FeatureFlagsProvider</code> loads the config and distributes it via
        Context; a <code>useFeatureFlag(flagName)</code> hook reads individual flags; and a
        <code>&lt;FeatureFlag name="x"&gt;</code> component renders children conditionally.
      </p>
      <p>
        Critical rules: feature flags should be boolean (on/off), have a clear owner, and have a
        planned expiry. Long-lived flags become technical debt — once a feature is fully rolled out,
        the flag and its conditional branches should be removed.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// 1. Feature flag config (can be a static JSON or fetched from a service)
// config/featureFlags.json
{
  "newCheckout": true,
  "darkMode": false,
  "aiSearch": true,
  "betaDashboard": false
}

// 2. Provider
const FeatureFlagsContext = React.createContext({});

export function FeatureFlagsProvider({ children }) {
  const [flags, setFlags] = useState({});
  const [loading, setLoading] = useState(true);

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    // Could be static JSON, API call, or LaunchDarkly SDK
    import('./config/featureFlags.json')
      .then(module =&gt; setFlags(module.default))
      .finally(() =&gt; setLoading(false));
  }, []);

  if (loading) return null;

  return (
    &lt;FeatureFlagsContext.Provider value={flags}&gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/FeatureFlagsContext.Provider&gt;
  );
}

// 3. Hook
export function useFeatureFlag(flagName) {
  const flags = useContext(FeatureFlagsContext);
  return !!flags[flagName];
}

// 4. Declarative component
export function FeatureFlag({ name, fallback = null, children }) {
  const enabled = useFeatureFlag(name);
  return enabled ? children : fallback;
}

// 5. Usage
function App() {
  const isNewCheckout = useFeatureFlag('newCheckout');

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      {/* Imperative */}
      {isNewCheckout ? &lt;NewCheckout /&gt; : &lt;LegacyCheckout /&gt;}

      {/* Declarative */}
      &lt;FeatureFlag name="aiSearch" fallback={&lt;BasicSearch /&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;AISearch /&gt;
      &lt;/FeatureFlag&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q14 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q14">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q14"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q14">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      14. What is the adapter pattern for API calls?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q14" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q14"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>adapter pattern</strong> wraps a third-party or external interface with a custom
        interface that matches what your application expects. Applied to API calls, it means creating a
        thin abstraction layer between your React components/hooks and the actual HTTP library
        (fetch, axios, GraphQL client). Components call your adapter; the adapter calls the library.
      </p>
      <p>
        This provides three concrete benefits. First, it normalises data shapes — the backend API may
        return <code>snake_case</code> or oddly-nested objects; the adapter transforms them to the
        camelCase, flat shape your TypeScript types expect. Second, it makes the HTTP library swappable
        — switching from axios to fetch or from REST to GraphQL requires changing only the adapter, not
        dozens of component files. Third, it makes testing trivial — you mock the adapter, not the
        underlying HTTP call.
      </p>
      <p>
        In this YMS codebase, <code>src/api/apiAxios.ts</code> is the base adapter layer — it wraps
        axios with request ID injection and error interceptors. Domain-specific adapters like
        <code>src/api/trailer.ts</code> build on top of it.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// The backend API returns:
// { user_id: 1, first_name: "Alice", last_name: "Smith", created_at: "2024-01-01T00:00:00Z" }

// Our app's type (camelCase, flat)
interface User {
  id: number;
  firstName: string;
  lastName: string;
  fullName: string;
  createdAt: Date;
}

// Raw API response type
interface UserApiResponse {
  user_id: number;
  first_name: string;
  last_name: string;
  created_at: string;
}

// Adapter layer: transforms + abstracts HTTP
const userAdapter = {
  // Transform API shape → app shape
  fromApi(raw: UserApiResponse): User {
    return {
      id: raw.user_id,
      firstName: raw.first_name,
      lastName: raw.last_name,
      fullName: `${raw.first_name} ${raw.last_name}`,
      createdAt: new Date(raw.created_at),
    };
  },

  // All HTTP calls go through here
  async getById(id: number): Promise&lt;User&gt; {
    const { data } = await apiAxios.get&lt;UserApiResponse&gt;(`/users/${id}`);
    return this.fromApi(data);
  },

  async getAll(): Promise&lt;User[]&gt; {
    const { data } = await apiAxios.get&lt;UserApiResponse[]&gt;('/users');
    return data.map(this.fromApi);
  },

  async update(id: number, patch: Partial&lt;User&gt;): Promise&lt;User&gt; {
    const { data } = await apiAxios.patch&lt;UserApiResponse&gt;(`/users/${id}`, patch);
    return this.fromApi(data);
  },
};

// Custom hook consumes the adapter — not axios directly
function useUser(userId: number) {
  const [user, setUser] = useState&lt;User | null&gt;(null);
  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    userAdapter.getById(userId).then(setUser);
  }, [userId]);
  return user;
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q15 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q15">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q15"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q15">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      15. What is the repository pattern applied to React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q15" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q15"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>repository pattern</strong>, borrowed from Domain-Driven Design, provides an
        abstraction layer over data sources — whether REST API, GraphQL, localStorage, IndexedDB, or
        a mock. Application code (hooks, components) depends on the repository interface, not on any
        specific data source implementation. Swapping implementations (e.g., replacing a mock with a
        real API, or REST with GraphQL) requires zero changes in consuming code.
      </p>
      <p>
        In React, a repository is typically a plain object or class with methods that return Promises.
        The repository is injected via Context (for React-idiomatic feel) or instantiated as a module
        singleton. The key discipline is that repositories never know about React — they don't call
        hooks or interact with the DOM.
      </p>
      <p>
        This pattern is especially valuable for testability: tests can supply a fake in-memory
        repository that fulfils requests synchronously, making tests fast and deterministic without
        network mocking.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Repository interface (TypeScript)
interface IProductRepository {
  findAll(): Promise&lt;Product[]&gt;;
  findById(id: string): Promise&lt;Product | null&gt;;
  create(data: Omit&lt;Product, 'id'&gt;): Promise&lt;Product&gt;;
  update(id: string, data: Partial&lt;Product&gt;): Promise&lt;Product&gt;;
  delete(id: string): Promise&lt;void&gt;;
}

// REST implementation
class RestProductRepository implements IProductRepository {
  async findAll() {
    const { data } = await apiAxios.get('/products');
    return data.map(productAdapter.fromApi);
  }
  async findById(id) {
    const { data } = await apiAxios.get(`/products/${id}`);
    return productAdapter.fromApi(data);
  }
  async create(productData) {
    const { data } = await apiAxios.post('/products', productData);
    return productAdapter.fromApi(data);
  }
  async update(id, patch) {
    const { data } = await apiAxios.patch(`/products/${id}`, patch);
    return productAdapter.fromApi(data);
  }
  async delete(id) { await apiAxios.delete(`/products/${id}`); }
}

// In-memory implementation (for tests / offline mode)
class InMemoryProductRepository implements IProductRepository {
  private items: Product[] = [...seedProducts];
  async findAll() { return [...this.items]; }
  async findById(id) { return this.items.find(p =&gt; p.id === id) ?? null; }
  async create(data) {
    const product = { ...data, id: crypto.randomUUID() };
    this.items.push(product);
    return product;
  }
  async update(id, patch) {
    const idx = this.items.findIndex(p =&gt; p.id === id);
    this.items[idx] = { ...this.items[idx], ...patch };
    return this.items[idx];
  }
  async delete(id) { this.items = this.items.filter(p =&gt; p.id !== id); }
}

// Context injection
const ProductRepoContext = React.createContext&lt;IProductRepository&gt;(
  new RestProductRepository()
);

// Hook
function useProducts() {
  const repo = useContext(ProductRepoContext);
  const [products, setProducts] = useState&lt;Product[]&gt;([]);
  useEffect(() =&gt; { repo.findAll().then(setProducts); }, [repo]);
  return products;
}

// In tests: wrap with InMemoryProductRepository
render(
  &lt;ProductRepoContext.Provider value={new InMemoryProductRepository()}&gt;
    &lt;ProductGrid /&gt;
  &lt;/ProductRepoContext.Provider&gt;
);
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q16 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q16">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q16"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q16">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      16. What are micro-frontends?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q16" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q16"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong>Micro-frontends</strong> apply microservices thinking to the frontend: instead of one
        large monolithic React app, multiple independent teams own and deploy separate frontend
        applications (or modules) that compose into a unified user experience. Each micro-frontend can
        use different frameworks, different release cycles, and independent CI/CD pipelines.
      </p>
      <p>
        There are several composition strategies. <em>Build-time composition</em> (npm packages) is
        the simplest but tightest coupling. <em>Runtime composition via iframes</em> offers the
        strongest isolation but limits communication. <em>Runtime composition via JavaScript</em>
        (Module Federation, import maps, or single-spa) allows shared dependencies and smooth UI
        integration. <em>Server-side composition</em> stitches HTML fragments on the server.
      </p>
      <p>
        The trade-offs are significant: micro-frontends add infrastructure complexity, potential
        bundle duplication, harder cross-team coordination for shared state, and a more complex local
        development setup. They are best suited for very large organisations where multiple independent
        teams work on distinct product areas and deployment independence is a real need — not for
        medium-sized applications where a well-structured monorepo suffices.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Conceptual structure of a micro-frontend architecture
//
// shell/                    ← orchestrator / app shell
//   src/
//     App.tsx               ← loads remote modules dynamically
//     router.tsx
// mfe-auth/                 ← owned by Auth team
//   src/
//     LoginPage.tsx
//     SignupPage.tsx
//   webpack.config.js       ← exposes LoginPage via Module Federation
// mfe-products/             ← owned by Catalog team
//   src/
//     ProductGrid.tsx
//     ProductDetail.tsx
//   webpack.config.js       ← exposes ProductGrid
// mfe-checkout/             ← owned by Commerce team
//   src/
//     CheckoutFlow.tsx
//   webpack.config.js

// Shell dynamically loads remote components at runtime
const RemoteLoginPage = React.lazy(() =&gt;
  import('mfe_auth/LoginPage')    // resolved by Module Federation
);

const RemoteProductGrid = React.lazy(() =&gt;
  import('mfe_products/ProductGrid')
);

function Shell() {
  return (
    &lt;BrowserRouter&gt;
      &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;GlobalSpinner /&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;Routes&gt;
          &lt;Route path="/login"    element={&lt;RemoteLoginPage /&gt;} /&gt;
          &lt;Route path="/products" element={&lt;RemoteProductGrid /&gt;} /&gt;
        &lt;/Routes&gt;
      &lt;/Suspense&gt;
    &lt;/BrowserRouter&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q17 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q17">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q17"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q17">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      17. What is Module Federation in Webpack 5?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q17" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q17"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong>Module Federation</strong> is a Webpack 5 feature that allows separately compiled
        JavaScript bundles to share code at runtime. A <em>host</em> application can dynamically load
        <em>remote</em> modules from other deployed applications as if they were local imports. Remotes
        can also declare <em>shared</em> libraries (like React) that are deduplicated — preventing
        multiple React instances from loading.
      </p>
      <p>
        Each federated app has a <code>remoteEntry.js</code> manifest file loaded by the host. This
        manifest tells Webpack what modules are exposed and which shared libraries are available. If
        a shared library's version is compatible, the already-loaded version is reused; otherwise a
        fallback is loaded.
      </p>
      <p>
        This is the technical backbone of production micro-frontend architectures at scale. It enables
        independent deployments: the checkout team can ship a new <code>CheckoutFlow</code> without
        the shell app redeploying. The main gotchas are version mismatch between shared libraries,
        error boundaries needed for failed remote loads, and local development complexity requiring
        all remotes to run simultaneously.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// mfe-products/webpack.config.js — REMOTE
const { ModuleFederationPlugin } = require('webpack').container;

module.exports = {
  plugins: [
    new ModuleFederationPlugin({
      name: 'mfe_products',
      filename: 'remoteEntry.js',
      exposes: {
        './ProductGrid':   './src/components/ProductGrid',
        './ProductDetail': './src/components/ProductDetail',
      },
      shared: {
        react:        { singleton: true, requiredVersion: '^18.0.0' },
        'react-dom':  { singleton: true, requiredVersion: '^18.0.0' },
      },
    }),
  ],
};

// shell/webpack.config.js — HOST
module.exports = {
  plugins: [
    new ModuleFederationPlugin({
      name: 'shell',
      remotes: {
        mfe_products: 'mfe_products@https://products.example.com/remoteEntry.js',
        mfe_auth:     'mfe_auth@https://auth.example.com/remoteEntry.js',
      },
      shared: {
        react:       { singleton: true, requiredVersion: '^18.0.0' },
        'react-dom': { singleton: true, requiredVersion: '^18.0.0' },
      },
    }),
  ],
};

// shell/src/App.tsx — consuming the remote
const ProductGrid = React.lazy(() =&gt; import('mfe_products/ProductGrid'));

function App() {
  return (
    &lt;ErrorBoundary fallback={&lt;RemoteLoadError /&gt;}&gt;
      &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;Skeleton /&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;ProductGrid category="electronics" /&gt;
      &lt;/Suspense&gt;
    &lt;/ErrorBoundary&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q18 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q18">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q18"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q18">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      18. What is the strangler fig pattern applied to React migrations?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q18" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q18"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>strangler fig pattern</strong>, named after the strangler fig tree that grows
        around and eventually replaces its host tree, is a migration strategy where a new system
        is built incrementally around the old one. Traffic is gradually routed to the new system,
        and the old system is "strangled" piece by piece until it can be removed entirely.
      </p>
      <p>
        Applied to React: you might be migrating from an AngularJS or jQuery app to React, or from
        React class components to function components and hooks, or from a monolith to micro-frontends.
        The key is that both the old and new code coexist during the transition — you never do a
        "big bang" rewrite that blocks feature development for months.
      </p>
      <p>
        Practical tactics: mount React components inside legacy AngularJS templates using
        <code>ReactDOM.render</code>; use an adapter layer to call legacy API services from new React
        code; use feature flags to swap routes between old and new implementations; migrate
        route-by-route rather than component-by-component to deliver user value with each step.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Scenario: migrating a legacy jQuery/server-rendered page to React route-by-route

// 1. Shell app routes some paths to React, others still to legacy server
//    Typically done with a reverse proxy (nginx) or a thin shell app

// nginx.conf (simplified)
// location /app/products {
//   proxy_pass http://react-app:3000;   ← new React app
// }
// location /app/checkout {
//   proxy_pass http://legacy-app:8080;  ← old server-rendered app
// }

// 2. React app can embed legacy pages in an iframe during transition
function LegacyPageWrapper({ legacyPath }) {
  return (
    &lt;iframe
      src={`https://legacy.example.com${legacyPath}`}
      style={{ width: '100%', height: '100vh', border: 'none' }}
      title="Legacy page"
    /&gt;
  );
}

// 3. Feature flag controls which implementation renders per route
function CheckoutRoute() {
  const useNewCheckout = useFeatureFlag('newReactCheckout');
  return useNewCheckout
    ? &lt;NewReactCheckout /&gt;
    : &lt;LegacyPageWrapper legacyPath="/checkout" /&gt;;
}

// 4. Once all routes migrated, remove legacy wrapper and flag
// The "strangler" has replaced the host tree.

// 5. Class → function component migration follows same principle
// Old: class UserProfile extends React.Component { ... }
// New: function UserProfile(props) { ... }
// Swap one component at a time, run tests, ship — never a full rewrite.
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q19 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q19">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q19"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q19">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      19. What is design system architecture in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q19" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q19"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        A <strong>design system</strong> is a collection of reusable components, design tokens, and
        guidelines that ensure visual and behavioural consistency across products. In React, it is
        typically a standalone npm package (or monorepo workspace) that product teams install as a
        dependency. It separates "how things look and behave" from "what the product does".
      </p>
      <p>
        The architecture has clear layers: <em>design tokens</em> (CSS variables or JS constants for
        colours, spacing, typography, breakpoints) at the foundation; <em>primitive components</em>
        (Button, Input, Text, Icon) built directly on tokens; <em>composite components</em>
        (Card, Modal, DataTable) built from primitives; and <em>patterns/templates</em> for common
        page layouts and flows.
      </p>
      <p>
        Key architectural decisions: headless vs. styled (Radix UI / Headless UI provide accessible
        behaviour without styles; teams add their own styles on top), CSS-in-JS vs. CSS Modules vs.
        design token CSS variables, and theming strategy (single theme vs. multi-brand themes via
        token overrides). Storybook is the standard tool for developing, documenting, and testing
        design system components in isolation.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// design-system/
//   tokens/
//     colors.ts
//     spacing.ts
//     typography.ts
//   primitives/
//     Button/
//     Input/
//     Text/
//   composites/
//     Card/
//     Modal/
//     DataTable/
//   index.ts

// tokens/colors.ts — design tokens as JS constants & CSS variables
export const colors = {
  primary:   { 50: '#eff6ff', 500: '#3b82f6', 900: '#1e3a8a' },
  danger:    { 50: '#fef2f2', 500: '#ef4444', 900: '#7f1d1d' },
  neutral:   { 0: '#ffffff', 100: '#f3f4f6', 900: '#111827' },
} as const;

// Applied as CSS custom properties for runtime theming
export const cssTokens = `
  :root {
    --color-primary: ${colors.primary[500]};
    --color-danger:  ${colors.danger[500]};
    --spacing-1:     4px;
    --spacing-2:     8px;
    --spacing-4:     16px;
    --radius-md:     6px;
    --font-sans:     Inter, system-ui, sans-serif;
  }
`;

// primitives/Button/Button.tsx
interface ButtonProps extends React.ButtonHTMLAttributes&lt;HTMLButtonElement&gt; {
  variant?: 'primary' | 'secondary' | 'danger' | 'ghost';
  size?:    'sm' | 'md' | 'lg';
  loading?: boolean;
}

export function Button({
  variant = 'primary', size = 'md', loading = false,
  children, disabled, className, ...rest
}: ButtonProps) {
  return (
    &lt;button
      className={clsx('btn', `btn--${variant}`, `btn--${size}`, className)}
      disabled={disabled || loading}
      aria-busy={loading}
      {...rest}
    &gt;
      {loading &amp;&amp; &lt;Spinner size="sm" aria-hidden /&gt;}
      {children}
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q20 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q20">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q20"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q20">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;
      20. What is atomic design and how does it apply to React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q20" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q20"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong>Atomic design</strong>, introduced by Brad Frost, is a methodology for building UI
        systems by composing components at five levels of complexity, inspired by chemistry:
        <em>atoms</em>, <em>molecules</em>, <em>organisms</em>, <em>templates</em>, and <em>pages</em>.
        Each level is built from the level below it, creating a clear compositional hierarchy.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Atoms</strong> are the smallest indivisible UI elements: Button, Input, Label, Icon,
        Badge. <strong>Molecules</strong> combine atoms into small functional units: SearchBar
        (Input + Button + Icon), FormField (Label + Input + ValidationMessage).
        <strong>Organisms</strong> are meaningful, standalone UI sections: NavigationHeader
        (Logo + NavLinks + UserMenu), ProductCard (Image + Heading + Price + CartButton).
        <strong>Templates</strong> define layout structure without real content (wireframes with
        slots). <strong>Pages</strong> are templates filled with real data — they are the rendered
        routes in a React app.
      </p>
      <p>
        Atomic design maps naturally to a design system / Storybook structure. Atoms and molecules
        live in the design system package; organisms and templates are application-level components;
        pages are route-level components. The discipline enforces that higher levels don't leak
        implementation details down to lower ones.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// ATOMS — single-purpose, no composition
function Button({ children, ...props }) { ... }
function Input({ label, ...props }) { ... }
function Avatar({ src, alt, size }) { ... }
function Badge({ label, color }) { ... }

// MOLECULES — small functional combos of atoms
function SearchBar({ onSearch }) {
  const [query, setQuery] = useState('');
  return (
    &lt;div className="search-bar"&gt;
      &lt;Input value={query} onChange={e =&gt; setQuery(e.target.value)}
             placeholder="Search..." /&gt;
      &lt;Button onClick={() =&gt; onSearch(query)}&gt;Search&lt;/Button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

function UserMeta({ user }) {
  return (
    &lt;div className="user-meta"&gt;
      &lt;Avatar src={user.avatar} alt={user.name} size="sm" /&gt;
      &lt;span&gt;{user.name}&lt;/span&gt;
      &lt;Badge label={user.role} color="blue" /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// ORGANISMS — meaningful UI sections
function ProductCard({ product, onAddToCart }) {
  return (
    &lt;div className="product-card"&gt;
      &lt;img src={product.image} alt={product.name} /&gt;
      &lt;h3&gt;{product.name}&lt;/h3&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;${product.price}&lt;/p&gt;
      &lt;Badge label={product.category} /&gt;
      &lt;Button onClick={() =&gt; onAddToCart(product.id)}&gt;Add to Cart&lt;/Button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// TEMPLATES — layout scaffolding, no real data
function TwoColumnTemplate({ sidebar, main }) {
  return (
    &lt;div className="layout-two-col"&gt;
