      &lt;aside className="layout-two-col__sidebar"&gt;{sidebar}&lt;/aside&gt;
      &lt;main className="layout-two-col__main"&gt;{main}&lt;/main&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// PAGES — templates filled with real data (route components)
function ProductsPage() {
  const { products, isLoading } = useProducts();
  return (
    &lt;TwoColumnTemplate
      sidebar={&lt;FilterPanel /&gt;}
      main={&lt;ProductGrid products={products} isLoading={isLoading} /&gt;}
    /&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q21 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q21">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q21"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q21">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;
      21. What is the SOLID principles applied to React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q21" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q21"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        SOLID is a set of five object-oriented design principles that translate remarkably well to
        React component and hook design, even though React is not class-based (in modern practice).
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>S — Single Responsibility:</strong> Each component/hook does one thing. A
        <code>useUserProfile</code> hook fetches user data; a <code>UserProfileCard</code> component
        renders it. They do not bleed into each other's concerns.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>O — Open/Closed:</strong> Components should be open for extension but closed for
        modification. A <code>Button</code> component accepts a <code>className</code>, <code>as</code>
        (polymorphic), and render prop overrides so consumers can customise it without changing the
        Button source. Adding a new variant doesn't require touching the Button internals if it uses
        a <code>variant</code> prop.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>L — Liskov Substitution:</strong> Child components should be substitutable for their
        parent's expected interface. If a route accepts any component implementing
        <code>{ isLoading, data }</code> props, any component with that shape should work — a mock,
        a skeleton, or the real thing.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>I — Interface Segregation:</strong> Don't force components to depend on props they
        don't use. A <code>UserAvatar</code> that only needs <code>name</code> and <code>avatarUrl</code>
        should not receive the entire <code>User</code> object. Split large prop interfaces into
        focused ones.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>D — Dependency Inversion:</strong> High-level components should not depend on
        specific low-level implementations. Use Context / dependency injection to supply data sources;
        use render props or component slots to supply rendering; depend on interfaces (TypeScript types)
        not concrete classes.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// O — Open/Closed: Button open for extension via props, closed to modification
function Button({ variant = 'primary', size = 'md', as: Tag = 'button',
                  leftIcon, rightIcon, children, ...rest }) {
  return (
    &lt;Tag className={`btn btn--${variant} btn--${size}`} {...rest}&gt;
      {leftIcon}&lt;span&gt;{children}&lt;/span&gt;{rightIcon}
    &lt;/Tag&gt;
  );
}
// Consumer can make it a link without touching Button source:
&lt;Button as="a" href="/checkout" variant="primary"&gt;Checkout&lt;/Button&gt;

// I — Interface Segregation: narrow props, not a god object
// BAD:
function UserAvatar({ user }: { user: User }) {  // User has 20 fields
  return &lt;img src={user.avatarUrl} alt={user.fullName} /&gt;;
}

// GOOD: only depend on what you need
interface AvatarProps { avatarUrl: string; name: string; size?: 'sm' | 'md' | 'lg'; }
function UserAvatar({ avatarUrl, name, size = 'md' }: AvatarProps) {
  return &lt;img src={avatarUrl} alt={name} className={`avatar avatar--${size}`} /&gt;;
}

// D — Dependency Inversion: depend on interface, inject implementation
interface ILogger { log(msg: string): void; error(msg: string, err?: unknown): void; }

const LoggerContext = React.createContext&lt;ILogger&gt;(console);

function useLogger() { return useContext(LoggerContext); }

// Component depends on ILogger interface, not console directly
function PaymentForm() {
  const logger = useLogger();
  const handleSubmit = () =&gt; {
    try { processPayment(); }
    catch (e) { logger.error('Payment failed', e); }
  };
  return &lt;form onSubmit={handleSubmit}&gt;...&lt;/form&gt;;
}

// Tests inject a silent mock logger:
render(
  &lt;LoggerContext.Provider value={{ log: vi.fn(), error: vi.fn() }}&gt;
    &lt;PaymentForm /&gt;
  &lt;/LoggerContext.Provider&gt;
);
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q22 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q22">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q22"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q22">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;
      22. How do you implement dependency injection in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q22" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q22"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong>Dependency Injection (DI)</strong> in React is the practice of supplying a component's
        dependencies from outside rather than having the component create or import them directly.
        This decouples components from specific implementations and makes them trivially testable and
        configurable. React provides three natural DI mechanisms: <em>props</em>, <em>Context</em>,
        and <em>render props / component slots</em>.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Props-based DI</strong> is the simplest form — pass services or functions as props.
        It is explicit and easy to trace but becomes unwieldy when the dependency must cross many
        component layers (prop drilling). <strong>Context-based DI</strong> solves the cross-layer
        problem — the dependency is registered once at a provider and consumed anywhere in the subtree.
        This is how theme providers, auth providers, and analytics providers work.
        <strong>Component slot DI</strong> injects rendering behaviour — a component accepts a
        <code>renderItem</code> or <code>itemComponent</code> prop so consumers can swap the rendering
        implementation without touching the container.
      </p>
      <p>
        For complex applications, libraries like InversifyJS or tsyringe can provide a full IoC
        container integrated with React via Context, but this is rarely needed — React's primitives
        cover virtually all real use cases.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// 1. PROPS-BASED DI — simple, explicit
interface AnalyticsService {
  track(event: string, props?: Record&lt;string, unknown&gt;): void;
}

function SignupButton({ analytics }: { analytics: AnalyticsService }) {
  return (
    &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; analytics.track('signup_clicked')}&gt;
      Sign Up
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
}

// Production usage
&lt;SignupButton analytics={segmentAnalytics} /&gt;
// Test usage
&lt;SignupButton analytics={{ track: vi.fn() }} /&gt;

// 2. CONTEXT-BASED DI — cross-layer, no prop drilling
const ServicesContext = React.createContext&lt;{
  analytics: AnalyticsService;
  logger: ILogger;
  featureFlags: IFeatureFlags;
} | null&gt;(null);

export function ServicesProvider({ children, overrides = {} }) {
  const services = useMemo(() =&gt; ({
    analytics: overrides.analytics ?? segmentAnalytics,
    logger:    overrides.logger    ?? console,
    featureFlags: overrides.featureFlags ?? productionFlags,
  }), [overrides]);

  return (
    &lt;ServicesContext.Provider value={services}&gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/ServicesContext.Provider&gt;
  );
}

export function useServices() {
  const ctx = useContext(ServicesContext);
  if (!ctx) throw new Error('Must be inside ServicesProvider');
  return ctx;
}

// 3. COMPONENT SLOT DI — inject rendering behaviour
interface ListProps&lt;T&gt; {
  items: T[];
  renderItem: (item: T, index: number) =&gt; React.ReactNode;
  keyExtractor: (item: T) =&gt; string;
}

function VirtualList&lt;T&gt;({ items, renderItem, keyExtractor }: ListProps&lt;T&gt;) {
  return (
    &lt;ul&gt;
      {items.map((item, i) =&gt; (
        &lt;li key={keyExtractor(item)}&gt;{renderItem(item, i)}&lt;/li&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/ul&gt;
  );
}

// Consumer injects rendering:
&lt;VirtualList
  items={products}
  keyExtractor={p =&gt; p.id}
  renderItem={product =&gt; &lt;ProductCard product={product} /&gt;}
/&gt;
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q23 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q23">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q23"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q23">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;
      23. What is the factory pattern for React components?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q23" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q23"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>factory pattern</strong> provides a way to create objects (or in React's case,
        components) without specifying the exact class or function upfront. A factory function
        encapsulates the creation logic, returning different component implementations based on
        configuration or runtime conditions. This is particularly useful for dynamic form builders,
        plugin systems, CMS-driven UIs, or any situation where the component to render is determined
        by data.
      </p>
      <p>
        In React, factories manifest as: a <em>component factory function</em> that returns a new
        component based on configuration (similar to HOCs but focused on creation rather than
        enhancement); a <em>component registry/map</em> that maps a type string to a component; or a
        dynamic <code>createElement</code>-based approach for truly runtime-determined rendering.
      </p>
      <p>
        The component registry pattern is the most common and readable form in production React code.
        A <code>COMPONENT_MAP</code> maps a <code>type</code> field from API data to a React component;
        a single renderer component looks up and renders the correct component. This is how CMS
        block renderers, drag-and-drop page builders, and dynamic form libraries work internally.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// 1. COMPONENT REGISTRY — most common factory pattern
const BLOCK_REGISTRY: Record&lt;string, React.ComponentType&lt;any&gt;&gt; = {
  hero:       HeroBlock,
  text:       RichTextBlock,
  image:      ImageBlock,
  video:      VideoBlock,
  cta:        CtaBlock,
  testimonial: TestimonialBlock,
  form:       FormBlock,
};

// Factory component that selects the right component
function BlockRenderer({ block }: { block: ContentBlock }) {
  const Component = BLOCK_REGISTRY[block.type];

  if (!Component) {
    console.warn(`Unknown block type: ${block.type}`);
    return null;
  }

  return &lt;Component {...block.props} key={block.id} /&gt;;
}

// CMS page built from API data — no if/else chains
function Page({ blocks }: { blocks: ContentBlock[] }) {
  return (
    &lt;main&gt;
      {blocks.map(block =&gt; (
        &lt;BlockRenderer block={block} key={block.id} /&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/main&gt;
  );
}

// 2. COMPONENT FACTORY FUNCTION — creates configured components
function createFormField(type: 'text' | 'select' | 'checkbox' | 'date') {
  const fieldComponents = {
    text:     TextInput,
    select:   SelectInput,
    checkbox: CheckboxInput,
    date:     DatePicker,
  };

  const FieldComponent = fieldComponents[type];

  return function ConfiguredField({ name, label, validation, ...rest }) {
    const { register, formState: { errors } } = useFormContext();
    return (
      &lt;div className="form-field"&gt;
        &lt;label htmlFor={name}&gt;{label}&lt;/label&gt;
        &lt;FieldComponent
          id={name}
          {...register(name, validation)}
          aria-invalid={!!errors[name]}
          {...rest}
        /&gt;
        {errors[name] &amp;&amp; &lt;span className="error"&gt;{errors[name].message}&lt;/span&gt;}
      &lt;/div&gt;
    );
  };
}

const TextField    = createFormField('text');
const SelectField  = createFormField('select');
const DateField    = createFormField('date');
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q24 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q24">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q24"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q24">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;
      24. What is the strategy pattern applied to React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q24" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q24"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        The <strong>strategy pattern</strong> defines a family of algorithms (strategies), encapsulates
        each one, and makes them interchangeable. The client code selects which strategy to use at
        runtime without knowing the implementation details of any individual strategy. In React, this
        translates to passing behaviour as props — functions, components, or objects — rather than
        hardcoding conditional logic inside a component.
      </p>
      <p>
        Common applications in React: sorting strategies for a data table (pass a
        <code>sortFn</code> prop); validation strategies for a form (pass a <code>validator</code>
        object); rendering strategies for a list (pass a <code>renderItem</code> function); pricing
        strategies for a cart (pass a <code>pricingStrategy</code> object). Each strategy is a
        self-contained unit; the host component delegates to it without if/else chains.
      </p>
      <p>
        The strategy pattern dramatically improves testability — each strategy can be tested in
        complete isolation without mounting any React components. It also makes the host component
        stable: new strategies are added without modifying the host.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Validation strategies (objects with a validate method)
interface ValidationStrategy {
  validate(value: string): string | null; // null = valid
}

const requiredStrategy: ValidationStrategy = {
  validate: (value) =&gt; value.trim() ? null : 'This field is required',
};

const emailStrategy: ValidationStrategy = {
  validate: (value) =&gt; /^[^\s@]+@[^\s@]+\.[^\s@]+$/.test(value)
    ? null : 'Please enter a valid email',
};

const minLengthStrategy = (min: number): ValidationStrategy =&gt; ({
  validate: (value) =&gt; value.length &gt;= min
    ? null : `Must be at least ${min} characters`,
});

// Composite strategy — runs multiple strategies in sequence
function composeStrategies(...strategies: ValidationStrategy[]): ValidationStrategy {
  return {
    validate(value) {
      for (const strategy of strategies) {
        const error = strategy.validate(value);
        if (error) return error;
      }
      return null;
    },
  };
}

// Generic input that delegates validation to whatever strategy is passed
function ValidatedInput({
  name, label, value, onChange, validationStrategy
}: {
  name: string; label: string; value: string;
  onChange: (val: string) =&gt; void;
  validationStrategy?: ValidationStrategy;
}) {
  const [touched, setTouched] = useState(false);
  const error = touched && validationStrategy
    ? validationStrategy.validate(value)
    : null;

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;label htmlFor={name}&gt;{label}&lt;/label&gt;
      &lt;input
        id={name}
        value={value}
        onChange={e =&gt; onChange(e.target.value)}
        onBlur={() =&gt; setTouched(true)}
        aria-describedby={error ? `${name}-error` : undefined}
      /&gt;
      {error &amp;&amp; &lt;p id={`${name}-error`} className="error"&gt;{error}&lt;/p&gt;}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Sorting strategy for a data table
interface SortStrategy&lt;T&gt; { sort(items: T[]): T[]; }

const alphabeticalSort: SortStrategy&lt;Product&gt; = {
  sort: items =&gt; [...items].sort((a, b) =&gt; a.name.localeCompare(b.name)),
};
const priceAscSort: SortStrategy&lt;Product&gt; = {
  sort: items =&gt; [...items].sort((a, b) =&gt; a.price - b.price),
};
const relevanceSort = (query: string): SortStrategy&lt;Product&gt; =&gt; ({
  sort: items =&gt; [...items].sort((a, b) =&gt;
    scoreRelevance(b, query) - scoreRelevance(a, query)
  ),
});

function ProductTable({ products, sortStrategy }: {
  products: Product[];
  sortStrategy: SortStrategy&lt;Product&gt;;
}) {
  const sorted = useMemo(
    () =&gt; sortStrategy.sort(products),
    [products, sortStrategy]
  );
  return &lt;table&gt;...{sorted.map(renderRow)}&lt;/table&gt;;
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q25 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-architecture-q25">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button"
            data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q25"
            aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="architecture-q25">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;
      25. How do you architect a large-scale React application?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="architecture-q25" class="accordion-collapse collapse"
       aria-labelledby="heading-architecture-q25"
       data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        Architecting a large-scale React application requires disciplined decisions across multiple
        dimensions: <em>project structure</em>, <em>state management</em>, <em>data fetching</em>,
        <em>routing</em>, <em>code splitting</em>, <em>testing strategy</em>, and <em>team conventions</em>.
        The goal is a codebase that remains productive as both the feature count and the team size grow.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Project structure:</strong> Feature-based organisation (see Q10) with strict barrel
        exports. Each feature is a self-contained module with its own components, hooks, API layer,
        state slice, and tests. Cross-feature shared code lives in a dedicated <code>shared/</code>
        folder. This structure ensures that adding a feature is a local operation, not a cross-repo
        surgery.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>State management:</strong> Apply the "state colocation" principle rigorously. Local
        UI state in component state, subtree state in Context, server/async state in React Query or
        RTK Query, global client state (auth, settings) in Redux Toolkit or Zustand. Avoid overloading
        global state with server data — that is React Query's job.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Performance:</strong> Route-based code splitting with <code>React.lazy</code> and
        <code>Suspense</code> as a baseline. Memoize expensive computations with <code>useMemo</code>;
        stabilise callbacks with <code>useCallback</code>; use <code>React.memo</code> on components
        that render frequently with stable props. For large lists, use virtualisation (TanStack Virtual).
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Quality gates:</strong> TypeScript strict mode, ESLint with architecture-enforcement
        rules (import/no-cycle to prevent circular dependencies, boundaries between features),
        Vitest unit + React Testing Library integration tests, and Playwright/Cypress E2E tests for
        critical flows. CI blocks merges on failing tests, lint errors, or type errors.
      </p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Large-scale architecture blueprint

// ── Directory layout ──────────────────────────────────────────────
// src/
//   app/              ← app bootstrap, router, global providers, store
//     App.tsx
//     router.tsx
//     store.ts
//     providers.tsx   ← composes all providers
//   features/         ← one folder per business domain
//     auth/
//     products/
//     cart/
//     orders/
//     admin/
//   shared/           ← reusable across features
//     components/     ← design system primitives
//     hooks/          ← generic hooks (useDebounce, useMediaQuery)
//     utils/          ← pure functions
//     types/          ← shared TypeScript types
//   pages/            ← route entry points, thin wrappers
//   assets/

// ── State management layers ───────────────────────────────────────
// Layer 1: Local UI state
const [isOpen, setIsOpen] = useState(false);

// Layer 2: Server state (React Query)
const { data: products, isLoading } = useQuery({
  queryKey: ['products', filters],
  queryFn: () =&gt; productApi.getAll(filters),
  staleTime: 5 * 60 * 1000,
});

// Layer 3: Global client state (Redux Toolkit)
// Only for: auth session, user preferences, global notifications
const user = useSelector(state =&gt; state.auth.user);

// ── Code splitting ────────────────────────────────────────────────
const AdminPanel = React.lazy(() =&gt; import('@/features/admin'));
const OrdersPage = React.lazy(() =&gt; import('@/pages/OrdersPage'));

function AppRouter() {
  return (
    &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;PageSkeleton /&gt;}&gt;
      &lt;Routes&gt;
        &lt;Route path="/admin/*"  element={&lt;ProtectedRoute roles={['admin']}&gt;&lt;AdminPanel /&gt;&lt;/ProtectedRoute&gt;} /&gt;
        &lt;Route path="/orders"   element={&lt;OrdersPage /&gt;} /&gt;
      &lt;/Routes&gt;
    &lt;/Suspense&gt;
  );
}

// ── ESLint architectural boundaries ──────────────────────────────
// .eslintrc — enforce no cross-feature direct imports (use barrels)
// "import/no-restricted-paths": [
//   "error",
//   { "zones": [
//     { "target": "./src/features/products",
//       "from": "./src/features/cart",
//       "message": "products must not import from cart directly — use shared/" }
//   ]}
// ]

// ── Error boundary strategy ───────────────────────────────────────
// Root boundary: catches catastrophic failures
// Route boundary: isolates page-level failures
// Feature boundary: isolates widget-level failures
function AppProviders({ children }) {
  return (
    &lt;ErrorBoundary fallback={&lt;AppCrashPage /&gt;}&gt;
      &lt;AuthProvider&gt;
        &lt;QueryClientProvider client={queryClient}&gt;
          &lt;Provider store={store}&gt;
            &lt;ThemeProvider&gt;
              &lt;FeatureFlagsProvider&gt;
                {children}
              &lt;/FeatureFlagsProvider&gt;
            &lt;/ThemeProvider&gt;
          &lt;/Provider&gt;
        &lt;/QueryClientProvider&gt;
      &lt;/AuthProvider&gt;
    &lt;/ErrorBoundary&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>
        <!-- Q26 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q26" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">26</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              What is domain-driven design (DDD) applied to React?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q26" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Domain-Driven Design applied to React means organising your codebase around business domains rather than technical layers, so each domain owns its own components, state, API calls, hooks, and types in a single cohesive slice.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list blue">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Bounded contexts:</strong> Each domain (Orders, Inventory, Users) is a self-contained module with a clear public API surface exported from an <code>index.ts</code> barrel</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Ubiquitous language:</strong> Component, hook, and type names match the business vocabulary the domain experts use — <code>Order</code>, <code>Shipment</code>, <code>Carrier</code> not <code>Item</code>, <code>Record</code>, <code>Entity</code></li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Domain models:</strong> TypeScript interfaces live inside the domain and map 1-to-1 with backend aggregates; API adapters translate raw JSON into domain objects at the boundary</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Anti-corruption layer:</strong> An adapter/mapper function absorbs API shape changes so domain code never needs to change when the backend evolves</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Domain services:</strong> Business logic that does not belong to a single component lives in plain TypeScript functions inside the domain, independently testable</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// src/domains/orders/</span>
├── <span class="cm">components/</span>   <span class="cm">// OrderCard, OrderTable, OrderStatusBadge</span>
├── <span class="cm">hooks/</span>        <span class="cm">// useOrders, useOrderById, useCreateOrder</span>
├── <span class="cm">api/</span>          <span class="cm">// ordersApi.ts — raw fetch calls</span>
├── <span class="cm">adapters/</span>     <span class="cm">// mapApiOrderToOrder.ts — anti-corruption layer</span>
├── <span class="cm">store/</span>        <span class="cm">// ordersSlice.ts, ordersSelectors.ts</span>
├── <span class="cm">types/</span>        <span class="cm">// Order.ts, OrderStatus.ts, OrderLine.ts</span>
└── index.ts      <span class="cm">// public API — only export what other domains need</span>

<span class="cm">// adapters/mapApiOrderToOrder.ts — anti-corruption layer</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">mapApiOrderToOrder</span>(raw: ApiOrder): Order {
  <span class="kw">return</span> {
    id:        raw.order_id,
    status:    raw.order_status <span class="kw">as</span> OrderStatus,
    placedAt:  <span class="kw">new</span> <span class="fn">Date</span>(raw.created_ts),
    lines:     raw.line_items.<span class="fn">map</span>(mapApiLineToOrderLine),
  };
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Tests whether candidates can reason about large-scale codebase organisation beyond simple file-type folders — a key senior-level skill.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q27 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q27" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">27</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              How do you implement an event bus in a React application?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q27" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>An event bus is a publish/subscribe mechanism that decouples components by letting any part of the app emit named events and any other part subscribe to them, without requiring a shared parent or prop drilling.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list green">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>When to use:</strong> Cross-cutting concerns — analytics events, toast notifications, global modal triggers — where wiring through the component tree is impractical</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Simple implementation:</strong> A singleton <code>EventEmitter</code> class built on a <code>Map</code> of listener arrays — no third-party library needed</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>React integration:</strong> Subscribe in <code>useEffect</code>, always return a cleanup that unsubscribes to prevent memory leaks</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>TypeScript safety:</strong> Use a typed event map so every emit/subscribe call is checked at compile time</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Caution:</strong> Overusing an event bus creates implicit coupling that is hard to trace — prefer React state/context for component communication first</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// services/eventBus.ts</span>
<span class="kw">type</span> EventMap = {
  <span class="st">'cart:item-added'</span>:   { productId: <span class="kw">string</span>; qty: <span class="kw">number</span> };
  <span class="st">'auth:logged-out'</span>:   <span class="kw">void</span>;
  <span class="st">'toast:show'</span>:        { message: <span class="kw">string</span>; variant: <span class="kw">string</span> };
};

<span class="kw">type</span> Listener&lt;T&gt; = (payload: T) => <span class="kw">void</span>;

<span class="kw">class</span> <span class="fn">EventBus</span> {
  <span class="kw">private</span> listeners = <span class="kw">new</span> <span class="fn">Map</span>&lt;<span class="kw">string</span>, <span class="fn">Set</span>&lt;Listener&lt;<span class="kw">any</span>&gt;&gt;&gt;();

  <span class="fn">on</span>&lt;K <span class="kw">extends keyof</span> EventMap&gt;(event: K, cb: Listener&lt;EventMap[K]&gt;) {
    <span class="kw">if</span> (!<span class="kw">this</span>.listeners.<span class="fn">has</span>(event))
      <span class="kw">this</span>.listeners.<span class="fn">set</span>(event, <span class="kw">new</span> <span class="fn">Set</span>());
    <span class="kw">this</span>.listeners.<span class="fn">get</span>(event)!.<span class="fn">add</span>(cb);
    <span class="kw">return</span> () => <span class="kw">this</span>.listeners.<span class="fn">get</span>(event)!.<span class="fn">delete</span>(cb);
  }

  <span class="fn">emit</span>&lt;K <span class="kw">extends keyof</span> EventMap&gt;(event: K, payload: EventMap[K]) {
    <span class="kw">this</span>.listeners.<span class="fn">get</span>(event)?.<span class="fn">forEach</span>(cb => <span class="fn">cb</span>(payload));
  }
}

<span class="kw">export const</span> eventBus = <span class="kw">new</span> <span class="fn">EventBus</span>();

<span class="cm">// Usage in a component</span>
<span class="fn">useEffect</span>(() => {
  <span class="kw">const</span> off = eventBus.<span class="fn">on</span>(<span class="st">'toast:show'</span>, ({ message, variant }) => {
    <span class="fn">showToast</span>(message, variant);
  });
  <span class="kw">return</span> off; <span class="cm">// cleanup on unmount</span>
}, []);</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Demonstrates knowledge of decoupling patterns and whether the candidate knows when pub/sub is appropriate vs. when React's built-in mechanisms suffice.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q28 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q28" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">28</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              What is the facade pattern for external integrations in React?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q28" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>The facade pattern wraps a complex or volatile external dependency behind a thin, stable interface so the rest of the application interacts only with your interface, never directly with the third-party API.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list purple">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Problem it solves:</strong> Third-party SDKs change their APIs, get replaced, or need swapping per environment — a facade absorbs all of that churn in one file</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Analytics example:</strong> A <code>useAnalytics</code> hook that internally calls Segment, Amplitude, or a custom logger — callers use <code>track('button_clicked', data)</code> and never import the SDK directly</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Auth facade:</strong> Wrap Okta/Auth0/Cognito behind an <code>authService</code> with methods <code>login()</code>, <code>logout()</code>, <code>getUser()</code> — swapping providers means editing one file</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Testability:</strong> Mock the facade in tests rather than mocking the entire SDK, keeping tests fast and SDK-agnostic</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// services/analytics/index.ts  — the facade</span>
<span class="kw">import</span> * <span class="kw">as</span> amplitude <span class="kw">from</span> <span class="st">'@amplitude/analytics-browser'</span>;

<span class="kw">export const</span> analytics = {
  <span class="fn">track</span>(event: <span class="kw">string</span>, props?: Record&lt;<span class="kw">string</span>, <span class="kw">unknown</span>&gt;) {
    amplitude.<span class="fn">track</span>(event, props);
  },
  <span class="fn">identify</span>(userId: <span class="kw">string</span>, traits?: Record&lt;<span class="kw">string</span>, <span class="kw">unknown</span>&gt;) {
    amplitude.<span class="fn">setUserId</span>(userId);
    <span class="kw">if</span> (traits) amplitude.<span class="fn">identify</span>(<span class="kw">new</span> amplitude.<span class="fn">Identify</span>());
  },
  <span class="fn">page</span>(name: <span class="kw">string</span>) {
    amplitude.<span class="fn">track</span>(<span class="st">`page_view`</span>, { page: name });
  },
};

<span class="cm">// Anywhere in the app — never imports amplitude directly</span>
analytics.<span class="fn">track</span>(<span class="st">'checkout_started'</span>, { cartValue: <span class="st">99.99</span> });</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Reveals architectural maturity — senior devs isolate third-party risk; junior devs scatter SDK calls throughout components.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q29 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q29" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">29</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              How do you implement plugin architecture in React?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q29" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>A plugin architecture lets you extend an application's behaviour at defined extension points without modifying the core — plugins register themselves against a registry and the host app discovers and mounts them at runtime.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list amber">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Plugin registry:</strong> A central <code>PluginRegistry</code> singleton that plugins call <code>register()</code> on; the host app iterates the registry to render or execute each plugin</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Extension points:</strong> Named slots in the UI where plugins can inject components — <code>'sidebar'</code>, <code>'toolbar'</code>, <code>'dashboard-widget'</code></li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Plugin contract:</strong> A TypeScript interface that every plugin must implement — <code>id</code>, <code>name</code>, <code>component</code>, optional <code>route</code>, optional <code>permissions</code></li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Lazy loading:</strong> Each plugin is a dynamic import so it only loads when activated, keeping the core bundle small</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Real-world use:</strong> VS Code, Figma, Grafana dashboards — all use this pattern; in React it powers extensible admin panels and SaaS platforms</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// plugin.types.ts</span>
<span class="kw">export interface</span> Plugin {
  id:         <span class="kw">string</span>;
  slot:       <span class="st">'sidebar'</span> | <span class="st">'dashboard'</span> | <span class="st">'toolbar'</span>;
  component:  <span class="fn">React.ComponentType</span>;
  requiredRole?: <span class="kw">string</span>;
}

<span class="cm">// pluginRegistry.ts</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> registry: Plugin[] = [];
<span class="kw">export const</span> registerPlugin = (p: Plugin) => registry.<span class="fn">push</span>(p);
<span class="kw">export const</span> getPlugins = (slot: Plugin[<span class="st">'slot'</span>']) =>
  registry.<span class="fn">filter</span>(p => p.slot === slot);

<span class="cm">// PluginSlot.tsx — renders all plugins for a slot</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">PluginSlot</span>({ slot }: { slot: Plugin[<span class="st">'slot'</span>'] }) {
  <span class="kw">const</span> plugins = <span class="fn">getPlugins</span>(slot);
  <span class="kw">return</span> &lt;&gt;{plugins.<span class="fn">map</span>(({ id, component: C }) =>
    &lt;C key={id} /&gt;
  )}&lt;/&gt;;
}

<span class="cm">// analytics-plugin/index.ts — self-registers</span>
<span class="fn">registerPlugin</span>({
  id: <span class="st">'analytics-dashboard'</span>,
  slot: <span class="st">'dashboard'</span>,
  component: AnalyticsDashboardWidget,
  requiredRole: <span class="st">'admin'</span>,
});</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> A hallmark of platform-thinking — important for candidates building extensible products where third-party or internal teams add features without touching core code.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q30 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q30" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">30</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              What is the command pattern applied to React user actions?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q30" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>The command pattern encapsulates a user action — and everything needed to execute, undo, or replay it — as a plain object (the command), decoupling the action's invocation from its implementation.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list blue">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Command object:</strong> Contains <code>execute()</code> and optionally <code>undo()</code> methods plus all the data needed to perform the action</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Command history:</strong> A stack of executed commands makes undo/redo trivial — pop the last command and call <code>undo()</code></li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Redux is the command pattern:</strong> Every dispatched action is a command; Redux Dev Tools replay is command replay in action</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Offline queue:</strong> Commands can be serialised to <code>localStorage</code> and replayed when connectivity is restored</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Audit logs:</strong> Each command object is a self-describing event that can be sent to a backend audit trail verbatim</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// types/command.ts</span>
<span class="kw">interface</span> Command&lt;T = <span class="kw">void</span>&gt; {
  <span class="fn">execute</span>(): T;
  <span class="fn">undo</span>():    <span class="kw">void</span>;
  label:     <span class="kw">string</span>;
}

<span class="cm">// commands/renameItemCommand.ts</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">renameItemCommand</span>(
  id: <span class="kw">string</span>, prev: <span class="kw">string</span>, next: <span class="kw">string</span>,
  dispatch: AppDispatch
): Command {
  <span class="kw">return</span> {
    label: <span class="st">`Rename "${prev}" to "${next}"`</span>,
    <span class="fn">execute</span>() { dispatch(<span class="fn">renameItem</span>({ id, name: next })); },
    <span class="fn">undo</span>()    { dispatch(<span class="fn">renameItem</span>({ id, name: prev })); },
  };
}

<span class="cm">// useCommandHistory.ts</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">useCommandHistory</span>() {
  <span class="kw">const</span> [history, setHistory] = <span class="fn">useState</span>&lt;Command[]&gt;([]);

  <span class="kw">const</span> run = <span class="fn">useCallback</span>((cmd: Command) => {
    cmd.<span class="fn">execute</span>();
    setHistory(h => [...h, cmd]);
  }, []);

  <span class="kw">const</span> undo = <span class="fn">useCallback</span>(() => {
    setHistory(h => {
      <span class="kw">const</span> last = h.<span class="fn">at</span>(-<span class="st">1</span>);
      last?.<span class="fn">undo</span>();
      <span class="kw">return</span> h.<span class="fn">slice</span>(<span class="st">0</span>, -<span class="st">1</span>);
    });
  }, []);

  <span class="kw">return</span> { run, undo, history };
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Tests whether candidates understand design patterns beyond CRUD — especially relevant for collaborative or document-editing applications.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q31 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q31" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">31</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              How do you implement internationalization (i18n) in React?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q31" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Internationalization in React is most commonly implemented with <strong>react-i18next</strong> (backed by i18next), which provides a <code>useTranslation</code> hook, namespace-based message files, and runtime language switching without a page reload.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list green">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Setup:</strong> Initialise i18next with language resources (JSON files per locale) and wrap the app in <code>I18nextProvider</code></li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Namespaces:</strong> Split translations into domain namespaces (<code>common</code>, <code>orders</code>, <code>errors</code>) so only needed strings are loaded per route</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Interpolation:</strong> Pass dynamic values as <code>t('greeting', { name: 'Karan' })</code> mapped to <code>"Hello {{name}}"</code> in the JSON</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Pluralisation:</strong> i18next handles plural forms automatically with <code>_one</code> / <code>_other</code> key suffixes</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Lazy loading:</strong> Use <code>i18next-http-backend</code> to load locale JSON on demand instead of bundling all languages upfront</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Date/Number formatting:</strong> Use the native <code>Intl</code> API or <code>react-intl</code> for locale-aware dates and currency — i18next handles text only</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// i18n/en/orders.json</span>
{
  <span class="st">"title"</span>:       <span class="st">"Your Orders"</span>,
  <span class="st">"count_one"</span>:   <span class="st">"{{count}} order"</span>,
  <span class="st">"count_other"</span>: <span class="st">"{{count}} orders"</span>,
  <span class="st">"status"</span>: {
    <span class="st">"pending"</span>:   <span class="st">"Pending"</span>,
    <span class="st">"shipped"</span>:   <span class="st">"Shipped"</span>
  }
}

<span class="cm">// OrdersPage.tsx</span>
<span class="kw">import</span> { <span class="fn">useTranslation</span> } <span class="kw">from</span> <span class="st">'react-i18next'</span>;

<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">OrdersPage</span>() {
  <span class="kw">const</span> { t } = <span class="fn">useTranslation</span>(<span class="st">'orders'</span>);
  <span class="kw">const</span> count = <span class="st">3</span>;

  <span class="kw">return</span> (
    &lt;h1&gt;{<span class="fn">t</span>(<span class="st">'title'</span>)}&lt;/h1&gt;      <span class="cm">// "Your Orders"</span>
    &lt;p&gt;{<span class="fn">t</span>(<span class="st">'count'</span>, { count })}&lt;/p&gt; <span class="cm">// "3 orders"</span>
  );
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Any production app serving global users needs i18n — interviewers check for practical library knowledge and awareness of pitfalls like hardcoded strings scattered through JSX.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q32 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q32" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">32</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              What is the difference between static and dynamic feature flags?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q32" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Static feature flags are baked into the build (environment variables or config JSON) and require a redeploy to change, while dynamic feature flags are resolved at runtime from a remote service and can be toggled instantly without touching the codebase.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list purple">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Static flags:</strong> <code>import.meta.env.VITE_ENABLE_BETA</code> or a committed <code>app-settings.json</code> — simple, zero latency, but needs a deploy to flip</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Dynamic flags:</strong> Fetched from LaunchDarkly, Unleash, or a custom endpoint at app boot; can be updated without a deploy and targeted per user/segment</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Use static for:</strong> Build-time optimisation, permanent feature splits between environments (dev/staging/prod)</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Use dynamic for:</strong> Kill switches, gradual rollouts, A/B tests, per-tenant feature grants</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Best practice:</strong> Load flags early (before rendering) and provide a React context so any component can read them without prop drilling</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// Static — build-time, in app-settings.json</span>
{ <span class="st">"featureFlags"</span>: { <span class="st">"newCheckout"</span>: <span class="kw">true</span> } }

<span class="cm">// Dynamic — runtime, fetched once at boot</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> { flags } = <span class="fn">useLDClient</span>();
<span class="kw">const</span> showNewCheckout = flags[<span class="st">'new-checkout'</span>];

<span class="cm">// Context pattern for both cases</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> FlagsContext = <span class="fn">createContext</span>&lt;Flags&gt;({});

<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">FlagsProvider</span>({ children }: PropsWithChildren) {
  <span class="kw">const</span> [flags, setFlags] = <span class="fn">useState</span>&lt;Flags&gt;({});
  <span class="fn">useEffect</span>(() => {
    <span class="fn">fetchFlags</span>().<span class="fn">then</span>(setFlags);
  }, []);
  <span class="kw">return</span> &lt;FlagsContext.Provider value={flags}&gt;{children}&lt;/FlagsContext.Provider&gt;;
}

<span class="kw">export const</span> useFlags = () => <span class="fn">useContext</span>(FlagsContext);</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Feature flags are fundamental to modern CI/CD and trunk-based development — understanding the trade-offs signals deployment maturity.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q33 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q33" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">33</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              How do you implement A/B testing in React?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q33" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>A/B testing in React involves assigning users to experiment variants (usually via a feature flag service), rendering the appropriate variant, and tracking conversion events — all in a way that is deterministic per user across sessions.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list amber">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Variant assignment:</strong> Use LaunchDarkly, GrowthBook, or a custom hash of the user ID modulo 100 to determine which bucket (A or B) a user falls into</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Persistence:</strong> Store the assigned variant in <code>localStorage</code> or a cookie so the same user always sees the same variant</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Exposure tracking:</strong> Fire an analytics event the moment the variant is first rendered — not when the page loads — to avoid counting users who never saw the experiment</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Isolation:</strong> Wrap A/B logic in a dedicated hook or component so experiment code is easy to remove once a winner is declared</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// hooks/useExperiment.ts</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">useExperiment</span>(experimentId: <span class="kw">string</span>) {
  <span class="kw">const</span> userId = <span class="fn">useCurrentUserId</span>();

  <span class="kw">const</span> variant = <span class="fn">useMemo</span>(() => {
    <span class="kw">const</span> stored = localStorage.<span class="fn">getItem</span>(<span class="st">`exp_${experimentId}`</span>);
    <span class="kw">if</span> (stored) <span class="kw">return</span> stored;
    <span class="kw">const</span> v = <span class="fn">hashToVariant</span>(userId + experimentId); <span class="cm">// 'A' or 'B'</span>
    localStorage.<span class="fn">setItem</span>(<span class="st">`exp_${experimentId}`</span>, v);
    <span class="kw">return</span> v;
  }, [experimentId, userId]);

  <span class="fn">useEffect</span>(() => {
    analytics.<span class="fn">track</span>(<span class="st">'experiment_exposed'</span>, { experimentId, variant });
  }, [experimentId, variant]);

  <span class="kw">return</span> variant;
}

<span class="cm">// CheckoutButton.tsx</span>
<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">CheckoutButton</span>() {
  <span class="kw">const</span> variant = <span class="fn">useExperiment</span>(<span class="st">'checkout-cta-2024'</span>);
  <span class="kw">return</span> variant === <span class="st">'B'</span>
    ? &lt;<span class="fn">Button</span> color=<span class="st">"green"</span>&gt;Buy Now — Save 10%&lt;/<span class="fn">Button</span>&gt;
    : &lt;<span class="fn">Button</span>&gt;Proceed to Checkout&lt;/<span class="fn">Button</span>&gt;;
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Growth-focused teams run constant experiments — candidates should understand how to implement them cleanly without polluting every component with experiment logic.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q34 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q34" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">34</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              What is analytics instrumentation architecture in React?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q34" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Analytics instrumentation architecture defines how user events are captured, enriched, and delivered to analytics platforms in a consistent, maintainable way — without scattering raw SDK calls throughout every component.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list blue">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Centralised facade:</strong> A single <code>analytics</code> module wraps the SDK so the rest of the codebase never imports Segment/Amplitude/GTM directly</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Typed event catalogue:</strong> Define a TypeScript union or object type listing every possible event name and its required properties — prevents typos and missing required fields</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Automatic context enrichment:</strong> Middleware in the facade automatically attaches <code>userId</code>, <code>sessionId</code>, <code>appVersion</code>, and <code>timestamp</code> to every event</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Page view automation:</strong> A router listener (React Router <code>useLocation</code> effect) fires page-view events automatically on route change</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Click tracking:</strong> A <code>data-track</code> attribute pattern plus a global click listener can capture low-priority interactions without adding code to individual components</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// analytics/events.ts — typed catalogue</span>
<span class="kw">type</span> AnalyticsEvents = {
  <span class="st">'product_viewed'</span>:      { productId: <span class="kw">string</span>; category: <span class="kw">string</span> };
  <span class="st">'add_to_cart'</span>:         { productId: <span class="kw">string</span>; qty: <span class="kw">number</span>; price: <span class="kw">number</span> };
  <span class="st">'checkout_completed'</span>:  { orderId: <span class="kw">string</span>; total: <span class="kw">number</span> };
};

<span class="cm">// analytics/index.ts — typed track call</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> track&lt;K <span class="kw">extends keyof</span> AnalyticsEvents&gt;(
  event: K,
  props: AnalyticsEvents[K]
) {
  segment.<span class="fn">track</span>(event, { ...props, ...<span class="fn">getBaseContext</span>() });
}

<span class="cm">// Auto page-view in router</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> location = <span class="fn">useLocation</span>();
<span class="fn">useEffect</span>(() => {
  analytics.<span class="fn">page</span>(location.pathname);
}, [location.pathname]);</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Analytics debt is painful at scale — a well-thought-out instrumentation architecture signals the candidate has built and maintained real production applications.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q35 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q35" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">35</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              How do you implement error reporting (e.g., Sentry) in React?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q35" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Error reporting integrates Sentry (or a similar tool) to automatically capture uncaught exceptions and React render errors, enrich them with user and release context, and surface them in a dashboard for triage.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list green">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>SDK init:</strong> Call <code>Sentry.init()</code> at app entry with your DSN, release version, and environment — Sentry auto-instruments unhandled promise rejections and XHR errors from this point</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Error boundaries:</strong> Wrap route-level or section-level components in Sentry's <code>ErrorBoundary</code> (or your own) to catch React render errors and report them with component stack</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>User context:</strong> After login call <code>Sentry.setUser({ id, email })</code> so every subsequent error is linked to the affected user</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Source maps:</strong> Upload source maps during CI so stack traces point to original TypeScript lines, not minified output</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Manual capture:</strong> Use <code>Sentry.captureException(err)</code> inside catch blocks for expected error paths (API failures, validation errors) that would otherwise be silently swallowed</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// main.tsx — init before React renders</span>
<span class="fn">Sentry.init</span>({
  dsn:         <span class="kw">import</span>.meta.env.VITE_SENTRY_DSN,
  environment: <span class="kw">import</span>.meta.env.VITE_APP_ENV,
  release:     <span class="st">`my-app@${__APP_VERSION__}`</span>,
  integrations: [<span class="kw">new</span> Sentry.<span class="fn">BrowserTracing</span>(), <span class="kw">new</span> Sentry.<span class="fn">Replay</span>()],
  tracesSampleRate: <span class="st">0.2</span>,
});

<span class="cm">// App.tsx — boundary around the whole app</span>
<span class="kw">import</span> { <span class="fn">ErrorBoundary</span> } <span class="kw">from</span> <span class="st">'@sentry/react'</span>;

&lt;<span class="fn">ErrorBoundary</span> fallback={&lt;<span class="fn">CrashPage</span> /&gt;}&gt;
  &lt;<span class="fn">App</span> /&gt;
&lt;/<span class="fn">ErrorBoundary</span>&gt;

<span class="cm">// In an async catch block</span>
<span class="kw">try</span> {
  <span class="kw">await</span> <span class="fn">submitOrder</span>(cart);
} <span class="kw">catch</span> (err) {
  Sentry.<span class="fn">captureException</span>(err, { extra: { cartId: cart.id } });
  <span class="fn">showErrorToast</span>(<span class="st">'Order failed — please try again'</span>);
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Error observability is a production readiness baseline — candidates who have never set it up properly tend to learn about bugs from user complaints instead of dashboards.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q36 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q36" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">36</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              How do you implement a white-label theming system in React?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q36" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>A white-label theming system lets multiple clients (tenants) each have a distinct visual identity — colours, typography, logos, border radius — while sharing the same codebase, by loading a per-tenant theme token set at runtime.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list purple">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Design tokens:</strong> Define the full visual language as a flat token object — <code>primaryColor</code>, <code>fontFamily</code>, <code>borderRadius</code> — in a TypeScript interface so all themes are type-checked</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>CSS custom properties:</strong> Inject token values as CSS variables on <code>:root</code> at boot; components only ever reference <code>var(--color-primary)</code> so swapping the whole theme is a single DOM operation</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Tenant resolution:</strong> Determine the tenant from the subdomain, URL parameter, or auth token, then fetch that tenant's theme JSON from a CDN or API</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>MUI integration:</strong> Dynamically create a MUI theme with <code>createTheme(tenantTokens)</code> and wrap the app in <code>&lt;ThemeProvider theme={theme}&gt;</code></li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Fallback:</strong> Always ship a default theme as a bundled constant so the app renders correctly if the tenant API is slow or unavailable</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// theme/types.ts</span>
<span class="kw">export interface</span> ThemeTokens {
  colorPrimary:   <span class="kw">string</span>;
  colorSecondary: <span class="kw">string</span>;
  fontFamily:     <span class="kw">string</span>;
  borderRadius:   <span class="kw">string</span>;
  logoUrl:        <span class="kw">string</span>;
}

<span class="cm">// theme/applyTheme.ts</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">applyTheme</span>(tokens: ThemeTokens) {
  <span class="kw">const</span> root = document.documentElement;
  root.style.<span class="fn">setProperty</span>(<span class="st">'--color-primary'</span>,   tokens.colorPrimary);
  root.style.<span class="fn">setProperty</span>(<span class="st">'--color-secondary'</span>, tokens.colorSecondary);
  root.style.<span class="fn">setProperty</span>(<span class="st">'--font-family'</span>,     tokens.fontFamily);
  root.style.<span class="fn">setProperty</span>(<span class="st">'--border-radius'</span>,   tokens.borderRadius);
}

<span class="cm">// ThemeLoader.tsx — resolves tenant and applies theme</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">ThemeLoader</span>({ children }: PropsWithChildren) {
  <span class="kw">const</span> [ready, setReady] = <span class="fn">useState</span>(<span class="kw">false</span>);

  <span class="fn">useEffect</span>(() => {
    <span class="kw">const</span> tenantId = <span class="fn">resolveTenantFromSubdomain</span>();
    <span class="fn">fetchTenantTheme</span>(tenantId)
      .<span class="fn">then</span>(applyTheme)
      .<span class="fn">finally</span>(() => <span class="fn">setReady</span>(<span class="kw">true</span>));
  }, []);

  <span class="kw">if</span> (!ready) <span class="kw">return</span> &lt;<span class="fn">SplashScreen</span> /&gt;;
  <span class="kw">return</span> &lt;&gt;{children}&lt;/&gt;;
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> White-labelling is a common SaaS requirement — it tests knowledge of CSS variables, runtime configuration, and multi-tenancy patterns simultaneously.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q37 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q37" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">37</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              What is the hub-and-spoke architecture for React applications?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q37" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Hub-and-spoke architecture positions a central shell application (the hub) as the entry point that hosts multiple domain-specific applications (the spokes), orchestrating shared concerns like authentication, navigation, and design tokens centrally while each spoke owns its own domain fully.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list blue">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Hub responsibilities:</strong> Global auth, top-level routing, shared design system, notification centre, user preferences — everything that must be consistent across domains</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Spoke responsibilities:</strong> A fully owned vertical — its own state, API layer, components, and sub-routing; ideally deployable independently via Module Federation</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Communication contract:</strong> Spokes receive user/auth context from the hub via a shared context or standard props; they communicate back through defined events or a shared event bus</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>vs. Monolith:</strong> A monolith has all domains in one repo with shared state. Hub-and-spoke keeps domain code isolated but shares the shell</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>vs. Pure micro-frontends:</strong> Micro-frontends have no central orchestrator — hub-and-spoke deliberately centralises shell concerns for consistency</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// Hub (shell) — App.tsx</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> OrdersApp    = <span class="fn">lazy</span>(() => <span class="kw">import</span>(<span class="st">'ordersApp/App'</span>));   <span class="cm">// spoke 1</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> InventoryApp = <span class="fn">lazy</span>(() => <span class="kw">import</span>(<span class="st">'inventoryApp/App'</span>)); <span class="cm">// spoke 2</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> ReportsApp   = <span class="fn">lazy</span>(() => <span class="kw">import</span>(<span class="st">'reportsApp/App'</span>));   <span class="cm">// spoke 3</span>

<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">Shell</span>() {
  <span class="kw">return</span> (
    &lt;<span class="fn">AuthProvider</span>&gt;           <span class="cm">// hub owns auth</span>
      &lt;<span class="fn">DesignSystemProvider</span>&gt; <span class="cm">// hub owns design tokens</span>
        &lt;<span class="fn">GlobalNav</span> /&gt;
        &lt;<span class="fn">Routes</span>&gt;
          &lt;<span class="fn">Route</span> path=<span class="st">"/orders/*"</span>    element={&lt;<span class="fn">OrdersApp</span> /&gt;}    /&gt;
          &lt;<span class="fn">Route</span> path=<span class="st">"/inventory/*"</span> element={&lt;<span class="fn">InventoryApp</span> /&gt;} /&gt;
          &lt;<span class="fn">Route</span> path=<span class="st">"/reports/*"</span>   element={&lt;<span class="fn">ReportsApp</span> /&gt;}   /&gt;
        &lt;/<span class="fn">Routes</span>&gt;
      &lt;/<span class="fn">DesignSystemProvider</span>&gt;
    &lt;/<span class="fn">AuthProvider</span>&gt;
  );
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Evaluates whether candidates understand enterprise-scale decomposition and can articulate trade-offs between consistency (hub concerns) and autonomy (spoke independence).</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q38 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q38" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">38</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              How do you handle backward compatibility in shared component libraries?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q38" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Backward compatibility in shared component libraries means consumers can upgrade to a new version without changing their code — enforced through semantic versioning, deprecation cycles, prop aliasing, and automated codemods for breaking changes.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list green">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Never remove, deprecate first:</strong> Mark old props with a <code>@deprecated</code> JSDoc comment and a console warning in dev mode; keep them working for at least one major version</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Prop aliasing:</strong> When renaming a prop, accept both the old and new name internally — <code>const resolvedLabel = label ?? title</code> — and warn when the old name is used</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Strict semver:</strong> Any prop removal, type narrowing, or behaviour change is a major version bump; additive changes (new optional props) are minor; bug fixes are patch</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Codemods:</strong> Ship a jscodeshift or Grit codemod with every breaking change so teams can run <code>npx your-lib-codemod v3</code> to auto-migrate their usage</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Consumer tests:</strong> Maintain a test suite in the library repo that imports and renders common consumer usage patterns — acts as a regression suite for breaking changes</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// Button.tsx — prop aliasing with deprecation warning</span>
<span class="kw">interface</span> ButtonProps {
  label?:    <span class="kw">string</span>;  <span class="cm">// new name</span>
  <span class="cm">/** @deprecated Use `label` instead */</span>
  text?:     <span class="kw">string</span>;  <span class="cm">// old name kept for compat</span>
  children?: React.ReactNode;
}

<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">Button</span>({ label, text, children, ...rest }: ButtonProps) {
  <span class="kw">if</span> (text !== <span class="kw">undefined</span> &amp;&amp; process.env.NODE_ENV !== <span class="st">'production'</span>) {
    console.<span class="fn">warn</span>(<span class="st">'[Button] `text` prop is deprecated — use `label` instead'</span>);
  }
  <span class="kw">const</span> resolvedLabel = label ?? text;
  <span class="kw">return</span> &lt;button {...rest}&gt;{resolvedLabel ?? children}&lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Library maintainers must balance innovation with stability — poor backward compatibility breaks dozens of consuming teams and erodes trust in the library.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q39 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q39" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">39</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              What is semantic versioning for component libraries?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q39" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Semantic versioning (semver) for component libraries follows the <strong>MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH</strong> format where each number communicates the safety of upgrading — patch is always safe, minor is additive and safe, major requires migration work.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list amber">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>PATCH (1.0.x):</strong> Bug fixes, visual micro-corrections, accessibility fixes — consumers should always upgrade, no API changes whatsoever</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>MINOR (1.x.0):</strong> New optional props, new components, new variants — fully backward compatible; consumers can adopt new features but don't have to</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>MAJOR (x.0.0):</strong> Removed props, renamed components, changed default behaviour, peer dependency bumps (React 17→18) — consumers must review the migration guide before upgrading</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Pre-release tags:</strong> Use <code>1.0.0-beta.1</code> or <code>2.0.0-rc.0</code> for early access versions — consumers opt in explicitly, not captured by <code>^</code> or <code>~</code> ranges</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Changelogs:</strong> Every version must have a <code>CHANGELOG.md</code> entry written for consumers — not commit messages, but user-facing descriptions of what changed and why</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// package.json versions communicated to consumers</span>
<span class="st">"@company/ui": "^2.5.0"</span>  <span class="cm">// safe: gets 2.5.x and 2.6.0 but NOT 3.0.0</span>
<span class="st">"@company/ui": "~2.5.0"</span>  <span class="cm">// stricter: gets only 2.5.x patches</span>
<span class="st">"@company/ui": "2.5.0"</span>   <span class="cm">// pinned: never auto-updates</span>

<span class="cm">// CHANGELOG.md entry for a major bump</span>
<span class="cm">## [3.0.0] — 2024-11-01</span>
<span class="cm">### Breaking Changes</span>
<span class="cm">- `Button` prop `text` removed — use `label`</span>
<span class="cm">- `Modal` now requires `id` prop for a11y</span>
<span class="cm">### Migration</span>
<span class="cm">Run: npx @company/ui-codemod v3 ./src</span></pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Misunderstanding semver leads to accidental breaking changes and angry teams — this question tests whether the candidate has maintained a library used by others.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q40 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q40" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">40</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              How do you document a React component library with Storybook?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q40" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Storybook is the industry-standard tool for documenting React component libraries — each component gets a <code>.stories.tsx</code> file that defines interactive examples (stories), auto-generated prop tables from TypeScript types, and written documentation in MDX.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list blue">
