                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>CSF3 format:</strong> Component Story Format 3 uses a default export for component meta and named exports for each story — concise and discoverable by Storybook automatically</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Controls addon:</strong> Storybook reads TypeScript prop types and generates interactive controls in the UI so designers can tweak props without writing code</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Actions addon:</strong> Log callback prop invocations (onClick, onChange) in the Actions panel to show how events fire</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>MDX docs:</strong> Write <code>component.mdx</code> files for narrative documentation, usage guidelines, and design rationale alongside the live examples</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Accessibility checks:</strong> The <code>@storybook/addon-a11y</code> addon runs axe-core on every story and highlights violations directly in the Storybook UI</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Visual regression:</strong> Connect Chromatic (Storybook's cloud) to capture screenshots of every story on every PR, catching unintended visual changes</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// Button.stories.tsx — CSF3 format</span>
<span class="kw">import type</span> { Meta, StoryObj } <span class="kw">from</span> <span class="st">'@storybook/react'</span>;
<span class="kw">import</span> { <span class="fn">Button</span> } <span class="kw">from</span> <span class="st">'./Button'</span>;

<span class="kw">const</span> meta: Meta&lt;<span class="kw">typeof</span> Button&gt; = {
  title:     <span class="st">'Components/Button'</span>,
  component: Button,
  tags:      [<span class="st">'autodocs'</span>],  <span class="cm">// auto-generate Docs page</span>
  argTypes: {
    variant: { control: <span class="st">'select'</span>, options: [<span class="st">'primary'</span>, <span class="st">'secondary'</span>, <span class="st">'ghost'</span>] },
  },
};
<span class="kw">export default</span> meta;

<span class="kw">type</span> Story = StoryObj&lt;<span class="kw">typeof</span> Button&gt;;

<span class="kw">export const</span> Primary: Story = {
  args: { label: <span class="st">'Click me'</span>, variant: <span class="st">'primary'</span> },
};

<span class="kw">export const</span> Disabled: Story = {
  args: { label: <span class="st">'Disabled'</span>, disabled: <span class="kw">true</span> },
};

<span class="kw">export const</span> Loading: Story = {
  args: { label: <span class="st">'Saving...'</span>, loading: <span class="kw">true</span> },
};</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> A component library without documentation is unusable — Storybook proficiency is expected of any senior frontend engineer working on a shared design system.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q41 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q41" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">41</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              What is the skeleton pattern for loading states?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q41" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>The skeleton pattern replaces content areas with animated placeholder shapes that match the layout of the expected content, giving users a visual cue about the structure that is loading and reducing perceived wait time compared to a spinner.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list green">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Why skeletons beat spinners:</strong> Spinners communicate "waiting" but give no information about what will appear; skeletons set spatial expectations and feel faster psychologically</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Implementation:</strong> Replace text with a grey shimmer rectangle of the same approximate width; replace images with a grey rectangle of the same aspect ratio</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Shimmer animation:</strong> A CSS <code>@keyframes</code> animation on <code>background-position</code> of a gradient creates the moving shimmer effect with zero JS</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Libraries:</strong> <code>react-loading-skeleton</code> provides a <code>&lt;Skeleton&gt;</code> component with theming support; MUI provides <code>&lt;Skeleton&gt;</code> natively</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Accessibility:</strong> Wrap the skeleton in <code>aria-busy="true"</code> and <code>aria-label="Loading..."</code> so screen readers announce the loading state correctly</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// ProductCard — skeleton vs loaded state</span>
<span class="kw">import</span> Skeleton <span class="kw">from</span> <span class="st">'react-loading-skeleton'</span>;

<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">ProductCard</span>({ product, isLoading }: Props) {
  <span class="kw">if</span> (isLoading) {
    <span class="kw">return</span> (
      &lt;div className=<span class="st">"card"</span> aria-busy=<span class="st">"true"</span>&gt;
        &lt;<span class="fn">Skeleton</span> height={<span class="st">200</span>} /&gt;              <span class="cm">// image placeholder</span>
        &lt;<span class="fn">Skeleton</span> width=<span class="st">"60%"</span> height={<span class="st">20</span>} /&gt;  <span class="cm">// title placeholder</span>
        &lt;<span class="fn">Skeleton</span> count={<span class="st">2</span>} /&gt;                  <span class="cm">// description lines</span>
        &lt;<span class="fn">Skeleton</span> width={<span class="st">80</span>} height={<span class="st">36</span>} /&gt;   <span class="cm">// button placeholder</span>
      &lt;/div&gt;
    );
  }
  <span class="kw">return</span> (
    &lt;div className=<span class="st">"card"</span>&gt;
      &lt;img src={product.imageUrl} alt={product.name} /&gt;
      &lt;h3&gt;{product.name}&lt;/h3&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;{product.description}&lt;/p&gt;
      &lt;<span class="fn">Button</span>&gt;Add to Cart&lt;/<span class="fn">Button</span>&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Loading UX is a direct measure of UX quality — interviewers want to see awareness of perceived performance beyond raw metric optimisation.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q42 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q42" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">42</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              What is optimistic UI and when do you use it?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q42" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Optimistic UI updates the interface immediately as if an action succeeded, before the server responds, then rolls back if the request fails — trading a small risk of visible correction for a dramatically snappier user experience.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list amber">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Best for:</strong> High-probability-of-success actions on low-stakes data — liking a post, ticking a todo, reordering a list, toggling a setting</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Avoid for:</strong> Payments, destructive deletes, or anything where an incorrect optimistic update could confuse or harm the user</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Rollback:</strong> Store the previous state before the optimistic update; if the API call rejects, restore it and show an error notification</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>React Query:</strong> The <code>onMutate</code> / <code>onError</code> / <code>onSettled</code> callbacks in <code>useMutation</code> make the optimistic pattern trivial — React Query handles cache snapshots automatically</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// Optimistic like toggle with React Query</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> likeMutation = <span class="fn">useMutation</span>({
  mutationFn: (postId: <span class="kw">string</span>) => api.<span class="fn">toggleLike</span>(postId),

  <span class="kw">async</span> <span class="fn">onMutate</span>(postId) {
    <span class="kw">await</span> queryClient.<span class="fn">cancelQueries</span>({ queryKey: [<span class="st">'post'</span>, postId] });
    <span class="cm">// snapshot current state</span>
    <span class="kw">const</span> previous = queryClient.<span class="fn">getQueryData</span>([<span class="st">'post'</span>, postId]);
    <span class="cm">// apply optimistic update immediately</span>
    queryClient.<span class="fn">setQueryData</span>([<span class="st">'post'</span>, postId], (old: Post) => ({
      ...old, liked: !old.liked, likeCount: old.liked ? old.likeCount - <span class="st">1</span> : old.likeCount + <span class="st">1</span>,
    }));
    <span class="kw">return</span> { previous };
  },

  <span class="fn">onError</span>(_, postId, context) {
    <span class="cm">// roll back on failure</span>
    queryClient.<span class="fn">setQueryData</span>([<span class="st">'post'</span>, postId], context?.previous);
  },

  <span class="fn">onSettled</span>(_, __, postId) {
    queryClient.<span class="fn">invalidateQueries</span>({ queryKey: [<span class="st">'post'</span>, postId] });
  },
});</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Optimistic UI is a hallmark of polished UX — candidates who know it demonstrate that they think about user experience at the interaction level, not just the data level.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q43 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q43" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">43</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              What is the error state pattern for components?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q43" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>The error state pattern means every data-driven component explicitly handles and renders a distinct error UI rather than silently showing nothing, displaying a stale value, or crashing — giving users the context they need to understand what went wrong and what to do next.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list blue">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Three-state model:</strong> Any async component has three states — loading, error, and success — each requiring a dedicated UI branch; skipping the error branch is the most common omission</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Error granularity:</strong> Distinguish between network errors (retry button), permission errors (contact support), and not-found errors (navigate away) — each warrants a different message and action</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Inline vs. boundary:</strong> Use inline error UI for section-level failures; use React Error Boundaries for unexpected render errors that should not unmount the entire page</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Retry pattern:</strong> For transient network failures, show a retry button and reuse the same data-fetching function — React Query's <code>refetch</code> makes this trivial</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">ProductList</span>() {
  <span class="kw">const</span> { data, isLoading, isError, error, refetch } = <span class="fn">useProducts</span>();

  <span class="kw">if</span> (isLoading) <span class="kw">return</span> &lt;<span class="fn">ProductListSkeleton</span> /&gt;;

  <span class="kw">if</span> (isError) {
    <span class="kw">const</span> message = error <span class="kw">instanceof</span> ForbiddenError
      ? <span class="st">'You do not have permission to view products.'</span>
      : <span class="st">'Could not load products. Check your connection.'</span>;

    <span class="kw">return</span> (
      &lt;div className=<span class="st">"error-state"</span> role=<span class="st">"alert"</span>&gt;
        &lt;p&gt;{message}&lt;/p&gt;
        {!(error <span class="kw">instanceof</span> ForbiddenError) &amp;&amp;
          &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; <span class="fn">refetch</span>()}&gt;Try Again&lt;/button&gt;}
      &lt;/div&gt;
    );
  }

  <span class="kw">return</span> &lt;<span class="fn">ProductGrid</span> products={data} /&gt;;
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Error handling is where junior and senior engineers diverge most visibly — junior code assumes the happy path, senior code treats errors as first-class citizens.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q44 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q44" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">44</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              What is the empty state pattern?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q44" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>The empty state pattern renders a purposeful, helpful UI when a list, table, or section has no data — rather than showing blank space or nothing — providing context about why it is empty and guiding the user toward the next action.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list green">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Types of empty states:</strong> First use (user has not created any data yet), zero results from a filter/search, and cleared state (user deleted everything) — each warrants a different message</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Anatomy:</strong> An illustration or icon, a clear headline, a one-sentence explanation, and a primary CTA button — every element optional but each adding value</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>First-use empty states:</strong> The most important type — this is onboarding. Guide the user to their first meaningful action rather than showing a dead screen</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Search empty states:</strong> Confirm what the user searched for (<code>"No results for 'widget'"</code>), suggest corrective action (check spelling, clear filters)</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Pattern:</strong> Create a reusable <code>&lt;EmptyState&gt;</code> component accepting <code>icon</code>, <code>title</code>, <code>description</code>, and <code>action</code> props so every team uses the same design</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// components/EmptyState.tsx</span>
<span class="kw">interface</span> EmptyStateProps {
  icon:        React.ReactNode;
  title:       <span class="kw">string</span>;
  description: <span class="kw">string</span>;
  action?:     { label: <span class="kw">string</span>; onClick: () => <span class="kw">void</span> };
}

<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">EmptyState</span>({ icon, title, description, action }: EmptyStateProps) {
  <span class="kw">return</span> (
    &lt;div className=<span class="st">"empty-state"</span>&gt;
      &lt;div className=<span class="st">"empty-state__icon"</span>&gt;{icon}&lt;/div&gt;
      &lt;h3&gt;{title}&lt;/h3&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;{description}&lt;/p&gt;
      {action &amp;&amp; &lt;button onClick={action.onClick}&gt;{action.label}&lt;/button&gt;}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

<span class="cm">// Usage — first-use empty state</span>
&lt;<span class="fn">EmptyState</span>
  icon={&lt;<span class="fn">ShoppingBagIcon</span> /&gt;}
  title=<span class="st">"Your cart is empty"</span>
  description=<span class="st">"Browse products and add items to get started."</span>
  action={{ label: <span class="st">"Browse Products"</span>, onClick: () =&gt; <span class="fn">navigate</span>(<span class="st">'/products'</span>) }}
/&gt;</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Empty states are often overlooked and left as blank space — attention to this detail signals that a candidate thinks holistically about the complete user experience.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q45 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q45" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">45</span>
              <span class="diff-medium me-2">Medium</span>
              What is accessibility-first component design?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q45" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Accessibility-first design means treating WCAG compliance and keyboard/screen-reader support as non-negotiable requirements built into component architecture from the start — not bolted on afterward — by using semantic HTML, ARIA roles, focus management, and colour-contrast standards by default.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list purple">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Semantic HTML first:</strong> Use <code>&lt;button&gt;</code> not <code>&lt;div onClick&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;nav&gt;</code> not <code>&lt;div id="nav"&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;table&gt;</code> for tabular data — semantics give screen readers meaning for free</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Keyboard navigation:</strong> Every interactive element must be reachable and operable via keyboard; custom components (dropdowns, modals, date pickers) must implement the correct ARIA keyboard patterns from WAI-ARIA Authoring Practices</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Focus management:</strong> When a modal opens, move focus to the first interactive element inside it; when it closes, return focus to the trigger element</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>ARIA:</strong> Use <code>aria-label</code> for icon buttons with no visible text, <code>aria-expanded</code> on accordions, <code>aria-live="polite"</code> for dynamic content updates</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Tooling:</strong> Install <code>eslint-plugin-jsx-a11y</code> to catch issues at write-time; add <code>@storybook/addon-a11y</code> for runtime axe-core checks; test with VoiceOver and NVDA before shipping</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// Accessible modal — focus trap + return focus</span>
<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">Modal</span>({ isOpen, onClose, title, children }: ModalProps) {
  <span class="kw">const</span> modalRef = <span class="fn">useRef</span>&lt;HTMLDivElement&gt;(<span class="kw">null</span>);
  <span class="kw">const</span> triggerRef = <span class="fn">useRef</span>&lt;Element | null&gt;(<span class="kw">null</span>);

  <span class="fn">useEffect</span>(() => {
    <span class="kw">if</span> (isOpen) {
      triggerRef.current = document.activeElement;
      modalRef.current?.focus();                   <span class="cm">// move focus in</span>
    } <span class="kw">else</span> {
      (triggerRef.current <span class="kw">as</span> HTMLElement)?.<span class="fn">focus</span>(); <span class="cm">// return focus out</span>
    }
  }, [isOpen]);

  <span class="kw">if</span> (!isOpen) <span class="kw">return null</span>;
  <span class="kw">return</span> (
    &lt;div
      role=<span class="st">"dialog"</span> aria-modal=<span class="st">"true"</span> aria-labelledby=<span class="st">"modal-title"</span>
      ref={modalRef} tabIndex={-<span class="st">1</span>}
    &gt;
      &lt;h2 id=<span class="st">"modal-title"</span>&gt;{title}&lt;/h2&gt;
      {children}
      &lt;button onClick={onClose} aria-label=<span class="st">"Close dialog"</span>&gt;×&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Accessibility is both a legal requirement and a quality signal — companies increasingly mandate WCAG AA compliance, and senior engineers are expected to champion it.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q46 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q46" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">46</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              How do you implement real-time collaboration features (like Google Docs)?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q46" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Real-time collaboration requires a conflict-resolution algorithm (OT or CRDT), a live transport layer (WebSockets), presence awareness, and careful UI design to show cursor positions and user identities — all without causing render thrashing in React.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list blue">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>CRDTs (Conflict-free Replicated Data Types):</strong> The modern standard — Yjs and Automerge are the leading libraries. CRDTs guarantee that any two clients applying the same set of operations (in any order) converge to the same state, making them ideal for offline + async collaboration</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Transport:</strong> WebSockets (via Socket.io, Liveblocks, or a raw WebSocket server) push document deltas to all connected clients in sub-100ms; long-polling is too slow for collaborative editing</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Presence:</strong> Each client broadcasts its cursor position, selection range, and user identity on each change; store this as ephemeral state (not in the document CRDT) to avoid polluting history</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Liveblocks / PartyKit:</strong> Managed infrastructure options that provide presence, storage (Yjs-backed), and conflict resolution as a service, eliminating the need to run your own WebSocket servers</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>React integration:</strong> Use a context that holds the Yjs document; components subscribe to Yjs awareness events and update local React state, keeping the CRDT as the single source of truth</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// Minimal Yjs + Liveblocks collaboration setup</span>
<span class="kw">import</span> * <span class="kw">as</span> Y <span class="kw">from</span> <span class="st">'yjs'</span>;
<span class="kw">import</span> { <span class="fn">createClient</span> } <span class="kw">from</span> <span class="st">'@liveblocks/client'</span>;

<span class="kw">const</span> ydoc = <span class="kw">new</span> Y.<span class="fn">Doc</span>();
<span class="kw">const</span> yText = ydoc.<span class="fn">getText</span>(<span class="st">'document'</span>);

<span class="cm">// Subscribe to remote changes</span>
yText.<span class="fn">observe</span>(event => {
  <span class="cm">// event.delta contains insert/delete/retain operations</span>
  setContent(yText.<span class="fn">toString</span>());
});

<span class="cm">// Presence hook — broadcast cursor position</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> { updateMyPresence, others } = <span class="fn">useRoom</span>();

<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">onSelectionChange</span>(range: Range) {
  <span class="fn">updateMyPresence</span>({ cursor: { start: range.from, end: range.to } });
}

<span class="cm">// Render other users' cursors</span>
{others.<span class="fn">map</span>(({ connectionId, presence }) =>
  presence.cursor &amp;&amp; (
    &lt;<span class="fn">RemoteCursor</span>
      key={connectionId}
      position={presence.cursor}
      color={<span class="fn">getUserColor</span>(connectionId)}
    /&gt;
  )
)}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Real-time collaboration is one of the hardest frontend problems — even a high-level answer about CRDTs, WebSockets, and presence architecture demonstrates significant architectural depth.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q47 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q47" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">47</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              How do you build an offline-first React application?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q47" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>An offline-first React application is designed to function fully without a network connection by default — serving assets from a Service Worker cache, persisting data locally, and synchronising mutations when connectivity is restored.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list green">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Service Worker (Workbox):</strong> Pre-cache the app shell (JS, CSS, HTML) at install time so the app loads instantly even offline; use Workbox's <code>StaleWhileRevalidate</code> or <code>CacheFirst</code> strategies per asset type</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Local data store:</strong> Persist domain data in IndexedDB (via <code>idb</code>, <code>Dexie.js</code>, or <code>localForage</code>) so reads never require a network; React Query's <code>persistQueryClient</code> plugin rehydrates the query cache from IndexedDB on boot</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Mutation queue:</strong> Write mutations to an IndexedDB queue immediately; a background sync service drains the queue when the network returns (use Background Sync API or a polling reconnect handler)</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Conflict resolution:</strong> Decide a strategy — last-write-wins, server-wins, or CRDT merge — before the first conflict occurs, not after</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Network status UI:</strong> Use the <code>navigator.onLine</code> event and a <code>useNetworkStatus</code> hook to show an offline banner and disable actions that require connectivity (e.g., payment processing)</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// hooks/useNetworkStatus.ts</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">useNetworkStatus</span>() {
  <span class="kw">const</span> [isOnline, setIsOnline] = <span class="fn">useState</span>(navigator.onLine);
  <span class="fn">useEffect</span>(() => {
    <span class="kw">const</span> on  = () => <span class="fn">setIsOnline</span>(<span class="kw">true</span>);
    <span class="kw">const</span> off = () => <span class="fn">setIsOnline</span>(<span class="kw">false</span>);
    window.<span class="fn">addEventListener</span>(<span class="st">'online'</span>, on);
    window.<span class="fn">addEventListener</span>(<span class="st">'offline'</span>, off);
    <span class="kw">return</span> () => {
      window.<span class="fn">removeEventListener</span>(<span class="st">'online'</span>, on);
      window.<span class="fn">removeEventListener</span>(<span class="st">'offline'</span>, off);
    };
  }, []);
  <span class="kw">return</span> { isOnline };
}

<span class="cm">// Mutation queue — enqueue when offline, drain when online</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> { isOnline } = <span class="fn">useNetworkStatus</span>();
<span class="fn">useEffect</span>(() => {
  <span class="kw">if</span> (isOnline) <span class="fn">drainMutationQueue</span>();
}, [isOnline]);</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Offline-first is required for PWAs, field-worker apps, and any product used in unreliable network conditions — it tests Service Worker knowledge, local storage strategies, and sync architecture simultaneously.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q48 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q48" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">48</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              What is the CQRS pattern applied to React state?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q48" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>CQRS (Command Query Responsibility Segregation) applied to React state means separating the code path for reading data (queries) from the code path for writing data (commands), using different models, hooks, and sometimes different stores for each concern.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list purple">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Query side:</strong> React Query / SWR handles all reads — it owns the server cache, loading states, and background refetching; components call <code>useProducts()</code> and get normalised, cached data</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Command side:</strong> Redux Toolkit (or <code>useMutation</code>) handles all writes — it owns the action dispatch, optimistic updates, and side effects; components dispatch <code>renameProduct()</code> without needing to think about refetching</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Why separate:</strong> Read models are often shaped for display (denormalised, paginated, filtered); write models are shaped for validation and domain rules — combining them forces awkward compromises</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Synchronisation:</strong> After a command succeeds, invalidate the relevant React Query cache keys so the query side refetches with the new state</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Practical application:</strong> A form that writes data uses a Zustand write store; the table that displays it uses React Query — they are completely decoupled, individually testable, and composable</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// QUERY side — React Query owns reads</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">useProducts</span>(filters: ProductFilters) {
  <span class="kw">return</span> <span class="fn">useQuery</span>({
    queryKey: [<span class="st">'products'</span>, filters],
    queryFn:  () => api.products.<span class="fn">list</span>(filters),
    staleTime: <span class="st">30_000</span>,
  });
}

<span class="cm">// COMMAND side — mutation owns writes</span>
<span class="kw">export function</span> <span class="fn">useCreateProduct</span>() {
  <span class="kw">const</span> queryClient = <span class="fn">useQueryClient</span>();
  <span class="kw">return</span> <span class="fn">useMutation</span>({
    mutationFn: api.products.create,
    <span class="fn">onSuccess</span>() {
      <span class="cm">// synchronise: tell query side to refetch</span>
      queryClient.<span class="fn">invalidateQueries</span>({ queryKey: [<span class="st">'products'</span>] });
    },
  });
}

<span class="cm">// Component — query and command sides never directly touch</span>
<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">ProductsPage</span>() {
  <span class="kw">const</span> { data }    = <span class="fn">useProducts</span>({});         <span class="cm">// query</span>
  <span class="kw">const</span> createMutation = <span class="fn">useCreateProduct</span>();   <span class="cm">// command</span>
  ...
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> CQRS is a backend-origin pattern that has a clear, practical mapping to modern React data-fetching libraries — knowing it shows the candidate thinks in architectural principles, not just API calls.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q49 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q49" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">49</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              How do you implement undo/redo in a React application?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q49" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Undo/redo in React is implemented by maintaining a history stack of past states (or commands), moving a pointer backward (undo) or forward (redo) through that stack, and applying the state or command at the new pointer position.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list amber">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>State snapshot approach:</strong> Store the full state snapshot on every meaningful change in a <code>past[]</code> array; undo pops from <code>past</code> and pushes to <code>future[]</code>; redo does the reverse — simple but memory-intensive for large state</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Command approach:</strong> Store command objects with <code>execute()</code> and <code>undo()</code> methods (see Q30) — memory-efficient because you store the diff not the snapshot, but requires every mutation to be encapsulated as a command</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Redux + redux-undo:</strong> The <code>redux-undo</code> higher-order reducer wraps any Redux slice and gives it <code>past</code>, <code>present</code>, and <code>future</code> arrays automatically — the easiest path for Redux-based state</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Immer patches:</strong> Immer's <code>produceWithPatches</code> generates forward and inverse patch arrays for every state transition — apply forward patches to redo, inverse patches to undo, with minimal memory usage</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Debouncing:</strong> For text input, debounce history entries (e.g., save a snapshot every 500ms or on word boundary) rather than on every keypress — mirrors how most editors behave</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// useUndoReducer.ts — state snapshot approach</span>
<span class="kw">interface</span> History&lt;T&gt; {
  past:    T[];
  present: T;
  future:  T[];
}

<span class="kw">type</span> UndoAction&lt;A&gt; = A | { type: <span class="st">'UNDO'</span> } | { type: <span class="st">'REDO'</span> };

<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">undoReducer</span>&lt;S, A&gt;(inner: React.Reducer&lt;S, A&gt;) {
  <span class="kw">return</span> (hist: History&lt;S&gt;, action: UndoAction&lt;A&gt;): History&lt;S&gt; => {
    <span class="kw">if</span> ((<span class="kw">action as any</span>).type === <span class="st">'UNDO'</span> &amp;&amp; hist.past.length) {
      <span class="kw">const</span> previous = hist.past.<span class="fn">at</span>(-<span class="st">1</span>)!;
      <span class="kw">return</span> {
        past:    hist.past.<span class="fn">slice</span>(<span class="st">0</span>, -<span class="st">1</span>),
        present: previous,
        future:  [hist.present, ...hist.future],
      };
    }
    <span class="kw">if</span> ((<span class="kw">action as any</span>).type === <span class="st">'REDO'</span> &amp;&amp; hist.future.length) {
      <span class="kw">const</span> next = hist.future[<span class="st">0</span>];
      <span class="kw">return</span> {
        past:    [...hist.past, hist.present],
        present: next,
        future:  hist.future.<span class="fn">slice</span>(<span class="st">1</span>),
      };
    }
    <span class="kw">const</span> next = <span class="fn">inner</span>(hist.present, action <span class="kw">as</span> A);
    <span class="kw">return</span> {
      past:    [...hist.past, hist.present],
      present: next,
      future:  [],
    };
  };
}</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Undo/redo is a classic algorithm question with a direct frontend application — it tests both data structure reasoning (stacks) and React state management simultaneously.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

        <!-- Q50 -->
        <div class="accordion-item border-0 mb-2 rounded-3 overflow-hidden shadow-sm">
          <h2 class="accordion-header">
            <button class="accordion-button collapsed py-3" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#architecture-q50" data-bs-parent="#acc-architecture">
              <span class="q-num">50</span>
              <span class="diff-hard me-2">Hard</span>
              What is event sourcing and can it apply to frontend React state?
            </button>
          </h2>
          <div id="architecture-q50" class="accordion-collapse collapse">
            <div class="accordion-body">
              <p class="ans-hero"><i class="bi bi-check-circle me-2"></i>Event sourcing stores the full sequence of events that led to the current state rather than just the current state snapshot — the state is always derived by replaying the event log, making the complete history a first-class citizen of the data model.</p>
              <ul class="ans-list blue">
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Core principle:</strong> State = <code>events.reduce(reducer, initialState)</code> — this is exactly what Redux does. Every dispatched action is an immutable event appended to the store's implicit event log</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Redux IS event sourcing (lightweight):</strong> Redux DevTools' time-travel debugger is event sourcing in practice — it replays actions from the beginning to jump to any historical state</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Full event sourcing on the frontend:</strong> Persist the entire action log to <code>localStorage</code> or IndexedDB; replay it on boot to restore state without ever storing the state snapshot itself</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Projections:</strong> Derive multiple different views (read models) from the same event log by writing different reducer functions — one event log, many state representations</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>When to use on frontend:</strong> Collaborative editors, audit trails, offline-first sync (events are the sync unit), and complex undo/redo — not for typical CRUD apps where it adds unnecessary complexity</li>
                <li><i class="bi bi-arrow-right me-1"></i><strong>Trade-offs:</strong> The event log grows unboundedly — implement snapshotting (save a state snapshot + truncate older events) to cap storage and replay time</li>
              </ul>
              <pre class="code-block"><span class="cm">// Event sourcing with Redux — persist the action log</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> eventLog: Action[] = <span class="fn">loadEventLogFromStorage</span>() ?? [];

<span class="cm">// Middleware that appends every action to the log</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> eventSourcingMiddleware: Middleware = () => next => action => {
  eventLog.<span class="fn">push</span>(action);
  <span class="fn">saveEventLogToStorage</span>(eventLog); <span class="cm">// persist after every action</span>
  <span class="kw">return</span> <span class="fn">next</span>(action);
};

<span class="cm">// On boot — replay the persisted log to restore state</span>
<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">replayEventLog</span>(log: Action[], reducer: Reducer, initial: State) {
  <span class="kw">return</span> log.<span class="fn">reduce</span>(reducer, initial);
}

<span class="cm">// Snapshotting — keep only last 100 events + snapshot</span>
<span class="kw">function</span> <span class="fn">snapshot</span>(currentState: State) {
  <span class="fn">saveSnapshot</span>(currentState);
  eventLog.<span class="fn">splice</span>(<span class="st">0</span>); <span class="cm">// clear the log after snapshotting</span>
}

<span class="cm">// Multiple projections from the same event log</span>
<span class="kw">const</span> auditView   = eventLog.<span class="fn">reduce</span>(auditReducer,   {});
<span class="kw">const</span> summaryView = eventLog.<span class="fn">reduce</span>(summaryReducer, {});</pre>
              <div class="why-asks"><i class="bi bi-question-circle me-1"></i><strong>Why interviewers ask:</strong> Event sourcing is an advanced architectural concept — knowing it and recognising that Redux already implements its core principle demonstrates a candidate's ability to connect backend patterns to frontend implementations.</div>
            </div>
          </div>
        </div>

  </div>
</div>

<div class="tab-pane fade" id="tab-styling" role="tabpanel">
  <div class="accordion" id="acc-styling">
<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 1 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q1">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q1" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q1">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;1. What are CSS Modules in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q1" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q1" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>CSS Modules is a CSS file in which all class names and animation names are scoped locally by default. When you import a CSS Module into a JavaScript or TypeScript file, the build tool (Webpack, Vite, etc.) transforms the class names into unique, auto-generated strings, preventing any accidental name collisions with classes defined elsewhere in the application. This mechanism works by treating every CSS file with the <code>.module.css</code> extension as a module, where each selector is compiled into a unique hash-based identifier. The original author-written class name exists only in your source code; the browser sees a transformed, collision-safe version.</p>
      <p>In React you import the module as a JavaScript object and reference class names as object properties. This makes your CSS entirely co-located and component-scoped without any runtime overhead, since the transformation happens at build time. CSS Modules support composition via the <code>composes</code> keyword, allowing one rule to inherit styles from another without duplication.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">/* Button.module.css */
.button {
  padding: 8px 16px;
  border-radius: 4px;
  background-color: #0070f3;
  color: white;
  border: none;
  cursor: pointer;
}

.button:hover {
  background-color: #005bb5;
}

.large {
  composes: button;
  padding: 12px 24px;
  font-size: 1.1rem;
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Button.tsx
import styles from './Button.module.css';

interface ButtonProps {
  label: string;
  size?: 'default' | 'large';
  onClick?: () => void;
}

export function Button({ label, size = 'default', onClick }: ButtonProps) {
  const className = size === 'large' ? styles.large : styles.button;
  return (
    &lt;button className={className} onClick={onClick}&gt;
      {label}
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>The compiled output in the browser will render something like <code>class="Button_button__3xYzK"</code>, guaranteeing uniqueness even across a large application with thousands of components. Because everything is standard CSS, browser DevTools, linters, and IDE auto-complete all work without special plugins.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 2 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q2">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q2" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q2">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;2. What is the benefit of CSS Modules over global CSS?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q2" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q2" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>The primary benefit of CSS Modules over global CSS is <strong>automatic local scoping</strong>. In global CSS, any class name you define can clash with another class name defined anywhere else in your application or in a third-party library. In a large codebase with many developers, this leads to unintentional style overrides, specificity wars, and fragile code where changing one component's style can silently break a completely unrelated component. CSS Modules eliminate this problem at the tooling level — you never need to manually namespace classes like <code>.Header__logo</code> because the build tool does it for you.</p>
      <p>A second major benefit is <strong>dead-code elimination</strong>. Because class names are imported as JavaScript object properties, a bundler or static analysis tool can determine which class names are actually referenced and which are not, enabling tree-shaking of unused CSS. Global CSS has no such mechanism; a stylesheet is either entirely included or entirely excluded. CSS Modules also improve maintainability because the CSS for a component lives right next to it, making it immediately obvious which styles belong to which component — you delete the component file, you delete its CSS Module, and nothing else is affected.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">/* Global CSS — collision-prone */
/* styles.css */
.title { font-size: 2rem; }      /* Header component */
.title { font-size: 1.2rem; }    /* Card component — OVERWRITES Header! */

/* CSS Modules — collision-safe */
/* Header.module.css */
.title { font-size: 2rem; }      /* → compiled: Header_title__a1b2c */

/* Card.module.css */
.title { font-size: 1.2rem; }    /* → compiled: Card_title__d3e4f — no conflict */</pre>
      <p>CSS Modules also work seamlessly with TypeScript using the <code>typed-css-modules</code> or the Vite/Webpack built-in types, providing compile-time errors when you reference a class that does not exist in the module. This gives developers the same type safety for styling that TypeScript provides for component props.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 3 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q3">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q3" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q3">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;3. What is Styled Components?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q3" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q3" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Styled Components is a CSS-in-JS library that allows you to write actual CSS syntax directly inside your JavaScript/TypeScript files using ES6 tagged template literals. Instead of creating separate CSS files or applying class names, you define a styled React component by calling <code>styled.div</code>, <code>styled.button</code>, etc., and embedding CSS inside backtick strings. The library generates unique class names at runtime, injects the styles into a <code>&lt;style&gt;</code> tag in the document head, and returns a React component that applies those styles.</p>
      <p>The most compelling feature of Styled Components is its ability to receive React props and dynamically adapt styles based on them without switching class names. This eliminates the need for utility functions like <code>clsx</code> for simple prop-driven styling and keeps the logic co-located with the style declaration. It also supports theming through a <code>ThemeProvider</code> context, inheritance via the <code>styled(AnotherComponent)</code> pattern, and global styles via <code>createGlobalStyle</code>.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">import styled from 'styled-components';

interface ButtonProps {
  variant?: 'primary' | 'secondary';
  size?: 'sm' | 'md' | 'lg';
}

const Button = styled.button&lt;ButtonProps&gt;`
  padding: ${({ size }) =>
    size === 'lg' ? '12px 24px' : size === 'sm' ? '4px 8px' : '8px 16px'};
  background-color: ${({ variant }) =>
    variant === 'secondary' ? 'transparent' : '#0070f3'};
  color: ${({ variant }) => (variant === 'secondary' ? '#0070f3' : '#fff')};
  border: 2px solid #0070f3;
  border-radius: 4px;
  cursor: pointer;
  font-weight: 600;
  transition: all 0.2s ease;

  &:hover {
    opacity: 0.85;
    transform: translateY(-1px);
  }
`;

// Usage
function App() {
  return (
    &lt;&gt;
      &lt;Button variant="primary" size="md"&gt;Submit&lt;/Button&gt;
      &lt;Button variant="secondary" size="sm"&gt;Cancel&lt;/Button&gt;
    &lt;/&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>Styled Components v6 introduced improvements including better TypeScript inference, a smaller runtime, and compatibility with React Server Components when used with the Babel or SWC plugin that extracts styles at build time. Despite its popularity, Styled Components does carry a runtime cost because styles are generated and injected dynamically, which can affect First Contentful Paint in SSR scenarios.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 4 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q4">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q4" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q4">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;4. What is Tailwind CSS and how do you use it with React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q4" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q4" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Tailwind CSS is a utility-first CSS framework that provides a large set of low-level, single-purpose CSS classes — such as <code>flex</code>, <code>pt-4</code>, <code>text-center</code>, <code>bg-blue-500</code>, and <code>hover:opacity-80</code> — which you compose directly in your HTML or JSX to build designs. Instead of writing custom CSS and inventing class names, you build your UI entirely from these atomic utility classes. Tailwind's build step (PostCSS plugin) scans your source files for used class names and generates a CSS file containing only those classes, resulting in extremely small production bundles.</p>
      <p>To use Tailwind with a React (Vite) project you install the package and configure PostCSS. In React you apply utility classes via the standard <code>className</code> prop. Conditional classes are typically managed with the <code>clsx</code> or <code>cn</code> utility to keep JSX readable. Tailwind v3+ ships with JIT (Just-In-Time) mode enabled by default, which generates styles on-demand and supports arbitrary values like <code>w-[137px]</code>.</p>
      <pre class="code-block"># Installation
npm install -D tailwindcss postcss autoprefixer
npx tailwindcss init -p</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// tailwind.config.js
/** @type {import('tailwindcss').Config} */
export default {
  content: ['./index.html', './src/**/*.{js,ts,jsx,tsx}'],
  theme: {
    extend: {
      colors: {
        brand: { 500: '#0070f3', 600: '#005bb5' },
      },
    },
  },
  plugins: [],
};</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Card.tsx
import { clsx } from 'clsx';

interface CardProps {
  title: string;
  description: string;
  highlighted?: boolean;
}

export function Card({ title, description, highlighted }: CardProps) {
  return (
    &lt;div
      className={clsx(
        'rounded-xl p-6 shadow-md transition-shadow hover:shadow-lg',
        highlighted ? 'bg-brand-500 text-white' : 'bg-white text-gray-800'
      )}
    &gt;
      &lt;h3 className="text-xl font-semibold mb-2"&gt;{title}&lt;/h3&gt;
      &lt;p className="text-sm leading-relaxed"&gt;{description}&lt;/p&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>Tailwind encourages a design system mindset by centralizing spacing, colors, typography, and breakpoints in <code>tailwind.config.js</code>. This means every developer on the team uses the same scale, leading to visual consistency without writing a single line of custom CSS for common patterns.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 5 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q5">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q5" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q5">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;5. What is inline styling in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q5" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q5" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Inline styling in React means passing a JavaScript object directly to the <code>style</code> prop of a JSX element instead of using a CSS class. The object keys are camelCased versions of CSS property names (<code>backgroundColor</code> instead of <code>background-color</code>, <code>fontSize</code> instead of <code>font-size</code>), and the values are strings or numbers. When a number is provided for properties that accept pixel values (like <code>width</code>, <code>margin</code>, <code>padding</code>), React automatically appends <code>px</code>.</p>
      <p>Inline styles are useful for truly dynamic styles that must be computed at render time from props or state — for instance, animating a progress bar whose width is a percentage calculated from data. They are also zero-setup: no CSS file, no class name management. However, they have significant limitations: you cannot use pseudo-classes (<code>:hover</code>, <code>:focus</code>), pseudo-elements (<code>::before</code>), media queries, or CSS animations. Inline styles also bypass the browser's CSS cascade and have the highest specificity, which can make them difficult to override and debug.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Simple usage
function Badge({ count }: { count: number }) {
  const style: React.CSSProperties = {
    display: 'inline-flex',
    alignItems: 'center',
    justifyContent: 'center',
    width: 24,
    height: 24,
    borderRadius: '50%',
    backgroundColor: count > 0 ? '#e53e3e' : '#a0aec0',
    color: '#fff',
    fontSize: 12,
    fontWeight: 700,
  };

  return &lt;span style={style}&gt;{count}&lt;/span&gt;;
}

// Dynamic style based on props
function ProgressBar({ percent }: { percent: number }) {
  return (
    &lt;div style={{ width: '100%', height: 8, backgroundColor: '#e2e8f0', borderRadius: 4 }}&gt;
      &lt;div
        style={{
          width: `${Math.min(percent, 100)}%`,
          height: '100%',
          backgroundColor: '#0070f3',
          borderRadius: 4,
          transition: 'width 0.3s ease',
        }}
      /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>For most production use cases, inline styles should be reserved for genuinely dynamic values; all static and semi-static styles belong in CSS Modules, Tailwind utilities, or a CSS-in-JS solution that supports the full CSS feature set. Defining the style object outside the component or memoizing it with <code>useMemo</code> also prevents unnecessary object re-creation on every render.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 6 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q6">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q6" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q6">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;6. What is CSS-in-JS? Name some libraries.
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q6" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q6" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>CSS-in-JS is a styling paradigm where CSS rules are authored inside JavaScript or TypeScript files, co-located with the component logic, and processed by a JavaScript library rather than a standalone stylesheet. The library is responsible for generating unique class names, injecting <code>&lt;style&gt;</code> tags into the document, and optionally extracting styles to static files at build time. The core motivation is that JavaScript is a more powerful language than CSS for expressing conditional and dynamic styles — you get full access to variables, functions, loops, theme objects, and component props without any CSS-specific workarounds.</p>
      <p>CSS-in-JS libraries fall into two categories: <strong>runtime</strong> (styles are generated in the browser at runtime using JavaScript) and <strong>zero-runtime / build-time</strong> (styles are extracted to static CSS files during the build, with zero JavaScript execution cost in the browser). Popular libraries include:</p>
      <ul>
        <li><strong>Styled Components</strong> — runtime, tagged template literals, very popular</li>
        <li><strong>Emotion</strong> — runtime with optional zero-runtime mode, smaller bundle than Styled Components</li>
        <li><strong>Stitches</strong> — near-zero runtime, token-based design system focus</li>
        <li><strong>Vanilla Extract</strong> — zero-runtime, generates static CSS at build time, TypeScript-first</li>
        <li><strong>Linaria</strong> — zero-runtime, extracts CSS at build time, similar API to Styled Components</li>
        <li><strong>Panda CSS</strong> — zero-runtime, works with React Server Components, design token–first</li>
      </ul>
      <pre class="code-block">// Emotion (runtime) — @emotion/react
/** @jsxImportSource @emotion/react */
import { css } from '@emotion/react';

const buttonStyle = css`
  padding: 8px 16px;
  background: #0070f3;
  color: white;
  border-radius: 4px;
  border: none;
  cursor: pointer;
  &:hover { background: #005bb5; }
`;

export const Button = ({ children }) =&gt; (
  &lt;button css={buttonStyle}&gt;{children}&lt;/button&gt;
);

// Vanilla Extract (zero-runtime)
// button.css.ts
import { style } from '@vanilla-extract/css';
export const button = style({
  padding: '8px 16px',
  background: '#0070f3',
  color: 'white',
  borderRadius: 4,
  ':hover': { background: '#005bb5' },
});</pre>
      <p>The choice between runtime and zero-runtime CSS-in-JS is one of the most important architectural decisions for a React project, especially if you are using SSR or React Server Components, which are incompatible with runtime CSS-in-JS that relies on React context.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 7 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q7">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q7" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q7">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;7. What are the tradeoffs of CSS-in-JS?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q7" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q7" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>CSS-in-JS offers significant developer experience advantages: styles are co-located with components, dead-code elimination is automatic, dynamic styles are expressed naturally in JavaScript, TypeScript provides type safety for theme tokens, and there is no risk of class name collisions. These benefits make it attractive for design system development and component libraries. However, the tradeoffs depend heavily on whether you choose a runtime or a zero-runtime solution.</p>
      <p><strong>Runtime CSS-in-JS tradeoffs (Styled Components, Emotion):</strong> Every component render may trigger style serialization and injection. On the server, styles must be collected and embedded in the HTML response, adding complexity to SSR setup. In React 18 concurrent mode, style injection can cause issues with streaming SSR. Most critically, runtime CSS-in-JS does not work with React Server Components because style injection relies on React context, which is unavailable in server components. Bundle size also increases by roughly 15–40 KB (gzipped) for the library itself.</p>
      <p><strong>Zero-runtime CSS-in-JS tradeoffs (Vanilla Extract, Linaria, Panda CSS):</strong> These avoid all runtime costs and work with Server Components, but require a build-time plugin (Babel, Vite, or Webpack transform) and do not support fully dynamic styles — styles must be deterministic at build time. The developer experience can feel more constrained than runtime alternatives.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Runtime cost example — new object created every render
// BAD: style object is recreated on every render
function MyComponent({ active }) {
  return &lt;div css={{ color: active ? 'blue' : 'gray' }}&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// BETTER: variants pattern avoids serialization cost
const variants = {
  active: css`color: blue;`,
  inactive: css`color: gray;`,
};
function MyComponent({ active }) {
  return &lt;div css={active ? variants.active : variants.inactive}&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
      <p>The React core team and Next.js team have explicitly recommended zero-runtime or static CSS solutions (CSS Modules, Tailwind, Vanilla Extract) for App Router projects. If you need full dynamic styling capabilities in a Server Component architecture, Panda CSS's atomic approach is currently one of the best options as it generates static CSS files from design tokens while allowing runtime class composition.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 8 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q8">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q8" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q8">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;8. What is Emotion (CSS-in-JS library)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q8" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q8" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Emotion is a performant and flexible CSS-in-JS library that provides two APIs: <code>@emotion/css</code> (framework-agnostic) and <code>@emotion/react</code> (React-specific, adds the <code>css</code> prop). It was designed to be faster than Styled Components through an optimized style serialization and insertion algorithm, and it supports both string and object syntax for declaring styles. Emotion is widely adopted as the underlying styling engine for Material UI (MUI), Chakra UI, and several other major component libraries.</p>
      <p>The <code>css</code> prop (enabled via a Babel pragma or JSX runtime configuration) is Emotion's most powerful feature — it lets you write styles directly on any JSX element without creating a wrapper component. The <code>styled</code> API mirrors Styled Components' tagged template literal syntax for those who prefer it. Emotion also provides <code>Global</code> for global styles, <code>keyframes</code> for animations, and <code>ThemeProvider</code> for token-based theming.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Setup: vite.config.ts
import { defineConfig } from 'vite';
import react from '@vitejs/plugin-react';

export default defineConfig({
  plugins: [
    react({
      jsxImportSource: '@emotion/react', // enables css prop
      babel: {
        plugins: ['@emotion/babel-plugin'],
      },
    }),
  ],
});</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">/** @jsxImportSource @emotion/react */
import { css, Global, keyframes, ThemeProvider, useTheme } from '@emotion/react';
import styled from '@emotion/styled';

const fadeIn = keyframes`
  from { opacity: 0; transform: translateY(8px); }
  to   { opacity: 1; transform: translateY(0); }
`;

// Object style syntax
const cardStyle = css({
  borderRadius: 8,
  padding: '16px 24px',
  boxShadow: '0 2px 8px rgba(0,0,0,0.1)',
  animation: `${fadeIn} 0.3s ease`,
});

// Styled API
const Title = styled.h2`
  font-size: 1.5rem;
  font-weight: 700;
  color: ${({ theme }) =&gt; theme.colors.primary};
`;

export function Card({ title, children }) {
  return (
    &lt;div css={cardStyle}&gt;
      &lt;Title&gt;{title}&lt;/Title&gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>Emotion also offers server-side rendering support via <code>@emotion/server</code>, which provides a <code>renderStylesToString</code> utility for extracting critical CSS during SSR. However, like all runtime CSS-in-JS solutions, Emotion does not work natively with React Server Components and requires the component using Emotion to be a Client Component.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 9 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q9">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q9" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q9">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;9. What is the cx/clsx utility for conditional class names?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q9" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q9" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><code>clsx</code> (and its predecessor <code>classnames</code>) is a tiny utility function (~228 bytes) for constructing <code>className</code> strings conditionally in React. Without it, combining conditional class names requires manual string concatenation with ternary operators or template literals, which becomes verbose and error-prone as the number of conditions grows. <code>clsx</code> accepts any combination of strings, objects (where the key is a class name and the value is a boolean), and arrays, and returns a single space-delimited class name string containing only the truthy entries.</p>
      <p>The <code>cn</code> utility — popularized by shadcn/ui — is a thin wrapper that combines <code>clsx</code> with <code>tailwind-merge</code>. The <code>tailwind-merge</code> layer is specifically designed for Tailwind CSS: it intelligently resolves conflicting Tailwind utilities (e.g., if both <code>p-2</code> and <code>p-4</code> are present, it keeps only the last one), preventing class duplication and unexpected style overrides that vanilla <code>clsx</code> cannot handle.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// clsx usage
import { clsx } from 'clsx';

function Button({ variant, disabled, className, children }) {
  return (
    &lt;button
      className={clsx(
        'px-4 py-2 rounded font-medium transition-colors',
        {
          'bg-blue-600 text-white hover:bg-blue-700': variant === 'primary',
          'bg-gray-100 text-gray-800 hover:bg-gray-200': variant === 'secondary',
          'opacity-50 cursor-not-allowed pointer-events-none': disabled,
        },
        className  // allows caller to pass additional classes
      )}
      disabled={disabled}
    &gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// cn utility (clsx + tailwind-merge)
import { clsx, type ClassValue } from 'clsx';
import { twMerge } from 'tailwind-merge';

export function cn(...inputs: ClassValue[]) {
  return twMerge(clsx(inputs));
}

// Usage — tailwind-merge resolves conflicting utilities
cn('px-4 py-2', 'px-6')        // → 'py-2 px-6'  (px-4 replaced)
cn('text-red-500', 'text-blue-500') // → 'text-blue-500' (last wins)</pre>
      <p>The <code>cn</code> pattern has become the de-facto standard in the Tailwind + React ecosystem. Component libraries like shadcn/ui, Radix UI themes, and Catalyst all ship with this utility. For non-Tailwind projects, plain <code>clsx</code> is sufficient and avoids the extra <code>tailwind-merge</code> dependency.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 10 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q10">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q10" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q10">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;10. How do you implement dark mode in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q10" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q10" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Dark mode in React is typically implemented by toggling a class (e.g., <code>dark</code>) on the <code>&lt;html&gt;</code> or <code>&lt;body&gt;</code> element, which then activates a set of CSS custom properties or Tailwind's dark-mode utilities that override the default light-mode values. The user's preference is stored in <code>localStorage</code> so it persists across sessions, and the system preference is read via the <code>prefers-color-scheme</code> media query for first-time visitors. A React context exposes the current theme and a toggle function to all components.</p>
      <p>With Tailwind CSS, adding <code>darkMode: 'class'</code> to <code>tailwind.config.js</code> enables the <code>dark:</code> variant prefix, which applies utilities only when the <code>dark</code> class is present on an ancestor element. With CSS custom properties, you define a <code>:root</code> block for light tokens and a <code>.dark</code> block that overrides them, so all components that reference the variables automatically switch.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// hooks/useTheme.ts
import { useState, useEffect } from 'react';

type Theme = 'light' | 'dark';

export function useTheme() {
  const [theme, setTheme] = useState&lt;Theme&gt;(() =&gt; {
    if (typeof window === 'undefined') return 'light';
    return (localStorage.getItem('theme') as Theme)
      ?? (window.matchMedia('(prefers-color-scheme: dark)').matches ? 'dark' : 'light');
  });

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    document.documentElement.classList.toggle('dark', theme === 'dark');
    localStorage.setItem('theme', theme);
  }, [theme]);

  const toggleTheme = () =&gt; setTheme(t =&gt; (t === 'dark' ? 'light' : 'dark'));
  return { theme, toggleTheme };
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">/* CSS custom properties approach */
:root {
  --bg-primary: #ffffff;
  --text-primary: #1a202c;
  --surface: #f7fafc;
}

.dark {
  --bg-primary: #1a202c;
  --text-primary: #f7fafc;
  --surface: #2d3748;
}

/* Components just use the variables — no dark-mode logic needed */
.card {
  background: var(--surface);
  color: var(--text-primary);
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Tailwind dark mode
// tailwind.config.js
export default { darkMode: 'class', ... };

// Component
function Card({ title }) {
  return (
    &lt;div className="bg-white dark:bg-gray-800 text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-100 rounded-lg p-6"&gt;
      {title}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>To prevent a flash of incorrect theme on page load (especially in SSR/Next.js), inject a blocking inline script in the <code>&lt;head&gt;</code> that reads <code>localStorage</code> and sets the class before React hydrates. Next.js's <code>next-themes</code> package handles this automatically and is the recommended solution for Next.js projects.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 11 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q11">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q11" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q11">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;11. What are CSS custom properties (variables) and how do you use them in React theming?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q11" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q11" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>CSS custom properties (also called CSS variables) are entities defined by CSS authors that contain specific values to be reused throughout the document. They are defined with a <code>--</code> prefix (e.g., <code>--color-primary: #0070f3</code>) and accessed with the <code>var()</code> function. Unlike preprocessor variables (Sass <code>$variable</code>), CSS custom properties are <strong>live in the browser</strong> — they participate in the cascade, can be scoped to specific elements, can be overridden by more specific selectors, and can be read and modified with JavaScript at runtime.</p>
      <p>For React theming, CSS custom properties are the most performant approach because changing a single variable on the root element automatically updates every component that references it, without any JavaScript re-renders. This is dramatically more efficient than updating a React context value, which causes all context consumers to re-render. You can even animate CSS variables using CSS transitions or the Web Animations API.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">/* src/styles/tokens.css */
:root {
  /* Color tokens */
  --color-primary-500: #0070f3;
  --color-primary-600: #005bb5;
  --color-neutral-100: #f7fafc;
  --color-neutral-900: #1a202c;

  /* Semantic tokens */
  --color-bg: var(--color-neutral-100);
  --color-text: var(--color-neutral-900);
  --color-action: var(--color-primary-500);

  /* Typography tokens */
  --font-size-base: 1rem;
  --font-size-lg: 1.125rem;
  --line-height-base: 1.6;

  /* Spacing */
  --space-4: 1rem;
  --space-8: 2rem;
}

[data-theme="dark"] {
  --color-bg: var(--color-neutral-900);
  --color-text: var(--color-neutral-100);
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// useThemeTokens.ts — update tokens via JavaScript
export function setTheme(tokens: Record&lt;string, string&gt;) {
  const root = document.documentElement;
  Object.entries(tokens).forEach(([key, value]) =&gt; {
    root.style.setProperty(`--${key}`, value);
  });
}

// Read a token value
export function getToken(name: string): string {
  return getComputedStyle(document.documentElement)
    .getPropertyValue(`--${name}`)
    .trim();
}</pre>
      <p>CSS custom properties compose well with every React styling solution: CSS Modules can reference them, Tailwind can use them as arbitrary values, and CSS-in-JS libraries can read them via JavaScript. This makes them an excellent foundation for a design token system that is framework-agnostic and can be driven from a design tool like Figma using token exporters.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 12 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q12">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q12" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q12">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;12. How do you implement responsive design in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q12" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q12" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Responsive design in React is primarily achieved through <strong>CSS media queries</strong>, which are the same mechanism used in plain HTML/CSS projects. The React-specific approaches involve how you author and apply those media queries: using CSS Modules with <code>@media</code> blocks, Tailwind's responsive prefixes (<code>sm:</code>, <code>md:</code>, <code>lg:</code>), or CSS-in-JS template literals. Flexbox and CSS Grid are the layout tools of choice; both are inherently flexible and reduce the need for breakpoint-specific overrides.</p>
