      <p>For layout decisions that require JavaScript (e.g., rendering a completely different component tree for mobile vs. desktop, or reading viewport dimensions to drive logic), React provides two approaches: a custom <code>useMediaQuery</code> hook using the browser's <code>matchMedia</code> API, or the <code>useWindowSize</code> pattern. However, these introduce hydration complexity in SSR apps; CSS-only responsive design should always be preferred over JavaScript-driven layout switching when possible.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">/* CSS Modules approach */
/* Layout.module.css */
.grid {
  display: grid;
  grid-template-columns: 1fr;
  gap: 1rem;
}

@media (min-width: 768px) {
  .grid { grid-template-columns: repeat(2, 1fr); }
}

@media (min-width: 1024px) {
  .grid { grid-template-columns: repeat(3, 1fr); }
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Tailwind responsive prefixes
function ProductGrid({ products }) {
  return (
    &lt;div className="grid grid-cols-1 sm:grid-cols-2 lg:grid-cols-3 gap-4"&gt;
      {products.map(p =&gt; &lt;ProductCard key={p.id} {...p} /&gt;)}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// useMediaQuery hook
import { useState, useEffect } from 'react';

export function useMediaQuery(query: string): boolean {
  const [matches, setMatches] = useState(
    () =&gt; typeof window !== 'undefined' &amp;&amp; window.matchMedia(query).matches
  );

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const mql = window.matchMedia(query);
    const handler = (e: MediaQueryListEvent) =&gt; setMatches(e.matches);
    mql.addEventListener('change', handler);
    return () =&gt; mql.removeEventListener('change', handler);
  }, [query]);

  return matches;
}

// Usage
function Navigation() {
  const isMobile = useMediaQuery('(max-width: 767px)');
  return isMobile ? &lt;MobileNav /&gt; : &lt;DesktopNav /&gt;;
}</pre>
      <p>For truly responsive typography and spacing, CSS <code>clamp()</code> with viewport units provides smooth scaling without discrete breakpoints: <code>font-size: clamp(1rem, 2.5vw, 1.5rem)</code>. Container queries (<code>@container</code>), now available in all modern browsers, allow components to be responsive to their parent container size rather than the viewport, enabling fully portable responsive components.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 13 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q13">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q13" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q13">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;13. What is the MUI (Material UI) theming system?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q13" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q13" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Material UI's theming system is a centralized configuration object (<code>theme</code>) that controls the visual appearance of all MUI components simultaneously. Created via <code>createTheme()</code> and provided to the application through <code>ThemeProvider</code>, the theme defines a complete design system: color palette (primary, secondary, error, warning, info, success palettes with shades), typography scale (h1–h6, body1, body2, etc.), spacing function, breakpoints, shadows, shape (border radius), and z-index values. Every MUI component references this theme object, so changing a single value in the theme propagates across your entire application.</p>
      <p>MUI v6 uses Emotion as its CSS-in-JS engine by default, but can be configured to use Styled Components or Pigment CSS (a zero-runtime build-time extraction system). The <code>sx</code> prop, available on all MUI components, provides a shorthand API for inline theme-aware styles that supports responsive breakpoints, pseudo-selectors, and nested selectors.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// src/theme/index.ts
import { createTheme, alpha } from '@mui/material/styles';

export const theme = createTheme({
  palette: {
    mode: 'light',
    primary: {
      main: '#0070f3',
      light: '#4da3ff',
      dark: '#004fc4',
      contrastText: '#ffffff',
    },
    secondary: { main: '#7928ca' },
  },
  typography: {
    fontFamily: '"Inter", "Roboto", sans-serif',
    h1: { fontSize: '2.5rem', fontWeight: 700 },
    button: { textTransform: 'none', fontWeight: 600 },
  },
  shape: { borderRadius: 8 },
  components: {
    // Global component overrides
    MuiButton: {
      defaultProps: { disableElevation: true },
      styleOverrides: {
        root: ({ theme }) =&gt; ({
          '&amp;:hover': {
            backgroundColor: alpha(theme.palette.primary.main, 0.85),
          },
        }),
      },
    },
  },
});</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// main.tsx
import { ThemeProvider, CssBaseline } from '@mui/material';
import { theme } from './theme';

ReactDOM.createRoot(document.getElementById('root')!).render(
  &lt;ThemeProvider theme={theme}&gt;
    &lt;CssBaseline /&gt;
    &lt;App /&gt;
  &lt;/ThemeProvider&gt;
);

// Component using sx prop
function Card() {
  return (
    &lt;Box sx={{
      p: 3,  // theme.spacing(3) = 24px
      borderRadius: 2,  // theme.shape.borderRadius * 2
      bgcolor: 'primary.main',
      color: 'primary.contrastText',
      '&amp;:hover': { bgcolor: 'primary.dark' },
    }}&gt;
      Hello
    &lt;/Box&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>MUI also supports CSS variables mode (<code>cssVariables: true</code>) in v6, which generates CSS custom properties from the theme object, enabling dark mode switching without a React re-render and eliminating the SSR color flicker problem. This bridges the gap between MUI's theming system and native CSS variable–based approaches.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 14 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q14">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q14" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q14">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;14. What is Tailwind's JIT (Just-In-Time) mode?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q14" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q14" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Tailwind's Just-In-Time (JIT) mode, introduced in Tailwind CSS v2.1 and made the default in v3.0, is a compiler that generates CSS utilities on-demand as you author your template files rather than pre-generating the entire utility set upfront. Before JIT, Tailwind would generate a massive CSS file (often 3–4 MB uncompressed) containing every possible combination of every utility, then purge unused classes in production builds. JIT inverts this: it watches your source files, detects which class names are actually used, and generates only those styles — both in development and production.</p>
      <p>JIT provides four major improvements over the classic approach. First, development builds are nearly instant and the CSS bundle is tiny — exactly the same size as the production build. Second, JIT enables <strong>arbitrary value support</strong> using bracket notation, where you can write any CSS value inline without extending the configuration: <code>w-[137px]</code>, <code>bg-[#1da1f2]</code>, <code>grid-cols-[1fr_2fr_1fr]</code>. Third, it makes variant stacking effortless: <code>dark:hover:md:text-lg</code> is generated on the fly. Fourth, it removed the need for <code>@layer</code> workarounds for specificity ordering.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">/* JIT arbitrary values — zero config required */

/* Custom colors */
&lt;div className="bg-[#1da1f2] text-[#fff]"&gt;Twitter blue&lt;/div&gt;

/* Custom sizes */
&lt;div className="w-[calc(100%-2rem)] h-[min(50vh,400px)]"&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;

/* Custom grid */
&lt;div className="grid grid-cols-[200px_1fr_200px] gap-4"&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;

/* CSS properties via arbitrary properties (v3.3+) */
&lt;div className="[mask-image:linear-gradient(to_bottom,black,transparent)]"&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;

/* Stacked variants */
&lt;button className="dark:hover:focus:ring-2 dark:hover:focus:ring-blue-400"&gt;
  Click
&lt;/button&gt;</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// tailwind.config.js — JIT is the default, content scanning is required
export default {
  content: [
    './index.html',
    './src/**/*.{js,ts,jsx,tsx}',
    // Include component libraries that use Tailwind classes
    './node_modules/@my-company/ui/**/*.{js,ts}',
  ],
  // ...
};

// IMPORTANT: Never construct class names dynamically with string interpolation!
// BAD — JIT scanner cannot detect this:
const color = 'blue';
&lt;div className={`text-${color}-500`}&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;  // text-blue-500 won't be generated

// GOOD — use full class name strings:
const colorClass = 'text-blue-500';
&lt;div className={colorClass}&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;</pre>
      <p>The key constraint of JIT is that class names must appear as complete, unbroken strings in files matched by the <code>content</code> glob patterns. Dynamic class name construction via string interpolation will not work because the static scanner cannot evaluate JavaScript expressions. The solution is to use a lookup object mapping values to full class name strings, or to use <code>safelist</code> in the config for programmatically generated classes.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 15 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q15">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q15" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q15">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;15. What are the runtime performance differences between CSS Modules, Styled Components, and Tailwind?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q15" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q15" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>The three approaches have fundamentally different performance characteristics at multiple levels: build time, initial load, and runtime rendering. Understanding these differences is critical for making an informed architectural decision for performance-sensitive applications.</p>
      <p><strong>CSS Modules:</strong> All CSS is extracted to static files at build time. The browser receives a normal CSS file which is parsed and applied before any JavaScript runs. There is zero JavaScript runtime cost for styling — no style serialization, no <code>&lt;style&gt;</code> tag injection, no style recalculation triggered by JavaScript. Class names are immutable strings resolved at build time, so there is no per-render computation. CSS Modules are the best-performing option for initial load and runtime updates when styles are static or semi-static.</p>
      <p><strong>Tailwind CSS:</strong> Also extracts to a static CSS file at build time, but the file contains only atomic single-property utility classes. Because Tailwind utilities are tiny and highly cacheable, the browser can apply them extremely fast during style recalculation. In React, applying Tailwind classes is just string manipulation on the <code>className</code> prop — a trivial CPU cost. The tradeoff is larger HTML due to many class names on each element, but the browser's style engine is highly optimized for atomic class matching. Tailwind's runtime performance is essentially identical to CSS Modules.</p>
      <p><strong>Styled Components / Emotion (runtime CSS-in-JS):</strong> Has three layers of runtime cost. First, style serialization: on every render, the library hashes the template literal or object to determine if the style has changed and generate a class name (typically a constant-time hash, but not free). Second, style injection: new styles require inserting a rule into a CSSStyleSheet via <code>insertRule</code>, which triggers a browser style recalculation. Third, style collection for SSR: the server must walk the React tree, collect all styles, and embed them in the HTML — this adds to TTFB. In React 18 with concurrent features and streaming SSR, runtime CSS-in-JS can cause style flicker because styles may arrive out of order relative to their component HTML.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Benchmarking style serialization cost
// This pattern avoids re-serialization on every render:

// BAD — new object/template on every render, full re-serialization
function Item({ color }) {
  return &lt;div css={{ color, padding: 8 }}&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// GOOD — stable reference, cached hash
const baseStyle = css`padding: 8px;`;
const colorStyles = {
  red: css`color: red;`,
  blue: css`color: blue;`,
};
function Item({ color }) {
  return &lt;div css={[baseStyle, colorStyles[color]]}&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// CSS Modules — zero runtime cost regardless of pattern
import s from './Item.module.css';
function Item({ color }) {
  return &lt;div className={clsx(s.base, s[color])}&gt;...&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
      <p>In practice, for most applications with &lt;500 styled components, the runtime cost of Styled Components or Emotion is imperceptible. The real performance cliff appears at large scale (1000+ component instances in the tree), on low-powered mobile devices, and in SSR scenarios. Meta's research into Stylex (their zero-runtime atomic CSS-in-JS solution) and Vercel/Facebook's work on React Server Components both point to zero-runtime solutions as the long-term direction for high-performance React styling.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 16 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q16">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q16" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q16">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;16. How do you implement a design token system in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q16" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q16" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>A design token system is a shared vocabulary of design decisions — colors, typography, spacing, elevation, motion — stored as named key-value pairs that serve as the single source of truth for both designers (in Figma) and engineers (in code). Tokens are organized in a two-layer hierarchy: <strong>primitive tokens</strong> (raw values like <code>blue-500: #0070f3</code>) and <strong>semantic tokens</strong> (purpose-driven aliases like <code>color-action-primary: {blue-500}</code>). This separation means a designer can change the brand color globally by updating one primitive token, and every semantic token that references it updates automatically.</p>
      <p>In React, tokens are typically delivered as CSS custom properties, a TypeScript constants file, or both. The pipeline is: Figma → token export (Style Dictionary, Tokens Studio plugin) → build step that transforms raw JSON tokens into CSS variables and TypeScript types → consumed by CSS Modules, Tailwind config, or MUI theme. The key tool is <strong>Style Dictionary</strong> by Amazon, which accepts a token JSON file and generates outputs for any target format.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// tokens/tokens.json (source of truth, often exported from Figma)
{
  "color": {
    "brand": {
      "blue": { "500": { "value": "#0070f3" }, "600": { "value": "#005bb5" } }
    },
    "semantic": {
      "action": { "primary": { "value": "{color.brand.blue.500}" } },
      "action": { "hover":   { "value": "{color.brand.blue.600}" } }
    }
  },
  "spacing": {
    "1": { "value": "4px" },
    "2": { "value": "8px" },
    "4": { "value": "16px" },
    "8": { "value": "32px" }
  }
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// style-dictionary.config.js
import StyleDictionary from 'style-dictionary';

export default {
  source: ['tokens/**/*.json'],
  platforms: {
    css: {
      transformGroup: 'css',
      prefix: 'ds',
      buildPath: 'src/styles/',
      files: [{ destination: 'tokens.css', format: 'css/variables' }],
    },
    ts: {
      transformGroup: 'js',
      buildPath: 'src/styles/',
      files: [{ destination: 'tokens.ts', format: 'javascript/esm' }],
    },
  },
};</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">/* Generated: src/styles/tokens.css */
:root {
  --ds-color-brand-blue-500: #0070f3;
  --ds-color-brand-blue-600: #005bb5;
  --ds-color-semantic-action-primary: var(--ds-color-brand-blue-500);
  --ds-spacing-4: 16px;
}

// Generated: src/styles/tokens.ts
export const tokens = {
  color: { brand: { blue: { 500: '#0070f3' } } },
  spacing: { 4: '16px' },
} as const;</pre>
      <p>The most robust implementations also generate TypeScript types from the token structure, so developers get autocomplete and compile-time errors when using a token that doesn't exist. At runtime, semantic tokens implemented as CSS variables can be swapped per theme (light/dark, brand A/brand B) by overriding the variable values on a scoped selector, without touching any component code. This makes white-labeling and multi-theme support trivial at scale.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 17 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q17">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q17" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q17">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;17. What is critical CSS and how does it affect React SSR?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q17" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q17" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Critical CSS is the minimum set of CSS rules required to render the above-the-fold content of a page without waiting for the full stylesheet to load. By inlining this subset directly in the <code>&lt;head&gt;</code> of the HTML response, the browser can paint the visible portion of the page immediately without being blocked by a network request for the external CSS file. The remaining non-critical CSS is then loaded asynchronously. For React SSR, where the goal is to ship a fully rendered HTML document from the server, critical CSS is especially important for achieving a good Largest Contentful Paint (LCP) metric.</p>
      <p>Critical CSS intersects with React SSR in two ways. First, for <strong>static CSS approaches</strong> (CSS Modules, Tailwind), tools like <code>critical</code> (npm) or Vite's SSR plugin can analyze the rendered HTML and extract only the rules that match elements in the initial HTML, inlining them in the <code>&lt;style&gt;</code> tag. Second, for <strong>runtime CSS-in-JS</strong> (Styled Components, Emotion), the library itself performs critical CSS extraction during SSR by collecting all styles generated while rendering the component tree and embedding them in the server response — this is why Styled Components has a <code>ServerStyleSheet</code> API and Emotion has <code>renderStylesToString</code>.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Styled Components SSR — manual critical CSS collection
import { ServerStyleSheet } from 'styled-components';
import { renderToString } from 'react-dom/server';

export function renderPage(url: string) {
  const sheet = new ServerStyleSheet();

  try {
    const html = renderToString(
      sheet.collectStyles(&lt;App /&gt;)
    );
    const styleTags = sheet.getStyleTags(); // &lt;style&gt; tags with critical CSS

    return `
      &lt;!DOCTYPE html&gt;
      &lt;html&gt;
        &lt;head&gt;
          ${styleTags}
        &lt;/head&gt;
        &lt;body&gt;
          &lt;div id="root"&gt;${html}&lt;/div&gt;
        &lt;/body&gt;
      &lt;/html&gt;
    `;
  } finally {
    sheet.seal();
  }
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Next.js App Router — uses Pigment CSS or CSS Modules
// No manual critical CSS needed; Next.js handles it automatically
// for CSS Modules and global CSS.

// For Emotion/MUI in Next.js App Router, use the registry pattern:
// app/registry.tsx
'use client';
import { useServerInsertedHTML } from 'next/navigation';
import { useState } from 'react';
import createCache from '@emotion/cache';
import { CacheProvider } from '@emotion/react';

export function MUIRegistry({ children }) {
  const [cache] = useState(() =&gt; createCache({ key: 'css' }));
  useServerInsertedHTML(() =&gt; (
    &lt;style
      data-emotion={`${cache.key} ${Object.keys(cache.inserted).join(' ')}`}
      dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{
        __html: Object.values(cache.inserted).join(''),
      }}
    /&gt;
  ));
  return &lt;CacheProvider value={cache}&gt;{children}&lt;/CacheProvider&gt;;
}</pre>
      <p>The <code>useServerInsertedHTML</code> hook in Next.js App Router is specifically designed for this streaming SSR scenario — it allows CSS-in-JS libraries to inject their collected styles into the HTML stream at the correct position, before the component HTML that depends on them, avoiding the flash of unstyled content (FOUC) that would occur if styles arrived after the HTML that needs them.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 18 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q18">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q18" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q18">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;18. How do you prevent CSS specificity conflicts in large React apps?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q18" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q18" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>CSS specificity conflicts in large applications arise when multiple rules target the same element and their specificity scores conflict in unintended ways — leading to styles that are hard to override without resorting to <code>!important</code>, deeply nested selectors, or arbitrary class stacking. The root cause is almost always global CSS without scoping conventions. Preventing specificity conflicts is therefore fundamentally a <strong>scoping and specificity budgeting</strong> problem.</p>
      <p><strong>Strategy 1: Adopt a scoping mechanism.</strong> CSS Modules (local scoping by build tool), Tailwind (atomic utilities with no custom selectors), or CSS-in-JS (unique generated class names) each eliminate specificity conflicts within their own scope. The issue appears at boundaries: component library styles vs. application styles vs. third-party styles.</p>
      <p><strong>Strategy 2: Use <code>@layer</code> for explicit cascade ordering.</strong> The CSS <code>@layer</code> rule (now baseline in all major browsers) allows you to declare named cascade layers and their order. Rules in a lower-priority layer are always overridden by rules in a higher-priority layer, regardless of specificity. This is the modern, standards-based solution to the "library vs. app styles" conflict problem.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">/* Define layer order upfront — lower layers lose to higher layers */
@layer reset, base, components, utilities, overrides;

@layer reset {
  *, *::before, *::after { box-sizing: border-box; }
}

@layer base {
  body { font-family: var(--font-sans); }
}

@layer components {
  /* Third-party component library styles */
  .btn { padding: 8px 16px; } /* specificity 0-1-0 */
}

@layer utilities {
  /* Application-level utilities */
  .mt-4 { margin-top: 1rem; } /* overrides .btn even though same specificity */
}

@layer overrides {
  /* Emergency overrides — always win without !important */
  .visually-hidden { ... }
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Tailwind's approach — prefix configuration for conflict avoidance
// tailwind.config.js
export default {
  prefix: 'tw-',  // all utilities become tw-flex, tw-px-4, etc.
  // Now Tailwind cannot conflict with Bootstrap, MUI, or other class names
};

// MUI v6 — configure emotion's insertionPoint to control CSS order
// This ensures MUI styles appear before app styles in the document,
// giving app styles higher priority even at equal specificity
const emotionCache = createCache({
  key: 'mui',
  insertionPoint: document.querySelector('#emotion-insertion-point'),
});</pre>
      <p>Additional strategies include: using the <code>:where()</code> pseudo-class to write zero-specificity selectors (useful for reset layers); keeping component styles at the lowest possible specificity (single class, no element qualifiers); using CSS custom properties for values that need to be overridden rather than overriding the property declaration itself; and enforcing naming conventions like BEM (<code>.block__element--modifier</code>) which, while not technically preventing specificity issues, makes the intended specificity hierarchy explicit through structure.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 19 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q19">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q19" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q19">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;19. What is the CSS cascade and how do CSS-in-JS libraries handle it?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q19" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q19" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>The CSS cascade is the algorithm browsers use to determine which CSS declaration wins when multiple rules target the same property on the same element. It evaluates rules in priority order: <strong>importance</strong> (<code>!important</code> beats normal declarations), then <strong>origin</strong> (user agent stylesheet &lt; author stylesheet &lt; user stylesheet, though this is rarely relevant in apps), then <strong>specificity</strong> (inline styles &gt; IDs &gt; classes/attributes/pseudo-classes &gt; elements/pseudo-elements, scored as a four-part value), and finally <strong>source order</strong> (later declarations win when everything else is equal).</p>
      <p>CSS-in-JS libraries interact with the cascade in distinctive ways. Runtime CSS-in-JS (Styled Components, Emotion) generates a unique class name per style rule and injects the rule into a <code>CSSStyleSheet</code> via <code>insertRule</code>. Because each rule has exactly one class selector (specificity 0-1-0) and all rules are injected dynamically, the cascade winner is determined entirely by source order — the last-injected rule wins. This is predictable within a single library but can cause subtle conflicts with external stylesheets at equal specificity.</p>
      <p>Zero-runtime CSS-in-JS libraries like Vanilla Extract and StyleX take a different approach: they generate atomic CSS at build time (one class per property-value pair) and use the <code>@layer</code> cascade layer to control ordering. StyleX (Meta's solution) is particularly rigorous — it composes styles by array order at call time, and the last style in the array always wins because all StyleX rules live in the same cascade layer at the same specificity, with source order determined at build time by the composition order.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Emotion's cascade behavior
const baseStyle = css`color: red; font-size: 16px;`;
const overrideStyle = css`color: blue;`;  // injected after base

// This works correctly because overrideStyle is injected later:
&lt;div css={[baseStyle, overrideStyle]}&gt;  // final: color=blue, font-size=16px

// BUT: ordering is fragile. If overrideStyle was already injected before
// baseStyle (from another component instance), you might get color=red.
// This is the "style injection order" problem in runtime CSS-in-JS.</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Vanilla Extract — deterministic atomic CSS with @layer
// button.css.ts
import { style, composeStyles } from '@vanilla-extract/css';

const base = style({
  '@layer': {
    components: { color: 'red', fontSize: 16 }
  }
});

const override = style({
  '@layer': {
    utilities: { color: 'blue' }  // utilities layer wins over components layer
  }
});

// Composition is order-independent — layer hierarchy determines the winner
export const button = composeStyles(base, override); // always blue</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// StyleX — explicit composition order determines winner
import * as stylex from '@stylexjs/stylex';

const styles = stylex.create({
  base: { color: 'red', fontSize: 16 },
  highlighted: { color: 'blue' },
});

// Last prop wins — deterministic regardless of injection order
&lt;div {...stylex.props(styles.base, styles.highlighted)}&gt;  // blue
&lt;div {...stylex.props(styles.highlighted, styles.base)}&gt;  // red</pre>
      <p>Understanding the cascade is critical when mixing CSS-in-JS with external stylesheets (e.g., a component library, Bootstrap base, or third-party widget). The safest approach is to place your CSS-in-JS styles in an explicit <code>@layer</code> with a defined position in the layer order, removing specificity as a variable and relying purely on deliberate layer hierarchy for override behavior.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 20 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-styling-q20">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#styling-q20" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="styling-q20">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;20. How do you implement animation in React? Compare CSS transitions, Framer Motion, and React Spring.
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="styling-q20" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-styling-q20" data-bs-parent="#acc-styling">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Animation in React spans a spectrum from simple CSS transitions to complex orchestrated sequences, and the right tool depends on the animation's nature — whether it's state-driven, physics-based, gesture-linked, or triggered by mount/unmount lifecycle events. The three dominant approaches are pure CSS transitions/animations, the Framer Motion library, and React Spring.</p>
      <p><strong>CSS Transitions and Animations:</strong> The lowest-overhead option. CSS <code>transition</code> smoothly interpolates property changes triggered by class or style updates. CSS <code>@keyframes</code> handles looping or multi-step sequences. React integrates via <code>className</code> toggling or the <code>style</code> prop. The limitation is that CSS cannot animate elements entering or leaving the DOM (mounting/unmounting) without the help of a lifecycle-aware wrapper like <code>react-transition-group</code>, which adds/removes classes at the right lifecycle moment.</p>
      <p><strong>Framer Motion:</strong> A production-ready, declarative animation library with a React-first API. The core primitive is the <code>motion</code> component (e.g., <code>&lt;motion.div&gt;</code>), which accepts <code>initial</code>, <code>animate</code>, and <code>exit</code> props for enter/exit transitions, <code>whileHover</code> and <code>whileTap</code> for gesture animations, <code>variants</code> for orchestrated parent-child sequences, and <code>layout</code> for automatic layout change animations. Framer Motion uses the Web Animations API internally for GPU-accelerated transforms. Its <code>AnimatePresence</code> component handles exit animations when components unmount — a notoriously difficult problem with pure CSS.</p>
      <p><strong>React Spring:</strong> A physics-based animation library that models animations as spring simulations rather than duration-based easing curves. Instead of specifying <code>duration: 300ms, easing: ease-out</code>, you specify spring parameters like <code>tension</code>, <code>friction</code>, and <code>mass</code>. This produces animations that feel physically natural and can be interrupted mid-animation with the motion naturally reversing, unlike CSS transitions which snap back. React Spring uses <code>useSpring</code>, <code>useSprings</code>, <code>useTrail</code>, and <code>useTransition</code> hooks.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// CSS Transitions + react-transition-group
import { CSSTransition } from 'react-transition-group';
import './fade.css';

function Modal({ show, children }) {
  return (
    &lt;CSSTransition in={show} timeout={300} classNames="fade" unmountOnExit&gt;
      &lt;div className="modal"&gt;{children}&lt;/div&gt;
    &lt;/CSSTransition&gt;
  );
}
/* fade.css */
.fade-enter { opacity: 0; }
.fade-enter-active { opacity: 1; transition: opacity 300ms ease; }
.fade-exit { opacity: 1; }
.fade-exit-active { opacity: 0; transition: opacity 300ms ease; }</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Framer Motion
import { motion, AnimatePresence } from 'framer-motion';

const cardVariants = {
  hidden: { opacity: 0, y: 20, scale: 0.95 },
  visible: { opacity: 1, y: 0, scale: 1, transition: { duration: 0.3, ease: 'easeOut' } },
  exit: { opacity: 0, y: -20, transition: { duration: 0.2 } },
};

function CardList({ cards, selectedId }) {
  return (
    &lt;AnimatePresence&gt;
      {cards.map(card =&gt; (
        &lt;motion.div
          key={card.id}
          variants={cardVariants}
          initial="hidden"
          animate="visible"
          exit="exit"
          layout  // animates position changes automatically
          whileHover={{ scale: 1.02 }}
          whileTap={{ scale: 0.98 }}
        &gt;
          {card.content}
        &lt;/motion.div&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/AnimatePresence&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// React Spring — physics-based
import { useSpring, useTransition, animated } from '@react-spring/web';

// Simple spring
function Toggle({ open }) {
  const springs = useSpring({
    height: open ? 200 : 0,
    opacity: open ? 1 : 0,
    config: { tension: 280, friction: 20 },  // spring physics
  });
  return &lt;animated.div style={springs}&gt;content&lt;/animated.div&gt;;
}

// List transitions (mount/unmount)
function AnimatedList({ items }) {
  const transitions = useTransition(items, {
    keys: item =&gt; item.id,
    from: { opacity: 0, transform: 'translate3d(-40px,0,0)' },
    enter: { opacity: 1, transform: 'translate3d(0,0,0)' },
    leave: { opacity: 0, transform: 'translate3d(40px,0,0)' },
  });
  return transitions((style, item) =&gt; (
    &lt;animated.div style={style}&gt;{item.content}&lt;/animated.div&gt;
  ));
}</pre>
      <p><strong>Performance note:</strong> All three approaches should animate only GPU-composited properties — <code>transform</code> and <code>opacity</code> — to avoid layout thrashing. Properties like <code>width</code>, <code>height</code>, <code>top</code>, and <code>left</code> trigger layout recalculation on every frame and should be replaced with <code>translate()</code> and <code>scale()</code>. Framer Motion's <code>layout</code> prop uses the FLIP technique (First, Last, Invert, Play) to animate layout changes using only transforms, achieving smooth 60fps animations even for complex reflow-heavy changes. For the simplest hover/focus effects and entry animations, CSS transitions remain the best choice due to zero JavaScript overhead.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

  </div>
</div>

<div class="tab-pane fade" id="tab-nextjs" role="tabpanel">
  <div class="accordion" id="acc-nextjs">
<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 1 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q1">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q1" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q1">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;1. What is Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q1" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q1" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Next.js is a React framework built and maintained by Vercel that adds production-grade capabilities on top of React: server-side rendering, static site generation, file-based routing, API routes, image optimization, font optimization, and more — all with zero configuration out of the box. While plain React is a UI library that handles only the view layer, Next.js is a full-stack framework that handles routing, data fetching, bundling (via Turbopack or Webpack), and deployment concerns. It is one of the most widely adopted React frameworks and is officially recommended in the React documentation as a starting point for new projects.</p>
      <p>Next.js supports multiple rendering strategies in the same application — individual pages or even individual components can be statically generated, server-rendered, or client-rendered depending on their data requirements. This flexibility is its defining characteristic. With the App Router introduced in v13 and stabilized in v13.4, Next.js adopted React Server Components as the default rendering model, fundamentally changing how data fetching and component composition work. The framework integrates tightly with the Vercel deployment platform but can also be self-hosted on any Node.js server or exported as a static site.</p>
      <pre class="code-block"># Create a new Next.js project
npx create-next-app@latest my-app --typescript --tailwind --app

# Project structure (App Router)
my-app/
├── app/
│   ├── layout.tsx       # Root layout (Server Component)
│   ├── page.tsx         # Home page route /
│   ├── about/
│   │   └── page.tsx     # Route /about
│   └── api/
│       └── hello/
│           └── route.ts # API route /api/hello
├── public/              # Static assets
├── next.config.ts       # Next.js configuration
└── package.json</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 2 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q2">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q2" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q2">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;2. What is SSR (Server-Side Rendering)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q2" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q2" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Server-Side Rendering (SSR) means that the HTML for a page is generated on the server on each incoming request, rather than in the browser. When a user navigates to an SSR page, the server fetches the necessary data, renders the React component tree to an HTML string, and sends the fully-formed HTML to the browser. The browser can display meaningful content immediately — before any JavaScript is downloaded or executed — which significantly improves Time to First Byte (TTFB) and First Contentful Paint (FCP) compared to a pure client-rendered Single Page Application (SPA).</p>
      <p>After the HTML is delivered, React "hydrates" the page by attaching event listeners and making it interactive. SSR is ideal for pages whose content changes frequently and must be personalized or real-time (e.g., a dashboard showing live data, a logged-in user's profile page). In the Pages Router, SSR is opted into via <code>getServerSideProps</code>. In the App Router, any Server Component that fetches data without caching is effectively SSR. The main tradeoff of SSR vs. SSG is that the server must do work on every request, increasing server load and TTFB relative to serving a pre-built static file.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Pages Router — SSR via getServerSideProps
// pages/dashboard.tsx
import type { GetServerSideProps, InferGetServerSidePropsType } from 'next';

interface DashboardProps { user: { name: string; email: string } }

export const getServerSideProps: GetServerSideProps&lt;DashboardProps&gt; = async (ctx) =&gt; {
  const session = await getSession(ctx.req);
  if (!session) return { redirect: { destination: '/login', permanent: false } };

  const user = await fetchUser(session.userId);
  return { props: { user } };
};

export default function Dashboard({ user }: InferGetServerSidePropsType&lt;typeof getServerSideProps&gt;) {
  return &lt;h1&gt;Welcome, {user.name}&lt;/h1&gt;;
}

// App Router — SSR is the default for Server Components with no-store cache
// app/dashboard/page.tsx
async function DashboardPage() {
  const user = await fetch('/api/user', { cache: 'no-store' }).then(r =&gt; r.json());
  return &lt;h1&gt;Welcome, {user.name}&lt;/h1&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 3 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q3">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q3" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q3">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;3. What is SSG (Static Site Generation)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q3" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q3" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Static Site Generation (SSG) means that HTML pages are pre-rendered at <strong>build time</strong> — when you run <code>next build</code> — rather than on each request. The generated HTML files are stored on disk (or in a CDN) and served directly to every user without any server-side computation per request. This makes SSG the fastest possible delivery method: a CDN serves a static file with extremely low latency, and the server never processes any logic per request. SSG is ideal for content that is the same for every user and does not change frequently — marketing pages, blog posts, documentation, product listings.</p>
      <p>In the Pages Router, a page uses SSG by default if it has no data-fetching function, or explicitly via <code>getStaticProps</code>. For pages with dynamic routes (e.g., <code>/blog/[slug]</code>), <code>getStaticPaths</code> tells Next.js which paths to pre-render. In the App Router, a Server Component that fetches data with the default <code>force-cache</code> behavior is statically generated. The practical limitation of SSG is staleness: if data changes after the build, the page shows outdated content until the next build. This is addressed by ISR (Incremental Static Regeneration).</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Pages Router — SSG via getStaticProps
// pages/blog/[slug].tsx
import type { GetStaticProps, GetStaticPaths } from 'next';

export const getStaticPaths: GetStaticPaths = async () =&gt; {
  const posts = await fetchAllPosts();
  return {
    paths: posts.map(p =&gt; ({ params: { slug: p.slug } })),
    fallback: 'blocking', // ISR for new posts not built yet
  };
};

export const getStaticProps: GetStaticProps = async ({ params }) =&gt; {
  const post = await fetchPost(params!.slug as string);
  if (!post) return { notFound: true };
  return { props: { post }, revalidate: 60 }; // ISR: rebuild every 60s
};

// App Router — static by default
// app/blog/[slug]/page.tsx
export async function generateStaticParams() {
  const posts = await fetchAllPosts();
  return posts.map(p =&gt; ({ slug: p.slug }));
}

async function BlogPost({ params }: { params: { slug: string } }) {
  const post = await fetchPost(params.slug); // cached = static
  return &lt;article&gt;{post.content}&lt;/article&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 4 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q4">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q4" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q4">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;4. What is ISR (Incremental Static Regeneration)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q4" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q4" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Incremental Static Regeneration (ISR) is a hybrid rendering strategy that combines the performance of SSG with the freshness of SSR. With ISR, pages are statically generated at build time, but Next.js will regenerate individual pages in the background after a specified time interval (<code>revalidate</code> seconds) when a new request comes in. The first request after the interval expires triggers a background rebuild; the current (possibly stale) page is served to that visitor, and the freshly rebuilt page is stored and served to subsequent visitors. This is called the "stale-while-revalidate" pattern.</p>
      <p>ISR solves the primary pain point of SSG — stale content — without the per-request server cost of SSR. It is ideal for content that changes occasionally: e-commerce product pages, news articles, event listings. Next.js also supports <strong>on-demand ISR</strong>, where instead of a time-based interval, you explicitly trigger revalidation via an API call (e.g., from a CMS webhook), ensuring pages are always fresh immediately after content changes. In the App Router, on-demand ISR is handled via <code>revalidatePath()</code> and <code>revalidateTag()</code> from <code>next/cache</code>.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Pages Router ISR — time-based
export const getStaticProps: GetStaticProps = async ({ params }) =&gt; {
  const product = await fetchProduct(params!.id);
  return {
    props: { product },
    revalidate: 300, // regenerate at most every 5 minutes
  };
};

// On-demand ISR via API route (triggered by CMS webhook)
// pages/api/revalidate.ts
export default async function handler(req, res) {
  if (req.query.secret !== process.env.REVALIDATION_SECRET) {
    return res.status(401).json({ message: 'Invalid token' });
  }
  try {
    await res.revalidate(`/products/${req.query.id}`);
    return res.json({ revalidated: true });
  } catch (err) {
    return res.status(500).send('Error revalidating');
  }
}

// App Router — on-demand revalidation via Server Action or Route Handler
// app/api/revalidate/route.ts
import { revalidatePath, revalidateTag } from 'next/cache';
import { NextRequest } from 'next/server';

export async function POST(request: NextRequest) {
  const { path, tag } = await request.json();
  if (path) revalidatePath(path);
  if (tag) revalidateTag(tag);
  return Response.json({ revalidated: true, now: Date.now() });
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 5 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q5">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q5" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q5">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;5. What is CSR (Client-Side Rendering)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q5" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q5" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Client-Side Rendering (CSR) means that the browser receives a minimal HTML shell with a JavaScript bundle, and React builds the entire page in the browser by executing JavaScript. The server sends no meaningful HTML content — typically just a <code>&lt;div id="root"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</code> — and all rendering, data fetching, and UI construction happens on the client after the JavaScript loads. This is the model used by a standard Create React App (CRA) or Vite SPA setup. CSR results in a fast initial server response (just the shell HTML) but a slow Time to Interactive because the user sees a blank page or loading spinner until React bootstraps and fetches data.</p>
      <p>In Next.js, CSR is used selectively for portions of the page that are highly interactive, user-specific, or real-time — things like dashboards with live polling, rich form interfaces, or components that depend on browser-only APIs (<code>window</code>, <code>localStorage</code>). In the App Router, a Client Component (<code>'use client'</code>) that fetches data via <code>useEffect</code> or React Query is effectively CSR. Next.js also provides a <code>dynamic</code> wrapper with <code>ssr: false</code> to explicitly opt a component into CSR and skip it during SSR, which is necessary for components that cannot run in a Node.js environment.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Next.js — disable SSR for a component that uses browser-only APIs
import dynamic from 'next/dynamic';

const MapComponent = dynamic(() =&gt; import('@/components/Map'), {
  ssr: false,  // skip server rendering; render only in browser
  loading: () =&gt; &lt;div&gt;Loading map...&lt;/div&gt;,
});

export default function LocationPage() {
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;h1&gt;Store Locations&lt;/h1&gt;
      &lt;MapComponent /&gt; {/* rendered only in the browser */}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Client Component with CSR data fetching (useEffect pattern)
'use client';
import { useState, useEffect } from 'react';

export function LiveStockPrice({ ticker }: { ticker: string }) {
  const [price, setPrice] = useState&lt;number | null&gt;(null);

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const interval = setInterval(async () =&gt; {
      const data = await fetch(`/api/price/${ticker}`).then(r =&gt; r.json());
      setPrice(data.price);
    }, 5000);
    return () =&gt; clearInterval(interval);
  }, [ticker]);

  return &lt;span&gt;{ticker}: {price ?? 'Loading...'}&lt;/span&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 6 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q6">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q6" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q6">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;6. What is the Pages Router in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q6" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q6" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>The Pages Router is Next.js's original routing system, present since v1.0. In the Pages Router, every file inside the <code>pages/</code> directory automatically becomes a URL route — <code>pages/about.tsx</code> becomes <code>/about</code>, <code>pages/blog/[slug].tsx</code> becomes <code>/blog/:slug</code>. Data fetching is done through special exported async functions: <code>getStaticProps</code> for SSG, <code>getServerSideProps</code> for SSR, and <code>getStaticPaths</code> for dynamic SSG routes. All components in the Pages Router are React Client Components by default and run in the browser after hydration.</p>
      <p>The Pages Router uses a single root layout defined in <code>pages/_app.tsx</code> and a custom HTML document in <code>pages/_document.tsx</code>. API routes live in <code>pages/api/</code>. Despite being superseded by the App Router in Next.js 13+, the Pages Router remains fully supported, stable, and production-ready. Many large applications still use it, and migration to the App Router is opt-in. Both routers can coexist in the same Next.js project, allowing incremental migration.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// pages/_app.tsx — root layout for Pages Router
import type { AppProps } from 'next/app';
import { SessionProvider } from 'next-auth/react';
import '../styles/globals.css';

export default function App({ Component, pageProps: { session, ...pageProps } }: AppProps) {
  return (
    &lt;SessionProvider session={session}&gt;
      &lt;Component {...pageProps} /&gt;
    &lt;/SessionProvider&gt;
  );
}

// pages/blog/[slug].tsx — dynamic route
import type { GetStaticProps, GetStaticPaths } from 'next';
import Head from 'next/head';
import { useRouter } from 'next/router';

export default function BlogPost({ post }) {
  const router = useRouter();
  if (router.isFallback) return &lt;div&gt;Loading...&lt;/div&gt;;
  return (
    &lt;&gt;
      &lt;Head&gt;&lt;title&gt;{post.title}&lt;/title&gt;&lt;/Head&gt;
      &lt;article dangerouslySetInnerHTML={{ __html: post.htmlContent }} /&gt;
    &lt;/&gt;
  );
}

export const getStaticPaths: GetStaticPaths = async () =&gt; ({
  paths: [], fallback: 'blocking',
});

export const getStaticProps: GetStaticProps = async ({ params }) =&gt; {
  const post = await fetchPost(params!.slug as string);
  return post ? { props: { post } } : { notFound: true };
};</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 7 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q7">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q7" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q7">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;7. What is the App Router in Next.js 13+?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q7" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q7" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>The App Router, introduced in Next.js 13 and stabilized in 13.4, is a completely new routing system built on top of React Server Components. It lives in the <code>app/</code> directory alongside (or instead of) the <code>pages/</code> directory. The App Router introduces several fundamental changes: components are Server Components by default (they run only on the server and never ship JavaScript to the client), data fetching happens directly inside components using async/await rather than special exported functions, layouts are defined with <code>layout.tsx</code> files that persist across navigations, and colocation of UI files (<code>loading.tsx</code>, <code>error.tsx</code>, <code>not-found.tsx</code>) is built into the routing convention.</p>
      <p>The App Router enables nested layouts, where each route segment can have its own persistent layout that does not remount on navigation. It also supports parallel routes and intercepting routes for complex UI patterns like modals that are bookmarkable. Route handlers (<code>route.ts</code>) replace API routes (<code>pages/api/</code>). The App Router is the future of Next.js and the recommended choice for new projects, but it has a steeper learning curve than the Pages Router, particularly around understanding Server vs. Client Components and the new caching model.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// app/layout.tsx — root layout (persistent across all routes)
import type { Metadata } from 'next';
import { Inter } from 'next/font/google';

const inter = Inter({ subsets: ['latin'] });

export const metadata: Metadata = {
  title: { template: '%s | My App', default: 'My App' },
  description: 'My Next.js App Router application',
};

export default function RootLayout({ children }: { children: React.ReactNode }) {
  return (
    &lt;html lang="en"&gt;
      &lt;body className={inter.className}&gt;
        &lt;NavBar /&gt;       {/* Server Component — zero JS sent to client */}
        &lt;main&gt;{children}&lt;/main&gt;
        &lt;Footer /&gt;
      &lt;/body&gt;
    &lt;/html&gt;
  );
}

// app/blog/layout.tsx — nested layout (only wraps /blog/* routes)
export default function BlogLayout({ children }) {
  return (
    &lt;div className="grid grid-cols-[1fr_300px] gap-8"&gt;
      &lt;div&gt;{children}&lt;/div&gt;
      &lt;aside&gt;&lt;RecentPosts /&gt;&lt;/aside&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// app/blog/[slug]/page.tsx — page component
async function BlogPost({ params }: { params: { slug: string } }) {
  const post = await fetchPost(params.slug);
  return &lt;article&gt;{post.content}&lt;/article&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 8 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q8">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q8" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q8">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;8. What are React Server Components (RSC)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q8" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q8" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>React Server Components (RSC) are a new type of React component that execute exclusively on the server and never run in the browser. They were introduced by the React team as a first-class primitive (not a Next.js-specific feature), but Next.js App Router is currently the primary production implementation. Because RSCs run on the server, they can directly access databases, file systems, and backend services without an intermediate API layer. Their output — a serialized React component tree (RSC payload, not HTML) — is sent to the client, where it is stitched together with the rest of the UI without shipping the component's JavaScript code to the browser.</p>
      <p>The key insight of RSCs is the separation of "server concerns" from "client concerns." Components that display data, render markdown, query databases, or access server-only secrets can be Server Components — they have zero impact on the client JavaScript bundle size. Components that need interactivity (event handlers, state, effects, browser APIs) must be Client Components marked with <code>'use client'</code>. This enables a new architecture where the bulk of an application's logic stays on the server, and only the interactive leaves of the component tree are Client Components.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Server Component — runs ONLY on the server
// app/products/page.tsx (no 'use client' directive = Server Component by default)
import { db } from '@/lib/db'; // Direct DB access — impossible in client components!

async function ProductsPage() {
  // Fetch directly in the component — no useEffect, no API route needed
  const products = await db.product.findMany({
    where: { inStock: true },
    orderBy: { createdAt: 'desc' },
  });

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;h1&gt;Products&lt;/h1&gt;
      {/* ProductCard is also a Server Component */}
      {products.map(p =&gt; &lt;ProductCard key={p.id} product={p} /&gt;)}
      {/* AddToCartButton needs interactivity — must be Client Component */}
      &lt;AddToCartButton productId={products[0].id} /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Client Component — runs on server (for HTML) AND in the browser
// components/AddToCartButton.tsx
'use client';
import { useState } from 'react';

export function AddToCartButton({ productId }: { productId: string }) {
  const [added, setAdded] = useState(false);
  return (
    &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; { addToCart(productId); setAdded(true); }}&gt;
      {added ? 'Added!' : 'Add to Cart'}
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>RSCs communicate with Client Components by passing serializable props (strings, numbers, plain objects, arrays). You cannot pass functions, class instances, or non-serializable values as props from a Server Component to a Client Component. Server Components can import Client Components, but Client Components cannot import Server Components (though they can receive them as <code>children</code> props — a critical pattern for composing the two).</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 9 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q9">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q9" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q9">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;9. What is the difference between Server Components and Client Components in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q9" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q9" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>The distinction is about <strong>where the component executes and what capabilities it has access to</strong>. Server Components execute only on the server (at request time or build time), have access to server-side resources (databases, file system, environment variables, backend services), and their code is never included in the browser's JavaScript bundle. Client Components are marked with <code>'use client'</code>, their code ships to the browser, and they run on both the server (for the initial HTML render) and in the browser (for hydration and subsequent interactions).</p>
      <p>The practical rule is: default to Server Components, and reach for <code>'use client'</code> only when you need React state (<code>useState</code>), effects (<code>useEffect</code>), event handlers (<code>onClick</code>, <code>onChange</code>), browser APIs (<code>window</code>, <code>document</code>, <code>localStorage</code>), or third-party libraries that depend on these. Pushing the <code>'use client'</code> boundary as far down the component tree as possible maximizes the portion of your app that runs on the server, minimizing the client JavaScript bundle.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Feature comparison table implemented as code:

// SERVER COMPONENTS — can do:
// ✅ async/await at the component level
// ✅ Direct database queries
// ✅ Access process.env (including secrets)
// ✅ Import heavy server-only libraries (zero bundle cost)
// ✅ Fetch with automatic deduplication and caching

// SERVER COMPONENTS — cannot do:
// ❌ useState, useReducer, useRef
// ❌ useEffect, useLayoutEffect
// ❌ Event handlers (onClick, onChange)
// ❌ Browser APIs (window, document, localStorage)
// ❌ Context (createContext, useContext)

// CLIENT COMPONENTS — can do:
// ✅ All React hooks
// ✅ Event handlers
// ✅ Browser APIs
// ✅ Third-party libraries (Framer Motion, React Query, etc.)
// ✅ Real-time subscriptions

// CLIENT COMPONENTS — cannot do:
// ❌ Direct DB/filesystem access (must use API routes or Server Actions)
// ❌ Access server-only secrets directly</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Pattern: Server Component passes data as props to Client Component
// app/user-profile/page.tsx (Server Component)
async function UserProfilePage({ params }) {
  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: params.id } }); // server-only
  return &lt;ProfileEditor user={user} /&gt;; // Client Component handles interactions
}

// components/ProfileEditor.tsx (Client Component)
'use client';
export function ProfileEditor({ user }) {
  const [name, setName] = useState(user.name);
  // ...interactive form logic
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 10 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q10">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q10" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q10">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;10. What is the "use client" directive?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q10" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q10" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><code>'use client'</code> is a React directive (a string literal at the top of a file) that marks a module as a Client Component and defines a boundary between Server and Client Component trees. It does not mean the component only runs on the client — it still renders on the server for the initial HTML — but it means the component's JavaScript code is included in the browser bundle and the component runs in the browser for hydration and subsequent interactions. Without <code>'use client'</code>, a component in the App Router is a Server Component by default.</p>
      <p>The <code>'use client'</code> directive is <strong>contagious downward</strong>: once a file is marked as a Client Component, all modules it imports are also treated as Client Components (they are part of the client bundle). This is why the placement of the boundary matters — ideally it should be at the leaves of the component tree (small interactive widgets) rather than at the root, to minimize how much code is shipped to the browser. A single <code>'use client'</code> at the root layout would make your entire application a client bundle, negating the benefits of RSC.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// WRONG — 'use client' at a high level pulls all children into client bundle
'use client'; // ← This file + ALL its imports become client code
// app/layout.tsx
import { Header } from './Header';       // now client code
import { Footer } from './Footer';       // now client code
import { DataTable } from './DataTable'; // now client code — bad!

// RIGHT — 'use client' only on the interactive leaf
// app/layout.tsx (Server Component — no directive)
import { Header } from './Header';         // Server Component ✅
import { DataTable } from './DataTable';   // Server Component ✅
import { SearchBar } from './SearchBar';   // Client Component (has 'use client')

// components/SearchBar.tsx
'use client'; // Only this component + its specific imports become client code
import { useState } from 'react';

export function SearchBar() {
  const [query, setQuery] = useState('');
  return (
    &lt;input
      value={query}
      onChange={e =&gt; setQuery(e.target.value)}
      placeholder="Search..."
    /&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>An important pattern for working around the Client Component boundary is passing Server Components as <code>children</code> or other props to Client Components. The Client Component does not "own" the children — it renders a slot — so the children can remain Server Components even though they are rendered inside a Client Component. This enables a "donut" pattern: a Client Component shell with a Server Component hole in the middle.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 11 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q11">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q11" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q11">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;11. What is the "use server" directive?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q11" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q11" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><code>'use server'</code> is a React directive that marks a function (or all exports from a file) as a <strong>Server Action</strong> — a function that executes exclusively on the server but can be called from Client Components. Server Actions are the App Router's mechanism for handling form submissions and data mutations without writing a separate API route. When a Client Component calls a Server Action, Next.js automatically serializes the arguments, sends them to the server over an encrypted POST request, executes the function server-side, and returns the result to the client.</p>
      <p>The <code>'use server'</code> directive can be placed at the top of an async function inside a Server Component file (inline Server Action), or at the top of a separate <code>actions.ts</code> file where every exported async function is a Server Action. Server Actions integrate natively with HTML <code>&lt;form&gt;</code> elements via the <code>action</code> prop, enabling progressive enhancement — forms work even before JavaScript loads. They also integrate with React's <code>useActionState</code> and <code>useFormStatus</code> hooks for managing pending states and validation errors.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// app/actions.ts — Server Actions file
'use server';
import { db } from '@/lib/db';
import { revalidatePath } from 'next/cache';
import { redirect } from 'next/navigation';
import { z } from 'zod';

const CreatePostSchema = z.object({
  title: z.string().min(1).max(200),
  content: z.string().min(10),
});

export async function createPost(prevState: unknown, formData: FormData) {
  const parsed = CreatePostSchema.safeParse({
    title: formData.get('title'),
    content: formData.get('content'),
  });

  if (!parsed.success) {
    return { errors: parsed.error.flatten().fieldErrors };
  }

  const post = await db.post.create({ data: parsed.data });
  revalidatePath('/blog');
  redirect(`/blog/${post.slug}`);
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Client Component consuming the Server Action
'use client';
import { useActionState } from 'react';
import { useFormStatus } from 'react-dom';
import { createPost } from '@/app/actions';

function SubmitButton() {
  const { pending } = useFormStatus();
  return &lt;button disabled={pending}&gt;{pending ? 'Creating...' : 'Create Post'}&lt;/button&gt;;
}

export function CreatePostForm() {
  const [state, action] = useActionState(createPost, undefined);
  return (
    &lt;form action={action}&gt;
      &lt;input name="title" /&gt;
      {state?.errors?.title &amp;&amp; &lt;p className="error"&gt;{state.errors.title}&lt;/p&gt;}
      &lt;textarea name="content" /&gt;
      &lt;SubmitButton /&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 12 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q12">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q12" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q12">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;12. What is file-based routing in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q12" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q12" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>File-based routing means that the URL structure of your application is determined by the directory and file structure inside a special folder (<code>pages/</code> in the Pages Router, <code>app/</code> in the App Router). There is no central router configuration file; Next.js automatically creates routes by scanning the filesystem. This convention-over-configuration approach eliminates boilerplate and makes the route structure immediately obvious to anyone reading the project directory — the file tree IS the route tree.</p>
      <p>In the App Router, a route is created whenever a directory contains a <code>page.tsx</code> file. Special files like <code>layout.tsx</code>, <code>loading.tsx</code>, <code>error.tsx</code>, and <code>route.ts</code> have reserved purposes within their directory segment. Route groups (directories wrapped in parentheses, e.g., <code>(auth)/</code>) allow organizing files without affecting the URL. Private folders (prefixed with underscore, e.g., <code>_components/</code>) are excluded from routing entirely.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// App Router file structure → URL mapping:
app/
├── page.tsx                    →  /
├── about/
│   └── page.tsx                →  /about
├── blog/
│   ├── page.tsx                →  /blog
│   └── [slug]/
│       └── page.tsx            →  /blog/:slug
├── (marketing)/                →  Route group — no URL segment
│   ├── layout.tsx              →  Shared layout for marketing pages
│   ├── pricing/
│   │   └── page.tsx            →  /pricing
│   └── contact/
│       └── page.tsx            →  /contact
├── (dashboard)/
│   ├── layout.tsx              →  Different layout for dashboard
│   └── settings/
│       └── page.tsx            →  /settings
├── api/
│   └── users/
│       └── route.ts            →  GET/POST /api/users
└── _components/                →  Private folder — NOT a route
    └── Navbar.tsx</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Catch-all routes
app/
└── docs/
    └── [...slug]/
        └── page.tsx   →  /docs/anything/nested/as/deep/as/needed
// params.slug = ['anything', 'nested', 'as', 'deep', 'as', 'needed']

// Optional catch-all
└── [[...slug]]/
    └── page.tsx       →  /docs AND /docs/anything (slug is optional)</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 13 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q13">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q13" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q13">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;13. What are dynamic routes in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q13" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q13" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Dynamic routes are routes with URL segments that are variable — they match a pattern rather than a fixed string. In Next.js, dynamic segments are denoted by wrapping the folder or file name in square brackets: <code>[id]</code>, <code>[slug]</code>, <code>[...slug]</code> (catch-all), or <code>[[...slug]]</code> (optional catch-all). The dynamic segment value is available in the component via the <code>params</code> prop in the App Router, or <code>useRouter().query</code> / <code>getStaticProps</code> context in the Pages Router. Dynamic routes are essential for content-driven applications where the number of pages is determined by data — blog posts, product pages, user profiles.</p>
      <p>In the App Router, dynamic routes work seamlessly with Server Components. The <code>params</code> prop is automatically provided to the <code>page.tsx</code>, <code>layout.tsx</code>, and other special files within the route segment. For static generation of dynamic routes, <code>generateStaticParams()</code> (App Router) or <code>getStaticPaths</code> (Pages Router) tells Next.js which param values to pre-render at build time. Routes not included in <code>generateStaticParams</code> are rendered on-demand (either SSR or ISR depending on the <code>dynamicParams</code> config).</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// App Router dynamic routes
// app/products/[id]/page.tsx
interface Props {
  params: { id: string };
  searchParams: { [key: string]: string | string[] | undefined };
}

// Optional: pre-generate some product pages at build time
export async function generateStaticParams() {
  const featuredProducts = await fetchFeaturedProducts();
  return featuredProducts.map(p =&gt; ({ id: p.id.toString() }));
}

// Optional: generate metadata dynamically
export async function generateMetadata({ params }: Props) {
  const product = await fetchProduct(params.id);
  return { title: product.name, description: product.description };
}

export default async function ProductPage({ params, searchParams }: Props) {
  const product = await fetchProduct(params.id);
  const variant = searchParams.variant ?? 'default';

  if (!product) notFound(); // renders not-found.tsx

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;h1&gt;{product.name}&lt;/h1&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;Variant: {variant}&lt;/p&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Catch-all: app/docs/[...slug]/page.tsx
// Matches: /docs/intro, /docs/api/reference, /docs/a/b/c
export default function DocsPage({ params }: { params: { slug: string[] } }) {
  // params.slug = ['api', 'reference'] for /docs/api/reference
  return &lt;div&gt;Viewing: {params.slug.join('/')}&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 14 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q14">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q14" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q14">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;14. What are API routes in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q14" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q14" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>API routes in Next.js allow you to build backend API endpoints as part of the same Next.js project, without needing a separate server. In the Pages Router, API routes are created by placing files in <code>pages/api/</code>; each file exports a default handler function that receives <code>req</code> and <code>res</code> objects (similar to Express). In the App Router, API routes are replaced by <strong>Route Handlers</strong> defined in <code>route.ts</code> files — these use the standard Web <code>Request</code> and <code>Response</code> APIs and export named functions per HTTP method (<code>GET</code>, <code>POST</code>, <code>PUT</code>, <code>DELETE</code>, etc.).</p>
      <p>API routes run on the server and are not included in the client JavaScript bundle. They are useful for: proxying external APIs to hide credentials, handling webhooks, processing form data, building a BFF (Backend for Frontend) layer, and any server-side operation that needs to be triggered by the client. However, in the App Router, many use cases previously requiring an API route can now be handled directly by Server Components (for reads) or Server Actions (for mutations), eliminating the need for the API route entirely.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Pages Router API route — pages/api/users/[id].ts
import type { NextApiRequest, NextApiResponse } from 'next';

export default async function handler(req: NextApiRequest, res: NextApiResponse) {
  const { id } = req.query;

  switch (req.method) {
    case 'GET': {
      const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: String(id) } });
      return user ? res.json(user) : res.status(404).json({ error: 'Not found' });
    }
    case 'PUT': {
      const user = await db.user.update({ where: { id: String(id) }, data: req.body });
      return res.json(user);
    }
    default:
      res.setHeader('Allow', ['GET', 'PUT']);
      return res.status(405).end();
  }
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// App Router Route Handler — app/api/users/[id]/route.ts
import { NextRequest, NextResponse } from 'next/server';

export async function GET(
  request: NextRequest,
  { params }: { params: { id: string } }
) {
  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: params.id } });
  if (!user) return NextResponse.json({ error: 'Not found' }, { status: 404 });
  return NextResponse.json(user);
}

export async function PUT(
  request: NextRequest,
  { params }: { params: { id: string } }
) {
  const body = await request.json();
  const user = await db.user.update({ where: { id: params.id }, data: body });
  return NextResponse.json(user);
}

// Route Handlers also support streaming responses:
export async function GET() {
  const stream = new ReadableStream({ /* ... */ });
  return new Response(stream, { headers: { 'Content-Type': 'text/event-stream' } });
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 15 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q15">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q15" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q15">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;15. What is getStaticProps?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q15" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q15" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><code>getStaticProps</code> is a Pages Router function that you export from a page file to tell Next.js to pre-render the page at build time using the data it returns. It runs at build time on the server (never in the browser), so it can access databases, the filesystem, and private environment variables safely. The returned <code>props</code> object is serialized to JSON and passed to the page component.
