It can also return <code>notFound: true</code> to render the 404 page, <code>redirect</code> to redirect to another URL, and <code>revalidate</code> (a number in seconds) to enable ISR.</p>
      <p><code>getStaticProps</code> is the right choice when the page data is available at build time, is the same for all users, and changes infrequently. It produces the fastest possible page because the HTML is pre-built and served from a CDN. The main limitation is that you need to rebuild the project (or trigger ISR revalidation) to reflect data changes. In the App Router, the equivalent is simply fetching data in a Server Component — if the fetch is cached (the default), the page is statically generated; the ceremony of <code>getStaticProps</code> is eliminated.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// pages/blog/index.tsx
import type { GetStaticProps, InferGetStaticPropsType } from 'next';

interface Post { id: number; title: string; slug: string; excerpt: string; }

export const getStaticProps: GetStaticProps&lt;{ posts: Post[] }&gt; = async () =&gt; {
  try {
    const posts = await fetchPosts(); // runs at BUILD TIME, not per request
    return {
      props: { posts },
      revalidate: 3600, // ISR: regenerate at most every hour
    };
  } catch (error) {
    // Return notFound if data fetch fails
    return { notFound: true };
  }
};

export default function BlogIndex({ posts }: InferGetStaticPropsType&lt;typeof getStaticProps&gt;) {
  return (
    &lt;ul&gt;
      {posts.map(post =&gt; (
        &lt;li key={post.id}&gt;
          &lt;a href={`/blog/${post.slug}`}&gt;{post.title}&lt;/a&gt;
          &lt;p&gt;{post.excerpt}&lt;/p&gt;
        &lt;/li&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/ul&gt;
  );
}

// App Router equivalent — no getStaticProps needed:
// app/blog/page.tsx
async function BlogIndex() {
  const posts = await fetchPosts(); // cached by default = static
  return &lt;ul&gt;{posts.map(post =&gt; &lt;li key={post.id}&gt;...&lt;/li&gt;)}&lt;/ul&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 16 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q16">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q16" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q16">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;16. What is getServerSideProps?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q16" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q16" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><code>getServerSideProps</code> is a Pages Router function that opts a page into SSR (Server-Side Rendering). Unlike <code>getStaticProps</code> which runs once at build time, <code>getServerSideProps</code> runs on <strong>every request</strong> to the page. It receives a <code>context</code> object containing the request (<code>req</code>), response (<code>res</code>), query parameters (<code>query</code>), route params (<code>params</code>), preview mode data, and locale information. Like <code>getStaticProps</code>, it returns a <code>props</code> object, or can return <code>notFound</code> or <code>redirect</code>.</p>
      <p>Use <code>getServerSideProps</code> when the page content must be personalized per user (authentication-dependent data), must reflect real-time data (inventory levels, live feeds), or needs to read request headers or cookies to determine what to render. The tradeoff versus <code>getStaticProps</code> is performance: the server must do work on every request, increasing TTFB and server load. The response also cannot be cached by a CDN (unless you explicitly set <code>Cache-Control</code> headers). In the App Router, <code>getServerSideProps</code> is replaced by fetching with <code>cache: 'no-store'</code> or using <code>cookies()</code>/<code>headers()</code> from <code>next/headers</code>, which automatically opts the route into dynamic rendering.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// pages/dashboard.tsx
import type { GetServerSideProps, InferGetServerSidePropsType } from 'next';
import { getServerSession } from 'next-auth';
import { authOptions } from './api/auth/[...nextauth]';

interface DashboardProps {
  user: { name: string; email: string };
  stats: { orders: number; revenue: number };
}

export const getServerSideProps: GetServerSideProps&lt;DashboardProps&gt; = async (ctx) =&gt; {
  // Access cookies/session — only possible in getServerSideProps
  const session = await getServerSession(ctx.req, ctx.res, authOptions);

  if (!session) {
    return {
      redirect: { destination: `/login?callbackUrl=${ctx.resolvedUrl}`, permanent: false },
    };
  }

  // Fetch user-specific data
  const [user, stats] = await Promise.all([
    fetchUser(session.user.id),
    fetchUserStats(session.user.id),
  ]);

  return { props: { user, stats } };
};

export default function Dashboard({
  user, stats,
}: InferGetServerSidePropsType&lt;typeof getServerSideProps&gt;) {
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;h1&gt;Welcome, {user.name}&lt;/h1&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;Orders: {stats.orders} | Revenue: ${stats.revenue}&lt;/p&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 17 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q17">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q17" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q17">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;17. What is getStaticPaths?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q17" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q17" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><code>getStaticPaths</code> is a Pages Router function required alongside <code>getStaticProps</code> for pages with <strong>dynamic routes</strong>. Because Next.js must pre-render a finite set of pages at build time, it needs to know which dynamic route values to generate — <code>getStaticPaths</code> answers that question by returning a <code>paths</code> array and a <code>fallback</code> strategy. The function runs at build time and is never included in the client bundle. It must be exported from every dynamic route page that also uses <code>getStaticProps</code>.</p>
      <p>The <code>fallback</code> option controls what happens when a user visits a path not included in the <code>paths</code> array. <code>fallback: false</code> returns a 404 for unknown paths. <code>fallback: true</code> renders a loading state immediately, then generates the page on-demand in the background, caching it for future requests. <code>fallback: 'blocking'</code> waits server-side to fully generate the page before responding (no loading state), then caches it. In the App Router, <code>generateStaticParams()</code> is the direct replacement and the <code>dynamicParams</code> export controls fallback behavior.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// pages/products/[id].tsx
import type { GetStaticPaths, GetStaticProps } from 'next';

export const getStaticPaths: GetStaticPaths = async () =&gt; {
  // Pre-build the top 100 most popular products
  const topProducts = await fetchTopProducts(100);

  return {
    paths: topProducts.map(p =&gt; ({ params: { id: String(p.id) } })),
    fallback: 'blocking', // other products rendered on-demand and cached
  };
};

export const getStaticProps: GetStaticProps = async ({ params }) =&gt; {
  const product = await fetchProduct(params!.id as string);
  if (!product) return { notFound: true };
  return { props: { product }, revalidate: 600 };
};

// App Router equivalent
// app/products/[id]/page.tsx
export async function generateStaticParams() {
  const topProducts = await fetchTopProducts(100);
  return topProducts.map(p =&gt; ({ id: String(p.id) }));
}

// next.config.ts (or export in page.tsx)
export const dynamicParams = true; // = fallback: 'blocking' equivalent
// export const dynamicParams = false; // = fallback: false equivalent</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 18 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q18">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q18" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q18">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;18. What is the next/image component?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q18" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q18" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>The <code>next/image</code> component is Next.js's optimized image component that automatically handles the most impactful image performance optimizations: resizing images to the exact display size (avoiding sending a 4K image when a 400px thumbnail is needed), converting to modern formats (WebP, AVIF) when the browser supports them, lazy loading images below the fold, preventing layout shift by reserving space before the image loads, and serving images through a built-in on-demand image optimization API. These optimizations can dramatically improve Core Web Vitals, particularly LCP (Largest Contentful Paint) and CLS (Cumulative Layout Shift).</p>
      <p>The component requires either explicit <code>width</code> and <code>height</code> props (for sized images) or the <code>fill</code> prop (for images that fill their container). Remote image domains must be explicitly allowed in <code>next.config.ts</code> for security. The <code>priority</code> prop should be set on the above-the-fold LCP image to prevent lazy loading and preload it. Next.js v13+ improved the API by removing the required wrapper <code>&lt;div&gt;</code> and making <code>alt</code> required for accessibility.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// next.config.ts — allow remote image sources
import type { NextConfig } from 'next';

const config: NextConfig = {
  images: {
    remotePatterns: [
      { protocol: 'https', hostname: 'images.unsplash.com' },
      { protocol: 'https', hostname: 'cdn.mystore.com', pathname: '/products/**' },
    ],
  },
};
export default config;</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">import Image from 'next/image';
import heroImage from '@/public/hero.jpg'; // local image — auto width/height

// Local image — width/height inferred from import
function Hero() {
  return (
    &lt;Image
      src={heroImage}
      alt="Hero banner showing our product"
      priority // LCP image — disable lazy loading
      placeholder="blur" // blurred placeholder while loading
      className="rounded-xl"
    /&gt;
  );
}

// Remote image — explicit width/height required
function ProductCard({ product }) {
  return (
    &lt;div className="relative"&gt;
      &lt;Image
        src={product.imageUrl}
        alt={product.name}
        width={400}
        height={300}
        sizes="(max-width: 768px) 100vw, (max-width: 1200px) 50vw, 400px"
        className="object-cover rounded-lg"
      /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Fill mode — image fills parent container
function Banner() {
  return (
    &lt;div className="relative h-64 w-full"&gt;
      &lt;Image src="/banner.jpg" alt="Banner" fill className="object-cover" /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 19 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q19">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q19" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q19">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;19. What is the next/link component?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q19" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q19" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>The <code>next/link</code> component is Next.js's client-side navigation component that replaces the HTML <code>&lt;a&gt;</code> tag for internal navigation. It enables <strong>client-side page transitions</strong> without a full browser page reload, while still rendering as a standard <code>&lt;a&gt;</code> tag in the HTML for SEO and accessibility. Next.js automatically prefetches the linked page's JavaScript and data when the <code>&lt;Link&gt;</code> component enters the viewport (in production builds), making navigation feel nearly instant. This prefetching can be disabled with <code>prefetch={false}</code> for links that are expensive to prefetch.</p>
      <p>In Next.js 13+, the <code>&lt;Link&gt;</code> component no longer requires a nested <code>&lt;a&gt;</code> tag — it renders the <code>&lt;a&gt;</code> directly. You can pass any <code>&lt;a&gt;</code> attribute (like <code>className</code>, <code>aria-label</code>) directly to the <code>&lt;Link&gt;</code>. The <code>usePathname()</code>, <code>useRouter()</code>, and <code>useSearchParams()</code> hooks from <code>next/navigation</code> (App Router) enable programmatic navigation and reading current route information in Client Components.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">import Link from 'next/link';
import { usePathname } from 'next/navigation';

// Basic navigation
function Footer() {
  return (
    &lt;nav&gt;
      &lt;Link href="/"&gt;Home&lt;/Link&gt;
      &lt;Link href="/about"&gt;About&lt;/Link&gt;
      &lt;Link href="/blog"&gt;Blog&lt;/Link&gt;
      {/* Dynamic route */}
      &lt;Link href={`/products/${product.id}`}&gt;{product.name}&lt;/Link&gt;
    &lt;/nav&gt;
  );
}

// Active link highlighting (Client Component required for usePathname)
'use client';
function NavLink({ href, children }: { href: string; children: React.ReactNode }) {
  const pathname = usePathname();
  const isActive = pathname === href || pathname.startsWith(`${href}/`);
  return (
    &lt;Link
      href={href}
      className={isActive ? 'text-blue-600 font-semibold' : 'text-gray-600'}
      aria-current={isActive ? 'page' : undefined}
    &gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/Link&gt;
  );
}

// Programmatic navigation
'use client';
import { useRouter } from 'next/navigation';

function SearchForm() {
  const router = useRouter();
  const handleSearch = (query: string) =&gt; {
    router.push(`/search?q=${encodeURIComponent(query)}`);
    // router.replace() — replaces current history entry
    // router.back() / router.forward()
    // router.prefetch('/dashboard') — manual prefetch
  };
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 20 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q20">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q20" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q20">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;20. What is middleware in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q20" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q20" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Next.js Middleware is code that runs <strong>before a request is processed</strong>, at the edge (before the server) on every matched route. It is defined in a <code>middleware.ts</code> file at the root of the project and executes in a lightweight V8 isolate using the Edge Runtime, which has a subset of Node.js APIs but starts in microseconds. Middleware can read and modify the request, rewrite the URL, redirect, add headers, or return a response directly. Because it runs before the page is rendered, it is ideal for cross-cutting concerns that apply to many routes.</p>
      <p>Common middleware use cases include: authentication (redirect unauthenticated users before any page code runs), authorization (check roles/permissions), internationalization (detect locale and rewrite to localized routes), A/B testing (assign users to variants via cookies), rate limiting, bot protection, and request logging. Middleware uses the <code>matcher</code>  config to specify which routes it applies to, preventing it from running on every static asset request.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// middleware.ts (root of project)
import { NextResponse } from 'next/server';
import type { NextRequest } from 'next/server';
import { getToken } from 'next-auth/jwt';

export async function middleware(request: NextRequest) {
  const { pathname } = request.nextUrl;

  // Authentication check for protected routes
  const token = await getToken({ req: request, secret: process.env.NEXTAUTH_SECRET });

  if (!token &amp;&amp; pathname.startsWith('/dashboard')) {
    const loginUrl = new URL('/login', request.url);
    loginUrl.searchParams.set('callbackUrl', pathname);
    return NextResponse.redirect(loginUrl);
  }

  // Role-based authorization
  if (pathname.startsWith('/admin') &amp;&amp; token?.role !== 'admin') {
    return NextResponse.redirect(new URL('/403', request.url));
  }

  // Internationalization — detect locale and rewrite
  const locale = request.cookies.get('NEXT_LOCALE')?.value ?? 'en';
  if (!pathname.startsWith(`/${locale}`) &amp;&amp; !pathname.startsWith('/api')) {
    return NextResponse.rewrite(new URL(`/${locale}${pathname}`, request.url));
  }

  // Add security headers
  const response = NextResponse.next();
  response.headers.set('X-Frame-Options', 'DENY');
  response.headers.set('X-Content-Type-Options', 'nosniff');
  return response;
}

// Only run middleware on these paths
export const config = {
  matcher: [
    '/((?!_next/static|_next/image|favicon.ico|public/).*)',
  ],
};</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 21 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q21">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q21" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q21">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;21. How does Next.js App Router streaming work?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q21" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q21" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Streaming in Next.js App Router leverages React 18's <code>renderToPipeableStream</code> / <code>renderToReadableStream</code> APIs to send HTML to the browser progressively as each piece of the UI is ready, rather than waiting for the entire page to render before sending anything. Traditionally, SSR is "all or nothing" — if one slow data fetch blocks the render, the user sees a blank screen until everything is ready. With streaming, fast parts of the page (the shell, navigation, static content) are sent and displayed immediately, while slow parts (data-heavy sections) stream in later as their data resolves.</p>
      <p>The mechanism works through React's <code>Suspense</code> component. In the App Router, any Server Component that is wrapped in <code>&lt;Suspense&gt;</code> with a fallback will stream independently — the fallback is included in the initial HTML flush, and when the async component resolves, React sends a small script that swaps the fallback for the real content in the browser using DOM manipulation. The browser never needs to make an additional network request; everything arrives over the same initial HTTP connection via HTTP chunked transfer encoding.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// app/dashboard/page.tsx — streaming with Suspense
import { Suspense } from 'react';
import { DashboardSkeleton, StatsSkeleton } from '@/components/skeletons';

// These async Server Components fetch their own data independently
async function RevenueChart() {
  const data = await fetchRevenueData(); // might take 2 seconds
  return &lt;Chart data={data} /&gt;;
}

async function LatestInvoices() {
  const invoices = await fetchLatestInvoices(); // might take 1 second
  return &lt;InvoiceTable invoices={invoices} /&gt;;
}

async function KpiCards() {
  const stats = await fetchKpiStats(); // fast, ~100ms
  return &lt;StatCards stats={stats} /&gt;;
}

export default function DashboardPage() {
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      {/* KPI cards stream in first (~100ms) */}
      &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;StatsSkeleton /&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;KpiCards /&gt;
      &lt;/Suspense&gt;

      {/* Chart streams in second (~2s) — doesn't block invoices */}
      &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;DashboardSkeleton /&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;RevenueChart /&gt;
      &lt;/Suspense&gt;

      {/* Invoices stream in independently (~1s) */}
      &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;div&gt;Loading invoices...&lt;/div&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;LatestInvoices /&gt;
      &lt;/Suspense&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// loading.tsx — automatic Suspense boundary at the route segment level
// app/dashboard/loading.tsx
export default function DashboardLoading() {
  return &lt;DashboardSkeleton /&gt;;  // shown while page.tsx awaits
}

// The above is equivalent to wrapping the entire page.tsx in:
// &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;DashboardLoading /&gt;}&gt;
//   &lt;Page /&gt;
// &lt;/Suspense&gt;</pre>
      <p>Streaming dramatically improves Time to First Byte (TTFB) for pages that would otherwise be bottlenecked by a single slow query. The key insight is that component-level data fetching with Suspense allows each piece of UI to be sent as soon as its data is ready, making the effective TTFB equal to the fastest component rather than the slowest. This is one of the most significant performance advantages of the App Router over the Pages Router's monolithic <code>getServerSideProps</code>.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 22 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q22">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q22" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q22">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;22. What are Server Actions in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q22" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q22" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Server Actions are async functions marked with the <code>'use server'</code> directive that execute on the server and can be called from Client Components or from other Server Components. They are Next.js's mechanism for handling mutations (form submissions, data updates, deletes) without writing a separate API route. When a Server Action is invoked from the client, Next.js automatically creates a POST endpoint, serializes the arguments (using React's serialization protocol), executes the function on the server with full server-side capabilities (database access, secret environment variables, filesystem), and returns the result back to the client.</p>
      <p>Server Actions integrate deeply with the React ecosystem: they work as the <code>action</code> prop on HTML <code>&lt;form&gt;</code> elements (enabling progressive enhancement — forms submit even before JS loads), they work with <code>useActionState</code> for managing state and errors returned from the action, and they work with <code>useFormStatus</code> for showing pending states. On the Next.js side, Server Actions can call <code>revalidatePath()</code>, <code>revalidateTag()</code>, <code>redirect()</code>, and <code>cookies()</code> from within the action to update cached data and navigate the user after a successful mutation.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// app/todos/actions.ts
'use server';
import { db } from '@/lib/db';
import { revalidatePath } from 'next/cache';
import { redirect } from 'next/navigation';
import { z } from 'zod';

const TodoSchema = z.object({ title: z.string().min(1, 'Title is required') });

export type ActionState = { errors?: { title?: string[] }; message?: string };

export async function createTodo(prevState: ActionState, formData: FormData): Promise&lt;ActionState&gt; {
  const parsed = TodoSchema.safeParse({ title: formData.get('title') });

  if (!parsed.success) {
    return { errors: parsed.error.flatten().fieldErrors };
  }

  await db.todo.create({ data: { title: parsed.data.title, completed: false } });
  revalidatePath('/todos'); // invalidate the todos page cache
  return { message: 'Todo created!' };
}

export async function toggleTodo(id: string) {
  const todo = await db.todo.findUnique({ where: { id } });
  await db.todo.update({ where: { id }, data: { completed: !todo!.completed } });
  revalidatePath('/todos');
}

export async function deleteTodo(id: string) {
  await db.todo.delete({ where: { id } });
  revalidatePath('/todos');
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// app/todos/CreateTodoForm.tsx (Client Component)
'use client';
import { useActionState } from 'react';
import { useFormStatus } from 'react-dom';
import { createTodo, type ActionState } from './actions';

function SubmitButton() {
  const { pending } = useFormStatus();
  return (
    &lt;button type="submit" disabled={pending} className="btn btn-primary"&gt;
      {pending ? 'Adding...' : 'Add Todo'}
    &lt;/button&gt;
  );
}

export function CreateTodoForm() {
  const [state, formAction] = useActionState&lt;ActionState, FormData&gt;(createTodo, {});

  return (
    &lt;form action={formAction} className="flex gap-2"&gt;
      &lt;input
        name="title"
        placeholder="What needs to be done?"
        className="input"
        aria-describedby="title-error"
      /&gt;
      {state.errors?.title &amp;&amp; (
        &lt;p id="title-error" className="text-red-500"&gt;{state.errors.title[0]}&lt;/p&gt;
      )}
      &lt;SubmitButton /&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 23 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q23">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q23" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q23">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;23. What is the difference between layout.tsx and page.tsx?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q23" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q23" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Both <code>layout.tsx</code> and <code>page.tsx</code> are special reserved files in the Next.js App Router, but they serve fundamentally different purposes and have different lifecycle behaviors. A <code>page.tsx</code> is the unique UI for a specific route — it only renders when that exact route is matched, and it re-renders (or refetches) on every navigation to that route. A <code>layout.tsx</code> is a shared UI wrapper that <strong>persists across navigations</strong> between its child routes — it renders once and does not remount or re-execute its data fetching when the user navigates between routes nested within it.</p>
      <p>This persistence is the critical distinction. Because layouts do not remount on child route navigation, state and data in layouts survives navigation. This enables patterns impossible in the Pages Router: a sidebar with its own scroll position, a chat widget that keeps its open/closed state, or a layout-level data fetch that provides data to all child pages without being repeated. The root layout (<code>app/layout.tsx</code>) must render the <code>&lt;html&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;body&gt;</code> tags and is required in every Next.js App Router project. Nested layouts are composed automatically by Next.js based on the directory structure.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Layout hierarchy example:
// URL: /dashboard/settings

// 1. app/layout.tsx — PERSISTS for entire app lifecycle
export default function RootLayout({ children }) {
  // Renders ONCE — does not remount on any navigation
  const config = await fetchAppConfig(); // runs once, not per navigation
  return (
    &lt;html lang="en"&gt;
      &lt;body&gt;
        &lt;GlobalNav /&gt;
        {children} {/* → DashboardLayout */}
      &lt;/body&gt;
    &lt;/html&gt;
  );
}

// 2. app/dashboard/layout.tsx — PERSISTS across all /dashboard/* routes
export default async function DashboardLayout({ children }) {
  // Runs ONCE when entering /dashboard — persists while navigating within /dashboard
  const user = await getCurrentUser(); // NOT re-fetched on /dashboard → /dashboard/settings
  return (
    &lt;div className="flex"&gt;
      &lt;Sidebar user={user} /&gt;
      &lt;main&gt;{children}&lt;/main&gt; {/* → SettingsPage */}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// 3. app/dashboard/settings/page.tsx — renders ONLY at /dashboard/settings
export default async function SettingsPage() {
  // Re-fetches on EVERY navigation to /dashboard/settings
  const settings = await fetchUserSettings();
  return &lt;SettingsForm settings={settings} /&gt;;
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// Key behavioral differences:
// layout.tsx — receives `children` prop, does NOT receive `searchParams`
// page.tsx — receives `params` AND `searchParams`, does NOT receive `children`

// page.tsx props
interface PageProps {
  params: { slug: string };            // route params
  searchParams: { page?: string };     // query string ?page=2
}

// layout.tsx props
interface LayoutProps {
  children: React.ReactNode;           // nested page or layout
  params: { slug: string };            // available in nested layouts
  // No searchParams — layouts don't re-render on query string changes
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 24 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q24">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q24" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q24">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;24. What is the loading.tsx and error.tsx convention?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q24" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q24" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><code>loading.tsx</code> and <code>error.tsx</code> are special colocated files in the App Router that automatically create React Suspense and Error Boundary wrappers around the <code>page.tsx</code> in the same directory segment. They eliminate the need to manually write <code>&lt;Suspense&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;ErrorBoundary&gt;</code> wrapper components for every page — Next.js wires them up automatically based on their file names and location in the directory tree.</p>
      <p><code>loading.tsx</code> exports a React component that is shown as the Suspense fallback while the <code>page.tsx</code> (and its async Server Components) are loading. Because it's in a Suspense boundary, the loading UI is shown immediately on navigation — the layout above it stays visible and interactive while only the page content shows the skeleton. <code>error.tsx</code> is a Client Component (<code>'use client'</code> is required) that catches JavaScript errors thrown during rendering of the page segment — both synchronous render errors and async errors from Server Components. It receives the <code>error</code> and a <code>reset</code> function to retry rendering.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// app/dashboard/loading.tsx — automatically wraps page.tsx in Suspense
export default function DashboardLoading() {
  return (
    &lt;div className="animate-pulse space-y-4"&gt;
      &lt;div className="h-32 bg-gray-200 rounded-lg" /&gt;
      &lt;div className="grid grid-cols-3 gap-4"&gt;
        {[1, 2, 3].map(i =&gt; &lt;div key={i} className="h-24 bg-gray-200 rounded" /&gt;)}
      &lt;/div&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}
// Next.js treats this as:
// &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;DashboardLoading /&gt;}&gt;
//   &lt;DashboardPage /&gt;
// &lt;/Suspense&gt;</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// app/dashboard/error.tsx — automatically wraps page.tsx in ErrorBoundary
'use client'; // Error boundaries must be Client Components

import { useEffect } from 'react';

interface ErrorPageProps {
  error: Error &amp; { digest?: string }; // digest = server-side error ID
  reset: () =&gt; void;                  // retry — re-renders the segment
}

export default function DashboardError({ error, reset }: ErrorPageProps) {
  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    // Log to error tracking service (Sentry, Datadog, etc.)
    console.error('Dashboard error:', error);
  }, [error]);

  return (
    &lt;div className="flex flex-col items-center justify-center min-h-[400px] gap-4"&gt;
      &lt;h2 className="text-xl font-semibold"&gt;Something went wrong&lt;/h2&gt;
      &lt;p className="text-gray-500"&gt;
        {error.message ?? 'An unexpected error occurred'}
      &lt;/p&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={reset} className="btn btn-primary"&gt;
        Try again
      &lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Special files in the same segment only affect THEIR segment:
// app/dashboard/error.tsx catches errors in app/dashboard/page.tsx
// NOT errors in app/dashboard/layout.tsx (those bubble up to app/error.tsx)
// global-error.tsx at app root catches layout-level errors (replaces full page)</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 25 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q25">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q25" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q25">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;25. How does Next.js handle caching in the App Router?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q25" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q25" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>The App Router has a multi-layer caching system that is one of its most powerful and most misunderstood features. Understanding all four cache layers and their interaction is essential for building correct, performant applications. The four caches are: <strong>Request Memoization</strong>, <strong>Data Cache</strong>, <strong>Full Route Cache</strong>, and <strong>Router Cache</strong>.</p>
      <p><strong>1. Request Memoization (in-memory, per-request):</strong> React automatically deduplicates identical <code>fetch()</code> calls within a single server render cycle. If three Server Components call <code>fetchUser(id)</code> with the same URL, only one network request is made. This memo is cleared after the request completes. It enables safe data fetching co-location — every component can fetch what it needs without coordinating with siblings.</p>
      <p><strong>2. Data Cache (persistent, cross-request):</strong> Next.js extends the native <code>fetch</code> API with a persistent server-side Data Cache. By default, <code>fetch()</code> responses are cached indefinitely (<code>force-cache</code>). <code>cache: 'no-store'</code> opts out entirely. <code>next: { revalidate: 3600 }</code> sets a time-based TTL. <code>next: { tags: ['posts'] }</code> enables on-demand invalidation via <code>revalidateTag('posts')</code>. This cache survives between server restarts in production deployments (stored in the filesystem or a distributed cache with platforms like Vercel).</p>
      <p><strong>3. Full Route Cache (static HTML, per-deploy):</strong> Routes that are statically renderable (no dynamic functions, all fetches cached) have their HTML and RSC payload pre-rendered and stored at build time. These are served from the CDN without any server execution. ISR revalidation or on-demand revalidation can update entries in this cache post-deploy.</p>
      <p><strong>4. Router Cache (client-side, per-session):</strong> The browser caches RSC payloads for visited and prefetched routes. Navigating back is instant (from cache). <code>router.refresh()</code> clears this cache for the current route and refetches from the server.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Controlling the Data Cache per fetch call
async function getData() {
  // Cached indefinitely (default) → Full Route Cache candidate
  const staticData = await fetch('https://api.example.com/config');

  // Never cached → Dynamic rendering, fresh on every request
  const dynamicData = await fetch('https://api.example.com/live-prices', {
    cache: 'no-store',
  });

  // Time-based ISR — revalidate every 1 hour
  const semiStaticData = await fetch('https://api.example.com/products', {
    next: { revalidate: 3600 },
  });

  // Tag-based on-demand ISR
  const taggedData = await fetch('https://api.example.com/posts', {
    next: { tags: ['posts', 'blog'] },
  });
}

// Opt entire route into dynamic rendering (bypasses Full Route Cache)
export const dynamic = 'force-dynamic'; // equivalent to cache: 'no-store' on all fetches
export const revalidate = 60; // ISR for the whole route

// On-demand invalidation in a Server Action
import { revalidateTag, revalidatePath } from 'next/cache';
async function publishPost(id: string) {
  await db.post.update({ where: { id }, data: { published: true } });
  revalidateTag('posts');           // invalidates all fetches tagged 'posts'
  revalidatePath('/blog');          // invalidates the /blog Full Route Cache entry
  revalidatePath('/blog/[slug]', 'page'); // invalidates all dynamic blog post pages
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 26 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q26">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q26" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q26">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;26. What is partial prerendering (PPR) in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q26" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q26" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Partial Prerendering (PPR) is an experimental Next.js rendering model that combines static and dynamic rendering within a single route request, at the component level rather than the page level. Before PPR, a route was either entirely static (served from CDN instantly) or entirely dynamic (server-rendered on each request). PPR allows the static shell of a page (navigation, layout, static content) to be prerendered at build time and served from the CDN with the fastest possible TTFB, while dynamic "holes" (personalized content, real-time data) are streamed in from the server concurrently with the static shell delivery.</p>
      <p>PPR works by using React's <code>Suspense</code> boundaries as the signal for what is dynamic. The static shell (everything outside Suspense boundaries that has no dynamic dependencies) is built at compile time and stored as a static HTML+RSC payload. On a real request, the CDN serves the static shell immediately, and the server simultaneously begins computing the Suspense fallback content and streaming it in. The result is that users see the page shell in milliseconds (CDN speed) while personalized content arrives moments later via streaming — no hydration delay, no blank screen.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// next.config.ts — enable PPR (experimental)
const config: NextConfig = {
  experimental: { ppr: 'incremental' }, // 'incremental' enables per-route opt-in
};

// app/product/[id]/page.tsx — opt this route into PPR
export const experimental_ppr = true;

import { Suspense } from 'react';

// Static shell — prerendered at build time, served from CDN
async function ProductDetails({ id }: { id: string }) {
  const product = await fetchProduct(id); // cached fetch → static
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;h1&gt;{product.name}&lt;/h1&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;{product.description}&lt;/p&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;${product.price}&lt;/p&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Dynamic "hole" — computed at request time, streamed in
async function PersonalizedRecommendations({ productId }: { productId: string }) {
  const userId = await getCurrentUserId(); // reads cookies → dynamic
  const recommendations = await fetchRecommendations(userId, productId);
  return &lt;RecommendationList items={recommendations} /&gt;;
}

export default function ProductPage({ params }: { params: { id: string } }) {
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      {/* Static — served from CDN edge immediately */}
      &lt;ProductDetails id={params.id} /&gt;

      {/* Dynamic hole — skeleton shown, then streamed in */}
      &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;RecommendationSkeleton /&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;PersonalizedRecommendations productId={params.id} /&gt;
      &lt;/Suspense&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p>PPR represents a paradigm shift: rather than choosing between the performance of static sites and the personalization of dynamic sites, it delivers both simultaneously. It is analogous to "static hosting with real-time islands" — similar in concept to Astro's Island Architecture but applied to React Server Components with streaming. PPR became a stable feature in Next.js 15 after extensive testing and is positioned as the default rendering model for future Next.js versions.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 27 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q27">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q27" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q27">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;27. How do you implement authentication in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q27" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q27" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Authentication in Next.js spans middleware (route protection), Server Components (data-fetching with session context), Client Components (login/logout UI), and API routes or Server Actions (auth mutations). The recommended solution for most projects is <strong>Auth.js (formerly NextAuth.js) v5</strong>, which integrates natively with the App Router, supports 70+ OAuth providers, credentials-based login, JWT and database sessions, and provides first-class middleware integration for route protection.</p>
      <p>The authentication flow involves: (1) configuring Auth.js with providers and callbacks in <code>auth.ts</code>, (2) creating a Route Handler at <code>app/api/auth/[...nextauth]/route.ts</code> for OAuth redirects and session management, (3) protecting routes in <code>middleware.ts</code> by checking session before the page renders, (4) reading session in Server Components via the <code>auth()</code> helper, and (5) reading session in Client Components via the <code>useSession()</code> hook. Custom credential-based auth (username/password) uses Server Actions to validate credentials and call <code>signIn()</code>.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// auth.ts — Auth.js v5 configuration
import NextAuth from 'next-auth';
import GitHub from 'next-auth/providers/github';
import Credentials from 'next-auth/providers/credentials';
import { db } from '@/lib/db';
import bcrypt from 'bcryptjs';

export const { handlers, auth, signIn, signOut } = NextAuth({
  providers: [
    GitHub({ clientId: process.env.AUTH_GITHUB_ID!, clientSecret: process.env.AUTH_GITHUB_SECRET! }),
    Credentials({
      async authorize(credentials) {
        const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { email: String(credentials.email) } });
        if (!user?.hashedPassword) return null;
        const valid = await bcrypt.compare(String(credentials.password), user.hashedPassword);
        return valid ? user : null;
      },
    }),
  ],
  callbacks: {
    async session({ session, token }) {
      session.user.id = token.sub!;
      session.user.role = token.role as string;
      return session;
    },
    async jwt({ token, user }) {
      if (user) token.role = (user as any).role;
      return token;
    },
  },
});</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// app/api/auth/[...nextauth]/route.ts
import { handlers } from '@/auth';
export const { GET, POST } = handlers;

// middleware.ts — protect routes before rendering
import { auth } from '@/auth';
export default auth((req) =&gt; {
  const isAuthenticated = !!req.auth;
  const isProtected = req.nextUrl.pathname.startsWith('/dashboard');
  if (isProtected &amp;&amp; !isAuthenticated) {
    return Response.redirect(new URL('/login', req.url));
  }
});
export const config = { matcher: ['/((?!api|_next/static|_next/image|favicon.ico).*)'] };

// Server Component — read session
import { auth } from '@/auth';
async function DashboardPage() {
  const session = await auth(); // reads session cookie server-side
  if (!session) redirect('/login');
  return &lt;h1&gt;Welcome {session.user.name}&lt;/h1&gt;;
}

// Client Component — read session
'use client';
import { useSession, signOut } from 'next-auth/react';
function UserMenu() {
  const { data: session } = useSession();
  return &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; signOut()}&gt;Sign out {session?.user?.name}&lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 28 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q28">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q28" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q28">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;28. How do you optimize images and fonts in Next.js?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q28" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q28" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Next.js provides built-in, zero-configuration optimization for both images and fonts that addresses the most common performance pitfalls automatically. For images, the <code>next/image</code> component handles resizing, format conversion (WebP/AVIF), lazy loading, and layout shift prevention. For fonts, <code>next/font</code> downloads fonts at build time, self-hosts them (eliminating third-party DNS lookups), automatically inlines font-display swap, and generates <code>size-adjust</code> CSS to prevent layout shift during font swap. Both optimizations are designed to improve Core Web Vitals (LCP, CLS) with minimal developer effort.</p>
      <p>Advanced image optimization involves setting the <code>sizes</code> attribute correctly to tell the browser which image variant to download at each viewport width. Without <code>sizes</code>, the browser defaults to downloading the full-resolution variant. The <code>priority</code> attribute on the LCP image triggers resource hints (<code>&lt;link rel="preload"&gt;</code>) to start loading the image before React renders. For fonts, Next.js variable fonts are preferred (a single font file covers all weights and styles), and the <code>display: 'swap'</code> + <code>preload: true</code> combination ensures text is visible immediately with the fallback font, then swaps smoothly.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// next/font — Google Fonts with zero layout shift
import { Inter, Playfair_Display } from 'next/font/google';
import localFont from 'next/font/local';

// Variable font — single file for all weights
const inter = Inter({
  subsets: ['latin'],
  variable: '--font-inter',  // CSS variable for Tailwind usage
  display: 'swap',
  preload: true,
});

// Specific weights only
const playfair = Playfair_Display({
  subsets: ['latin'],
  weight: ['400', '700'],
  variable: '--font-playfair',
});

// Self-hosted local font
const brandFont = localFont({
  src: [
    { path: './fonts/Brand-Regular.woff2', weight: '400' },
    { path: './fonts/Brand-Bold.woff2',    weight: '700' },
  ],
  variable: '--font-brand',
});

// app/layout.tsx
export default function RootLayout({ children }) {
  return (
    &lt;html lang="en" className={`${inter.variable} ${playfair.variable}`}&gt;
      &lt;body className={inter.className}&gt;{children}&lt;/body&gt;
    &lt;/html&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// tailwind.config.js — use font CSS variables
export default {
  theme: {
    extend: {
      fontFamily: {
        sans: ['var(--font-inter)', 'system-ui', 'sans-serif'],
        display: ['var(--font-playfair)', 'serif'],
      },
    },
  },
};

// Advanced image optimization
import Image from 'next/image';

function HeroSection({ hero }) {
  return (
    &lt;div className="relative h-[600px]"&gt;
      &lt;Image
        src={hero.imageUrl}
        alt={hero.altText}
        fill
        priority        // LCP image — generate preload link
        quality={85}    // balance quality vs. file size (default: 75)
        sizes="100vw"   // full viewport width at all breakpoints
        className="object-cover object-center"
        placeholder="blur"
        blurDataURL={hero.blurDataUrl}  // base64 thumbnail for instant placeholder
      /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 29 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q29">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q29" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q29">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;29. What is the difference between revalidate tag and time-based revalidation?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q29" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q29" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Next.js offers two mechanisms for invalidating cached data in the Data Cache and Full Route Cache: <strong>time-based revalidation</strong> (ISR) and <strong>tag-based on-demand revalidation</strong>. They solve different problems and are often used together. Time-based revalidation sets a maximum age (TTL) for cached data — after the interval expires, the next request triggers a background regeneration using the stale-while-revalidate pattern. Tag-based revalidation allows you to explicitly and immediately invalidate specific cached data at any time, typically triggered by a CMS webhook, database mutation, or user action.</p>
      <p><strong>Time-based revalidation</strong> (<code>revalidate: N</code>) is set per fetch call or per route segment. It guarantees that data is never more than N seconds stale. It requires no external trigger — the system manages it automatically. The downside is that content remains stale until the interval expires even if the data changed seconds after the last regeneration. This is acceptable for content that updates on a schedule (daily blog posts, weekly pricing updates).</p>
      <p><strong>Tag-based revalidation</strong> (<code>revalidateTag('tag')</code>) lets you invalidate all fetches and routes that were tagged with a specific string, immediately, on demand. When a CMS publishes a new article, a webhook calls an API route that calls <code>revalidateTag('articles')</code>, which instantly marks all cached fetches tagged <code>'articles'</code> and all Full Route Cache entries that depend on them as stale. The next request to any affected route triggers a fresh regeneration. This enables true content freshness without polling overhead.</p>
      <pre class="code-block">// Setting up tagged fetches
async function fetchPosts() {
  return fetch('https://cms.example.com/posts', {
    next: {
      tags: ['posts', 'content'],   // multiple tags for granular control
      revalidate: 3600,              // fallback TTL if tag never invalidated
    },
  }).then(r =&gt; r.json());
}

async function fetchPost(slug: string) {
  return fetch(`https://cms.example.com/posts/${slug}`, {
    next: {
      tags: [`post:${slug}`, 'posts'],  // per-item tag for surgical invalidation
      revalidate: 86400,
    },
  }).then(r =&gt; r.json());
}

// Route-level revalidation
// app/blog/page.tsx
export const revalidate = 3600; // time-based ISR for the entire route

// app/blog/[slug]/page.tsx
export const revalidate = 86400; // each post page ISR daily</pre>
      <pre class="code-block">// On-demand tag revalidation — called from CMS webhook
// app/api/webhook/cms/route.ts
import { revalidateTag, revalidatePath } from 'next/cache';
import { NextRequest, NextResponse } from 'next/server';

export async function POST(request: NextRequest) {
  const secret = request.headers.get('x-webhook-secret');
  if (secret !== process.env.WEBHOOK_SECRET) {
    return NextResponse.json({ error: 'Unauthorized' }, { status: 401 });
  }

  const { event, data } = await request.json();

  switch (event) {
    case 'post.published':
    case 'post.updated':
      revalidateTag(`post:${data.slug}`); // invalidate specific post
      revalidateTag('posts');              // invalidate post listings
      revalidatePath('/blog');            // invalidate Full Route Cache for /blog
      revalidatePath(`/blog/${data.slug}`, 'page');
      break;

    case 'post.deleted':
      revalidateTag('posts');
      revalidatePath('/blog');
      break;
  }

  return NextResponse.json({ revalidated: true, timestamp: Date.now() });
}</pre>
      <p>The combination of tag-based and time-based revalidation gives you a robust content freshness strategy: tag-based for immediate updates triggered by known events, and time-based as a safety net that ensures even untagged stale content eventually gets refreshed. For a high-traffic site, tag-based revalidation is dramatically more efficient than short TTL time-based ISR because it avoids unnecessary regeneration when data hasn't changed.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ACCORDION ITEM 30 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-nextjs-q30">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#nextjs-q30" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="nextjs-q30">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;30. How do you deploy a Next.js application?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="nextjs-q30" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-nextjs-q30" data-bs-parent="#acc-nextjs">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Next.js applications can be deployed in three modes: <strong>Node.js server</strong> (the default, supports all features), <strong>static export</strong> (generates pure HTML/CSS/JS files, no server required), and <strong>Edge Runtime</strong> (for specific routes/middleware). The choice of deployment mode and platform determines which Next.js features are available — some features like ISR, image optimization, and middleware require a Node.js (or compatible) runtime and cannot be done with static export.</p>
      <p><strong>Vercel</strong> is the zero-configuration deployment platform built by Next.js creators. A <code>git push</code> to the linked repository automatically builds and deploys the application, with automatic ISR support, global edge CDN for static assets, serverless functions for dynamic routes and API routes, image optimization CDN, and preview deployments for every pull request. For teams already using GitHub/GitLab, Vercel is the fastest path to production.</p>
      <p><strong>Self-hosted Node.js</strong> is used when you need to run on your own infrastructure (AWS, GCP, Azure, on-prem). Run <code>next build</code> to create the optimized production build in <code>.next/</code>, then <code>node server.js</code> or <code>next start</code> to run the server. For containerized deployments, Next.js has a standalone output mode (<code>output: 'standalone'</code> in <code>next.config.ts</code>) that produces a minimal self-contained build folder (~5-10MB) suitable for Docker without <code>node_modules</code>.</p>
      <pre class="code-block"># Vercel deployment (zero config after initial setup)
npm i -g vercel
vercel          # deploy to preview
vercel --prod   # deploy to production

# Or connect GitHub repo at vercel.com for automatic deployments</pre>
      <pre class="code-block"># Self-hosted Node.js
npm run build   # creates .next/ directory
npm start       # starts Next.js production server on port 3000

# With PM2 for process management
pm2 start npm --name "nextjs-app" -- start
pm2 startup && pm2 save</pre>
      <pre class="code-block"># Docker deployment with standalone output
# next.config.ts
const config: NextConfig = { output: 'standalone' };

# Dockerfile
FROM node:22-alpine AS builder
WORKDIR /app
COPY package*.json ./
RUN npm ci
COPY . .
RUN npm run build

FROM node:22-alpine AS runner
WORKDIR /app
ENV NODE_ENV=production PORT=3000

# Standalone output copies only what's needed
COPY --from=builder /app/.next/standalone ./
COPY --from=builder /app/.next/static ./.next/static
COPY --from=builder /app/public ./public

EXPOSE 3000
CMD ["node", "server.js"]</pre>
      <pre class="code-block"># Static export (no SSR/ISR/API routes)
# next.config.ts
const config: NextConfig = { output: 'export' };

npm run build  # generates /out directory of static HTML files
# Deploy /out to any static host: S3, Cloudflare Pages, GitHub Pages, Netlify
aws s3 sync out/ s3://my-bucket --delete
aws cloudfront create-invalidation --distribution-id XXXXX --paths "/*"</pre>
      <p>For enterprise deployments, consider <strong>OpenNext</strong>, an open-source adapter that packages Next.js for deployment on AWS Lambda@Edge, Cloudflare Workers, and other serverless platforms without Vercel. It handles the complex parts of running the Next.js server on non-Node runtimes. Regardless of deployment platform, always set appropriate environment variables, configure error monitoring (Sentry), set up health check endpoints (<code>/api/health</code>), and implement proper cache headers for static assets to maximize CDN efficiency.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

  </div>
</div>

<div class="tab-pane fade" id="tab-advanced" role="tabpanel">
  <div class="accordion" id="acc-advanced">
<!-- ==================== Q1 ==================== -->
<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q1">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#advanced-q1-collapse" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="advanced-q1-collapse">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;1. What is Suspense in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="advanced-q1-collapse" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong>React Suspense</strong> is a built-in mechanism that lets you declaratively specify a loading state for a part of your component tree while that part is waiting for something asynchronous to complete — most commonly lazy-loaded code, but increasingly also data. Without Suspense you would manage loading flags manually in every component; Suspense moves that concern to a single, strategically placed boundary in the tree.
      </p>
      <p>
        Suspense works by catching a special <em>thrown Promise</em>. When a child component needs to wait (e.g., it is lazy-loading a chunk, or — with a Suspense-compatible data source — waiting for data), it throws a Promise. React walks up the tree looking for the nearest <code>&lt;Suspense&gt;</code> boundary and renders its <code>fallback</code> prop instead of the suspended subtree. When the Promise resolves, React re-renders the subtree with the ready content.
      </p>
      <p>
        The <code>fallback</code> prop accepts any renderable React node — a spinner, a skeleton screen, or null. Multiple Suspense boundaries can be nested; the closest ancestor catches the suspension, so you can create fine-grained loading experiences without an explosion of local state variables.
      </p>
      <p>
        React 18 greatly expanded Suspense: it now integrates with concurrent rendering, startTransition, and streaming SSR. In streaming SSR, a suspended boundary causes React to flush the rest of the HTML immediately and stream the suspended section's HTML as a separate chunk once it resolves, dramatically improving Time to First Byte.
      </p>
      <p>
        One important mental model: Suspense is <em>not</em> a data-fetching library. It is a coordination mechanism. The actual fetching is handled by a library (Relay, SWR, React Query with its <code>suspense</code> option, or the new <code>use()</code> hook) that knows how to throw a Promise at the right moment. Suspense simply provides the boundary where that thrown Promise is caught.
      </p>
      <pre class="code-block">
import React, { Suspense, lazy } from 'react';

// React.lazy creates a lazy component that suspends until the chunk loads
const HeavyChart = lazy(() => import('./HeavyChart'));

function Dashboard() {
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;h1&gt;Dashboard&lt;/h1&gt;

      {/* Closest Suspense catches the suspension from HeavyChart */}
      &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;div className="skeleton-chart" /&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;HeavyChart /&gt;
      &lt;/Suspense&gt;

      {/* Nested boundaries give independent loading states */}
      &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;p&gt;Loading sidebar…&lt;/p&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;p&gt;Loading widget…&lt;/p&gt;}&gt;
          &lt;SidebarWidget /&gt;
        &lt;/Suspense&gt;
        &lt;SidebarStats /&gt;
      &lt;/Suspense&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}
      </pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ==================== Q2 ==================== -->
<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q2">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#advanced-q2-collapse" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="advanced-q2-collapse">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;2. What is an error boundary?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="advanced-q2-collapse" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        An <strong>error boundary</strong> is a React class component that catches JavaScript errors anywhere in its child component tree, logs those errors, and displays a fallback UI instead of letting the whole application crash with a blank white screen. Error boundaries were introduced in React 16 as the recommended way to handle rendering errors gracefully in production applications.
      </p>
      <p>
        Error boundaries rely on two lifecycle methods available only on class components: <code>static getDerivedStateFromError(error)</code> and <code>componentDidCatch(error, info)</code>. <code>getDerivedStateFromError</code> is called during the render phase and should return a state object that triggers the fallback UI (e.g., <code>{ hasError: true }</code>). <code>componentDidCatch</code> is called during the commit phase and is the right place to log the error to an error-reporting service like Sentry, Datadog, or a custom endpoint.
      </p>
      <p>
        Error boundaries do <em>not</em> catch errors inside event handlers (because those do not happen during render), asynchronous code (setTimeout, fetch callbacks), server-side rendering, or errors thrown inside the error boundary itself. For async errors you still need try/catch or a global <code>window.onerror</code> handler.
      </p>
      <p>
        You can — and typically should — use multiple error boundaries at different granularities. Wrapping individual widgets means that one broken component will not take down an entire page. Placing one at the application root provides a last-resort safety net with a friendly "Something went wrong" message.
      </p>
      <p>
        As of React 19 (experimental), work is in progress to support error boundaries as function components via a new <code>use client</code> / <code>useErrorBoundary</code>-style API. Until then, the community library <code>react-error-boundary</code> provides a functional-friendly wrapper over the class-based API.
      </p>
      <pre class="code-block">
import React from 'react';

class ErrorBoundary extends React.Component {
  constructor(props) {
    super(props);
    this.state = { hasError: false, error: null };
  }

  static getDerivedStateFromError(error) {
    // Called during render; return new state to show fallback UI
    return { hasError: true, error };
  }

  componentDidCatch(error, info) {
    // Called after commit; good place for logging
    console.error('Caught by ErrorBoundary:', error);
    console.error('Component stack:', info.componentStack);
    // reportToSentry(error, info);
  }

  render() {
    if (this.state.hasError) {
      return (
        &lt;div role="alert"&gt;
          &lt;h2&gt;Something went wrong.&lt;/h2&gt;
          &lt;details&gt;{this.state.error?.message}&lt;/details&gt;
          &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; this.setState({ hasError: false, error: null })}&gt;
            Try again
          &lt;/button&gt;
        &lt;/div&gt;
      );
    }
    return this.props.children;
  }
}

// Usage
function App() {
  return (
    &lt;ErrorBoundary&gt;
      &lt;UserProfile /&gt;
    &lt;/ErrorBoundary&gt;
  );
}

// Using react-error-boundary (functional API)
import { ErrorBoundary } from 'react-error-boundary';

function FallbackComponent({ error, resetErrorBoundary }) {
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;Error: {error.message}&lt;/p&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={resetErrorBoundary}&gt;Retry&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

function App() {
  return (
    &lt;ErrorBoundary FallbackComponent={FallbackComponent} onReset={() =&gt; { /* clear cache */ }}&gt;
      &lt;UserProfile /&gt;
    &lt;/ErrorBoundary&gt;
  );
}
      </pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ==================== Q3 ==================== -->
<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q3">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#advanced-q3-collapse" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="advanced-q3-collapse">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;3. What is a React Portal?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="advanced-q3-collapse" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        A <strong>React Portal</strong> lets you render a child component into a different DOM node than its parent component occupies in the React tree, while still maintaining the component's position in the React logical tree. In other words, a Portal "teleports" DOM output to any node you choose, but React event bubbling and context still behave exactly as if the portal's content were rendered at its logical position.
      </p>
      <p>
        Portals are created with <code>ReactDOM.createPortal(children, domNode)</code>. The first argument is any renderable React content; the second is the real DOM node to mount into. You typically target a node like <code>document.getElementById('modal-root')</code> that lives outside the main <code>#root</code> div.
      </p>
      <p>
        The canonical use case is <strong>modals, tooltips, and dropdown menus</strong>. These elements need to visually break out of any <code>overflow: hidden</code> or <code>z-index</code> stacking context imposed by ancestor DOM nodes, but they should still receive props and context from their logical React parents. Without portals, you would have to hoist the modal to the root of the DOM tree and pass state through props or global state, causing tight coupling.
      </p>
      <p>
        Because events bubble through the React tree (not the DOM tree), a click inside a portal will still trigger <code>onClick</code> handlers on React ancestors of the portal component. This can occasionally cause surprises and requires careful thinking about event propagation when building dismissal logic (e.g., "click outside to close" detection).
      </p>
      <p>
        React 18 introduced a new <code>createRoot</code>-compatible portal API, but the semantics remain the same. Always ensure the target DOM node exists before calling <code>createPortal</code> — a common pattern is to create and append the node in a <code>useEffect</code> or simply add a static div to your <code>index.html</code>.
      </p>
      <pre class="code-block">
// index.html — add a portal target outside #root
// &lt;div id="modal-root"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;

import { createPortal } from 'react-dom';
import { useEffect, useRef } from 'react';

function Modal({ children, onClose }) {
  const el = useRef(document.getElementById('modal-root'));

  return createPortal(
    &lt;div className="modal-overlay" onClick={onClose}&gt;
      &lt;div className="modal-content" onClick={e =&gt; e.stopPropagation()}&gt;
        {children}
      &lt;/div&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;,
    el.current
  );
}

function Page() {
  const [open, setOpen] = React.useState(false);
  return (
    &lt;&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; setOpen(true)}&gt;Open Modal&lt;/button&gt;
      {open &amp;&amp; (
        &lt;Modal onClose={() =&gt; setOpen(false)}&gt;
          &lt;h2&gt;Hello from a portal!&lt;/h2&gt;
        &lt;/Modal&gt;
      )}
    &lt;/&gt;
  );
}
      </pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ==================== Q4 ==================== -->
<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q4">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#advanced-q4-collapse" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="advanced-q4-collapse">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;4. What is forwardRef?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="advanced-q4-collapse" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong><code>React.forwardRef</code></strong> is an API that allows a component to accept a <code>ref</code> passed by its parent and forward it down to a DOM element or to another component inside it. By default, the <code>ref</code> prop is reserved by React and does not flow into function components the way regular props do — <code>forwardRef</code> is the escape hatch that enables this pattern.
      </p>
      <p>
        The API wraps your component in a higher-order function: <code>React.forwardRef((props, ref) =&gt; JSX)</code>. The second argument <code>ref</code> can then be attached to any JSX element via <code>ref={ref}</code>. The caller can now pass a ref created with <code>useRef</code> or <code>createRef</code> and directly access the underlying DOM node or child component's imperative handle.
      </p>
      <p>
        Common use cases include design system components (e.g., a custom <code>&lt;Input&gt;</code> that wraps an <code>&lt;input&gt;</code>) where the consumer needs to call <code>inputRef.current.focus()</code>, animation libraries that need direct DOM manipulation, and third-party integration (e.g., passing a ref to a chart's canvas element). Without <code>forwardRef</code>, every consumer would have to reach inside a component unsafely or use a workaround like passing a separate <code>innerRef</code> prop.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>React 19 note:</strong> In React 19, <code>ref</code> is passed as a regular prop to function components, making <code>forwardRef</code> unnecessary for new code. However, <code>forwardRef</code> remains supported for backward compatibility. For React 16–18 you still need it.
      </p>
      <p>
        When combined with <code>useImperativeHandle</code>, <code>forwardRef</code> becomes even more powerful: instead of exposing the raw DOM node, you expose a custom object of imperative methods, giving consumers a controlled API surface without leaking DOM internals.
      </p>
      <pre class="code-block">
import React, { forwardRef, useRef } from 'react';

// A reusable Input that forwards the ref to the underlying &lt;input&gt;
const FancyInput = forwardRef(function FancyInput({ label, ...props }, ref) {
  return (
    &lt;label&gt;
      {label}
      &lt;input ref={ref} className="fancy-input" {...props} /&gt;
    &lt;/label&gt;
  );
});

// Consumer
function Form() {
  const inputRef = useRef(null);

  function handleFocus() {
    inputRef.current?.focus();
  }

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;FancyInput ref={inputRef} label="Username" placeholder="Enter username" /&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={handleFocus}&gt;Focus Input&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// React 19 style (ref as a plain prop — no forwardRef needed)
function FancyInputV2({ label, ref, ...props }) {
  return (
    &lt;label&gt;
      {label}
      &lt;input ref={ref} className="fancy-input" {...props} /&gt;
    &lt;/label&gt;
  );
}
      </pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ==================== Q5 ==================== -->
<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q5">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#advanced-q5-collapse" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="advanced-q5-collapse">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;5. What is React.lazy?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="advanced-q5-collapse" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong><code>React.lazy</code></strong> is a built-in function that enables code splitting by letting you defer loading a component's JavaScript bundle until it is actually rendered for the first time. It integrates directly with Suspense: while the chunk is being downloaded, the nearest Suspense boundary shows its fallback, and once the import resolves the component renders normally.
      </p>
      <p>
        The syntax is <code>const MyComponent = lazy(() =&gt; import('./MyComponent'))</code>. The argument must be a function that returns a Promise which resolves to a module whose <strong>default export</strong> is a React component. Under the hood, React.lazy stores the Promise, and when the component is first rendered it checks whether the Promise has resolved; if not, it throws the Promise for the Suspense boundary to catch.
      </p>
      <p>
        Code splitting with <code>React.lazy</code> is one of the most impactful performance optimizations for large SPAs. Rather than shipping a monolithic bundle, the bundler (webpack, Vite, Rollup) creates separate chunks for lazily imported modules. Users only download the code they actually visit, reducing initial bundle size and improving Time to Interactive significantly.
      </p>
      <p>
        A common strategy is <strong>route-based code splitting</strong>: each route's top-level component is wrapped in <code>lazy()</code> so the code for each page is loaded on demand. Another common pattern is <strong>component-level splitting</strong> for heavy components like rich text editors, chart libraries, or PDF viewers that are not needed on initial load.
      </p>
      <p>
        For named exports (non-default exports), you need a thin re-export shim: create a file that re-exports the named export as the default, or use <code>lazy(() =&gt; import('./module').then(m =&gt; ({ default: m.NamedExport })))</code>. The React team may relax this requirement in a future version.
      </p>
      <pre class="code-block">
import { lazy, Suspense } from 'react';
import { Routes, Route } from 'react-router-dom';

// Route-based code splitting
const Home      = lazy(() =&gt; import('./pages/Home'));
const Dashboard = lazy(() =&gt; import('./pages/Dashboard'));
const Settings  = lazy(() =&gt; import('./pages/Settings'));

// Named export workaround
const PdfViewer = lazy(() =&gt;
  import('./components/PdfViewer').then(m =&gt; ({ default: m.PdfViewer }))
);

function App() {
  return (
    &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;div className="page-loader"&gt;Loading…&lt;/div&gt;}&gt;
      &lt;Routes&gt;
        &lt;Route path="/"          element={&lt;Home /&gt;}      /&gt;
        &lt;Route path="/dashboard" element={&lt;Dashboard /&gt;} /&gt;
        &lt;Route path="/settings"  element={&lt;Settings /&gt;}  /&gt;
      &lt;/Routes&gt;
    &lt;/Suspense&gt;
  );
}

// Preloading a lazy component before user navigates (performance trick)
const AdminPanel = lazy(() =&gt; import('./AdminPanel'));

function NavBar() {
  // Trigger the import on hover so the chunk is ready before click
  function preload() {
    import('./AdminPanel');
  }
  return &lt;a href="/admin" onMouseEnter={preload}&gt;Admin&lt;/a&gt;;
}
      </pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- ==================== Q6 ==================== -->
<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q6">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#advanced-q6-collapse" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="advanced-q6-collapse">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;6. What is useImperativeHandle?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="advanced-q6-collapse" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>
        <strong><code>useImperativeHandle</code></strong> is a React hook used inside a <code>forwardRef</code> component to customise the value exposed on the ref that the parent component holds. Instead of exposing the raw underlying DOM node or child component instance, you define a plain object with exactly the imperative methods and properties you want consumers to be able to call. This creates a clean, stable public API surface for imperative interactions.
      </p>
      <p>
        The signature is <code>useImperativeHandle(ref, () =&gt; ({ method1, method2, ... }), [deps])</code>. The second argument is a factory function that returns the object to assign to <code>ref.current</code>. The optional dependencies array works just like <code>useMemo</code>: the factory re-runs when any dependency changes, keeping the ref current object up to date.
      </p>
      <p>
        This pattern is particularly valuable for design system components. Consider a custom <code>&lt;Dialog&gt;</code> component: rather than exposing the underlying <code>&lt;div&gt;</code> and letting callers manipulate it freely, you expose only <code>{ open, close, focus }</code> methods. This enforces encapsulation and makes refactoring much safer because the internal implementation can change without breaking consumers.
      </p>
      <p>
        A real-world subtlety: the deps array is often omitted, which means the factory runs on every render. This is safe because the factory is cheap and React only replaces <code>ref.current</code> if the factory returns a new object by reference. Providing an empty array <code>[]</code> memoises the object permanently — useful when the methods close over refs (like internal DOM refs) rather than reactive state, ensuring the methods always see the latest ref values.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Anti-pattern warning:</strong> Overusing imperative handles is a red flag. In React's declarative model, most interactions should flow through props and state. Reach for <code>useImperativeHandle</code> only when you genuinely need an imperative integration — focus management, scroll control, media playback, or integration with non-React code — not as a shortcut to avoid prop drilling.
      </p>
      <pre class="code-block">
import { forwardRef, useRef, useImperativeHandle } from 'react';

// Expose a controlled imperative API on a custom input
const SmartInput = forwardRef(function SmartInput({ label }, ref) {
  const inputRef = useRef(null);

  useImperativeHandle(ref, () =&gt; ({
    focus() {
      inputRef.current?.focus();
    },
    clear() {
      if (inputRef.current) inputRef.current.value = '';
    },
    getValue() {
