    () =&gt; selector(reduxStore.getState()),
    () =&gt; selector(serverState)
  );
}

// The useSyncExternalStore contract:
// - All components in one render pass use the SAME store snapshot
// - If the snapshot changes mid-render, React re-renders synchronously
// - This prevents parent/child seeing different versions of the store
// - Zombie children are eliminated because consistent snapshots
//   guarantee parent and child agree on which items exist

// Additional defensive coding:
function SafeItem({ id }) {
  const item = useSelector(state =&gt; state.items[id]);
  // Always guard against item being undefined/null
  if (!item) return null;
  return &lt;div&gt;{item.name}&lt;/div&gt;;
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q44">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#collapse-advanced-q44" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="collapse-advanced-q44">
      Q44. How do React DevTools work under the hood? <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="collapse-advanced-q44" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>React DevTools is a browser extension and standalone tool that provides a visual tree inspector, profiler, and component state/props editor for React applications. Under the hood, it relies on a dedicated communication channel that React itself exposes: a global object on the <code>window</code> called <code>__REACT_DEVTOOLS_GLOBAL_HOOK__</code>. React's renderer code checks for this hook at startup and, if present, calls hook methods to report every fiber tree mutation, render, commit, and effect.</p>
      <p>The global hook is injected by the DevTools extension before the page's JavaScript executes (via a content script). React's reconciler calls methods like <code>onCommitFiberRoot</code> after each commit, passing a reference to the root Fiber node. The DevTools then walk the fiber tree to extract component names, props, state, hooks, context values, and performance timing — building its own internal representation of the component tree that it displays in the "Components" panel.</p>
      <p>For the Profiler, React reports timing data for each fiber render: the base duration (time to render the component tree without memoization), actual duration (real render time with memoization applied), and the commit time. The DevTools store this data and render flame charts and ranked charts. The "Why did this render?" feature uses the same fiber data — React stores the "alternate" fiber (the previous render's fiber) alongside each current fiber, allowing the DevTools to diff props and state between renders to identify the cause of a re-render.</p>
      <p>Component editing (changing state/props from DevTools) works by calling internal React fiber APIs that React exposes specifically for DevTools through the hook interface. These are deliberately separate from the public React API and not intended for production use. Hot-reloading integration also uses this hook system, with Fast Refresh calling into the hook to signal component updates without full tree remounts.</p>
<pre class="code-block">
// React checks for DevTools hook at initialization
// Inside React's source (react-dom/src/client/ReactDOMRoot.js):
if (typeof __REACT_DEVTOOLS_GLOBAL_HOOK__ !== 'undefined') {
  __REACT_DEVTOOLS_GLOBAL_HOOK__.inject(internals);
}

// After each commit, React calls:
// __REACT_DEVTOOLS_GLOBAL_HOOK__.onCommitFiberRoot(rendererID, root, ...)

// The hook interface (simplified):
window.__REACT_DEVTOOLS_GLOBAL_HOOK__ = {
  renderers: new Map(),

  inject(renderer) {
    const id = nextRendererID++;
    this.renderers.set(id, renderer);
    return id;
  },

  onCommitFiberRoot(rendererID, root, priorityLevel) {
    // DevTools receives the entire fiber tree here
    const renderer = this.renderers.get(rendererID);
    // Walk the fiber tree to update the DevTools component tree
    walkFibers(root.current);
  },

  onPostCommitFiberRoot(rendererID, root) {
    // Called after passive effects run
  }
};

// Fiber tree walking (how DevTools reads component data):
function walkFibers(fiber) {
  if (!fiber) return;

  const info = {
    name: getDisplayName(fiber),    // Component name
    type: fiber.type,               // Function/class/HTML tag
    props: fiber.memoizedProps,     // Current props
    state: fiber.memoizedState,     // Linked list of hook states
    effects: fiber.flags,           // Pending effect flags
    actualDuration: fiber.actualDuration, // Profiling data
  };

  // DevTools builds its own tree representation
  devtoolsTree.set(fiber, info);

  // Recurse through children and siblings
  walkFibers(fiber.child);
  walkFibers(fiber.sibling);
}

// DevTools component editing — uses renderer internals:
// renderer.overrideHookState(fiber, hookIndex, path, value)
// renderer.overrideProps(fiber, path, value)
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q45">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#collapse-advanced-q45" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="collapse-advanced-q45">
      Q45. What is the difference between React's internal state and external state for concurrent safety? <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="collapse-advanced-q45" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>In React 18's concurrent model, "internal state" refers to state managed by React itself via <code>useState</code>, <code>useReducer</code>, and component state — stored inside React's fiber data structure. "External state" refers to state managed outside React: Redux stores, Zustand atoms, MobX observables, browser APIs (localStorage, URL), or any plain JavaScript variables. This distinction is critical for concurrent safety because React's internal state and external state have different guarantees during interrupted renders.</p>
      <p>React's internal state is <strong>automatically concurrent-safe</strong>. React ensures that during a render pass, all reads to internal state are consistent — if a render is interrupted and restarted, it reads the same state snapshot for the entire restart. React batches updates and applies them atomically, so you can never observe a partial state update in a render. The fiber architecture stores both the "current" and "work-in-progress" versions of state, and React manages the transition between them atomically during commit.</p>
      <p>External state is <strong>not automatically concurrent-safe</strong>. If external state updates while React has a paused concurrent render in progress, React has no way to automatically detect this or restart the render. Components may read the store at different points in time during a single interrupted render pass, seeing different values — causing tearing. The solution is <code>useSyncExternalStore</code>, which gives React a way to detect external state changes mid-render and force a synchronous re-render. Any external state management library targeting React 18 must use <code>useSyncExternalStore</code> (or the equivalent subscription model) to guarantee concurrent safety.</p>
<pre class="code-block">
// INTERNAL STATE: automatically concurrent-safe
function InternalStateComponent() {
  const [count, setCount] = useState(0); // Stored in React fiber

  // React guarantees: count is ALWAYS consistent
  // across all renders in a single render pass
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      {count}
      &lt;Child count={count} /&gt; {/* Always same count value */}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// EXTERNAL STATE: NOT concurrent-safe without useSyncExternalStore
let externalCounter = 0; // Plain JS variable
function setExternalCounter(val) { externalCounter = val; }

function UnsafeComponent() {
  // Reading external state directly — TEARING RISK!
  // If externalCounter updates between parent and child renders:
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      {externalCounter} {/* Might show 5 */}
      &lt;Child /&gt; {/* Child might see 6 if interrupted */}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// EXTERNAL STATE: safe with useSyncExternalStore
const externalStore = {
  _value: 0,
  _listeners: new Set(),
  get() { return this._value; },
  set(v) { this._value = v; this._listeners.forEach(l =&gt; l()); },
  subscribe(l) { this._listeners.add(l); return () =&gt; this._listeners.delete(l); }
};

function SafeComponent() {
  const value = useSyncExternalStore(
    externalStore.subscribe.bind(externalStore),
    externalStore.get.bind(externalStore),
    () =&gt; 0 // Server snapshot
  );

  // React guarantees: all components in this render pass
  // see the same 'value' snapshot
  return &lt;div&gt;{value}&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// Summary of concurrent safety:
// useState/useReducer:   SAFE — React manages snapshots
// useRef:                MUTABLE — reads are not snapshotted, use with care
// Context:               SAFE — built on React's state system
// Redux (pre-v8):        UNSAFE — use react-redux v8+ which uses useSyncExternalStore
// Zustand:               SAFE — uses useSyncExternalStore internally
// Plain JS variables:    UNSAFE — no snapshot guarantees
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q46">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#collapse-advanced-q46" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="collapse-advanced-q46">
      Q46. How do you implement an accessible modal with portals? <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="collapse-advanced-q46" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>An accessible modal requires both React portals (for correct DOM placement) and a suite of ARIA attributes and keyboard behaviors (for screen reader and keyboard user support). Using <code>ReactDOM.createPortal(children, domNode)</code> renders the modal's DOM node as a child of <code>document.body</code> (or another container outside the app root), which prevents CSS overflow clipping and z-index stacking context issues. Despite the portal being physically outside the React component tree in the DOM, React event bubbling and context still work as if the portal were in its logical parent — clicks inside the portal bubble up through React's component tree normally.</p>
      <p>For accessibility, a compliant modal must: set <code>role="dialog"</code> and <code>aria-modal="true"</code> on the modal container, include <code>aria-labelledby</code> pointing to the modal's heading and <code>aria-describedby</code> pointing to its body text, trap focus within the modal (users tabbing must not be able to leave the modal while it's open), return focus to the previously focused element when closed, and respond to the Escape key for closure. Setting <code>aria-hidden="true"</code> on the main application content while the modal is open prevents screen readers from reading the background content.</p>
      <p>Focus trapping is the most complex part: you must find all focusable elements within the modal (buttons, links, inputs, elements with tabindex), intercept Tab and Shift+Tab key events, and wrap focus from the last element back to the first (and vice versa). The focus trap must be set up on open and torn down on close. Using a well-tested library like <code>focus-trap-react</code> or the native browser <code>inert</code> attribute on the background content is preferable to implementing focus trapping manually, since edge cases (iframes, shadow DOM, dynamically added elements) are numerous.</p>
<pre class="code-block">
import { createPortal } from 'react-dom';
import { useEffect, useRef, useCallback } from 'react';

function Modal({ isOpen, onClose, title, children }) {
  const modalRef = useRef(null);
  const previousFocusRef = useRef(null);

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (isOpen) {
      // Store currently focused element to restore on close
      previousFocusRef.current = document.activeElement;

      // Focus the modal (or first focusable element)
      modalRef.current?.focus();

      // Hide background from screen readers
      document.getElementById('app-root')?.setAttribute('aria-hidden', 'true');

      return () =&gt; {
        document.getElementById('app-root')?.removeAttribute('aria-hidden');
        previousFocusRef.current?.focus(); // Restore focus on close
      };
    }
  }, [isOpen]);

  const handleKeyDown = useCallback((e) =&gt; {
    if (e.key === 'Escape') {
      onClose();
      return;
    }

    // Focus trap: Tab / Shift+Tab
    if (e.key === 'Tab') {
      const focusable = modalRef.current?.querySelectorAll(
        'button, [href], input, select, textarea, [tabindex]:not([tabindex="-1"])'
      );
      const first = focusable[0];
      const last = focusable[focusable.length - 1];

      if (e.shiftKey) {
        if (document.activeElement === first) {
          e.preventDefault();
          last?.focus();
        }
      } else {
        if (document.activeElement === last) {
          e.preventDefault();
          first?.focus();
        }
      }
    }
  }, [onClose]);

  if (!isOpen) return null;

  return createPortal(
    &lt;div
      role="presentation"
      style={{ position: 'fixed', inset: 0, background: 'rgba(0,0,0,0.5)', zIndex: 1000 }}
      onClick={(e) =&gt; { if (e.target === e.currentTarget) onClose(); }}
    &gt;
      &lt;div
        role="dialog"
        aria-modal="true"
        aria-labelledby="modal-title"
        aria-describedby="modal-desc"
        ref={modalRef}
        tabIndex={-1}
        onKeyDown={handleKeyDown}
        style={{ background: 'white', padding: '24px', borderRadius: '8px',
                 maxWidth: '500px', margin: '10vh auto', outline: 'none' }}
      &gt;
        &lt;h2 id="modal-title"&gt;{title}&lt;/h2&gt;
        &lt;div id="modal-desc"&gt;{children}&lt;/div&gt;
        &lt;button onClick={onClose} aria-label="Close dialog"&gt;Close&lt;/button&gt;
      &lt;/div&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;,
    document.body
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q47">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#collapse-advanced-q47" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="collapse-advanced-q47">
      Q47. What is the act() boundary in React tests and concurrent mode? <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="collapse-advanced-q47" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><code>act()</code> is a test utility that ensures all pending React state updates, effects, and re-renders are processed and flushed before your test's assertions run. Without <code>act()</code>, tests can make assertions against a stale DOM that hasn't yet reflected the latest state updates — a particularly common problem with async operations, <code>useEffect</code>, and <code>useLayoutEffect</code>. The name "act" comes from the testing convention of "Arrange, Act, Assert" — <code>act()</code> wraps the "Act" phase to ensure React's work is complete before "Assert".</p>
      <p>In React 18's concurrent mode, <code>act()</code> became async-aware. The async form <code>await act(async () =&gt; { ... })</code> is required when your test involves async operations (like <code>useEffect</code> with a fetch, or <code>startTransition</code>) because concurrent React schedules work asynchronously. When you wrap an interaction in <code>act()</code>, React processes all pending synchronous updates, flushes the microtask queue, runs all scheduled effects, and then processes any state updates triggered by those effects — recursively, until no more work is pending.</p>
      <p>Testing Library (React Testing Library) wraps all its user interaction utilities (<code>userEvent.click</code>, <code>fireEvent</code>, <code>render</code>) in <code>act()</code> automatically, which is one reason it's the recommended testing approach. If you see the warning "An update to X inside a test was not wrapped in act()", it means an async state update (typically from a useEffect or an unresolved Promise) completed after your test assertions ran. The fix is to <code>await act(async () =&gt; {})</code> or use <code>await waitFor(() =&gt; expect(...).toBe(...))</code> from Testing Library.</p>
<pre class="code-block">
import { act, render, screen } from '@testing-library/react';
import userEvent from '@testing-library/user-event';

// Basic synchronous act (implicit via Testing Library)
test('counter increments on click', async () =&gt; {
  const user = userEvent.setup();
  render(&lt;Counter /&gt;);

  // userEvent wraps in act() automatically
  await user.click(screen.getByRole('button', { name: 'Increment' }));

  expect(screen.getByText('Count: 1')).toBeInTheDocument();
});

// Explicit act() for manual state updates
test('manual act usage', async () =&gt; {
  let setCount;
  function TestComponent() {
    const [count, sc] = useState(0);
    setCount = sc;
    return &lt;div&gt;{count}&lt;/div&gt;;
  }

  render(&lt;TestComponent /&gt;);

  // Wrap state update in act() to ensure re-render completes
  act(() =&gt; {
    setCount(5);
  });

  expect(screen.getByText('5')).toBeInTheDocument();
});

// Async act() for effects and async operations
test('data loads correctly', async () =&gt; {
  global.fetch = jest.fn().mockResolvedValue({
    json: () =&gt; Promise.resolve({ name: 'Alice' })
  });

  render(&lt;UserProfile userId="1" /&gt;);

  expect(screen.getByText('Loading...')).toBeInTheDocument();

  // Wait for async effects to complete
  await act(async () =&gt; {
    await Promise.resolve(); // Flush microtasks
  });

  expect(screen.getByText('Alice')).toBeInTheDocument();
});

// React Testing Library's waitFor handles act() internally
test('with waitFor', async () =&gt; {
  render(&lt;AsyncComponent /&gt;);

  // waitFor retries the assertion, wrapping each retry in act()
  await waitFor(() =&gt; {
    expect(screen.getByText('Loaded!')).toBeInTheDocument();
  });
});

// Concurrent mode: act() with startTransition
test('transition updates', async () =&gt; {
  render(&lt;SearchComponent /&gt;);
  const input = screen.getByRole('textbox');

  await act(async () =&gt; {
    await userEvent.type(input, 'react');
  });

  // Transitions are flushed inside act()
  await waitFor(() =&gt; {
    expect(screen.getByText('Results for: react')).toBeInTheDocument();
  });
});
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q48">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#collapse-advanced-q48" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="collapse-advanced-q48">
      Q48. How does React implement synthetic events and the event propagation system? <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="collapse-advanced-q48" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>React implements a synthetic event system that normalizes browser event APIs across different browsers and integrates with React's rendering model. In React 17+, React attaches all event listeners to the <strong>root container</strong> (the element passed to <code>ReactDOM.createRoot()</code>) rather than to the document or individual DOM nodes. This is a significant change from React 16, which attached listeners to <code>document</code> — the change was made to better support micro-frontends and multiple React roots on the same page.</p>
      <p>When a user event occurs (e.g., a click), the native browser event bubbles up to the React root container, where React's single delegated event listener catches it. React then walks the fiber tree from the event's target back up to the root, collecting all React event handlers (<code>onClick</code>, <code>onCapture</code>, etc.) along the way. React creates a <strong>SyntheticEvent</strong> — a cross-browser wrapper around the native event — and dispatches it through the collected handlers in the correct order (capture phase handlers top-down, bubble phase handlers bottom-up).</p>
      <p>SyntheticEvents were pooled in React 16 and earlier (reused for performance by nullifying properties after each event handler), which caused issues with async event handlers that tried to access event properties after the synchronous dispatch. React 17 removed event pooling entirely — SyntheticEvents are now regular objects that get garbage collected normally. However, the <code>nativeEvent</code> property still gives you access to the underlying DOM event if you need browser-specific properties that React's normalization doesn't expose.</p>
      <p>React handles some events specially: <code>onChange</code> on inputs fires on every keystroke (not just on blur like native <code>change</code>), events like <code>onFocus</code>/<code>onBlur</code> use the capture phase for delegation (because focus doesn't bubble natively), and <code>onScroll</code> does not bubble in React (matching browser behavior). The synthetic event system is why you should call <code>e.stopPropagation()</code> on the React synthetic event rather than the native event — doing it on the native event may not stop React's simulated propagation.</p>
<pre class="code-block">
// React 17+: events delegated to root container, not document
const root = ReactDOM.createRoot(document.getElementById('root'));
// All event listeners are on the 'root' div, not document

function EventExample() {
  const handleClick = (e) =&gt; {
    // e is a SyntheticEvent (normalized, cross-browser)
    console.log(e.type);          // 'click'
    console.log(e.target);        // Native DOM element
    console.log(e.nativeEvent);   // Original native MouseEvent

    // React 17+: event properties DO persist after handler (no pooling)
    setTimeout(() =&gt; {
      console.log(e.type); // Still 'click' — no longer null! (React 17+)
    }, 100);

    // Stop React's simulated propagation
    e.stopPropagation();

    // Prevent browser default
    e.preventDefault();
  };

  return &lt;button onClick={handleClick}&gt;Click me&lt;/button&gt;;
}

// Capture phase events (fire top-down before bubble)
function CaptureExample() {
  return (
    &lt;div onClickCapture={(e) =&gt; console.log('Captured at parent')}&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={(e) =&gt; console.log('Bubbled at child')}&gt;
        Click order: parent capture → child bubble
      &lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// Accessing native event for browser-specific properties
function NativeEventExample() {
  const handleWheel = (e) =&gt; {
    // deltaY is normalized, but deltaMode may need native event
    const native = e.nativeEvent;
    console.log(native.deltaMode); // 0=pixel, 1=line, 2=page
  };
  return &lt;div onWheel={handleWheel}&gt;Scroll here&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// onChange fires on every keystroke (unlike native change = on blur)
function InputExample() {
  const [value, setValue] = useState('');
  return (
    &lt;input
      value={value}
      onChange={(e) =&gt; setValue(e.target.value)} // Every keystroke
    /&gt;
  );
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q49">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#collapse-advanced-q49" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="collapse-advanced-q49">
      Q49. What is "interleaving" in React's rendering and why does it matter? <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="collapse-advanced-q49" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>"Interleaving" in React's concurrent rendering refers to the ability of React's scheduler to interleave the rendering work of multiple updates — pausing one update mid-render to process a higher-priority update, then resuming the original update. This is the fundamental capability that makes concurrent mode "concurrent": rather than rendering updates sequentially to completion, React can work on multiple render trees simultaneously, yielding control between them based on priority.</p>
      <p>In practice, interleaving enables the distinction between "urgent" and "non-urgent" updates. An urgent update (user typing in an input, button click) must be processed immediately to feel responsive. A non-urgent update (filtering a long list based on input, loading new route content in a transition) can be deferred. When an urgent update arrives while a non-urgent render is in progress, React can pause ("interrupt") the non-urgent render, synchronously apply the urgent update, paint to the screen, and then resume the non-urgent render — the user sees immediate feedback without waiting for the expensive operation.</p>
      <p>Interleaving has important implications for correctness. Because a single "render pass" for a component may be split across multiple execution frames, with other renders happening in between, your render functions must be pure (no side effects that depend on execution timing). Any side effect that assumes "my render runs to completion before anything else" will break with interleaving. This is why the React team has always emphasized pure render functions — in legacy mode it was a style guideline, in concurrent mode it becomes a hard requirement for correct behavior.</p>
      <p>The React compiler (introduced as an experimental feature in React 19) analyzes component code statically to identify and memoize only the parts that truly change, reducing the cost of interleaved renders. Understanding interleaving also explains why React calls render functions twice in Strict Mode during development — to help surface impure renders that produce different results on repeated invocation, catching bugs before they manifest as subtle interleaving issues in concurrent mode.</p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Interleaving example: urgent vs deferred updates
function SearchPage() {
  const [inputValue, setInputValue] = useState('');
  const [searchQuery, setSearchQuery] = useState('');
  const [isPending, startTransition] = useTransition();

  const handleChange = (e) =&gt; {
    const value = e.target.value;

    // URGENT: Input value updates immediately (interleaves at high priority)
    setInputValue(value);

    // NON-URGENT: Search query update is deferred (interleaved at low priority)
    startTransition(() =&gt; {
      setSearchQuery(value);
    });
  };

  // React's internal scheduling with interleaving:
  // 1. User types 'r':
  //    - Urgent render starts: setInputValue('r')
  //    - Concurrent render queued: setSearchQuery('r')
  // 2. User types 'e' before concurrent render finishes:
  //    - React INTERRUPTS the searchQuery='r' render
  //    - Urgent render: setInputValue('re')
  //    - Previous concurrent render DISCARDED
  //    - New concurrent render queued: setSearchQuery('re')
  // Result: Input always feels instantaneous

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;input value={inputValue} onChange={handleChange} /&gt;
      {/* Input shows instantly (urgent update) */}

      &lt;div style={{ opacity: isPending ? 0.5 : 1 }}&gt;
        {/* Results update when non-urgent render completes */}
        &lt;SearchResults query={searchQuery} /&gt;
      &lt;/div&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

// PURITY REQUIREMENT: Render functions must be pure for interleaving to work
// BAD: Side effect in render (breaks with interleaving)
let renderCount = 0;
function ImpureComponent() {
  renderCount++; // Side effect in render! React may call this multiple times
  return &lt;div&gt;Renders: {renderCount}&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// GOOD: Pure render, side effects in useEffect
function PureComponent() {
  const [count, setCount] = useState(0);
  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    // Side effects here, in the commit phase
    analytics.track('component_mounted');
  }, []);
  return &lt;div&gt;Count: {count}&lt;/div&gt;;
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item" id="advanced-q50">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#collapse-advanced-q50" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="collapse-advanced-q50">
      Q50. How do you implement a feature detection system for React 18 vs React 19 features? <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="collapse-advanced-q50" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-advanced">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p>Feature detection for React versions is necessary when building libraries, design systems, or applications that need to support multiple React versions simultaneously — such as a component library that targets both React 18 and React 19 consumers. Unlike browser feature detection (where you test the DOM API), React feature detection involves checking the React package version and the availability of specific APIs, hooks, and components that were added in particular versions.</p>
      <p>The most reliable approach is to check the React version string via <code>React.version</code> and parse the semver. However, version checking is fragile against pre-release versions (canary, experimental builds) that may have features from a higher version. A more robust approach is to directly check for the existence of the API: <code>typeof React.use === 'function'</code> for the <code>use()</code> hook, <code>typeof React.Activity !== 'undefined'</code> for the Activity component, <code>typeof ReactDOM.createRoot === 'function'</code> for React 18's createRoot, and so on. This "duck typing" approach is version-agnostic and handles canary builds correctly.</p>
      <p>For hook-based feature detection, you need to be careful — hooks can only be called inside components. Create dedicated compatibility hooks that detect availability at call time and return either the real implementation or a polyfill. A common pattern for libraries is to export compatibility wrappers: <code>useCompat_useActionState</code> which uses <code>useActionState</code> if available and falls back to a <code>useState</code>+<code>useEffect</code> implementation for React 18. The React team also ships the <code>react-dom/client</code> API checker which logs deprecation warnings for legacy render APIs.</p>
      <p>Build-time feature detection using bundler conditions (<code>package.json</code> <code>exports</code> field with peer dependency ranges) is the cleanest long-term approach for library authors: ship different entry points for React 18 vs React 19, and let the package manager resolve the correct version. For application developers, just upgrade to the latest React version — feature detection is primarily a library author concern.</p>
<pre class="code-block">
// Version-based detection
import React from 'react';

const reactVersion = React.version; // e.g., "19.0.0" or "18.3.1"
const [major, minor] = reactVersion.split('.').map(Number);

const REACT_VERSION = { major, minor, isReact18: major === 18, isReact19: major >= 19 };

// Duck-typing API detection (preferred over version strings)
const hasUseHook = typeof React.use === 'function';
const hasActivity = typeof (React as any).Activity !== 'undefined';
const hasViewTransition = typeof (React as any).ViewTransition !== 'undefined';
const hasUseActionState = typeof React.useActionState === 'function'; // React 19
const hasUseFormState = typeof (React as any).useFormState === 'function'; // React 18 canary

// Compatibility hook: useActionState with React 18 fallback
function useCompatActionState(action, initialState) {
  // React 19: use native hook
  if (typeof React.useActionState === 'function') {
    return React.useActionState(action, initialState);
  }

  // React 18 fallback implementation
  const [state, setState] = useState(initialState);
  const [isPending, setIsPending] = useState(false);

  const dispatch = useCallback(async (...args) =&gt; {
    setIsPending(true);
    try {
      const newState = await action(state, ...args);
      setState(newState);
    } finally {
      setIsPending(false);
    }
  }, [action, state]);

  return [state, dispatch, isPending];
}

// Compatibility wrapper for createRoot vs legacy render
function compatRender(element, container) {
  if (typeof ReactDOM.createRoot === 'function') {
    // React 18+: concurrent mode
    const root = ReactDOM.createRoot(container);
    root.render(element);
  } else {
    // React 17 and below (legacy)
    (ReactDOM as any).render(element, container);
  }
}

// Package.json exports for library authors (build-time splitting)
// {
//   "exports": {
//     "./react18": "./dist/react18/index.js",
//     "./react19": "./dist/react19/index.js",
//     ".": {
//       "react-19": "./dist/react19/index.js",
//       "default": "./dist/react18/index.js"
//     }
//   },
//   "peerDependencies": {
//     "react": "^18.0.0 || ^19.0.0"
//   }
// }

// Runtime feature matrix check
function getFeatureSupport() {
  return {
    concurrentMode: typeof ReactDOM.createRoot === 'function',
    useId: typeof React.useId === 'function',           // React 18
    useDeferredValue: typeof React.useDeferredValue === 'function', // React 18
    use: typeof React.use === 'function',                // React 19
    useActionState: typeof React.useActionState === 'function', // React 19
    useOptimistic: typeof React.useOptimistic === 'function',   // React 19
    Activity: typeof (React as any).Activity !== 'undefined',   // React 19
    ViewTransition: typeof (React as any).ViewTransition !== 'undefined', // React 19
  };
}
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q1 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q1-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q1" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q1">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 1. How would you implement infinite scroll in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q1" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q1-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>Overview:</strong> Infinite scroll loads more data as the user approaches the bottom of the list, replacing traditional pagination. The core mechanism is detecting when a sentinel element (or the last item) enters the viewport using the <code>IntersectionObserver</code> API.</p>

      <p><strong>State structure:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li><code>items: T[]</code> — accumulated data</li>
        <li><code>page: number</code> — current page cursor</li>
        <li><code>hasMore: boolean</code> — whether the API has more data</li>
        <li><code>loading: boolean</code> — request in flight</li>
      </ul>

      <p><strong>Component hierarchy:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li><code>InfiniteList</code> — owns state, renders list + sentinel</li>
        <li><code>ListItem</code> — pure display component</li>
        <li><code>useInfiniteScroll</code> — custom hook encapsulating the observer logic</li>
      </ul>

      <p><strong>Key implementation:</strong></p>
<pre class="code-block">// useInfiniteScroll.ts
import { useEffect, useRef, useCallback } from 'react';

export function useInfiniteScroll(
  onLoadMore: () => void,
  hasMore: boolean,
  loading: boolean
) {
  const sentinelRef = useRef&lt;HTMLDivElement | null&gt;(null);

  const handleObserver = useCallback(
    (entries: IntersectionObserverEntry[]) => {
      const [entry] = entries;
      if (entry.isIntersecting && hasMore && !loading) {
        onLoadMore();
      }
    },
    [onLoadMore, hasMore, loading]
  );

  useEffect(() => {
    const el = sentinelRef.current;
    if (!el) return;
    const observer = new IntersectionObserver(handleObserver, {
      root: null,
      rootMargin: '200px', // trigger 200px before bottom
      threshold: 0,
    });
    observer.observe(el);
    return () => observer.disconnect();
  }, [handleObserver]);

  return sentinelRef;
}

// InfiniteList.tsx
export function InfiniteList() {
  const [items, setItems] = useState&lt;Item[]&gt;([]);
  const [page, setPage] = useState(1);
  const [hasMore, setHasMore] = useState(true);
  const [loading, setLoading] = useState(false);

  const loadMore = useCallback(async () => {
    setLoading(true);
    const data = await fetchItems(page);
    setItems(prev => [...prev, ...data.items]);
    setHasMore(data.hasMore);
    setPage(p => p + 1);
    setLoading(false);
  }, [page]);

  const sentinelRef = useInfiniteScroll(loadMore, hasMore, loading);

  useEffect(() => { loadMore(); }, []); // initial load

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      {items.map(item =&gt; &lt;ListItem key={item.id} item={item} /&gt;)}
      {loading &amp;&amp; &lt;Spinner /&gt;}
      &lt;div ref={sentinelRef} style={{ height: 1 }} /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>

      <p><strong>Performance considerations:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li>Virtualise the list with <code>react-window</code> or <code>react-virtual</code> when item count grows large to avoid DOM bloat.</li>
        <li>Debounce or gate <code>loadMore</code> with the <code>loading</code> flag to prevent double-fetches.</li>
        <li>Use <code>useCallback</code> on <code>loadMore</code> so the observer is not recreated on every render.</li>
        <li>For server state, prefer <strong>TanStack Query</strong> (<code>useInfiniteQuery</code>) which handles caching, deduplication, and background refetch automatically.</li>
      </ul>

      <p><strong>Libraries to consider:</strong> <code>react-query</code> / TanStack Query (useInfiniteQuery), <code>react-window</code>, <code>react-intersection-observer</code> (wrapper around IntersectionObserver).</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q2 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q2-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q2" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q2">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 2. How would you build a drag-and-drop sortable list?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q2" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q2-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>Overview:</strong> A sortable list allows users to reorder items via drag-and-drop. The two primary library choices are <strong>dnd-kit</strong> (modern, accessible, pointer + touch) and <strong>react-beautiful-dnd</strong> (older but battle-tested). Building from scratch uses the HTML5 Drag and Drop API.</p>

      <p><strong>State structure:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li><code>items: T[]</code> — ordered array; reorder by swapping indices on drop</li>
        <li><code>activeId: string | null</code> — the item being dragged (for visual feedback)</li>
      </ul>

      <p><strong>dnd-kit approach (recommended):</strong></p>
<pre class="code-block">import {
  DndContext, closestCenter, PointerSensor,
  useSensor, useSensors, DragEndEvent
} from '@dnd-kit/core';
import {
  SortableContext, verticalListSortingStrategy,
  useSortable, arrayMove
} from '@dnd-kit/sortable';
import { CSS } from '@dnd-kit/utilities';

function SortableItem({ id, label }: { id: string; label: string }) {
  const { attributes, listeners, setNodeRef, transform, transition, isDragging } =
    useSortable({ id });

  const style = {
    transform: CSS.Transform.toString(transform),
    transition,
    opacity: isDragging ? 0.5 : 1,
  };

  return (
    &lt;div ref={setNodeRef} style={style} {...attributes} {...listeners}
      className="sortable-item"&gt;
      {label}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}

export function SortableList() {
  const [items, setItems] = useState([
    { id: '1', label: 'Item A' },
    { id: '2', label: 'Item B' },
    { id: '3', label: 'Item C' },
  ]);

  const sensors = useSensors(useSensor(PointerSensor));

  function handleDragEnd(event: DragEndEvent) {
    const { active, over } = event;
    if (over &amp;&amp; active.id !== over.id) {
      setItems(prev =&gt; {
        const oldIndex = prev.findIndex(i =&gt; i.id === active.id);
        const newIndex = prev.findIndex(i =&gt; i.id === over.id);
        return arrayMove(prev, oldIndex, newIndex);
      });
    }
  }

  return (
    &lt;DndContext sensors={sensors} collisionDetection={closestCenter}
      onDragEnd={handleDragEnd}&gt;
      &lt;SortableContext items={items.map(i =&gt; i.id)}
        strategy={verticalListSortingStrategy}&gt;
        {items.map(item =&gt; (
          &lt;SortableItem key={item.id} id={item.id} label={item.label} /&gt;
        ))}
      &lt;/SortableContext&gt;
    &lt;/DndContext&gt;
  );
}</pre>

      <p><strong>Persisting order:</strong> After <code>handleDragEnd</code>, POST the new ordered IDs to the server. Optimistic update locally first, then confirm or rollback on server error.</p>

      <p><strong>Performance &amp; accessibility:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li>dnd-kit uses pointer events (not HTML5 DnD) — works on touch devices.</li>
        <li>Announce drag operations with ARIA live regions for screen readers.</li>
        <li>Memoize <code>SortableItem</code> with <code>React.memo</code> to avoid re-rendering every item on each drag-over event.</li>
        <li>For multi-container (Kanban), use <code>DragOverlay</code> for a smooth drag preview.</li>
      </ul>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q3 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q3-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q3" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q3">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 3. How would you implement real-time data updates (e.g., a live dashboard)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q3" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q3-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>Transport options:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li><strong>WebSocket</strong> — full-duplex, lowest latency, best for high-frequency updates.</li>
        <li><strong>Server-Sent Events (SSE)</strong> — server-to-client only, auto-reconnect, simpler than WS.</li>
        <li><strong>Polling</strong> — simplest, highest server load; use as fallback.</li>
      </ul>

      <p><strong>Custom hook for WebSocket:</strong></p>
<pre class="code-block">// useWebSocket.ts
import { useEffect, useRef, useState, useCallback } from 'react';

export function useWebSocket&lt;T&gt;(url: string) {
  const ws = useRef&lt;WebSocket | null&gt;(null);
  const [data, setData] = useState&lt;T | null&gt;(null);
  const [status, setStatus] = useState&lt;'connecting'|'open'|'closed'&gt;('connecting');

  const connect = useCallback(() => {
    ws.current = new WebSocket(url);
    ws.current.onopen = () => setStatus('open');
    ws.current.onmessage = (e) => setData(JSON.parse(e.data) as T);
    ws.current.onclose = () => {
      setStatus('closed');
      setTimeout(connect, 3000); // reconnect after 3s
    };
  }, [url]);

  useEffect(() => {
    connect();
    return () => {
      ws.current?.close();
    };
  }, [connect]);

  const send = useCallback((msg: unknown) => {
    ws.current?.send(JSON.stringify(msg));
  }, []);

  return { data, status, send };
}

// Dashboard.tsx
function LiveDashboard() {
  const { data: metrics } = useWebSocket&lt;DashboardMetrics&gt;(
    'wss://api.example.com/dashboard'
  );

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;MetricCard label="Active Users" value={metrics?.activeUsers} /&gt;
      &lt;MetricCard label="Orders/min" value={metrics?.ordersPerMin} /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>

      <p><strong>State management at scale:</strong> For multiple subscribers to the same WS stream, lift the socket to a Context provider so the connection is shared. Dispatch incoming messages to a Redux slice or Zustand store so any component can subscribe.</p>

      <p><strong>Performance considerations:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li>Throttle or debounce high-frequency updates (e.g., max 60fps) using <code>requestAnimationFrame</code> or a time-based gate.</li>
        <li>Use <code>React.memo</code> and selector granularity to avoid re-rendering the entire dashboard on every message.</li>
        <li>For charts, update data via refs and call the chart library's own update method rather than triggering a React re-render.</li>
      </ul>

      <p><strong>Libraries:</strong> <code>socket.io-client</code>, <code>@tanstack/react-query</code> with <code>refetchInterval</code> for polling, <code>use-sse</code> for SSE.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q4 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q4-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q4" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q4">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 4. Design a multi-step form wizard with validation and back navigation.
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q4" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q4-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>State structure:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li><code>currentStep: number</code> — 0-indexed active step</li>
        <li><code>formData: Record&lt;string, unknown&gt;</code> — merged data across all steps</li>
        <li><code>errors: Record&lt;string, string&gt;</code> — per-field validation errors</li>
        <li><code>completedSteps: Set&lt;number&gt;</code> — enables step indicator UI</li>
      </ul>

      <p><strong>Component hierarchy:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li><code>WizardProvider</code> (Context) — owns state and exposes <code>next</code>, <code>back</code>, <code>updateData</code></li>
        <li><code>WizardShell</code> — renders step indicator + active step component</li>
        <li><code>Step1PersonalInfo</code>, <code>Step2Address</code>, <code>Step3Review</code> — individual step forms</li>
      </ul>

<pre class="code-block">// wizard-context.tsx
interface WizardState {
  step: number;
  data: Partial&lt;FormData&gt;;
  next: (stepData: Partial&lt;FormData&gt;) => void;
  back: () => void;
}

const WizardContext = createContext&lt;WizardState | null&gt;(null);

export function WizardProvider({ children }: { children: React.ReactNode }) {
  const [step, setStep] = useState(0);
  const [data, setData] = useState&lt;Partial&lt;FormData&gt;&gt;({});

  const next = (stepData: Partial&lt;FormData&gt;) => {
    setData(prev =&gt; ({ ...prev, ...stepData }));
    setStep(s =&gt; s + 1);
  };
  const back = () => setStep(s =&gt; Math.max(0, s - 1));

  return (
    &lt;WizardContext.Provider value={{ step, data, next, back }}&gt;
      {children}
    &lt;/WizardContext.Provider&gt;
  );
}

// Step1.tsx — using react-hook-form
function Step1PersonalInfo() {
  const { data, next } = useWizard();
  const { register, handleSubmit, formState: { errors } } =
    useForm({ defaultValues: data });

  return (
    &lt;form onSubmit={handleSubmit(next)}&gt;
      &lt;input {...register('firstName', { required: 'Required' })} /&gt;
      {errors.firstName &amp;&amp; &lt;span&gt;{errors.firstName.message}&lt;/span&gt;}
      &lt;button type="submit"&gt;Next&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>

      <p><strong>Validation strategy:</strong> Use <strong>react-hook-form</strong> per step with <strong>Zod</strong> schemas. Each step validates only its own fields before calling <code>next()</code>. Store step data in context so the Review step can display everything.</p>

      <p><strong>Back navigation:</strong> Calling <code>back()</code> decrements the step. Since data is in context, previously entered values are preserved when the user returns to a step (pass <code>defaultValues</code> from context to each form).</p>

      <p><strong>URL sync:</strong> Optionally sync step to URL query param (<code>?step=2</code>) so users can bookmark or share progress.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q5 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q5-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q5" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q5">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 5. How would you implement a global notification/toast system?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q5" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q5-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>Design goals:</strong> Any component (or even non-React code like an Axios interceptor) should be able to trigger a toast without prop-drilling.</p>

      <p><strong>Approach — Context + imperative API:</strong></p>
<pre class="code-block">// toast-context.tsx
interface Toast { id: string; message: string; type: 'success'|'error'|'info'; }

const ToastContext = createContext&lt;{
  addToast: (msg: string, type: Toast['type']) =&gt; void;
} | null&gt;(null);

export function ToastProvider({ children }: { children: React.ReactNode }) {
  const [toasts, setToasts] = useState&lt;Toast[]&gt;([]);

  const addToast = useCallback((message: string, type: Toast['type']) =&gt; {
    const id = crypto.randomUUID();
    setToasts(prev =&gt; [...prev, { id, message, type }]);
    setTimeout(() =&gt; removeToast(id), 4000); // auto-dismiss
  }, []);

  const removeToast = useCallback((id: string) =&gt; {
    setToasts(prev =&gt; prev.filter(t =&gt; t.id !== id));
  }, []);

  return (
    &lt;ToastContext.Provider value={{ addToast }}&gt;
      {children}
      &lt;ToastContainer toasts={toasts} onDismiss={removeToast} /&gt;
    &lt;/ToastContext.Provider&gt;
  );
}

// ToastContainer renders fixed-position toasts
function ToastContainer({ toasts, onDismiss }: { toasts: Toast[]; onDismiss: (id:string)=>void }) {
  return createPortal(
    &lt;div style={{ position: 'fixed', bottom: 24, right: 24, zIndex: 9999 }}&gt;
      {toasts.map(t =&gt; (
        &lt;div key={t.id} className={`toast toast-${t.type}`}&gt;
          {t.message}
          &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; onDismiss(t.id)}&gt;×&lt;/button&gt;
        &lt;/div&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/div&gt;,
    document.body
  );
}

// Usage
const { addToast } = useToast();
addToast('Saved successfully', 'success');</pre>

      <p><strong>Imperative API for non-React code:</strong> Export a singleton event emitter or a plain module with a <code>toast.success()</code> function that dispatches to the context via a custom event on <code>window</code>. The provider listens and calls <code>addToast</code>.</p>

      <p><strong>Animation:</strong> Wrap toasts in a <code>TransitionGroup</code> (react-transition-group) or Framer Motion <code>AnimatePresence</code> for enter/exit animations.</p>

      <p><strong>Libraries:</strong> <code>react-hot-toast</code>, <code>react-toastify</code> — both provide this pattern out of the box.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q6 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q6-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q6" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q6">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 6. How would you build a typeahead/autocomplete search component?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q6" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q6-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>Key concerns:</strong> debouncing API calls, keyboard navigation (ARIA combobox pattern), cancelling stale requests, loading/empty states.</p>

<pre class="code-block">function useDebounce&lt;T&gt;(value: T, delay: number): T {
  const [debounced, setDebounced] = useState(value);
  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const id = setTimeout(() =&gt; setDebounced(value), delay);
    return () =&gt; clearTimeout(id);
  }, [value, delay]);
  return debounced;
}

function Autocomplete({ onSelect }: { onSelect: (item: Item) =&gt; void }) {
  const [query, setQuery] = useState('');
  const [results, setResults] = useState&lt;Item[]&gt;([]);
  const [activeIndex, setActiveIndex] = useState(-1);
  const [loading, setLoading] = useState(false);
  const debouncedQuery = useDebounce(query, 300);

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (!debouncedQuery.trim()) { setResults([]); return; }
    const controller = new AbortController();
    setLoading(true);
    fetchSuggestions(debouncedQuery, controller.signal)
      .then(setResults)
      .catch(() =&gt; {}) // aborted requests throw
      .finally(() =&gt; setLoading(false));
    return () =&gt; controller.abort();
  }, [debouncedQuery]);

  function handleKeyDown(e: React.KeyboardEvent) {
    if (e.key === 'ArrowDown') setActiveIndex(i =&gt; Math.min(i + 1, results.length - 1));
    if (e.key === 'ArrowUp') setActiveIndex(i =&gt; Math.max(i - 1, 0));
    if (e.key === 'Enter' &amp;&amp; activeIndex &gt;= 0) onSelect(results[activeIndex]);
    if (e.key === 'Escape') setResults([]);
  }

  return (
    &lt;div role="combobox" aria-expanded={results.length &gt; 0} aria-haspopup="listbox"&gt;
      &lt;input value={query} onChange={e =&gt; setQuery(e.target.value)}
        onKeyDown={handleKeyDown} aria-autocomplete="list"
        aria-activedescendant={activeIndex &gt;= 0 ? `opt-${activeIndex}` : undefined} /&gt;
      {loading &amp;&amp; &lt;span&gt;Loading...&lt;/span&gt;}
      &lt;ul role="listbox"&gt;
        {results.map((item, i) =&gt; (
          &lt;li key={item.id} id={`opt-${i}`} role="option"
            aria-selected={i === activeIndex}
            onClick={() =&gt; onSelect(item)}&gt;
            {item.label}
          &lt;/li&gt;
        ))}
      &lt;/ul&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>

      <p><strong>Performance:</strong> The <code>AbortController</code> pattern ensures that if the user types quickly, only the latest request's result is applied. Memoize result list items.</p>
      <p><strong>Libraries:</strong> <code>downshift</code> (headless), <code>@radix-ui/react-combobox</code>, <code>react-select</code>.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q7 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q7-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q7" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q7">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 7. How would you implement a data table with sorting, filtering, and pagination?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q7" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q7-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>Two modes:</strong> client-side (all data loaded, filter/sort in JS) vs. server-side (pass sort/filter/page params to API). Choose server-side for large datasets.</p>

      <p><strong>State structure (server-side):</strong></p>
<pre class="code-block">interface TableState {
  page: number;
  pageSize: number;
  sortBy: string;
  sortDir: 'asc' | 'desc';
  filters: Record&lt;string, string&gt;;
}

function DataTable() {
  const [tableState, setTableState] = useState&lt;TableState&gt;({
    page: 1, pageSize: 20, sortBy: 'createdAt', sortDir: 'desc', filters: {}
  });

  const { data, isLoading } = useQuery({
    queryKey: ['table', tableState],
    queryFn: () =&gt; fetchTableData(tableState),
    keepPreviousData: true, // avoids flash on page change
  });

  const handleSort = (column: string) =&gt;
    setTableState(prev =&gt; ({
      ...prev,
      sortBy: column,
      sortDir: prev.sortBy === column &amp;&amp; prev.sortDir === 'asc' ? 'desc' : 'asc',
      page: 1,
    }));

  const handleFilter = (key: string, value: string) =&gt;
    setTableState(prev =&gt; ({ ...prev, filters: { ...prev.filters, [key]: value }, page: 1 }));

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;FilterBar filters={tableState.filters} onChange={handleFilter} /&gt;
      &lt;table&gt;
        &lt;thead&gt;
          {columns.map(col =&gt; (
            &lt;th key={col.key} onClick={() =&gt; handleSort(col.key)}&gt;
              {col.label}
              {tableState.sortBy === col.key ? (tableState.sortDir === 'asc' ? '↑' : '↓') : ''}
            &lt;/th&gt;
          ))}
        &lt;/thead&gt;
        &lt;tbody&gt;
          {data?.rows.map(row =&gt; &lt;TableRow key={row.id} row={row} /&gt;)}
        &lt;/tbody&gt;
      &lt;/table&gt;
      &lt;Pagination total={data?.total} page={tableState.page}
        pageSize={tableState.pageSize}
        onChange={page =&gt; setTableState(prev =&gt; ({ ...prev, page }))} /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>

      <p><strong>Performance:</strong> <code>keepPreviousData: true</code> in TanStack Query keeps old data visible while new data loads. Memoize <code>TableRow</code>. For very large client-side datasets use <code>@tanstack/react-table</code> (headless) with virtual rows via <code>react-virtual</code>.</p>
      <p><strong>Libraries:</strong> <code>@tanstack/react-table</code> (TanStack Table v8), AG Grid, MUI DataGrid.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q8 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q8-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q8" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q8">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 8. How would you build a modal/dialog system that can be triggered from anywhere?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q8" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q8-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>Pattern — Modal Registry via Context:</strong> Register modal components by name; open by name + props from any component.</p>
<pre class="code-block">// modal-context.tsx
type ModalConfig = { component: React.ComponentType&lt;any&gt;; props: Record&lt;string, unknown&gt; };

const ModalContext = createContext&lt;{
  openModal: (id: string, props?: Record&lt;string, unknown&gt;) =&gt; void;
  closeModal: (id: string) =&gt; void;
} | null&gt;(null);

const MODAL_REGISTRY: Record&lt;string, React.ComponentType&lt;any&gt;&gt; = {
  confirm: ConfirmModal,
  userEdit: UserEditModal,
};

export function ModalProvider({ children }: { children: React.ReactNode }) {
  const [activeModals, setActiveModals] = useState&lt;ModalConfig[]&gt;([]);

  const openModal = useCallback((id: string, props = {}) =&gt; {
    const component = MODAL_REGISTRY[id];
    if (!component) throw new Error(`No modal registered: ${id}`);
    setActiveModals(prev =&gt; [...prev, { component, props }]);
  }, []);

  const closeModal = useCallback((id: string) =&gt; {
    setActiveModals(prev =&gt; prev.filter((_, i) =&gt; i !== prev.length - 1));
  }, []);

  return (
    &lt;ModalContext.Provider value={{ openModal, closeModal }}&gt;
      {children}
      {activeModals.map(({ component: Modal, props }, i) =&gt;
        createPortal(&lt;Modal key={i} {...props} onClose={() =&gt; closeModal('')} /&gt;, document.body)
      )}
    &lt;/ModalContext.Provider&gt;
  );
}

// Usage from any component
const { openModal } = useModal();
openModal('confirm', { message: 'Delete this item?', onConfirm: handleDelete });</pre>
      <p><strong>Accessibility:</strong> Modals must trap focus (<code>focus-trap-react</code>), set <code>role="dialog"</code>, <code>aria-modal="true"</code>, and return focus on close. Use <code>@radix-ui/react-dialog</code> for a fully accessible headless implementation.</p>
      <p><strong>Portal rendering</strong> ensures z-index stacking is not affected by parent CSS.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 9. How would you implement authentication flow with Okta/Auth0?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q9" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q9-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">

      <p><strong>PKCE flow overview:</strong> React SPA uses Authorization Code + PKCE. The library handles redirects, token storage, and silent renewal.</p>
<pre class="code-block">// main.tsx — Okta setup
import { OktaAuth } from '@okta/okta-auth-js';
import { Security } from '@okta/okta-react';

const oktaAuth = new OktaAuth({
  issuer: 'https://your-domain.okta.com/oauth2/default',
  clientId: import.meta.env.VITE_OKTA_CLIENT_ID,
  redirectUri: `${window.location.origin}/login/callback`,
  scopes: ['openid', 'profile', 'email'],
  pkce: true,
});

function App() {
  return (
    &lt;Security oktaAuth={oktaAuth} restoreOriginalUri={restoreOriginalUri}&gt;
      &lt;Router&gt;
        &lt;Route path="/login/callback" element={&lt;LoginCallback /&gt;} /&gt;
        &lt;Route path="/dashboard" element={&lt;RequiredAuth /&gt;&gt;
          &lt;Dashboard /&gt;
        &lt;/Route&gt;
      &lt;/Router&gt;
    &lt;/Security&gt;
  );
}

// Protected route guard
function RequiredAuth({ children }: { children: React.ReactNode }) {
  const { authState } = useOktaAuth();
  if (!authState?.isAuthenticated) return &lt;Navigate to="/login" /&gt;;
  return &lt;&gt;{children}&lt;/&gt;;
}

// Attaching token to API calls
const { oktaAuth } = useOktaAuth();
const token = await oktaAuth.getAccessToken();
axios.defaults.headers.common['Authorization'] = `Bearer ${token}`;</pre>
      <p><strong>Token refresh:</strong> Okta SDK handles silent token renewal automatically. Set up an Axios interceptor to catch 401s, force a token refresh, and retry the request.</p>
      <p><strong>Role-based access:</strong> Decode the JWT claims (<code>groups</code> or custom claims) from <code>authState.idToken.claims</code> and store roles in Redux for use in <code>AuthRestricted</code> route wrappers.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q10 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q10-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q10" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q10">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 10. How would you build a real-time collaborative editing feature?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q10" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q10-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>Core challenge:</strong> Multiple users editing simultaneously causes conflicts. Two main algorithms solve this: <strong>Operational Transformation (OT)</strong> and <strong>CRDTs</strong> (Conflict-free Replicated Data Types).</p>
      <p><strong>Architecture:</strong></p>
      <ul>
        <li>Client sends operations (not full document) over WebSocket.</li>
        <li>Server transforms concurrent ops using OT or merges with CRDT logic.</li>
        <li>All clients receive the transformed op and apply it locally.</li>
      </ul>
<pre class="code-block">// Using Yjs (CRDT library) + y-websocket
import * as Y from 'yjs';
import { WebsocketProvider } from 'y-websocket';
import { useEffect, useRef } from 'react';

function CollaborativeEditor({ docId }: { docId: string }) {
  const editorRef = useRef&lt;HTMLDivElement&gt;(null);

  useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const ydoc = new Y.Doc();
    const provider = new WebsocketProvider(
      'wss://collab.example.com', docId, ydoc
    );
    const ytext = ydoc.getText('content');

    // Awareness = cursor positions of other users
    provider.awareness.setLocalStateField('user', {
      name: 'Alice', color: '#ff0000'
    });

    // Bind to a rich text editor (e.g. Quill, TipTap)
    const binding = new QuillBinding(ytext, quillInstance, provider.awareness);

    return () =&gt; {
      binding.destroy();
      provider.disconnect();
      ydoc.destroy();
    };
  }, [docId]);

  return &lt;div ref={editorRef} /&gt;;
}</pre>
      <p><strong>Presence indicators:</strong> Use <code>provider.awareness</code> to broadcast and receive cursor positions and user colors. Render remote cursors as absolutely-positioned overlays in the editor.</p>
      <p><strong>Libraries:</strong> <code>yjs</code> + <code>y-websocket</code>, <code>ShareDB</code> (OT), <code>liveblocks</code> (hosted CRDT service), TipTap editor (built-in Yjs support).</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 11. How would you implement a shopping cart with persistence?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q11" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q11-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">

      <p><strong>State structure:</strong></p>
<pre class="code-block">interface CartItem { id: string; name: string; price: number; qty: number; }
interface CartState { items: CartItem[]; }

// Redux slice with localStorage middleware
const cartSlice = createSlice({
  name: 'cart',
  initialState: loadFromStorage&lt;CartState&gt;('cart', { items: [] }),
  reducers: {
    addItem(state, action: PayloadAction&lt;CartItem&gt;) {
      const existing = state.items.find(i =&gt; i.id === action.payload.id);
      if (existing) existing.qty += action.payload.qty;
      else state.items.push(action.payload);
    },
    removeItem(state, action: PayloadAction&lt;string&gt;) {
      state.items = state.items.filter(i =&gt; i.id !== action.payload);
    },
    clearCart(state) { state.items = []; },
  },
});

// Persist middleware
const persistMiddleware: Middleware = store =&gt; next =&gt; action =&gt; {
  const result = next(action);
  localStorage.setItem('cart', JSON.stringify(store.getState().cart));
  return result;
};

function loadFromStorage&lt;T&gt;(key: string, fallback: T): T {
  try { return JSON.parse(localStorage.getItem(key) ?? '') ?? fallback; }
  catch { return fallback; }
}</pre>
      <p><strong>Derived selectors:</strong></p>
<pre class="code-block">const selectCartTotal = createSelector(
  (s: RootState) =&gt; s.cart.items,
  items =&gt; items.reduce((sum, i) =&gt; sum + i.price * i.qty, 0)
);</pre>
      <p><strong>Persistence options:</strong> localStorage for guest carts; sync to server on login so cart survives devices. Use <code>redux-persist</code> for a battle-tested persistence layer that handles hydration, versioning, and migration.</p>
      <p><strong>Optimistic updates:</strong> For logged-in users, update Redux immediately and fire a background PATCH to the server. On error, rollback the Redux state and show a toast.</p>
    </div>
  </div>

<!-- Q12 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q12-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q12" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q12">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 12. How would you build a file upload component with progress tracking?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q12" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q12-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>Key requirements:</strong> drag-and-drop zone, file type/size validation, per-file progress bars, cancel/retry.</p>
<pre class="code-block">interface UploadFile {
  id: string; file: File; progress: number;
  status: 'pending' | 'uploading' | 'done' | 'error';
  abortController: AbortController;
}

function FileUploader() {
  const [uploads, setUploads] = useState&lt;UploadFile[]&gt;([]);

  const updateFile = (id: string, patch: Partial&lt;UploadFile&gt;) =&gt;
    setUploads(prev =&gt; prev.map(f =&gt; f.id === id ? { ...f, ...patch } : f));

  async function uploadFile(uf: UploadFile) {
    updateFile(uf.id, { status: 'uploading' });
    const formData = new FormData();
    formData.append('file', uf.file);

    try {
      await axios.post('/api/upload', formData, {
        signal: uf.abortController.signal,
        onUploadProgress: (evt) =&gt; {
          const progress = Math.round((evt.loaded / (evt.total ?? 1)) * 100);
          updateFile(uf.id, { progress });
        },
      });
      updateFile(uf.id, { status: 'done', progress: 100 });
    } catch (e) {
      if (!axios.isCancel(e)) updateFile(uf.id, { status: 'error' });
    }
  }

  function handleDrop(e: React.DragEvent) {
    e.preventDefault();
    const files = Array.from(e.dataTransfer.files).map(file =&gt; ({
      id: crypto.randomUUID(), file, progress: 0,
      status: 'pending' as const,
      abortController: new AbortController(),
    }));
    setUploads(prev =&gt; [...prev, ...files]);
    files.forEach(uploadFile);
  }

  return (
    &lt;div onDrop={handleDrop} onDragOver={e =&gt; e.preventDefault()}
      className="drop-zone"&gt;
      Drop files here
      {uploads.map(uf =&gt; (
        &lt;div key={uf.id}&gt;
          {uf.file.name} — {uf.status}
          &lt;progress value={uf.progress} max={100} /&gt;
          &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; uf.abortController.abort()}&gt;Cancel&lt;/button&gt;
        &lt;/div&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p><strong>Chunked uploads:</strong> For large files, split with <code>file.slice(start, end)</code> and upload chunks sequentially or in parallel, tracking chunk-level progress. Use a resumable upload protocol (tus.io) for reliability.</p>
      <p><strong>Libraries:</strong> <code>react-dropzone</code>, <code>uppy</code>, <code>filepond</code>.</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<!-- Q13 -->
<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header" id="scenarios-q13-heading">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q13" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q13">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp; 13. How would you implement a virtualized list for 100,000 items?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="scenarios-q13" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="scenarios-q13-heading" data-bs-parent="#acc-scenarios">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <p><strong>The problem:</strong> Rendering 100,000 DOM nodes causes layout thrashing and extreme memory usage. Virtualization renders only the items visible in the viewport plus a small buffer.</p>
<pre class="code-block">// react-virtual (TanStack Virtual v3)
import { useVirtualizer } from '@tanstack/react-virtual';

function VirtualList({ items }: { items: Item[] }) {
  const parentRef = useRef&lt;HTMLDivElement&gt;(null);

  const virtualizer = useVirtualizer({
    count: items.length,
    getScrollElement: () =&gt; parentRef.current,
    estimateSize: () =&gt; 50,       // estimated row height in px
    overscan: 10,                  // extra rows above/below viewport
  });

  return (
    &lt;div ref={parentRef} style={{ height: '600px', overflowY: 'auto' }}&gt;
      {/* Total scrollable height */}
      &lt;div style={{ height: virtualizer.getTotalSize(), position: 'relative' }}&gt;
        {virtualizer.getVirtualItems().map(vItem =&gt; (
          &lt;div key={vItem.key}
            style={{
              position: 'absolute',
              top: 0,
              transform: `translateY(${vItem.start}px)`,
              height: `${vItem.size}px`,
              width: '100%',
            }}&gt;
            &lt;ListRow item={items[vItem.index]} /&gt;
          &lt;/div&gt;
        ))}
      &lt;/div&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
      <p><strong>Variable heights:</strong> Use <code>measureElement</code> callback from TanStack Virtual to measure actual row heights after render, enabling accurate scroll calculation for rows with dynamic content.</p>
      <p><strong>Combining with infinite scroll:</strong> When the last virtual item is near the bottom, trigger <code>fetchNextPage()</code> from TanStack Query's <code>useInfiniteQuery</code>.</p>
      <p><strong>Libraries:</strong> <code>@tanstack/react-virtual</code>, <code>react-window</code>, <code>react-virtuoso</code> (easiest API for variable heights).</p>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">

    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#scenarios-q14" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="scenarios-q14">
